CHAPTER XXXVIII    The Bend in the road\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nANNE OF GREEN GABLES\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER I. Mrs. Rachel Lynde is Surprised\r\n\r\n\r\n|MRS. Rachel Lynde lived just where the Avonlea main road dipped down\r\ninto a little hollow, fringed with alders and ladies\xe2\x80\x99 eardrops and\r\ntraversed by a brook that had its source away back in the woods of the\r\nold Cuthbert place; it was reputed to be an intricate, headlong brook\r\nin its earlier course through those woods, with dark secrets of pool\r\nand cascade; but by the time it reached Lynde\xe2\x80\x99s Hollow it was a quiet,\r\nwell-conducted little stream, for not even a brook could run past Mrs.\r\nRachel Lynde\xe2\x80\x99s door without due regard for decency and decorum; it\r\nprobably was conscious that Mrs. Rachel was sitting at her window,\r\nkeeping a sharp eye on everything that passed, from brooks and children\r\nup, and that if she noticed anything odd or out of place she would never\r\nrest until she had ferreted out the whys and wherefores thereof.\r\n\r\nThere are plenty of people in Avonlea and out of it, who can attend\r\nclosely to their neighbor\xe2\x80\x99s business by dint of neglecting their own;\r\nbut Mrs. Rachel Lynde was one of those capable creatures who can manage\r\ntheir own concerns and those of other folks into the bargain. She was a\r\nnotable housewife; her work was always done and well done; she \xe2\x80\x9cran\xe2\x80\x9d the\r\nSewing Circle, helped run the Sunday-school, and was the strongest prop\r\nof the Church Aid Society and Foreign Missions Auxiliary. Yet with all\r\nthis Mrs. Rachel found abundant time to sit for hours at her kitchen\r\nwindow, knitting \xe2\x80\x9ccotton warp\xe2\x80\x9d quilts--she had knitted sixteen of them,\r\nas Avonlea housekeepers were wont to tell in awed voices--and keeping\r\na sharp eye on the main road that crossed the hollow and wound up\r\nthe steep red hill beyond. Since Avonlea occupied a little triangular\r\npeninsula jutting out into the Gulf of St. Lawrence with water on two\r\nsides of it, anybody who went out of it or into it had to pass over that\r\nhill road and so run the unseen gauntlet of Mrs. Rachel\xe2\x80\x99s all-seeing\r\neye.\r\n\r\nShe was sitting there one afternoon in early June. The sun was coming in\r\nat the window warm and bright; the orchard on the slope below the house\r\nwas in a bridal flush of pinky-white bloom, hummed over by a myriad of\r\nbees. Thomas Lynde--a meek little man whom Avonlea people called \xe2\x80\x9cRachel\r\nLynde\xe2\x80\x99s husband\xe2\x80\x9d--was sowing his late turnip seed on the hill field\r\nbeyond the barn; and Matthew Cuthbert ought to have been sowing his on\r\nthe big red brook field away over by Green Gables. Mrs. Rachel knew\r\nthat he ought because she had heard him tell Peter Morrison the evening\r\nbefore in William J. Blair\xe2\x80\x99s store over at Carmody that he meant to sow\r\nhis turnip seed the next afternoon. Peter had asked him, of course, for\r\nMatthew Cuthbert had never been known to volunteer information about\r\nanything in his whole life.\r\n\r\nAnd yet here was Matthew Cuthbert, at half-past three on the afternoon\r\nof a busy day, placidly driving over the hollow and up the hill;\r\nmoreover, he wore a white collar and his best suit of clothes, which was\r\nplain proof that he was going out of Avonlea; and he had the buggy\r\nand the sorrel mare, which betokened that he was going a considerable\r\ndistance. Now, where was Matthew Cuthbert going and why was he going\r\nthere?\r\n\r\nHad it been any other man in Avonlea, Mrs. Rachel, deftly putting this\r\nand that together, might have given a pretty good guess as to both\r\nquestions. But Matthew so rarely went from home that it must be\r\nsomething pressing and unusual which was taking him; he was the shyest\r\nman alive and hated to have to go among strangers or to any place where\r\nhe might have to talk. Matthew, dressed up with a white collar and\r\ndriving in a buggy, was something that didn\xe2\x80\x99t happen often. Mrs. Rachel,\r\nponder as she might, could make nothing of it and her afternoon\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nenjoyment was spoiled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll just step over to Green Gables after tea and find out from Marilla\r\nwhere he\xe2\x80\x99s gone and why,\xe2\x80\x9d the worthy woman finally concluded. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\r\ndoesn\xe2\x80\x99t generally go to town this time of year and he _never_ visits; if\r\nhe\xe2\x80\x99d run out of turnip seed he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t dress up and take the buggy to\r\ngo for more; he wasn\xe2\x80\x99t driving fast enough to be going for a doctor.\r\nYet something must have happened since last night to start him off. I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\nclean puzzled, that\xe2\x80\x99s what, and I won\xe2\x80\x99t know a minute\xe2\x80\x99s peace of mind or\r\nconscience until I know what has taken Matthew Cuthbert out of Avonlea\r\ntoday.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAccordingly after tea Mrs. Rachel set out; she had not far to go; the\r\nbig, rambling, orchard-embowered house where the Cuthberts lived was a\r\nscant quarter of a mile up the road from Lynde\xe2\x80\x99s Hollow. To be sure, the\r\nlong lane made it a good deal further. Matthew Cuthbert\xe2\x80\x99s father, as\r\nshy and silent as his son after him, had got as far away as he possibly\r\ncould from his fellow men without actually retreating into the woods\r\nwhen he founded his homestead. Green Gables was built at the furthest\r\nedge of his cleared land and there it was to this day, barely visible\r\nfrom the main road along which all the other Avonlea houses were so\r\nsociably situated. Mrs. Rachel Lynde did not call living in such a place\r\n_living_ at all.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s just _staying_, that\xe2\x80\x99s what,\xe2\x80\x9d she said as she stepped along the\r\ndeep-rutted, grassy lane bordered with wild rose bushes. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s no wonder\r\nMatthew and Marilla are both a little odd, living away back here by\r\nthemselves. Trees aren\xe2\x80\x99t much company, though dear knows if they were\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x99d be enough of them. I\xe2\x80\x99d ruther look at people. To be sure, they\r\nseem contented enough; but then, I suppose, they\xe2\x80\x99re used to it. A body\r\ncan get used to anything, even to being hanged, as the Irishman said.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWith this Mrs. Rachel stepped out of the lane into the backyard of Green\r\nGables. Very green and neat and precise was that yard, set about on one\r\nside with great patriarchal willows and the other with prim Lombardies.\r\nNot a stray stick nor stone was to be seen, for Mrs. Rachel would have\r\nseen it if there had been. Privately she was of the opinion that Marilla\r\nCuthbert swept that yard over as often as she swept her house. One could\r\nhave eaten a meal off the ground without over-brimming the proverbial\r\npeck of dirt.\r\n\r\nMrs. Rachel rapped smartly at the kitchen door and stepped in\r\nwhen bidden to do so. The kitchen at Green Gables was a cheerful\r\napartment--or would have been cheerful if it had not been so painfully\r\nclean as to give it something of the appearance of an unused parlor. Its\r\nwindows looked east and west; through the west one, looking out on\r\nthe back yard, came a flood of mellow June sunlight; but the east one,\r\nwhence you got a glimpse of the bloom white cherry-trees in the left\r\norchard and nodding, slender birches down in the hollow by the brook,\r\nwas greened over by a tangle of vines. Here sat Marilla Cuthbert, when\r\nshe sat at all, always slightly distrustful of sunshine, which seemed to\r\nher too dancing and irresponsible a thing for a world which was meant to\r\nbe taken seriously; and here she sat now, knitting, and the table behind\r\nher was laid for supper.\r\n\r\nMrs. Rachel, before she had fairly closed the door, had taken a mental\r\nnote of everything that was on that table. There were three plates laid,\r\nso that Marilla must be expecting some one home with Matthew to tea; but\r\nthe dishes were everyday dishes and there was only crab-apple preserves\r\nand one kind of cake, so that the expected company could not be any\r\nparticular company. Yet what of Matthew\xe2\x80\x99s white collar and the sorrel\r\nmare? Mrs. Rachel was getting fairly dizzy with this unusual mystery\r\nabout quiet, unmysterious Green Gables.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood evening, Rachel,\xe2\x80\x9d Marilla said briskly. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is a real fine\r\nevening, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it? Won\xe2\x80\x99t you sit down? How are all your folks?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSomething that for lack of any other name might be called friendship\r\nexisted and always had existed between Marilla Cuthbert and Mrs. Rachel,\r\nin spite of--or perhaps because of--their dissimilarity.\r\n\r\nMarilla was a tall, thin woman, with angles and without curves; her dark\r\nhair showed some gray streaks and was always twisted up in a hard little\r\nknot behind with two wire hairpins stuck aggressively through it. She\r\nlooked like a woman of narrow experience and rigid conscience, which she\r\nwas; but there was a saving something about her mouth which, if it had\r\nbeen ever so slightly developed, might have been considered indicative\r\nof a sense of humor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re all pretty well,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Rachel. \xe2\x80\x9cI was kind of afraid _you_\r\nweren\xe2\x80\x99t, though, when I saw Matthew starting off today. I thought maybe\r\nhe was going to the doctor\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarilla\xe2\x80\x99s lips twitched understandingly. She had expected Mrs.\r\nRachel up; she had known that the sight of Matthew jaunting off so\r\nunaccountably would be too much for her neighbor\xe2\x80\x99s curiosity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, no, I\xe2\x80\x99m quite well although I had a bad headache yesterday,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nsaid. \xe2\x80\x9cMatthew went to Bright River. We\xe2\x80\x99re getting a little boy from an\r\norphan asylum in Nova Scotia and he\xe2\x80\x99s coming on the train tonight.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIf Marilla had said that Matthew had gone to Bright River to meet a\r\nkangaroo from Australia Mrs. Rachel could not have been more astonished.\r\nShe was actually stricken dumb for five seconds. It was unsupposable\r\nthat Marilla was making fun of her, but Mrs. Rachel was almost forced to\r\nsuppose it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you in earnest, Marilla?\xe2\x80\x9d she demanded when voice returned to her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, of course,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla, as if getting boys from orphan asylums\r\nin Nova Scotia were part of the usual spring work on any well-regulated\r\nAvonlea farm instead of being an unheard of innovation.\r\n\r\nMrs. Rachel felt that she had received a severe mental jolt. She thought\r\nin exclamation points. A boy! Marilla and Matthew Cuthbert of all people\r\nadopting a boy! From an orphan asylum! Well, the world was certainly\r\nturning upside down! She would be surprised at nothing after this!\r\nNothing!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat on earth put such a notion into your head?\xe2\x80\x9d she demanded\r\ndisapprovingly.\r\n\r\nThis had been done without her advice being asked, and must perforce be\r\ndisapproved.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, we\xe2\x80\x99ve been thinking about it for some time--all winter in fact,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n returned Marilla. \xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Alexander Spencer was up here one day before\r\nChristmas and she said she was going to get a little girl from the\r\nasylum over in Hopeton in the spring. Her cousin lives there and Mrs.\r\nSpencer has visited here and knows all about it. So Matthew and I have\r\ntalked it over off and on ever since. We thought we\xe2\x80\x99d get a boy. Matthew\r\nis getting up in years, you know--he\xe2\x80\x99s sixty--and he isn\xe2\x80\x99t so spry as he\r\nonce was. His heart troubles him a good deal. And you know how desperate\r\nhard it\xe2\x80\x99s got to be to get hired help. There\xe2\x80\x99s never anybody to be had\r\nbut those stupid, half-grown little French boys; and as soon as you do\r\nget one broke into your ways and taught something he\xe2\x80\x99s up and off to the\r\nlobster canneries or the States. At first Matthew suggested getting a\r\nHome boy. But I said \xe2\x80\x98no\xe2\x80\x99 flat to that. \xe2\x80\x98They may be all right--I\xe2\x80\x99m not\r\nsaying they\xe2\x80\x99re not--but no London street Arabs for me,\xe2\x80\x99 I said. \xe2\x80\x98Give\r\nme a native born at least. There\xe2\x80\x99ll be a risk, no matter who we get. But\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll feel easier in my mind and sleep sounder at nights if we get a born\r\nCanadian.\xe2\x80\x99 So in the end we decided to ask Mrs. Spencer to pick us out\r\none when she went over to get her little girl. We heard last week she\r\nwas going, so we sent her word by Richard Spencer\xe2\x80\x99s folks at Carmody\r\nto bring us a smart, likely boy of about ten or eleven. We decided that\r\nwould be the best age--old enough to be of some use in doing chores\r\nright off and young enough to be trained up proper. We mean to give him\r\na good home and schooling. We had a telegram from Mrs. Alexander Spencer\r\ntoday--the mail-man brought it from the station--saying they were coming\r\non the five-thirty train tonight. So Matthew went to Bright River to\r\nmeet him. Mrs. Spencer will drop him off there. Of course she goes on to\r\nWhite Sands station herself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Rachel prided herself on always speaking her mind; she proceeded to\r\nspeak it now, having adjusted her mental attitude to this amazing piece\r\nof news.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, Marilla, I\xe2\x80\x99ll just tell you plain that I think you\xe2\x80\x99re doing a\r\nmighty foolish thing--a risky thing, that\xe2\x80\x99s what. You don\xe2\x80\x99t know what\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99re getting. You\xe2\x80\x99re bringing a strange child into your house and home\r\nand you don\xe2\x80\x99t know a single thing about him nor what his disposition is\r\nlike nor what sort of parents he had nor how he\xe2\x80\x99s likely to turn out.\r\nWhy, it was only last week I read in the paper how a man and his wife up\r\nwest of the Island took a boy out of an orphan asylum and he set fire to\r\nthe house at night--set it _on purpose_, Marilla--and nearly burnt them to\r\na crisp in their beds. And I know another case where an adopted boy used\r\nto suck the eggs--they couldn\xe2\x80\x99t break him of it. If you had asked my\r\nadvice in the matter--which you didn\xe2\x80\x99t do, Marilla--I\xe2\x80\x99d have said for\r\nmercy\xe2\x80\x99s sake not to think of such a thing, that\xe2\x80\x99s what.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis Job\xe2\x80\x99s comforting seemed neither to offend nor to alarm Marilla. She\r\nknitted steadily on.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t deny there\xe2\x80\x99s something in what you say, Rachel. I\xe2\x80\x99ve had some\r\nqualms myself. But Matthew was terrible set on it. I could see that, so\r\nI gave in. It\xe2\x80\x99s so seldom Matthew sets his mind on anything that when he\r\ndoes I always feel it\xe2\x80\x99s my duty to give in. And as for the risk, there\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nrisks in pretty near everything a body does in this world. There\xe2\x80\x99s risks\r\nin people\xe2\x80\x99s having children of their own if it comes to that--they don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nalways turn out well. And then Nova Scotia is right close to the Island.\r\nIt isn\xe2\x80\x99t as if we were getting him from England or the States. He can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nbe much different from ourselves.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I hope it will turn out all right,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Rachel in a tone\r\nthat plainly indicated her painful doubts. \xe2\x80\x9cOnly don\xe2\x80\x99t say I didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nwarn you if he burns Green Gables down or puts strychnine in the well--I\r\nheard of a case over in New Brunswick where an orphan asylum child did\r\nthat and the whole family died in fearful agonies. Only, it was a girl\r\nin that instance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, we\xe2\x80\x99re not getting a girl,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla, as if poisoning wells\r\nwere a purely feminine accomplishment and not to be dreaded in the case\r\nof a boy. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d never dream of taking a girl to bring up. I wonder at\r\nMrs. Alexander Spencer for doing it. But there, _she_ wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t shrink\r\nfrom adopting a whole orphan asylum if she took it into her head.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Rachel would have liked to stay until Matthew came home with his\r\nimported orphan. But reflecting that it would be a good two hours at\r\nleast before his arrival she concluded to go up the road to Robert\r\nBell\xe2\x80\x99s and tell the news. It would certainly make a sensation second\r\nto none, and Mrs. Rachel dearly loved to make a sensation. So she took\r\nherself away, somewhat to Marilla\xe2\x80\x99s relief, for the latter felt\r\nher doubts and fears reviving under the influence of Mrs. Rachel\xe2\x80\x99s\r\npessimism.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, of all things that ever were or will be!\xe2\x80\x9d ejaculated Mrs. Rachel\r\nwhen she was safely out in the lane. \xe2\x80\x9cIt does really seem as if I must\r\nbe dreaming. Well, I\xe2\x80\x99m sorry for that poor young one and no mistake.\r\nMatthew and Marilla don\xe2\x80\x99t know anything about children and they\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nexpect him to be wiser and steadier that his own grandfather, if so be\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhe ever had a grandfather, which is doubtful. It seems uncanny to think\r\nof a child at Green Gables somehow; there\xe2\x80\x99s never been one there, for\r\nMatthew and Marilla were grown up when the new house was built--if they\r\never _were_ children, which is hard to believe when one looks at them.\r\nI wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be in that orphan\xe2\x80\x99s shoes for anything. My, but I pity him,\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x99s what.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo said Mrs. Rachel to the wild rose bushes out of the fulness of her\r\nheart; but if she could have seen the child who was waiting patiently\r\nat the Bright River station at that very moment her pity would have been\r\nstill deeper and more profound.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER II. Matthew Cuthbert is surprised\r\n\r\n\r\n|MATTHEW Cuthbert and the sorrel mare jogged comfortably over the eight\r\nmiles to Bright River. It was a pretty road, running along between\r\nsnug farmsteads, with now and again a bit of balsamy fir wood to drive\r\nthrough or a hollow where wild plums hung out their filmy bloom. The air\r\nwas sweet with the breath of many apple orchards and the meadows sloped\r\naway in the distance to horizon mists of pearl and purple; while\r\n\r\n     \xe2\x80\x9cThe little birds sang as if it were\r\n     The one day of summer in all the year.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMatthew enjoyed the drive after his own fashion, except during the\r\nmoments when he met women and had to nod to them--for in Prince Edward\r\nisland you are supposed to nod to all and sundry you meet on the road\r\nwhether you know them or not.\r\n\r\nMatthew dreaded all women except Marilla and Mrs. Rachel; he had an\r\nuncomfortable feeling that the mysterious creatures were secretly\r\nlaughing at him. He may have been quite right in thinking so, for he\r\nwas an odd-looking personage, with an ungainly figure and long iron-gray\r\nhair that touched his stooping shoulders, and a full, soft brown beard\r\nwhich he had worn ever since he was twenty. In fact, he had looked\r\nat twenty very much as he looked at sixty, lacking a little of the\r\ngrayness.\r\n\r\nWhen he reached Bright River there was no sign of any train; he thought\r\nhe was too early, so he tied his horse in the yard of the small Bright\r\nRiver hotel and went over to the station house. The long platform was\r\nalmost deserted; the only living creature in sight being a girl who was\r\nsitting on a pile of shingles at the extreme end. Matthew, barely noting\r\nthat it _was_ a girl, sidled past her as quickly as possible without\r\nlooking at her. Had he looked he could hardly have failed to notice the\r\ntense rigidity and expectation of her attitude and expression. She was\r\nsitting there waiting for something or somebody and, since sitting and\r\nwaiting was the only thing to do just then, she sat and waited with all\r\nher might and main.\r\n\r\nMatthew encountered the stationmaster locking up the ticket office\r\npreparatory to going home for supper, and asked him if the five-thirty\r\ntrain would soon be along.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe five-thirty train has been in and gone half an hour ago,\xe2\x80\x9d answered\r\nthat brisk official. \xe2\x80\x9cBut there was a passenger dropped off for you--a\r\nlittle girl. She\xe2\x80\x99s sitting out there on the shingles. I asked her to\r\ngo into the ladies\xe2\x80\x99 waiting room, but she informed me gravely that she\r\npreferred to stay outside. \xe2\x80\x98There was more scope for imagination,\xe2\x80\x99 she\r\nsaid. She\xe2\x80\x99s a case, I should say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m not expecting a girl,\xe2\x80\x9d said Matthew blankly. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a boy I\xe2\x80\x99ve come\r\nfor. He should be here. Mrs. Alexander Spencer was to bring him over\r\nfrom Nova Scotia for me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe stationmaster whistled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGuess there\xe2\x80\x99s some mistake,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Spencer came off the train\r\nwith that girl and gave her into my charge. Said you and your sister\r\nwere adopting her from an orphan asylum and that you would be along for\r\nher presently. That\xe2\x80\x99s all I know about it--and I haven\xe2\x80\x99t got any more\r\norphans concealed hereabouts.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t understand,\xe2\x80\x9d said Matthew helplessly, wishing that Marilla was\r\nat hand to cope with the situation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you\xe2\x80\x99d better question the girl,\xe2\x80\x9d said the station-master\r\ncarelessly. \xe2\x80\x9cI dare say she\xe2\x80\x99ll be able to explain--she\xe2\x80\x99s got a tongue\r\nof her own, that\xe2\x80\x99s certain. Maybe they were out of boys of the brand you\r\nwanted.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe walked jauntily away, being hungry, and the unfortunate Matthew was\r\nleft to do that which was harder for him than bearding a lion in its\r\nden--walk up to a girl--a strange girl--an orphan girl--and demand of\r\nher why she wasn\xe2\x80\x99t a boy. Matthew groaned in spirit as he turned about\r\nand shuffled gently down the platform towards her.\r\n\r\nShe had been watching him ever since he had passed her and she had her\r\neyes on him now. Matthew was not looking at her and would not have seen\r\nwhat she was really like if he had been, but an ordinary observer would\r\nhave seen this: A child of about eleven, garbed in a very short, very\r\ntight, very ugly dress of yellowish-gray wincey. She wore a faded brown\r\nsailor hat and beneath the hat, extending down her back, were two braids\r\nof very thick, decidedly red hair. Her face was small, white and thin,\r\nalso much freckled; her mouth was large and so were her eyes, which\r\nlooked green in some lights and moods and gray in others.\r\n\r\nSo far, the ordinary observer; an extraordinary observer might have seen\r\nthat the chin was very pointed and pronounced; that the big eyes\r\nwere full of spirit and vivacity; that the mouth was sweet-lipped\r\nand expressive; that the forehead was broad and full; in short,\r\nour discerning extraordinary observer might have concluded that no\r\ncommonplace soul inhabited the body of this stray woman-child of whom\r\nshy Matthew Cuthbert was so ludicrously afraid.\r\n\r\nMatthew, however, was spared the ordeal of speaking first, for as soon\r\nas she concluded that he was coming to her she stood up, grasping with\r\none thin brown hand the handle of a shabby, old-fashioned carpet-bag;\r\nthe other she held out to him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose you are Mr. Matthew Cuthbert of Green Gables?\xe2\x80\x9d she said in\r\na peculiarly clear, sweet voice. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m very glad to see you. I was\r\nbeginning to be afraid you weren\xe2\x80\x99t coming for me and I was imagining\r\nall the things that might have happened to prevent you. I had made up\r\nmy mind that if you didn\xe2\x80\x99t come for me to-night I\xe2\x80\x99d go down the track to\r\nthat big wild cherry-tree at the bend, and climb up into it to stay all\r\nnight. I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be a bit afraid, and it would be lovely to sleep in a\r\nwild cherry-tree all white with bloom in the moonshine, don\xe2\x80\x99t you think?\r\nYou could imagine you were dwelling in marble halls, couldn\xe2\x80\x99t you? And\r\nI was quite sure you would come for me in the morning, if you didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nto-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMatthew had taken the scrawny little hand awkwardly in his; then and\r\nthere he decided what to do. He could not tell this child with the\r\nglowing eyes that there had been a mistake; he would take her home and\r\nlet Marilla do that. She couldn\xe2\x80\x99t be left at Bright River anyhow, no\r\nmatter what mistake had been made, so all questions and explanations\r\nmight as well be deferred until he was safely back at Green Gables.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sorry I was late,\xe2\x80\x9d he said shyly. \xe2\x80\x9cCome along. It\xe2\x80\x99s worse than anything you\r\ncould imagine. Mrs. Spencer said it was wicked of me to talk like\r\nthat, but I didn\xe2\x80\x99t mean to be wicked. It\xe2\x80\x99s so easy to be wicked without\r\nknowing it, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it? They were good, you know--the asylum people. But\r\nthere is so little scope for the imagination in an asylum--only just\r\nin the other orphans. It was pretty interesting to imagine things about\r\nthem--to imagine that perhaps the girl who sat next to you was really\r\nthe daughter of a belted earl, who had been stolen away from her parents\r\nin her infancy by a cruel nurse who died before she could confess. I\r\nused to lie awake at nights and imagine things like that, because\r\nI didn\xe2\x80\x99t have time in the day. I suppose a foreign\r\nmissionary mightn\xe2\x80\x99t be very particular. But I do hope that some day I\r\nshall have a white dress. That is my highest ideal of earthly bliss. All the orphans had to wear them, you\r\nknow. A merchant in Hopeton last winter donated three hundred yards of\r\nwincey to the asylum. Isn\xe2\x80\x99t it splendid\r\nto think of all the things there are to find out about? It just makes\r\nme feel glad to be alive--it\xe2\x80\x99s such an interesting world. They had\r\nsimply rounded a curve in the road and found themselves in the \xe2\x80\x9cAvenue.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe \xe2\x80\x9cAvenue,\xe2\x80\x9d so called by the Newbridge people, was a stretch of road\r\nfour or five hundred yards long, completely arched over with huge,\r\nwide-spreading apple-trees, planted years ago by an eccentric old\r\nfarmer. It gives me that pleasant ache again just to think of coming\r\nto a really truly home. Oh, isn\xe2\x80\x99t that pretty!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey had driven over the crest of a hill. Below them was a pond, looking\r\nalmost like a river so long and winding was it. A bridge spanned it\r\nmidway and from there to its lower end, where an amber-hued belt of\r\nsand-hills shut it in from the dark blue gulf beyond, the water was a\r\nglory of many shifting hues--the most spiritual shadings of crocus and\r\nrose and ethereal green, with other elusive tintings for which no name\r\nhas ever been found. Above the bridge the pond ran up into fringing\r\ngroves of fir and maple and lay all darkly translucent in their wavering\r\nshadows. Here and there a wild plum leaned out from the bank like a\r\nwhite-clad girl tip-toeing to her own reflection. From the marsh at the\r\nhead of the pond came the clear, mournfully-sweet chorus of the frogs.\r\nThere was a little gray house peering around a white apple orchard on\r\na slope beyond and, although it was not yet quite dark, a light was\r\nshining from one of its windows.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s Barry\xe2\x80\x99s pond,\xe2\x80\x9d said Matthew.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I don\xe2\x80\x99t like that name, either. I shall call it--let me see--the\r\nLake of Shining Waters. Yes, that is the right name for it. I know\r\nbecause of the thrill. When I hit on a name that suits exactly it gives\r\nme a thrill. Do things ever give you a thrill?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMatthew ruminated.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell now, yes. It always kind of gives me a thrill to see them ugly\r\nwhite grubs that spade up in the cucumber beds. I hate the look of\r\nthem.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I don\xe2\x80\x99t think that can be exactly the same kind of a thrill. Do you\r\nthink it can? There doesn\xe2\x80\x99t seem to be much connection between grubs\r\nand lakes of shining waters, does there? But why do other people call it\r\nBarry\xe2\x80\x99s pond?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI reckon because Mr. Barry lives up there in that house. Orchard\r\nSlope\xe2\x80\x99s the name of his place. If it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t for that big bush behind it\r\nyou could see Green Gables from here. But we have to go over the bridge\r\nand round by the road, so it\xe2\x80\x99s near half a mile further.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHas Mr. Barry any little girls? Well, not so very little either--about\r\nmy size.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s got one about eleven. Her name is Diana.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh!\xe2\x80\x9d with a long indrawing of breath. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat a perfectly lovely name!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell now, I dunno. There\xe2\x80\x99s something dreadful heathenish about it,\r\nseems to me. I\xe2\x80\x99d ruther Jane or Mary or some sensible name like that.\r\nBut when Diana was born there was a schoolmaster boarding there and they\r\ngave him the naming of her and he called her Diana.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish there had been a schoolmaster like that around when I was born,\r\nthen. Oh, here we are at the bridge. I\xe2\x80\x99m going to shut my eyes tight.\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99m always afraid going over bridges. I can\xe2\x80\x99t help imagining that\r\nperhaps just as we get to the middle, they\xe2\x80\x99ll crumple up like a\r\njack-knife and nip us. So I shut my eyes. But I always have to open them\r\nfor all when I think we\xe2\x80\x99re getting near the middle. Because, you see, if\r\nthe bridge _did_ crumple up I\xe2\x80\x99d want to _see_ it crumple. What a jolly\r\nrumble it makes! I always like the rumble part of it. Isn\xe2\x80\x99t it splendid\r\nthere are so many things to like in this world? There we\xe2\x80\x99re over. Now\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll look back. Good night, dear Lake of Shining Waters. I always say\r\ngood night to the things I love, just as I would to people. I think they\r\nlike it. That water looks as if it was smiling at me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen they had driven up the further hill and around a corner Matthew\r\nsaid:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re pretty near home now. That\xe2\x80\x99s Green Gables over--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, don\xe2\x80\x99t tell me,\xe2\x80\x9d she interrupted breathlessly, catching at his\r\npartially raised arm and shutting her eyes that she might not see his\r\ngesture. \xe2\x80\x9cLet me guess. I\xe2\x80\x99m sure I\xe2\x80\x99ll guess right.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe opened her eyes and looked about her. They were on the crest of a\r\nhill. The sun had set some time since, but the landscape was still\r\nclear in the mellow afterlight. To the west a dark church spire rose\r\nup against a marigold sky. Below was a little valley and beyond a long,\r\ngently-rising slope with snug farmsteads scattered along it. From one\r\nto another the child\xe2\x80\x99s eyes darted, eager and wistful. At last they\r\nlingered on one away to the left, far back from the road, dimly white\r\nwith blossoming trees in the twilight of the surrounding woods. Over it,\r\nin the stainless southwest sky, a great crystal-white star was shining\r\nlike a lamp of guidance and promise.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s it, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d she said, pointing.\r\n\r\nMatthew slapped the reins on the sorrel\xe2\x80\x99s back delightedly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell now, you\xe2\x80\x99ve guessed it! But I reckon Mrs. Spencer described it\r\nso\xe2\x80\x99s you could tell.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, she didn\xe2\x80\x99t--really she didn\xe2\x80\x99t. All she said might just as well have\r\nbeen about most of those other places. I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t any real idea what it\r\nlooked like. But just as soon as I saw it I felt it was home. Oh, it\r\nseems as if I must be in a dream. Do you know, my arm must be black and\r\nblue from the elbow up, for I\xe2\x80\x99ve pinched myself so many times today.\r\nEvery little while a horrible sickening feeling would come over me and\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99d be so afraid it was all a dream. Then I\xe2\x80\x99d pinch myself to see if it\r\nwas real--until suddenly I remembered that even supposing it was only\r\na dream I\xe2\x80\x99d better go on dreaming as long as I could; so I stopped\r\npinching. But it _is_ real and we\xe2\x80\x99re nearly home.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWith a sigh of rapture she relapsed into silence. Matthew stirred\r\nuneasily. He felt glad that it would be Marilla and not he who would\r\nhave to tell this waif of the world that the home she longed for was\r\nnot to be hers after all. They drove over Lynde\xe2\x80\x99s Hollow, where it was\r\nalready quite dark, but not so dark that Mrs. Rachel could not see them\r\nfrom her window vantage, and up the hill and into the long lane of Green\r\nGables. By the time they arrived at the house Matthew was shrinking from\r\nthe approaching revelation with an energy he did not understand. It was\r\nnot of Marilla or himself he was thinking of the trouble this mistake\r\nwas probably going to make for them, but of the child\xe2\x80\x99s disappointment.\r\nWhen he thought of that rapt light being quenched in her eyes he had\r\nan uncomfortable feeling that he was going to assist at murdering\r\nsomething--much the same feeling that came over him when he had to kill\r\na lamb or calf or any other innocent little creature.\r\n\r\nThe yard was quite dark as they turned into it and the poplar leaves\r\nwere rustling silkily all round it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cListen to the trees talking in their sleep,\xe2\x80\x9d she whispered, as he\r\nlifted her to the ground. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat nice dreams they must have!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen, holding tightly to the carpet-bag which contained \xe2\x80\x9call her worldly\r\ngoods,\xe2\x80\x9d she followed him into the house.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER III. Marilla Cuthbert is Surprised\r\n\r\n\r\n|MARILLA came briskly forward as Matthew opened the door. But when her\r\neyes fell on the odd little figure in the stiff, ugly dress, with the\r\nlong braids of red hair and the eager, luminous eyes, she stopped short\r\nin amazement.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMatthew Cuthbert, who\xe2\x80\x99s that?\xe2\x80\x9d she ejaculated. \xe2\x80\x9cWhere is the boy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere wasn\xe2\x80\x99t any boy,\xe2\x80\x9d said Matthew wretchedly. \xe2\x80\x9cThere was only _her_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe nodded at the child, remembering that he had never even asked her\r\nname.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo boy! But there _must_ have been a boy,\xe2\x80\x9d insisted Marilla. \xe2\x80\x9cWe sent\r\nword to Mrs. Spencer to bring a boy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, she didn\xe2\x80\x99t. She brought _her_. I asked the station-master. And I\r\nhad to bring her home. She couldn\xe2\x80\x99t be left there, no matter where the\r\nmistake had come in.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, this is a pretty piece of business!\xe2\x80\x9d ejaculated Marilla.\r\n\r\nDuring this dialogue the child had remained silent, her eyes roving from\r\none to the other, all the animation fading out of her face. Suddenly\r\nshe seemed to grasp the full meaning of what had been said. Dropping her\r\nprecious carpet-bag she sprang forward a step and clasped her hands.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t want me!\xe2\x80\x9d she cried. \xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t want me because I\xe2\x80\x99m not a\r\nboy! I might have expected it. Nobody ever did want me. I might have\r\nknown it was all too beautiful to last. I might have known nobody really\r\ndid want me. Oh, what shall I do? I\xe2\x80\x99m going to burst into tears!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBurst into tears she did. Sitting down on a chair by the table, flinging\r\nher arms out upon it, and burying her face in them, she proceeded to cry\r\nstormily. Marilla and Matthew looked at each other deprecatingly across\r\nthe stove. Neither of them knew what to say or do. Finally Marilla\r\nstepped lamely into the breach.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, well, there\xe2\x80\x99s no need to cry so about it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, there _is_ need!\xe2\x80\x9d The child raised her head quickly, revealing a\r\ntear-stained face and trembling lips. \xe2\x80\x9c_You_ would cry, too, if you were\r\nan orphan and had come to a place you thought was going to be home and\r\nfound that they didn\xe2\x80\x99t want you because you weren\xe2\x80\x99t a boy. Oh, this is\r\nthe most _tragical_ thing that ever happened to me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSomething like a reluctant smile, rather rusty from long disuse,\r\nmellowed Marilla\xe2\x80\x99s grim expression.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, don\xe2\x80\x99t cry any more. We\xe2\x80\x99re not going to turn you out-of-doors\r\nto-night. You\xe2\x80\x99ll have to stay here until we investigate this affair.\r\nWhat\xe2\x80\x99s your name?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe child hesitated for a moment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you please call me Cordelia?\xe2\x80\x9d she said eagerly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Call_ you Cordelia? Is that your name?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo-o-o, it\xe2\x80\x99s not exactly my name, but I would love to be called\r\nCordelia. It\xe2\x80\x99s such a perfectly elegant name.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what on earth you mean. If Cordelia isn\xe2\x80\x99t your name, what\r\nis?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnne Shirley,\xe2\x80\x9d reluctantly faltered forth the owner of that name, \xe2\x80\x9cbut,\r\noh, please do call me Cordelia. It can\xe2\x80\x99t matter much to you what you\r\ncall me if I\xe2\x80\x99m only going to be here a little while, can it? And Anne is\r\nsuch an unromantic name.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUnromantic fiddlesticks!\xe2\x80\x9d said the unsympathetic Marilla. \xe2\x80\x9cAnne is a\r\nreal good plain sensible name. You\xe2\x80\x99ve no need to be ashamed of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I\xe2\x80\x99m not ashamed of it,\xe2\x80\x9d explained Anne, \xe2\x80\x9conly I like Cordelia\r\nbetter. I\xe2\x80\x99ve always imagined that my name was Cordelia--at least, I\r\nalways have of late years. When I was young I used to imagine it was\r\nGeraldine, but I like Cordelia better now. But if you call me Anne\r\nplease call me Anne spelled with an E.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat difference does it make how it\xe2\x80\x99s spelled?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Marilla with\r\nanother rusty smile as she picked up the teapot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it makes _such_ a difference. It _looks_ so much nicer. When you hear\r\na name pronounced can\xe2\x80\x99t you always see it in your mind, just as if it\r\nwas printed out? I can; and A-n-n looks dreadful, but A-n-n-e looks so\r\nmuch more distinguished. If you\xe2\x80\x99ll only call me Anne spelled with an E I\r\nshall try to reconcile myself to not being called Cordelia.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well, then, Anne spelled with an E, can you tell us how this\r\nmistake came to be made? We sent word to Mrs. Spencer to bring us a boy.\r\nWere there no boys at the asylum?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes, there was an abundance of them. But Mrs. Spencer said\r\n_distinctly_ that you wanted a girl about eleven years old. And the\r\nmatron said she thought I would do. You don\xe2\x80\x99t know how delighted I was.\r\nI couldn\xe2\x80\x99t sleep all last night for joy. Oh,\xe2\x80\x9d she added reproachfully,\r\nturning to Matthew, \xe2\x80\x9cwhy didn\xe2\x80\x99t you tell me at the station that you\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t want me and leave me there? If I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t seen the White Way of\r\nDelight and the Lake of Shining Waters it wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be so hard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat on earth does she mean?\xe2\x80\x9d demanded Marilla, staring at Matthew.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe--she\xe2\x80\x99s just referring to some conversation we had on the road,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said Matthew hastily. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m going out to put the mare in, Marilla. Have\r\ntea ready when I come back.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid Mrs. Spencer bring anybody over besides you?\xe2\x80\x9d continued Marilla\r\nwhen Matthew had gone out.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe brought Lily Jones for herself. Lily is only five years old and she\r\nis very beautiful and had nut-brown hair. If I was very beautiful and\r\nhad nut-brown hair would you keep me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. We want a boy to help Matthew on the farm. A girl would be of\r\nno use to us. Take off your hat. I\xe2\x80\x99ll lay it and your bag on the hall\r\ntable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne took off her hat meekly. Matthew came back presently and they sat\r\ndown to supper. But Anne could not eat. In vain she nibbled at the\r\nbread and butter and pecked at the crab-apple preserve out of the little\r\nscalloped glass dish by her plate. She did not really make any headway\r\nat all.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re not eating anything,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla sharply, eying her as if it\r\nwere a serious shortcoming. Anne sighed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t. I\xe2\x80\x99m in the depths of despair. Can you eat when you are in the\r\ndepths of despair?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve never been in the depths of despair, so I can\xe2\x80\x99t say,\xe2\x80\x9d responded\r\nMarilla.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWeren\xe2\x80\x99t you? Well, did you ever try to _imagine_ you were in the depths\r\nof despair?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I didn\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I don\xe2\x80\x99t think you can understand what it\xe2\x80\x99s like. It\xe2\x80\x99s a very\r\nuncomfortable feeling indeed. When you try to eat a lump comes right\r\nup in your throat and you can\xe2\x80\x99t swallow anything, not even if it was a\r\nchocolate caramel. I had one chocolate caramel once two years ago and it\r\nwas simply delicious. I\xe2\x80\x99ve often dreamed since then that I had a lot\r\nof chocolate caramels, but I always wake up just when I\xe2\x80\x99m going to eat\r\nthem. I do hope you won\xe2\x80\x99t be offended because I can\xe2\x80\x99t eat. Everything is\r\nextremely nice, but still I cannot eat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI guess she\xe2\x80\x99s tired,\xe2\x80\x9d said Matthew, who hadn\xe2\x80\x99t spoken since his return\r\nfrom the barn. \xe2\x80\x9cBest put her to bed, Marilla.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarilla had been wondering where Anne should be put to bed. She had\r\nprepared a couch in the kitchen chamber for the desired and expected\r\nboy. But, although it was neat and clean, it did not seem quite the\r\nthing to put a girl there somehow. But the spare room was out of the\r\nquestion for such a stray waif, so there remained only the east gable\r\nroom. Marilla lighted a candle and told Anne to follow her, which Anne\r\nspiritlessly did, taking her hat and carpet-bag from the hall table as\r\nshe passed. The hall was fearsomely clean; the little gable chamber in\r\nwhich she presently found herself seemed still cleaner.\r\n\r\nMarilla set the candle on a three-legged, three-cornered table and\r\nturned down the bedclothes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose you have a nightgown?\xe2\x80\x9d she questioned.\r\n\r\nAnne nodded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I have two. The matron of the asylum made them for me. They\xe2\x80\x99re\r\nfearfully skimpy. There is never enough to go around in an asylum, so\r\nthings are always skimpy--at least in a poor asylum like ours. I hate\r\nskimpy night-dresses. But one can dream just as well in them as\r\nin lovely trailing ones, with frills around the neck, that\xe2\x80\x99s one\r\nconsolation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, undress as quick as you can and go to bed. I\xe2\x80\x99ll come back in a\r\nfew minutes for the candle. I daren\xe2\x80\x99t trust you to put it out yourself.\r\nYou\xe2\x80\x99d likely set the place on fire.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen Marilla had gone Anne looked around her wistfully. The whitewashed\r\nwalls were so painfully bare and staring that she thought they must ache\r\nover their own bareness. The floor was bare, too, except for a round\r\nbraided mat in the middle such as Anne had never seen before. In\r\none corner was the bed, a high, old-fashioned one, with four dark,\r\nlow-turned posts. In the other corner was the aforesaid three-corner\r\ntable adorned with a fat, red velvet pin-cushion hard enough to turn the\r\npoint of the most adventurous pin. Above it hung a little six-by-eight\r\nmirror. Midway between table and bed was the window, with an icy white\r\nmuslin frill over it, and opposite it was the wash-stand. The whole\r\napartment was of a rigidity not to be described in words, but which\r\nsent a shiver to the very marrow of Anne\xe2\x80\x99s bones. With a sob she hastily\r\ndiscarded her garments, put on the skimpy nightgown and sprang into bed\r\nwhere she burrowed face downward into the pillow and pulled the clothes\r\nover her head. When Marilla came up for the light various skimpy\r\narticles of raiment scattered most untidily over the floor and a certain\r\ntempestuous appearance of the bed were the only indications of any\r\npresence save her own.\r\n\r\nShe deliberately picked up Anne\xe2\x80\x99s clothes, placed them neatly on a prim\r\nyellow chair, and then, taking up the candle, went over to the bed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood night,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, a little awkwardly, but not unkindly.\r\n\r\nAnne\xe2\x80\x99s white face and big eyes appeared over the bedclothes with a\r\nstartling suddenness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow can you call it a _good_ night when you know it must be the very\r\nworst night I\xe2\x80\x99ve ever had?\xe2\x80\x9d she said reproachfully.\r\n\r\nThen she dived down into invisibility again.\r\n\r\nMarilla went slowly down to the kitchen and proceeded to wash the supper\r\ndishes. Matthew was smoking--a sure sign of perturbation of mind. He\r\nseldom smoked, for Marilla set her face against it as a filthy habit;\r\nbut at certain times and seasons he felt driven to it and them Marilla\r\nwinked at the practice, realizing that a mere man must have some vent\r\nfor his emotions.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, this is a pretty kettle of fish,\xe2\x80\x9d she said wrathfully. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is\r\nwhat comes of sending word instead of going ourselves. Richard Spencer\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nfolks have twisted that message somehow. One of us will have to drive\r\nover and see Mrs. Spencer tomorrow, that\xe2\x80\x99s certain. This girl will have\r\nto be sent back to the asylum.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I suppose so,\xe2\x80\x9d said Matthew reluctantly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou _suppose_ so! Don\xe2\x80\x99t you know it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell now, she\xe2\x80\x99s a real nice little thing, Marilla. It\xe2\x80\x99s kind of a pity\r\nto send her back when she\xe2\x80\x99s so set on staying here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMatthew Cuthbert, you don\xe2\x80\x99t mean to say you think we ought to keep\r\nher!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarilla\xe2\x80\x99s astonishment could not have been greater if Matthew had\r\nexpressed a predilection for standing on his head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, now, no, I suppose not--not exactly,\xe2\x80\x9d stammered Matthew,\r\nuncomfortably driven into a corner for his precise meaning. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nsuppose--we could hardly be expected to keep her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should say not. What good would she be to us?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe might be some good to her,\xe2\x80\x9d said Matthew suddenly and unexpectedly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMatthew Cuthbert, I believe that child has bewitched you! I can see as\r\nplain as plain that you want to keep her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell now, she\xe2\x80\x99s a real interesting little thing,\xe2\x80\x9d persisted Matthew.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou should have heard her talk coming from the station.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, she can talk fast enough. I saw that at once. It\xe2\x80\x99s nothing in her\r\nfavour, either. I don\xe2\x80\x99t like children who have so much to say. I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nwant an orphan girl and if I did she isn\xe2\x80\x99t the style I\xe2\x80\x99d pick out.\r\nThere\xe2\x80\x99s something I don\xe2\x80\x99t understand about her. No, she\xe2\x80\x99s got to be\r\ndespatched straight-way back to where she came from.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI could hire a French boy to help me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Matthew, \xe2\x80\x9cand she\xe2\x80\x99d be\r\ncompany for you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m not suffering for company,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla shortly. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I\xe2\x80\x99m not\r\ngoing to keep her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell now, it\xe2\x80\x99s just as you say, of course, Marilla,\xe2\x80\x9d said Matthew\r\nrising and putting his pipe away. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m going to bed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTo bed went Matthew. And to bed, when she had put her dishes away, went\r\nMarilla, frowning most resolutely. And up-stairs, in the east gable, a\r\nlonely, heart-hungry, friendless child cried herself to sleep.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER IV. Morning at Green Gables\r\n\r\n\r\n|IT was broad daylight when Anne awoke and sat up in bed, staring\r\nconfusedly at the window through which a flood of cheery sunshine was\r\npouring and outside of which something white and feathery waved across\r\nglimpses of blue sky.\r\n\r\nFor a moment she could not remember where she was. First came a\r\ndelightful thrill, as something very pleasant; then a horrible\r\nremembrance. This was Green Gables and they didn\xe2\x80\x99t want her because she\r\nwasn\xe2\x80\x99t a boy!\r\n\r\nBut it was morning and, yes, it was a cherry-tree in full bloom outside\r\nof her window. With a bound she was out of bed and across the floor.\r\nShe pushed up the sash--it went up stiffly and creakily, as if it hadn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nbeen opened for a long time, which was the case; and it stuck so tight\r\nthat nothing was needed to hold it up.\r\n\r\nAnne dropped on her knees and gazed out into the June morning, her eyes\r\nglistening with delight. Oh, wasn\xe2\x80\x99t it beautiful? Wasn\xe2\x80\x99t it a lovely\r\nplace? Suppose she wasn\xe2\x80\x99t really going to stay here! She would imagine\r\nshe was. There was scope for imagination here.\r\n\r\nA huge cherry-tree grew outside, so close that its boughs tapped against\r\nthe house, and it was so thick-set with blossoms that hardly a leaf\r\nwas to be seen. On both sides of the house was a big orchard, one of\r\napple-trees and one of cherry-trees, also showered over with blossoms;\r\nand their grass was all sprinkled with dandelions. In the garden below\r\nwere lilac-trees purple with flowers, and their dizzily sweet fragrance\r\ndrifted up to the window on the morning wind.\r\n\r\nBelow the garden a green field lush with clover sloped down to the\r\nhollow where the brook ran and where scores of white birches grew,\r\nupspringing airily out of an undergrowth suggestive of delightful\r\npossibilities in ferns and mosses and woodsy things generally. Beyond it\r\nwas a hill, green and feathery with spruce and fir; there was a gap in\r\nit where the gray gable end of the little house she had seen from the\r\nother side of the Lake of Shining Waters was visible.\r\n\r\nOff to the left were the big barns and beyond them, away down over\r\ngreen, low-sloping fields, was a sparkling blue glimpse of sea.\r\n\r\nAnne\xe2\x80\x99s beauty-loving eyes lingered on it all, taking everything greedily\r\nin. She had looked on so many unlovely places in her life, poor child;\r\nbut this was as lovely as anything she had ever dreamed.\r\n\r\nShe knelt there, lost to everything but the loveliness around her, until\r\nshe was startled by a hand on her shoulder. Marilla had come in unheard\r\nby the small dreamer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s time you were dressed,\xe2\x80\x9d she said curtly.\r\n\r\nMarilla really did not know how to talk to the child, and her\r\nuncomfortable ignorance made her crisp and curt when she did not mean to\r\nbe.\r\n\r\nAnne stood up and drew a long breath.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it wonderful?\xe2\x80\x9d she said, waving her hand comprehensively at\r\nthe good world outside.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a big tree,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla, \xe2\x80\x9cand it blooms great, but the fruit\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t amount to much never--small and wormy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I don\xe2\x80\x99t mean just the tree; of course it\xe2\x80\x99s lovely--yes, it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n_radiantly_ lovely--it blooms as if it meant it--but I meant everything,\r\nthe garden and the orchard and the brook and the woods, the whole big\r\ndear world. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you feel as if you just loved the world on a morning\r\nlike this? And I can hear the brook laughing all the way up here.\r\nHave you ever noticed what cheerful things brooks are? They\xe2\x80\x99re always\r\nlaughing. Even in winter-time I\xe2\x80\x99ve heard them under the ice. I\xe2\x80\x99m so glad\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x99s a brook near Green Gables. Perhaps you think it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t make any\r\ndifference to me when you\xe2\x80\x99re not going to keep me, but it does. I shall\r\nalways like to remember that there is a brook at Green Gables even if\r\nI never see it again. If there wasn\xe2\x80\x99t a brook I\xe2\x80\x99d be _haunted_ by the\r\nuncomfortable feeling that there ought to be one. I\xe2\x80\x99m not in the depths\r\nof despair this morning. I never can be in the morning. Isn\xe2\x80\x99t it a\r\nsplendid thing that there are mornings? But I feel very sad. I\xe2\x80\x99ve just\r\nbeen imagining that it was really me you wanted after all and that I was\r\nto stay here for ever and ever. It was a great comfort while it lasted.\r\nBut the worst of imagining things is that the time comes when you have\r\nto stop and that hurts.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99d better get dressed and come down-stairs and never mind your\r\nimaginings,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla as soon as she could get a word in edgewise.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBreakfast is waiting. Wash your face and comb your hair. Leave the\r\nwindow up and turn your bedclothes back over the foot of the bed. Be as\r\nsmart as you can.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne could evidently be smart to some purpose for she was down-stairs\r\nin ten minutes\xe2\x80\x99 time, with her clothes neatly on, her hair brushed and\r\nbraided, her face washed, and a comfortable consciousness pervading her\r\nsoul that she had fulfilled all Marilla\xe2\x80\x99s requirements. As a matter of\r\nfact, however, she had forgotten to turn back the bedclothes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m pretty hungry this morning,\xe2\x80\x9d she announced as she slipped into the\r\nchair Marilla placed for her. \xe2\x80\x9cThe world doesn\xe2\x80\x99t seem such a howling\r\nwilderness as it did last night. I\xe2\x80\x99m so glad it\xe2\x80\x99s a sunshiny morning.\r\nBut I like rainy mornings real well, too. All sorts of mornings are\r\ninteresting, don\xe2\x80\x99t you think? You don\xe2\x80\x99t know what\xe2\x80\x99s going to happen\r\nthrough the day, and there\xe2\x80\x99s so much scope for imagination. But I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\nglad it\xe2\x80\x99s not rainy today because it\xe2\x80\x99s easier to be cheerful and bear\r\nup under affliction on a sunshiny day. I feel that I have a good deal\r\nto bear up under. It\xe2\x80\x99s all very well to read about sorrows and imagine\r\nyourself living through them heroically, but it\xe2\x80\x99s not so nice when you\r\nreally come to have them, is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor pity\xe2\x80\x99s sake hold your tongue,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla. \xe2\x80\x9cYou talk entirely too\r\nmuch for a little girl.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThereupon Anne held her tongue so obediently and thoroughly that her\r\ncontinued silence made Marilla rather nervous, as if in the presence of\r\nsomething not exactly natural. Matthew also held his tongue,--but this\r\nwas natural,--so that the meal was a very silent one.\r\n\r\nAs it progressed Anne became more and more abstracted, eating\r\nmechanically, with her big eyes fixed unswervingly and unseeingly on the\r\nsky outside the window. This made Marilla more nervous than ever; she\r\nhad an uncomfortable feeling that while this odd child\xe2\x80\x99s body might\r\nbe there at the table her spirit was far away in some remote airy\r\ncloudland, borne aloft on the wings of imagination. Who would want such\r\na child about the place?\r\n\r\nYet Matthew wished to keep her, of all unaccountable things! Marilla\r\nfelt that he wanted it just as much this morning as he had the night\r\nbefore, and that he would go on wanting it. That was Matthew\xe2\x80\x99s way--take\r\na whim into his head and cling to it with the most amazing silent\r\npersistency--a persistency ten times more potent and effectual in its\r\nvery silence than if he had talked it out.\r\n\r\nWhen the meal was ended Anne came out of her reverie and offered to wash\r\nthe dishes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan you wash dishes right?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Marilla distrustfully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPretty well. I\xe2\x80\x99m better at looking after children, though. I\xe2\x80\x99ve had so\r\nmuch experience at that. It\xe2\x80\x99s such a pity you haven\xe2\x80\x99t any here for me to\r\nlook after.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t feel as if I wanted any more children to look after than I\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\ngot at present. _You\xe2\x80\x99re_ problem enough in all conscience. What\xe2\x80\x99s to be\r\ndone with you I don\xe2\x80\x99t know. Matthew is a most ridiculous man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think he\xe2\x80\x99s lovely,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne reproachfully. \xe2\x80\x9cHe is so very\r\nsympathetic. He didn\xe2\x80\x99t mind how much I talked--he seemed to like it. I\r\nfelt that he was a kindred spirit as soon as ever I saw him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re both queer enough, if that\xe2\x80\x99s what you mean by kindred spirits,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said Marilla with a sniff. \xe2\x80\x9cYes, you may wash the dishes. Take plenty of\r\nhot water, and be sure you dry them well. I\xe2\x80\x99ve got enough to attend to\r\nthis morning for I\xe2\x80\x99ll have to drive over to White Sands in the afternoon\r\nand see Mrs. Spencer. You\xe2\x80\x99ll come with me and we\xe2\x80\x99ll settle what\xe2\x80\x99s to be\r\ndone with you. After you\xe2\x80\x99ve finished the dishes go up-stairs and make\r\nyour bed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne washed the dishes deftly enough, as Marilla who kept a sharp eye on\r\nthe process, discerned. Later on she made her bed less successfully, for\r\nshe had never learned the art of wrestling with a feather tick. But is\r\nwas done somehow and smoothed down; and then Marilla, to get rid of her,\r\ntold her she might go out-of-doors and amuse herself until dinner time.\r\n\r\nAnne flew to the door, face alight, eyes glowing. On the very threshold\r\nshe stopped short, wheeled about, came back and sat down by the table,\r\nlight and glow as effectually blotted out as if some one had clapped an\r\nextinguisher on her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the matter now?\xe2\x80\x9d demanded Marilla.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t dare go out,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne, in the tone of a martyr relinquishing\r\nall earthly joys. \xe2\x80\x9cIf I can\xe2\x80\x99t stay here there is no use in my loving\r\nGreen Gables. And if I go out there and get acquainted with all those\r\ntrees and flowers and the orchard and the brook I\xe2\x80\x99ll not be able to help\r\nloving it. It\xe2\x80\x99s hard enough now, so I won\xe2\x80\x99t make it any harder. I want\r\nto go out so much--everything seems to be calling to me, \xe2\x80\x98Anne, Anne,\r\ncome out to us. Anne, Anne, we want a playmate\xe2\x80\x99--but it\xe2\x80\x99s better not.\r\nThere is no use in loving things if you have to be torn from them, is\r\nthere? And it\xe2\x80\x99s so hard to keep from loving things, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it? That was\r\nwhy I was so glad when I thought I was going to live here. I thought\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99d have so many things to love and nothing to hinder me. But that brief\r\ndream is over. I am resigned to my fate now, so I don\xe2\x80\x99t think I\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\ngo out for fear I\xe2\x80\x99ll get unresigned again. What is the name of that\r\ngeranium on the window-sill, please?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the apple-scented geranium.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I don\xe2\x80\x99t mean that sort of a name. I mean just a name you gave it\r\nyourself. Didn\xe2\x80\x99t you give it a name? May I give it one then? May I call\r\nit--let me see--Bonny would do--may I call it Bonny while I\xe2\x80\x99m here? Oh,\r\ndo let me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGoodness, I don\xe2\x80\x99t care. But where on earth is the sense of naming a\r\ngeranium?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I like things to have handles even if they are only geraniums. It\r\nmakes them seem more like people. How do you know but that it hurts a\r\ngeranium\xe2\x80\x99s feelings just to be called a geranium and nothing else? You\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t like to be called nothing but a woman all the time. Yes, I\r\nshall call it Bonny. I named that cherry-tree outside my bedroom window\r\nthis morning. I called it Snow Queen because it was so white. Of course,\r\nit won\xe2\x80\x99t always be in blossom, but one can imagine that it is, can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\none?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never in all my life saw or heard anything to equal her,\xe2\x80\x9d muttered\r\nMarilla, beating a retreat down to the cellar after potatoes. \xe2\x80\x9cShe\r\nis kind of interesting as Matthew says. I can feel already that I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\nwondering what on earth she\xe2\x80\x99ll say next. She\xe2\x80\x99ll be casting a spell over\r\nme, too. She\xe2\x80\x99s cast it over Matthew. That look he gave me when he went\r\nout said everything he said or hinted last night over again. I wish he\r\nwas like other men and would talk things out. A body could answer back\r\nthen and argue him into reason. But what\xe2\x80\x99s to be done with a man who\r\njust _looks?_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne had relapsed into reverie, with her chin in her hands and her eyes\r\non the sky, when Marilla returned from her cellar pilgrimage. There\r\nMarilla left her until the early dinner was on the table.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose I can have the mare and buggy this afternoon, Matthew?\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMarilla.\r\n\r\nMatthew nodded and looked wistfully at Anne. Marilla intercepted the\r\nlook and said grimly:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m going to drive over to White Sands and settle this thing. I\xe2\x80\x99ll take\r\nAnne with me and Mrs. Spencer will probably make arrangements to send\r\nher back to Nova Scotia at once. I\xe2\x80\x99ll set your tea out for you and I\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nbe home in time to milk the cows.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nStill Matthew said nothing and Marilla had a sense of having wasted\r\nwords and breath. There is nothing more aggravating than a man who won\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ntalk back--unless it is a woman who won\xe2\x80\x99t.\r\n\r\nMatthew hitched the sorrel into the buggy in due time and Marilla and\r\nAnne set off. Matthew opened the yard gate for them and as they drove\r\nslowly through, he said, to nobody in particular as it seemed:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLittle Jerry Buote from the Creek was here this morning, and I told him\r\nI guessed I\xe2\x80\x99d hire him for the summer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarilla made no reply, but she hit the unlucky sorrel such a vicious\r\nclip with the whip that the fat mare, unused to such treatment, whizzed\r\nindignantly down the lane at an alarming pace. Marilla looked back once\r\nas the buggy bounced along and saw that aggravating Matthew leaning over\r\nthe gate, looking wistfully after them.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER V. Anne\xe2\x80\x99s History\r\n\r\n\r\n|DO you know,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne confidentially, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve made up my mind to enjoy\r\nthis drive. It\xe2\x80\x99s been my experience that you can nearly always enjoy\r\nthings if you make up your mind firmly that you will. Of course, you\r\nmust make it up _firmly_. I am not going to think about going back to\r\nthe asylum while we\xe2\x80\x99re having our drive. I\xe2\x80\x99m just going to think about\r\nthe drive. Oh, look, there\xe2\x80\x99s one little early wild rose out! Isn\xe2\x80\x99t it\r\nlovely? Don\xe2\x80\x99t you think it must be glad to be a rose? Wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t it be\r\nnice if roses could talk? I\xe2\x80\x99m sure they could tell us such lovely\r\nthings. And isn\xe2\x80\x99t pink the most bewitching color in the world? I love\r\nit, but I can\xe2\x80\x99t wear it. Redheaded people can\xe2\x80\x99t wear pink, not even in\r\nimagination. Did you ever know of anybody whose hair was red when she\r\nwas young, but got to be another color when she grew up?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know as I ever did,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla mercilessly, \xe2\x80\x9cand I\r\nshouldn\xe2\x80\x99t think it likely to happen in your case either.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne sighed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, that is another hope gone. \xe2\x80\x98My life is a perfect graveyard of\r\nburied hopes.\xe2\x80\x99 That\xe2\x80\x99s a sentence I read in a book once, and I say it\r\nover to comfort myself whenever I\xe2\x80\x99m disappointed in anything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t see where the comforting comes in myself,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, because it sounds so nice and romantic, just as if I were a\r\nheroine in a book, you know. I am so fond of romantic things, and a\r\ngraveyard full of buried hopes is about as romantic a thing as one can\r\nimagine isn\xe2\x80\x99t it? I\xe2\x80\x99m rather glad I have one. Are we going across the\r\nLake of Shining Waters today?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re not going over Barry\xe2\x80\x99s pond, if that\xe2\x80\x99s what you mean by your Lake\r\nof Shining Waters. We\xe2\x80\x99re going by the shore road.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShore road sounds nice,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne dreamily. \xe2\x80\x9cIs it as nice as it\r\nsounds? Just when you said \xe2\x80\x98shore road\xe2\x80\x99 I saw it in a picture in my\r\nmind, as quick as that! And White Sands is a pretty name, too; but I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t like it as well as Avonlea. Avonlea is a lovely name. It just\r\nsounds like music. How far is it to White Sands?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s five miles; and as you\xe2\x80\x99re evidently bent on talking you might as\r\nwell talk to some purpose by telling me what you know about yourself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, what I _know_ about myself isn\xe2\x80\x99t really worth telling,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne\r\neagerly. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you\xe2\x80\x99ll only let me tell you what I _imagine_ about myself\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ll think it ever so much more interesting.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I don\xe2\x80\x99t want any of your imaginings. Just you stick to bald facts.\r\nBegin at the beginning. Where were you born and how old are you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was eleven last March,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne, resigning herself to bald facts\r\nwith a little sigh. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I was born in Bolingbroke, Nova Scotia.\r\nMy father\xe2\x80\x99s name was Walter Shirley, and he was a teacher in the\r\nBolingbroke High School. My mother\xe2\x80\x99s name was Bertha Shirley. Aren\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nWalter and Bertha lovely names? I\xe2\x80\x99m so glad my parents had nice names.\r\nIt would be a real disgrace to have a father named--well, say Jedediah,\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI guess it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t matter what a person\xe2\x80\x99s name is as long as he behaves\r\nhimself,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla, feeling herself called upon to inculcate a good\r\nand useful moral.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know.\xe2\x80\x9d Anne looked thoughtful. \xe2\x80\x9cI read in a book once\r\nthat a rose by any other name would smell as sweet, but I\xe2\x80\x99ve never been\r\nable to believe it. I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe a rose _would_ be as nice if it was\r\ncalled a thistle or a skunk cabbage. I suppose my father could have been\r\na good man even if he had been called Jedediah; but I\xe2\x80\x99m sure it would\r\nhave been a cross. Well, my mother was a teacher in the High school,\r\ntoo, but when she married father she gave up teaching, of course. A\r\nhusband was enough responsibility. Mrs. Thomas said that they were\r\na pair of babies and as poor as church mice. They went to live in a\r\nweeny-teeny little yellow house in Bolingbroke. I\xe2\x80\x99ve never seen that\r\nhouse, but I\xe2\x80\x99ve imagined it thousands of times. I think it must have\r\nhad honeysuckle over the parlor window and lilacs in the front yard and\r\nlilies of the valley just inside the gate. Yes, and muslin curtains in\r\nall the windows. Muslin curtains give a house such an air. I was born\r\nin that house. Mrs. Thomas said I was the homeliest baby she ever saw, I\r\nwas so scrawny and tiny and nothing but eyes, but that mother thought I\r\nwas perfectly beautiful. I should think a mother would be a better judge\r\nthan a poor woman who came in to scrub, wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t you? I\xe2\x80\x99m glad she\r\nwas satisfied with me anyhow, I would feel so sad if I thought I was a\r\ndisappointment to her--because she didn\xe2\x80\x99t live very long after that, you\r\nsee. She died of fever when I was just three months old. I do wish she\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nlived long enough for me to remember calling her mother. I think it\r\nwould be so sweet to say \xe2\x80\x98mother,\xe2\x80\x99 don\xe2\x80\x99t you? And father died four days\r\nafterwards from fever too. That left me an orphan and folks were at\r\ntheir wits\xe2\x80\x99 end, so Mrs. Thomas said, what to do with me. You see,\r\nnobody wanted me even then. It seems to be my fate. Father and mother\r\nhad both come from places far away and it was well known they hadn\xe2\x80\x99t any\r\nrelatives living. Finally Mrs. Thomas said she\xe2\x80\x99d take me, though she was\r\npoor and had a drunken husband. She brought me up by hand. Do you know\r\nif there is anything in being brought up by hand that ought to make\r\npeople who are brought up that way better than other people? Because\r\nwhenever I was naughty Mrs. Thomas would ask me how I could be such a\r\nbad girl when she had brought me up by hand--reproachful-like.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. and Mrs. Thomas moved away from Bolingbroke to Marysville, and I\r\nlived with them until I was eight years old. I helped look after the\r\nThomas children--there were four of them younger than me--and I can tell\r\nyou they took a lot of looking after. Then Mr. Thomas was killed\r\nfalling under a train and his mother offered to take Mrs. Thomas and the\r\nchildren, but she didn\xe2\x80\x99t want me. Mrs. Thomas was at _her_ wits\xe2\x80\x99 end, so\r\nshe said, what to do with me. Then Mrs. Hammond from up the river came\r\ndown and said she\xe2\x80\x99d take me, seeing I was handy with children, and\r\nI went up the river to live with her in a little clearing among the\r\nstumps. It was a very lonesome place. I\xe2\x80\x99m sure I could never have\r\nlived there if I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t had an imagination. Mr. Hammond worked a little\r\nsawmill up there, and Mrs. Hammond had eight children. She had twins\r\nthree times. I like babies in moderation, but twins three times in\r\nsuccession is _too much_. I told Mrs. Hammond so firmly, when the last\r\npair came. I used to get so dreadfully tired carrying them about.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI lived up river with Mrs. Hammond over two years, and then Mr. Hammond\r\ndied and Mrs. Hammond broke up housekeeping. She divided her children\r\namong her relatives and went to the States. I had to go to the asylum\r\nat Hopeton, because nobody would take me. They didn\xe2\x80\x99t want me at the\r\nasylum, either; they said they were over-crowded as it was. But they had\r\nto take me and I was there four months until Mrs. Spencer came.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne finished up with another sigh, of relief this time. Evidently\r\nshe did not like talking about her experiences in a world that had not\r\nwanted her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you ever go to school?\xe2\x80\x9d demanded Marilla, turning the sorrel mare\r\ndown the shore road.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot a great deal. I went a little the last year I stayed with Mrs.\r\nThomas. When I went up river we were so far from a school that I\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t walk it in winter and there was a vacation in summer, so I\r\ncould only go in the spring and fall. But of course I went while I was\r\nat the asylum. I can read pretty well and I know ever so many pieces of\r\npoetry off by heart--\xe2\x80\x98The Battle of Hohenlinden\xe2\x80\x99 and \xe2\x80\x98Edinburgh after\r\nFlodden,\xe2\x80\x99 and \xe2\x80\x98Bingen of the Rhine,\xe2\x80\x99 and most of the \xe2\x80\x98Lady of the Lake\xe2\x80\x99\r\nand most of \xe2\x80\x98The Seasons\xe2\x80\x99 by James Thompson. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you just love poetry\r\nthat gives you a crinkly feeling up and down your back? There is a piece\r\nin the Fifth Reader--\xe2\x80\x98The Downfall of Poland\xe2\x80\x99--that is just full of\r\nthrills. Of course, I wasn\xe2\x80\x99t in the Fifth Reader--I was only in the\r\nFourth--but the big girls used to lend me theirs to read.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWere those women--Mrs. Thomas and Mrs. Hammond--good to you?\xe2\x80\x9d asked\r\nMarilla, looking at Anne out of the corner of her eye.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO-o-o-h,\xe2\x80\x9d faltered Anne. Her sensitive little face suddenly flushed\r\nscarlet and embarrassment sat on her brow. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, they _meant_ to be--I know\r\nthey meant to be just as good and kind as possible. And when people\r\nmean to be good to you, you don\xe2\x80\x99t mind very much when they\xe2\x80\x99re not\r\nquite--always. They had a good deal to worry them, you know. It\xe2\x80\x99s  a very\r\ntrying to have a drunken husband, you see; and it must be very trying to\r\nhave twins three times in succession, don\xe2\x80\x99t you think? But I feel sure\r\nthey meant to be good to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarilla asked no more questions. Anne gave herself up to a silent\r\nrapture over the shore road and Marilla guided the sorrel abstractedly\r\nwhile she pondered deeply. Pity was suddenly stirring in her heart for\r\nthe child. What a starved, unloved life she had had--a life of drudgery\r\nand poverty and neglect; for Marilla was shrewd enough to read between\r\nthe lines of Anne\xe2\x80\x99s history and divine the truth. No wonder she had been\r\nso delighted at the prospect of a real home. It was a pity she had to be\r\nsent back. What if she, Marilla, should indulge Matthew\xe2\x80\x99s unaccountable\r\nwhim and let her stay? He was set on it; and the child seemed a nice,\r\nteachable little thing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s got too much to say,\xe2\x80\x9d thought Marilla, \xe2\x80\x9cbut she might be trained\r\nout of that. And there\xe2\x80\x99s nothing rude or slangy in what she does say.\r\nShe\xe2\x80\x99s ladylike. It\xe2\x80\x99s likely her people were nice folks.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe shore road was \xe2\x80\x9cwoodsy and wild and lonesome.\xe2\x80\x9d On the right hand,\r\nscrub firs, their spirits quite unbroken by long years of tussle with\r\nthe gulf winds, grew thickly. On the left were the steep red sandstone\r\ncliffs, so near the track in places that a mare of less steadiness than\r\nthe sorrel might have tried the nerves of the people behind her. Down\r\nat the base of the cliffs were heaps of surf-worn rocks or little sandy\r\ncoves inlaid with pebbles as with ocean jewels; beyond lay the sea,\r\nshimmering and blue, and over it soared the gulls, their pinions\r\nflashing silvery in the sunlight.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t the sea wonderful?\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne, rousing from a long, wide-eyed\r\nsilence. \xe2\x80\x9cOnce, when I lived in Marysville, Mr. Thomas hired an express\r\nwagon and took us all to spend the day at the shore ten miles away.\r\nI enjoyed every moment of that day, even if I had to look after the\r\nchildren all the time. I lived it over in happy dreams for years.\r\nBut this shore is nicer than the Marysville shore. Aren\xe2\x80\x99t those gulls\r\nsplendid? Would you like to be a gull? I think I would--that is, if I\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be a human girl. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you think it would be nice to wake up at\r\nsunrise and swoop down over the water and away out over that lovely blue\r\nall day; and then at night to fly back to one\xe2\x80\x99s nest? Oh, I can just\r\nimagine myself doing it. What big house is that just ahead, please?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the White Sands Hotel. Mr. Kirke runs it, but the season hasn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nbegun yet. There are heaps of Americans come there for the summer. They\r\nthink this shore is just about right.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was afraid it might be Mrs. Spencer\xe2\x80\x99s place,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne mournfully.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t want to get there. Somehow, it will seem like the end of\r\neverything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VI. Marilla Makes Up Her Mind\r\n\r\n\r\n|GET there they did, however, in due season. Mrs. Spencer lived in a big\r\nyellow house at White Sands Cove, and she came to the door with surprise\r\nand welcome mingled on her benevolent face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear, dear,\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed, \xe2\x80\x9cyou\xe2\x80\x99re the last folks I was looking for\r\ntoday, but I\xe2\x80\x99m real glad to see you. You\xe2\x80\x99ll put your horse in? And how\r\nare you, Anne?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m as well as can be expected, thank you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne smilelessly. A\r\nblight seemed to have descended on her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose we\xe2\x80\x99ll stay a little while to rest the mare,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cbut I promised Matthew I\xe2\x80\x99d be home early. The fact is, Mrs. Spencer,\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x99s been a queer mistake somewhere, and I\xe2\x80\x99ve come over to see where\r\nit is. We send word, Matthew and I, for you to bring us a boy from the\r\nasylum.
