We told your brother Robert to tell you we wanted a boy ten or\r\neleven years old.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMarilla Cuthbert, you don\xe2\x80\x99t say so!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Spencer in distress.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, Robert sent word down by his daughter Nancy and she said you\r\nwanted a girl--didn\xe2\x80\x99t she Flora Jane?\xe2\x80\x9d appealing to her daughter who had\r\ncome out to the steps.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe certainly did, Miss Cuthbert,\xe2\x80\x9d corroborated Flora Jane earnestly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m dreadful sorry,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Spencer. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s too bad; but it certainly\r\nwasn\xe2\x80\x99t my fault, you see, Miss Cuthbert. I did the best I could and I\r\nthought I was following your instructions. Nancy is a terrible flighty\r\nthing. I\xe2\x80\x99ve often had to scold her well for her heedlessness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was our own fault,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla resignedly. \xe2\x80\x9cWe should have come\r\nto you ourselves and not left an important message to be passed along by\r\nword of mouth in that fashion. Anyhow, the mistake has been made and the\r\nonly thing to do is to set it right. Can we send the child back to the\r\nasylum? I suppose they\xe2\x80\x99ll take her back, won\xe2\x80\x99t they?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose so,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Spencer thoughtfully, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I don\xe2\x80\x99t think\r\nit will be necessary to send her back. Mrs. Peter Blewett was up here\r\nyesterday, and she was saying to me how much she wished she\xe2\x80\x99d sent by me\r\nfor a little girl to help her. Mrs. Peter has a large family, you know,\r\nand she finds it hard to get help. Anne will be the very girl for you. I\r\ncall it positively providential.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarilla did not look as if she thought Providence had much to do with\r\nthe matter. Here was an unexpectedly good chance to get this unwelcome\r\norphan off her hands, and she did not even feel grateful for it.\r\n\r\nShe knew Mrs. Peter Blewett only by sight as a small, shrewish-faced\r\nwoman without an ounce of superfluous flesh on her bones. But she had\r\nheard of her. \xe2\x80\x9cA terrible worker and driver,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Peter was said to\r\nbe; and discharged servant girls told fearsome tales of her temper and\r\nstinginess, and her family of pert, quarrelsome children. Marilla felt\r\na qualm of conscience at the thought of handing Anne over to her tender\r\nmercies.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I\xe2\x80\x99ll go in and we\xe2\x80\x99ll talk the matter over,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd if there isn\xe2\x80\x99t Mrs. Peter coming up the lane this blessed minute!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n exclaimed Mrs. Spencer, bustling her guests through the hall into the\r\nparlor, where a deadly chill struck on them as if the air had been\r\nstrained so long through dark green, closely drawn blinds that it had\r\nlost every particle of warmth it had ever possessed. \xe2\x80\x9cThat is real\r\nlucky, for we can settle the matter right away. Take the armchair, Miss\r\nCuthbert. Anne, you sit here on the ottoman and don\xe2\x80\x99t wiggle. Let\r\nme take your hats. Flora Jane, go out and put the kettle on. Good\r\nafternoon, Mrs. Blewett. We were just saying how fortunate it was you\r\nhappened along. Let me introduce you two ladies. Mrs. Blewett, Miss\r\nCuthbert. Please excuse me for just a moment. I forgot to tell Flora\r\nJane to take the buns out of the oven.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Spencer whisked away, after pulling up the blinds. Anne sitting\r\nmutely on the ottoman, with her hands clasped tightly in her lap, stared\r\nat Mrs Blewett as one fascinated. Was she to be given into the keeping\r\nof this sharp-faced, sharp-eyed woman? She felt a lump coming up in her\r\nthroat and her eyes smarted painfully. She was beginning to be afraid\r\nshe couldn\xe2\x80\x99t keep the tears back when Mrs. Spencer returned, flushed\r\nand beaming, quite capable of taking any and every difficulty, physical,\r\nmental or spiritual, into consideration and settling it out of hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt seems there\xe2\x80\x99s been a mistake about this little girl, Mrs. Blewett,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n she said. \xe2\x80\x9cI was under the impression that Mr. and Miss Cuthbert wanted\r\na little girl to adopt. I was certainly told so. But it seems it was a\r\nboy they wanted. So if you\xe2\x80\x99re still of the same mind you were yesterday,\r\nI think she\xe2\x80\x99ll be just the thing for you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Blewett darted her eyes over Anne from head to foot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow old are you and what\xe2\x80\x99s your name?\xe2\x80\x9d she demanded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnne Shirley,\xe2\x80\x9d faltered the shrinking child, not daring to make any\r\nstipulations regarding the spelling thereof, \xe2\x80\x9cand I\xe2\x80\x99m eleven years old.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHumph! You don\xe2\x80\x99t look as if there was much to you. But you\xe2\x80\x99re wiry. I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t know but the wiry ones are the best after all. Well, if I take you\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ll have to be a good girl, you know--good and smart and respectful.\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll expect you to earn your keep, and no mistake about that. Yes, I\r\nsuppose I might as well take her off your hands, Miss Cuthbert. The\r\nbaby\xe2\x80\x99s awful fractious, and I\xe2\x80\x99m clean worn out attending to him. If you\r\nlike I can take her right home now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarilla looked at Anne and softened at sight of the child\xe2\x80\x99s pale face\r\nwith its look of mute misery--the misery of a helpless little creature\r\nwho finds itself once more caught in the trap from which it had escaped.\r\nMarilla felt an uncomfortable conviction that, if she denied the appeal\r\nof that look, it would haunt her to her dying day. More-over, she did\r\nnot fancy Mrs. Blewett. To hand a sensitive, \xe2\x80\x9chighstrung\xe2\x80\x9d child over to\r\nsuch a woman! No, she could not take the responsibility of doing that!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know,\xe2\x80\x9d she said slowly. \xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t say that Matthew and I\r\nhad absolutely decided that we wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t keep her. In fact I may say that\r\nMatthew is disposed to keep her. I just came over to find out how the\r\nmistake had occurred. I think I\xe2\x80\x99d better take her home again and talk it\r\nover with Matthew. I feel that I oughtn\xe2\x80\x99t to decide on anything without\r\nconsulting him. If we make up our mind not to keep her we\xe2\x80\x99ll bring or\r\nsend her over to you tomorrow night. If we don\xe2\x80\x99t you may know that she\r\nis going to stay with us. Will that suit you, Mrs. Blewett?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose it\xe2\x80\x99ll have to,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Blewett ungraciously.\r\n\r\nDuring Marilla\xe2\x80\x99s speech a sunrise had been dawning on Anne\xe2\x80\x99s face. First\r\nthe look of despair faded out; then came a faint flush of hope;\r\nher eyes grew deep and bright as morning stars. The child was quite\r\ntransfigured; and, a moment later, when Mrs. Spencer and Mrs. Blewett\r\nwent out in quest of a recipe the latter had come to borrow she sprang\r\nup and flew across the room to Marilla.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Miss Cuthbert, did you really say that perhaps you would let me\r\nstay at Green Gables?\xe2\x80\x9d she said, in a breathless whisper, as if speaking\r\naloud might shatter the glorious possibility. \xe2\x80\x9cDid you really say it? Or\r\ndid I only imagine that you did?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think you\xe2\x80\x99d better learn to control that imagination of yours, Anne,\r\nif you can\xe2\x80\x99t distinguish between what is real and what isn\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMarilla crossly. \xe2\x80\x9cYes, you did hear me say just that and no more. It\r\nisn\xe2\x80\x99t decided yet and perhaps we will conclude to let Mrs. Blewett take\r\nyou after all. She certainly needs you much more than I do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d rather go back to the asylum than go to live with her,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne\r\npassionately. \xe2\x80\x9cShe looks exactly like a--like a gimlet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarilla smothered a smile under the conviction that Anne must be\r\nreproved for such a speech.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA little girl like you should be ashamed of talking so about a lady and\r\na stranger,\xe2\x80\x9d she said severely. \xe2\x80\x9cGo back and sit down quietly and hold\r\nyour tongue and behave as a good girl should.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll try to do and be anything you want me, if you\xe2\x80\x99ll only keep me,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said Anne, returning meekly to her ottoman.\r\n\r\nWhen they arrived back at Green Gables that evening Matthew met them in\r\nthe lane. Marilla from afar had noted him prowling along it and guessed\r\nhis motive. She was prepared for the relief she read in his face when he\r\nsaw that she had at least brought back Anne back with her. But she said\r\nnothing, to him, relative to the affair, until they were both out in the\r\nyard behind the barn milking the cows. Then she briefly told him Anne\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhistory and the result of the interview with Mrs. Spencer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t give a dog I liked to that Blewett woman,\xe2\x80\x9d said Matthew with\r\nunusual vim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t fancy her style myself,\xe2\x80\x9d admitted Marilla, \xe2\x80\x9cbut it\xe2\x80\x99s that\r\nor keeping her ourselves, Matthew. And since you seem to want her, I\r\nsuppose I\xe2\x80\x99m willing--or have to be. I\xe2\x80\x99ve been thinking over the idea\r\nuntil I\xe2\x80\x99ve got kind of used to it. It seems a sort of duty. I\xe2\x80\x99ve never\r\nbrought up a child, especially a girl, and I dare say I\xe2\x80\x99ll make a\r\nterrible mess of it. But I\xe2\x80\x99ll do my best. So far as I\xe2\x80\x99m concerned,\r\nMatthew, she may stay.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMatthew\xe2\x80\x99s shy face was a glow of delight.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell now, I reckoned you\xe2\x80\x99d come to see it in that light, Marilla,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid. \xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s such an interesting little thing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99d be more to the point if you could say she was a useful little\r\nthing,\xe2\x80\x9d retorted Marilla, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I\xe2\x80\x99ll make it my business to see she\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ntrained to be that. And mind, Matthew, you\xe2\x80\x99re not to go interfering with\r\nmy methods. Perhaps an old maid doesn\xe2\x80\x99t know much about bringing up\r\na child, but I guess she knows more than an old bachelor. So you just\r\nleave me to manage her. When I fail it\xe2\x80\x99ll be time enough to put your oar\r\nin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere, there, Marilla, you can have your own way,\xe2\x80\x9d said Matthew\r\nreassuringly. \xe2\x80\x9cOnly be as good and kind to her as you can without\r\nspoiling her. I kind of think she\xe2\x80\x99s one of the sort you can do anything\r\nwith if you only get her to love you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarilla sniffed, to express her contempt for Matthew\xe2\x80\x99s opinions\r\nconcerning anything feminine, and walked off to the dairy with the\r\npails.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t tell her tonight that she can stay,\xe2\x80\x9d she reflected, as she\r\nstrained the milk into the creamers. \xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99d be so excited that she\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t sleep a wink. Marilla Cuthbert, you\xe2\x80\x99re fairly in for it. Did\r\nyou ever suppose you\xe2\x80\x99d see the day when you\xe2\x80\x99d be adopting an orphan\r\ngirl? It\xe2\x80\x99s surprising enough; but not so surprising as that Matthew\r\nshould be at the bottom of it, him that always seemed to have such a\r\nmortal dread of little girls. Anyhow, we\xe2\x80\x99ve decided on the experiment\r\nand goodness only knows what will come of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VII. Anne Says Her Prayers\r\n\r\n\r\n|WHEN Marilla took Anne up to bed that night she said stiffly:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, Anne, I noticed last night that you threw your clothes all about\r\nthe floor when you took them off. That is a very untidy habit, and I\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t allow it at all. As soon as you take off any article of clothing\r\nfold it neatly and place it on the chair. I haven\xe2\x80\x99t any use at all for\r\nlittle girls who aren\xe2\x80\x99t neat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was so harrowed up in my mind last night that I didn\xe2\x80\x99t think about my\r\nclothes at all,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll fold them nicely tonight. They always\r\nmade us do that at the asylum. Half the time, though, I\xe2\x80\x99d forget, I\xe2\x80\x99d be\r\nin such a hurry to get into bed nice and quiet and imagine things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll have to remember a little better if you stay here,\xe2\x80\x9d admonished\r\nMarilla. \xe2\x80\x9cThere, that looks something like. Say your prayers now and get\r\ninto bed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never say any prayers,\xe2\x80\x9d announced Anne.\r\n\r\nMarilla looked horrified astonishment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, Anne, what do you mean? Were you never taught to say your prayers?\r\nGod always wants little girls to say their prayers. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you know who\r\nGod is, Anne?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98God is a spirit, infinite, eternal and unchangeable, in His being,\r\nwisdom, power, holiness, justice, goodness, and truth,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d responded Anne\r\npromptly and glibly.\r\n\r\nMarilla looked rather relieved.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo you do know something then, thank goodness! You\xe2\x80\x99re not quite a\r\nheathen. Where did you learn that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, at the asylum Sunday-school. They made us learn the whole\r\ncatechism. I liked it pretty well. There\xe2\x80\x99s something splendid about some\r\nof the words. \xe2\x80\x98Infinite, eternal and unchangeable.\xe2\x80\x99 Isn\xe2\x80\x99t that grand? It\r\nhas such a roll to it--just like a big organ playing. You couldn\xe2\x80\x99t quite\r\ncall it poetry, I suppose, but it sounds a lot like it, doesn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re not talking about poetry, Anne--we are talking about saying your\r\nprayers. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you know it\xe2\x80\x99s a terrible wicked thing not to say your\r\nprayers every night? I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid you are a very bad little girl.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99d find it easier to be bad than good if you had red hair,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nAnne reproachfully. \xe2\x80\x9cPeople who haven\xe2\x80\x99t red hair don\xe2\x80\x99t know what trouble\r\nis. Mrs. Thomas told me that God made my hair red _on purpose_, and I\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nnever cared about Him since. And anyhow I\xe2\x80\x99d always be too tired at night\r\nto bother saying prayers. People who have to look after twins can\xe2\x80\x99t be\r\nexpected to say their prayers. Now, do you honestly think they can?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarilla decided that Anne\xe2\x80\x99s religious training must be begun at once.\r\nPlainly there was no time to be lost.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must say your prayers while you are under my roof, Anne.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, of course, if you want me to,\xe2\x80\x9d assented Anne cheerfully. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d do\r\nanything to oblige you. But you\xe2\x80\x99ll have to tell me what to say for this\r\nonce. After I get into bed I\xe2\x80\x99ll imagine out a real nice prayer to say\r\nalways. I believe that it will be quite interesting, now that I come to\r\nthink of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must kneel down,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla in embarrassment.\r\n\r\nAnne knelt at Marilla\xe2\x80\x99s knee and looked up gravely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy must people kneel down to pray? If I really wanted to pray I\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\ntell you what I\xe2\x80\x99d do. I\xe2\x80\x99d go out into a great big field all alone\r\nor into the deep, deep, woods, and I\xe2\x80\x99d look up into the\r\nsky--up--up--up--into that lovely blue sky that looks as if there was no\r\nend to its blueness. And then I\xe2\x80\x99d just _feel_ a prayer. Well, I\xe2\x80\x99m ready.\r\nWhat am I to say?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarilla felt more embarrassed than ever. She had intended to teach Anne\r\nthe childish classic, \xe2\x80\x9cNow I lay me down to sleep.\xe2\x80\x9d But she had, as\r\nI have told you, the glimmerings of a sense of humor--which is simply\r\nanother name for a sense of fitness of things; and it suddenly occurred\r\nto her that that simple little prayer, sacred to white-robed childhood\r\nlisping at motherly knees, was entirely unsuited to this freckled witch\r\nof a girl who knew and cared nothing about God\xe2\x80\x99s love, since she had\r\nnever had it translated to her through the medium of human love.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re old enough to pray for yourself, Anne,\xe2\x80\x9d she said finally. \xe2\x80\x9cJust\r\nthank God for your blessings and ask Him humbly for the things you\r\nwant.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I\xe2\x80\x99ll do my best,\xe2\x80\x9d promised Anne, burying her face in Marilla\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nlap. \xe2\x80\x9cGracious heavenly Father--that\xe2\x80\x99s the way the ministers say it in\r\nchurch, so I suppose it\xe2\x80\x99s all right in private prayer, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\ninterjected, lifting her head for a moment.\r\n\r\n   \xe2\x80\x9cGracious heavenly Father, I thank Thee for the White\r\n   Way of Delight and the Lake of Shining Waters and Bonny\r\n   and the Snow Queen. I\xe2\x80\x99m really extremely grateful for\r\n   them. And that\xe2\x80\x99s all the blessings I can think of just\r\n   now to thank Thee for. As for the things I want,\r\n   they\xe2\x80\x99re so numerous that it would take a great deal of\r\n   time to name them all so I will only mention the two\r\n   most important. Please let me stay at Green Gables;\r\n   and please let me be good-looking when I grow up.\r\n   I remain,\r\n                    \xe2\x80\x9cYours respectfully,\r\n                       Anne Shirley.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere, did I do all right?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked eagerly, getting up. \xe2\x80\x9cI could\r\nhave made it much more flowery if I\xe2\x80\x99d had a little more time to think it\r\nover.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoor Marilla was only preserved from complete collapse by remembering\r\nthat it was not irreverence, but simply spiritual ignorance on the part\r\nof Anne that was responsible for this extraordinary petition. She tucked\r\nthe child up in bed, mentally vowing that she should be taught a prayer\r\nthe very next day, and was leaving the room with the light when Anne\r\ncalled her back.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve just thought of it now. I should have said, \xe2\x80\x98Amen\xe2\x80\x99 in place\r\nof \xe2\x80\x98yours respectfully,\xe2\x80\x99 shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t I?--the way the ministers do. I\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nforgotten it, but I felt a prayer should be finished off in some way, so\r\nI put in the other. Do you suppose it will make any difference?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI--I don\xe2\x80\x99t suppose it will,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla. \xe2\x80\x9cGo to sleep now like a good\r\nchild. Good night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can only say good night tonight with a clear conscience,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne,\r\ncuddling luxuriously down among her pillows.\r\n\r\nMarilla retreated to the kitchen, set the candle firmly on the table,\r\nand glared at Matthew.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMatthew Cuthbert, it\xe2\x80\x99s about time somebody adopted that child and\r\ntaught her something. She\xe2\x80\x99s next door to a perfect heathen. Will you\r\nbelieve that she never said a prayer in her life till tonight? I\xe2\x80\x99ll send\r\nher to the manse tomorrow and borrow the Peep of the Day series, that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwhat I\xe2\x80\x99ll do. And she shall go to Sunday-school just as soon as I can\r\nget some suitable clothes made for her. I foresee that I shall have\r\nmy hands full. Well, well, we can\xe2\x80\x99t get through this world without our\r\nshare of trouble. I\xe2\x80\x99ve had a pretty easy life of it so far, but my time\r\nhas come at last and I suppose I\xe2\x80\x99ll just have to make the best of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VIII. Anne\xe2\x80\x99s Bringing-up Is Begun\r\n\r\n\r\n|FOR reasons best known to herself, Marilla did not tell Anne that\r\nshe was to stay at Green Gables until the next afternoon. During the\r\nforenoon she kept the child busy with various tasks and watched over her\r\nwith a keen eye while she did them. By noon she had concluded that Anne\r\nwas smart and obedient, willing to work and quick to learn; her most\r\nserious shortcoming seemed to be a tendency to fall into daydreams in\r\nthe middle of a task and forget all about it until such time as she was\r\nsharply recalled to earth by a reprimand or a catastrophe.\r\n\r\nWhen Anne had finished washing the dinner dishes she suddenly confronted\r\nMarilla with the air and expression of one desperately determined to\r\nlearn the worst. Her thin little body trembled from head to foot; her\r\nface flushed and her eyes dilated until they were almost black; she\r\nclasped her hands tightly and said in an imploring voice:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, please, Miss Cuthbert, won\xe2\x80\x99t you tell me if you are going to send\r\nme away or not? I\xe2\x80\x99ve tried to be patient all the morning, but I really\r\nfeel that I cannot bear not knowing any longer. It\xe2\x80\x99s a dreadful feeling.\r\nPlease tell me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou haven\xe2\x80\x99t scalded the dishcloth in clean hot water as I told you to\r\ndo,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla immovably. \xe2\x80\x9cJust go and do it before you ask any more\r\nquestions, Anne.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne went and attended to the dishcloth. Then she returned to Marilla\r\nand fastened imploring eyes of the latter\xe2\x80\x99s face. \xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla,\r\nunable to find any excuse for deferring her explanation longer, \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nsuppose I might as well tell you. Matthew and I have decided to keep\r\nyou--that is, if you will try to be a good little girl and show yourself\r\ngrateful. Why, child, whatever is the matter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m crying,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne in a tone of bewilderment. \xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t think why.\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99m glad as glad can be. Oh, _glad_ doesn\xe2\x80\x99t seem the right word at all. I\r\nwas glad about the White Way and the cherry blossoms--but this! Oh, it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsomething more than glad. I\xe2\x80\x99m so happy. I\xe2\x80\x99ll try to be so good. It\r\nwill be uphill work, I expect, for Mrs. Thomas often told me I was\r\ndesperately wicked. However, I\xe2\x80\x99ll do my very best. But can you tell me\r\nwhy I\xe2\x80\x99m crying?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose it\xe2\x80\x99s because you\xe2\x80\x99re all excited and worked up,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla\r\ndisapprovingly. \xe2\x80\x9cSit down on that chair and try to calm yourself. I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\nafraid you both cry and laugh far too easily. Yes, you can stay here and\r\nwe will try to do right by you. You must go to school; but it\xe2\x80\x99s only a\r\nfortnight till vacation so it isn\xe2\x80\x99t worth while for you to start before\r\nit opens again in September.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat am I to call you?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Anne. \xe2\x80\x9cShall I always say Miss Cuthbert?\r\nCan I call you Aunt Marilla?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; you\xe2\x80\x99ll call me just plain Marilla. I\xe2\x80\x99m not used to being called\r\nMiss Cuthbert and it would make me nervous.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt sounds awfully disrespectful to just say Marilla,\xe2\x80\x9d protested Anne.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI guess there\xe2\x80\x99ll be nothing disrespectful in it if you\xe2\x80\x99re careful\r\nto speak respectfully. Everybody, young and old, in Avonlea calls me\r\nMarilla except the minister. He says Miss Cuthbert--when he thinks of\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d love to call you Aunt Marilla,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne wistfully. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve never\r\nhad an aunt or any relation at all--not even a grandmother. It would\r\nmake me feel as if I really belonged to you. Can\xe2\x80\x99t I call you Aunt\r\nMarilla?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. I\xe2\x80\x99m not your aunt and I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe in calling people names that\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t belong to them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut we could imagine you were my aunt.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI couldn\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla grimly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you never imagine things different from what they really are?\xe2\x80\x9d asked\r\nAnne wide-eyed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh!\xe2\x80\x9d Anne drew a long breath. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, Miss--Marilla, how much you miss!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t believe in imagining things different from what they really\r\nare,\xe2\x80\x9d retorted Marilla. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen the Lord puts us in certain circumstances\r\nHe doesn\xe2\x80\x99t mean for us to imagine them away. And that reminds me. Go\r\ninto the sitting room, Anne--be sure your feet are clean and don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nlet any flies in--and bring me out the illustrated card that\xe2\x80\x99s on the\r\nmantelpiece. The Lord\xe2\x80\x99s Prayer is on it and you\xe2\x80\x99ll devote your spare\r\ntime this afternoon to learning it off by heart. There\xe2\x80\x99s to be no more\r\nof such praying as I heard last night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose I was very awkward,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne apologetically, \xe2\x80\x9cbut then, you\r\nsee, I\xe2\x80\x99d never had any practice. You couldn\xe2\x80\x99t really expect a person\r\nto pray very well the first time she tried, could you? I thought out a\r\nsplendid prayer after I went to bed, just as I promised you I would.\r\nIt was nearly as long as a minister\xe2\x80\x99s and so poetical. But would you\r\nbelieve it? I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t remember one word when I woke up this morning.\r\nAnd I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid I\xe2\x80\x99ll never be able to think out another one as good.\r\nSomehow, things never are so good when they\xe2\x80\x99re thought out a second\r\ntime. Have you ever noticed that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere is something for you to notice, Anne. When I tell you to do\r\na thing I want you to obey me at once and not stand stock-still and\r\ndiscourse about it. Just you go and do as I bid you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne promptly departed for the sitting-room across the hall; she failed\r\nto return; after waiting ten minutes Marilla laid down her knitting\r\nand marched after her with a grim expression. She found Anne standing\r\nmotionless before a picture hanging on the wall between the two windows,\r\nwith her eyes a-star with dreams. The white and green light strained\r\nthrough apple trees and clustering vines outside fell over the rapt\r\nlittle figure with a half-unearthly radiance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnne, whatever are you thinking of?\xe2\x80\x9d demanded Marilla sharply.\r\n\r\nAnne came back to earth with a start.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, pointing to the picture--a rather vivid chromo\r\nentitled, \xe2\x80\x9cChrist Blessing Little Children\xe2\x80\x9d--\xe2\x80\x9cand I was just imagining I\r\nwas one of them--that I was the little girl in the blue dress, standing\r\noff by herself in the corner as if she didn\xe2\x80\x99t belong to anybody, like\r\nme. She looks lonely and sad, don\xe2\x80\x99t you think? I guess she hadn\xe2\x80\x99t any\r\nfather or mother of her own. But she wanted to be blessed, too, so she\r\njust crept shyly up on the outside of the crowd, hoping nobody would\r\nnotice her--except Him. I\xe2\x80\x99m sure I know just how she felt. Her heart\r\nmust have beat and her hands must have got cold, like mine did when I\r\nasked you if I could stay. She was afraid He mightn\xe2\x80\x99t notice her. But\r\nit\xe2\x80\x99s likely He did, don\xe2\x80\x99t you think? I\xe2\x80\x99ve been trying to imagine it all\r\nout--her edging a little nearer all the time until she was quite close\r\nto Him; and then He would look at her and put His hand on her hair and\r\noh, such a thrill of joy as would run over her! But I wish the artist\r\nhadn\xe2\x80\x99t painted Him so sorrowful looking. All His pictures are like that,\r\nif you\xe2\x80\x99ve noticed. But I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe He could really have looked so\r\nsad or the children would have been afraid of Him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnne,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla, wondering why she had not broken into this speech\r\nlong before, \xe2\x80\x9cyou shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t talk that way. It\xe2\x80\x99s irreverent--positively\r\nirreverent.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne\xe2\x80\x99s eyes marveled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, I felt just as reverent as could be. I\xe2\x80\x99m sure I didn\xe2\x80\x99t mean to be\r\nirreverent.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell I don\xe2\x80\x99t suppose you did--but it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t sound right to talk so\r\nfamiliarly about such things. And another thing, Anne, when I send you\r\nafter something you\xe2\x80\x99re to bring it at once and not fall into mooning and\r\nimagining before pictures. Remember that. Take that card and come right\r\nto the kitchen. Now, sit down in the corner and learn that prayer off by\r\nheart.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne set the card up against the jugful of apple blossoms she had\r\nbrought in to decorate the dinner-table--Marilla had eyed that\r\ndecoration askance, but had said nothing--propped her chin on her hands,\r\nand fell to studying it intently for several silent minutes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI like this,\xe2\x80\x9d she announced at length. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s beautiful. I\xe2\x80\x99ve heard it\r\nbefore--I heard the superintendent of the asylum Sunday school say it\r\nover once. But I didn\xe2\x80\x99t like it then. He had such a cracked voice and\r\nhe prayed it so mournfully. I really felt sure he thought praying was a\r\ndisagreeable duty. This isn\xe2\x80\x99t poetry, but it makes me feel just the same\r\nway poetry does. \xe2\x80\x98Our Father who art in heaven hallowed be Thy name.\xe2\x80\x99\r\nThat is just like a line of music. Oh, I\xe2\x80\x99m so glad you thought of making\r\nme learn this, Miss--Marilla.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, learn it and hold your tongue,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla shortly.\r\n\r\nAnne tipped the vase of apple blossoms near enough to bestow a soft\r\nkiss on a pink-cupped bud, and then studied diligently for some moments\r\nlonger.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMarilla,\xe2\x80\x9d she demanded presently, \xe2\x80\x9cdo you think that I shall ever have\r\na bosom friend in Avonlea?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA--a what kind of friend?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA bosom friend--an intimate friend, you know--a really kindred spirit\r\nto whom I can confide my inmost soul. I\xe2\x80\x99ve dreamed of meeting her all\r\nmy life. I never really supposed I would, but so many of my loveliest\r\ndreams have come true all at once that perhaps this one will, too. Do\r\nyou think it\xe2\x80\x99s possible?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDiana Barry lives over at Orchard Slope and she\xe2\x80\x99s about your age. She\xe2\x80\x99s\r\na very nice little girl, and perhaps she will be a playmate for you when\r\nshe comes home. She\xe2\x80\x99s visiting her aunt over at Carmody just now. You\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nhave to be careful how you behave yourself, though. Mrs. Barry is a\r\nvery particular woman. She won\xe2\x80\x99t let Diana play with any little girl who\r\nisn\xe2\x80\x99t nice and good.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne looked at Marilla through the apple blossoms, her eyes aglow with\r\ninterest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is Diana like? Her hair isn\xe2\x80\x99t red, is it? Oh, I hope not. It\xe2\x80\x99s bad\r\nenough to have red hair myself, but I positively couldn\xe2\x80\x99t endure it in a\r\nbosom friend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDiana is a very pretty little girl. She has black eyes and hair and\r\nrosy cheeks. And she is good and smart, which is better than being\r\npretty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarilla was as fond of morals as the Duchess in Wonderland, and was\r\nfirmly convinced that one should be tacked on to every remark made to a\r\nchild who was being brought up.\r\n\r\nBut Anne waved the moral inconsequently aside and seized only on the\r\ndelightful possibilities before it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I\xe2\x80\x99m so glad she\xe2\x80\x99s pretty. Next to being beautiful oneself--and\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x99s impossible in my case--it would be best to have a beautiful bosom\r\nfriend. When I lived with Mrs. Thomas she had a bookcase in her sitting\r\nroom with glass doors. There weren\xe2\x80\x99t any books in it; Mrs. Thomas kept\r\nher best china and her preserves there--when she had any preserves to\r\nkeep. One of the doors was broken. Mr. Thomas smashed it one night\r\nwhen he was slightly intoxicated. But the other was whole and I used to\r\npretend that my reflection in it was another little girl who lived in\r\nit. I called her Katie Maurice, and we were very intimate. I used to\r\ntalk to her by the hour, especially on Sunday, and tell her everything.\r\nKatie was the comfort and consolation of my life. We used to pretend\r\nthat the bookcase was enchanted and that if I only knew the spell I\r\ncould open the door and step right into the room where Katie Maurice\r\nlived, instead of into Mrs. Thomas\xe2\x80\x99 shelves of preserves and china. And\r\nthen Katie Maurice would have taken me by the hand and led me out into a\r\nwonderful place, all flowers and sunshine and fairies, and we would have\r\nlived there happy for ever after. When I went to live with Mrs. Hammond\r\nit just broke my heart to leave Katie Maurice. She felt it dreadfully,\r\ntoo, I know she did, for she was crying when she kissed me good-bye\r\nthrough the bookcase door. There was no bookcase at Mrs. Hammond\xe2\x80\x99s. But\r\njust up the river a little way from the house there was a long green\r\nlittle valley, and the loveliest echo lived there. It echoed back every\r\nword you said, even if you didn\xe2\x80\x99t talk a bit loud. So I imagined that it\r\nwas a little girl called Violetta and we were great friends and I loved\r\nher almost as well as I loved Katie Maurice--not quite, but almost, you\r\nknow. The night before I went to the asylum I said good-bye to Violetta,\r\nand oh, her good-bye came back to me in such sad, sad tones. I had\r\nbecome so attached to her that I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t the heart to imagine a bosom\r\nfriend at the asylum, even if there had been any scope for imagination\r\nthere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think it\xe2\x80\x99s just as well there wasn\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla drily. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t approve of such goings-on. You seem to half believe your own\r\nimaginations. It will be well for you to have a real live friend to\r\nput such nonsense out of your head. But don\xe2\x80\x99t let Mrs. Barry hear you\r\ntalking about your Katie Maurices and your Violettas or she\xe2\x80\x99ll think you\r\ntell stories.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I won\xe2\x80\x99t. I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t talk of them to everybody--their memories are\r\ntoo sacred for that. But I thought I\xe2\x80\x99d like to have you know about them.\r\nOh, look, here\xe2\x80\x99s a big bee just tumbled out of an apple blossom. Just\r\nthink what a lovely place to live--in an apple blossom! Fancy going to\r\nsleep in it when the wind was rocking it. If I wasn\xe2\x80\x99t a human girl I\r\nthink I\xe2\x80\x99d like to be a bee and live among the flowers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYesterday you wanted to be a sea gull,\xe2\x80\x9d sniffed Marilla. \xe2\x80\x9cI think you\r\nare very fickle minded. I told you to learn that prayer and not talk.\r\nBut it seems impossible for you to stop talking if you\xe2\x80\x99ve got anybody\r\nthat will listen to you. So go up to your room and learn it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I know it pretty nearly all now--all but just the last line.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, never mind, do as I tell you. Go to your room and finish learning\r\nit well, and stay there until I call you down to help me get tea.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan I take the apple blossoms with me for company?\xe2\x80\x9d pleaded Anne.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; you don\xe2\x80\x99t want your room cluttered up with flowers. You should have\r\nleft them on the tree in the first place.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did feel a little that way, too,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne. \xe2\x80\x9cI kind of felt I\r\nshouldn\xe2\x80\x99t shorten their lovely lives by picking them--I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t want\r\nto be picked if I were an apple blossom. But the temptation was\r\n_irresistible_. What do you do when you meet with an irresistible\r\ntemptation?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnne, did you hear me tell you to go to your room?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne sighed, retreated to the east gable, and sat down in a chair by the\r\nwindow.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere--I know this prayer. I learned that last sentence coming\r\nupstairs. Now I\xe2\x80\x99m going to imagine things into this room so that they\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nalways stay imagined. The floor is covered with a white velvet carpet\r\nwith pink roses all over it and there are pink silk curtains at the\r\nwindows. The walls are hung with gold and silver brocade tapestry. The\r\nfurniture is mahogany. I never saw any mahogany, but it does sound _so_\r\nluxurious. This is a couch all heaped with gorgeous silken cushions,\r\npink and blue and crimson and gold, and I am reclining gracefully on it.\r\nI can see my reflection in that splendid big mirror hanging on the wall.\r\nI am tall and regal, clad in a gown of trailing white lace, with a\r\npearl cross on my breast and pearls in my hair. My hair is of midnight\r\ndarkness and my skin is a clear ivory pallor. My name is the Lady\r\nCordelia Fitzgerald. No, it isn\xe2\x80\x99t--I can\xe2\x80\x99t make _that_ seem real.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe danced up to the little looking-glass and peered into it. Her\r\npointed freckled face and solemn gray eyes peered back at her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re only Anne of Green Gables,\xe2\x80\x9d she said earnestly, \xe2\x80\x9cand I see you,\r\njust as you are looking now, whenever I try to imagine I\xe2\x80\x99m the Lady\r\nCordelia. But it\xe2\x80\x99s a million times nicer to be Anne of Green Gables than\r\nAnne of nowhere in particular, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe bent forward, kissed her reflection affectionately, and betook\r\nherself to the open window.\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear Snow Queen, good afternoon. And good afternoon dear birches down\r\nin the hollow. And good afternoon, dear gray house up on the hill. I\r\nwonder if Diana is to be my bosom friend. I hope she will, and I shall\r\nlove her very much. But I must never quite forget Katie Maurice\r\nand Violetta. They would feel so hurt if I did and I\xe2\x80\x99d hate to hurt\r\nanybody\xe2\x80\x99s feelings, even a little bookcase girl\xe2\x80\x99s or a little echo\r\ngirl\xe2\x80\x99s. I must be careful to remember them and send them a kiss every\r\nday.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne blew a couple of airy kisses from her fingertips past the cherry\r\nblossoms and then, with her chin in her hands, drifted luxuriously out\r\non a sea of daydreams.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER IX. Mrs. Rachel Lynde Is Properly Horrified\r\n\r\n\r\n|ANNE had been a fortnight at Green Gables before Mrs. Lynde arrived to\r\ninspect her. Mrs. Rachel, to do her justice, was not to blame for this.\r\nA severe and unseasonable attack of grippe had confined that good lady\r\nto her house ever since the occasion of her last visit to Green Gables.\r\nMrs. Rachel was not often sick and had a well-defined contempt for\r\npeople who were; but grippe, she asserted, was like no other illness on\r\nearth and could only be interpreted as one of the special visitations\r\nof Providence. As soon as her doctor allowed her to put her foot\r\nout-of-doors she hurried up to Green Gables, bursting with curiosity to\r\nsee Matthew and Marilla\xe2\x80\x99s orphan, concerning whom all sorts of stories\r\nand suppositions had gone abroad in Avonlea.\r\n\r\nAnne had made good use of every waking moment of that fortnight. Already\r\nshe was acquainted with every tree and shrub about the place. She had\r\ndiscovered that a lane opened out below the apple orchard and ran up\r\nthrough a belt of woodland; and she had explored it to its furthest end\r\nin all its delicious vagaries of brook and bridge, fir coppice and wild\r\ncherry arch, corners thick with fern, and branching byways of maple and\r\nmountain ash.\r\n\r\nShe had made friends with the spring down in the hollow--that wonderful\r\ndeep, clear icy-cold spring; it was set about with smooth red sandstones\r\nand rimmed in by great palm-like clumps of water fern; and beyond it was\r\na log bridge over the brook.\r\n\r\nThat bridge led Anne\xe2\x80\x99s dancing feet up over a wooded hill beyond, where\r\nperpetual twilight reigned under the straight, thick-growing firs and\r\nspruces; the only flowers there were myriads of delicate \xe2\x80\x9cJune bells,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n those shyest and sweetest of woodland blooms, and a few pale, aerial\r\nstarflowers, like the spirits of last year\xe2\x80\x99s blossoms. Gossamers\r\nglimmered like threads of silver among the trees and the fir boughs and\r\ntassels seemed to utter friendly speech.\r\n\r\nAll these raptured voyages of exploration were made in the odd half\r\nhours which she was allowed for play, and Anne talked Matthew and\r\nMarilla half-deaf over her discoveries. Not that Matthew complained, to\r\nbe sure; he listened to it all with a wordless smile of enjoyment on his\r\nface; Marilla permitted the \xe2\x80\x9cchatter\xe2\x80\x9d until she found herself becoming\r\ntoo interested in it, whereupon she always promptly quenched Anne by a\r\ncurt command to hold her tongue.\r\n\r\nAnne was out in the orchard when Mrs. Rachel came, wandering at her\r\nown sweet will through the lush, tremulous grasses splashed with ruddy\r\nevening sunshine; so that good lady had an excellent chance to talk\r\nher illness fully over, describing every ache and pulse beat with\r\nsuch evident enjoyment that Marilla thought even grippe must bring its\r\ncompensations. When details were exhausted Mrs. Rachel introduced the\r\nreal reason of her call.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve been hearing some surprising things about you and Matthew.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t suppose you are any more surprised than I am myself,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMarilla. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m getting over my surprise now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was too bad there was such a mistake,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Rachel\r\nsympathetically. \xe2\x80\x9cCouldn\xe2\x80\x99t you have sent her back?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose we could, but we decided not to. Matthew took a fancy to her.\r\nAnd I must say I like her myself--although I admit she has her faults.\r\nThe house seems a different place already. She\xe2\x80\x99s a real bright little\r\nthing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarilla said more than she had intended to say when she began, for she\r\nread disapproval in Mrs. Rachel\xe2\x80\x99s expression.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a great responsibility you\xe2\x80\x99ve taken on yourself,\xe2\x80\x9d said that\r\nlady gloomily, \xe2\x80\x9cespecially when you\xe2\x80\x99ve never had any experience with\r\nchildren. You don\xe2\x80\x99t know much about her or her real disposition, I\r\nsuppose, and there\xe2\x80\x99s no guessing how a child like that will turn out.\r\nBut I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to discourage you I\xe2\x80\x99m sure, Marilla.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m not feeling discouraged,\xe2\x80\x9d was Marilla\xe2\x80\x99s dry response, \xe2\x80\x9cwhen I make\r\nup my mind to do a thing it stays made up. I suppose you\xe2\x80\x99d like to see\r\nAnne. I\xe2\x80\x99ll call her in.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne came running in presently, her face sparkling with the delight of\r\nher orchard rovings; but, abashed at finding the delight herself in\r\nthe unexpected presence of a stranger, she halted confusedly inside\r\nthe door. She certainly was an odd-looking little creature in the short\r\ntight wincey dress she had worn from the asylum, below which her thin\r\nlegs seemed ungracefully long. Her freckles were more numerous and\r\nobtrusive than ever; the wind had ruffled her hatless hair into\r\nover-brilliant disorder; it had never looked redder than at that moment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, they didn\xe2\x80\x99t pick you for your looks, that\xe2\x80\x99s sure and certain,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n was Mrs. Rachel Lynde\xe2\x80\x99s emphatic comment. Mrs. Rachel was one of those\r\ndelightful and popular people who pride themselves on speaking their\r\nmind without fear or favor. \xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s terrible skinny and homely, Marilla.\r\nCome here, child, and let me have a look at you. Lawful heart, did\r\nany one ever see such freckles? And hair as red as carrots! Come here,\r\nchild, I say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne \xe2\x80\x9ccame there,\xe2\x80\x9d but not exactly as Mrs. Rachel expected. With one\r\nbound she crossed the kitchen floor and stood before Mrs. Rachel, her\r\nface scarlet with anger, her lips quivering, and her whole slender form\r\ntrembling from head to foot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hate you,\xe2\x80\x9d she cried in a choked voice, stamping her foot on the\r\nfloor. \xe2\x80\x9cI hate you--I hate you--I hate you--\xe2\x80\x9d a louder stamp with each\r\nassertion of hatred. \xe2\x80\x9cHow dare you call me skinny and ugly? How dare\r\nyou say I\xe2\x80\x99m freckled and redheaded? You are a rude, impolite, unfeeling\r\nwoman!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnne!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Marilla in consternation.\r\n\r\nBut Anne continued to face Mrs. Rachel undauntedly, head up, eyes\r\nblazing, hands clenched, passionate indignation exhaling from her like\r\nan atmosphere.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow dare you say such things about me?\xe2\x80\x9d she repeated vehemently. \xe2\x80\x9cHow\r\nwould you like to have such things said about you? How would you like\r\nto be told that you are fat and clumsy and probably hadn\xe2\x80\x99t a spark of\r\nimagination in you? I don\xe2\x80\x99t care if I do hurt your feelings by saying\r\nso! I hope I hurt them. You have hurt mine worse than they were ever\r\nhurt before even by Mrs. Thomas\xe2\x80\x99 intoxicated husband. And I\xe2\x80\x99ll _never_\r\nforgive you for it, never, never!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nStamp! Stamp!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid anybody ever see such a temper!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed the horrified Mrs.\r\nRachel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnne go to your room and stay there until I come up,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla,\r\nrecovering her powers of speech with difficulty.\r\n\r\nAnne, bursting into tears, rushed to the hall door, slammed it until the\r\ntins on the porch wall outside rattled in sympathy, and fled through the\r\nhall and up the stairs like a whirlwind. A subdued slam above told that\r\nthe door of the east gable had been shut with equal vehemence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I don\xe2\x80\x99t envy you your job bringing _that_ up, Marilla,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs.\r\nRachel with unspeakable solemnity.\r\n\r\nMarilla opened her lips to say she knew not what of apology or\r\ndeprecation. What she did say was a surprise to herself then and ever\r\nafterwards.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have twitted her about her looks, Rachel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMarilla Cuthbert, you don\xe2\x80\x99t mean to say that you are upholding her in\r\nsuch a terrible display of temper as we\xe2\x80\x99ve just seen?\xe2\x80\x9d demanded Mrs.\r\nRachel indignantly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla slowly, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m not trying to excuse her. She\xe2\x80\x99s been\r\nvery naughty and I\xe2\x80\x99ll have to give her a talking to about it. But we\r\nmust make allowances for her. She\xe2\x80\x99s never been taught what is right. And\r\nyou _were_ too hard on her, Rachel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarilla could not help tacking on that last sentence, although she was\r\nagain surprised at herself for doing it. Mrs. Rachel got up with an air\r\nof offended dignity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I see that I\xe2\x80\x99ll have to be very careful what I say after this,\r\nMarilla, since the fine feelings of orphans, brought from goodness\r\nknows where, have to be considered before anything else. Oh, no, I\xe2\x80\x99m not\r\nvexed--don\xe2\x80\x99t worry yourself. I\xe2\x80\x99m too sorry for you to leave any room for\r\nanger in my mind. You\xe2\x80\x99ll have your own troubles with that child. But\r\nif you\xe2\x80\x99ll take my advice--which I suppose you won\xe2\x80\x99t do, although I\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nbrought up ten children and buried two--you\xe2\x80\x99ll do that \xe2\x80\x98talking to\xe2\x80\x99 you\r\nmention with a fair-sized birch switch. I should think _that_ would be the\r\nmost effective language for that kind of a child. Her temper matches her\r\nhair I guess. Well, good evening, Marilla. I hope you\xe2\x80\x99ll come down to\r\nsee me often as usual. But you can\xe2\x80\x99t expect me to visit here again in a\r\nhurry, if I\xe2\x80\x99m liable to be flown at and insulted in such a fashion. It\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsomething new in _my_ experience.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhereat Mrs. Rachel swept out and away--if a fat woman who always\r\nwaddled _could_ be said to sweep away--and Marilla with a very solemn face\r\nbetook herself to the east gable.\r\n\r\nOn the way upstairs she pondered uneasily as to what she ought to do.\r\nShe felt no little dismay over the scene that had just been enacted.\r\nHow unfortunate that Anne should have displayed such temper before Mrs.\r\nRachel Lynde, of all people! Then Marilla suddenly became aware of an\r\nuncomfortable and rebuking consciousness that she felt more humiliation\r\nover this than sorrow over the discovery of such a serious defect\r\nin Anne\xe2\x80\x99s disposition. And how was she to punish her? The amiable\r\nsuggestion of the birch switch--to the efficiency of which all of Mrs.\r\nRachel\xe2\x80\x99s own children could have borne smarting testimony--did not\r\nappeal to Marilla. She did not believe she could whip a child. No,\r\nsome other method of punishment must be found to bring Anne to a proper\r\nrealization of the enormity of her offense.\r\n\r\nMarilla found Anne face downward on her bed, crying bitterly, quite\r\noblivious of muddy boots on a clean counterpane.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnne,\xe2\x80\x9d she said not ungently.\r\n\r\nNo answer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnne,\xe2\x80\x9d with greater severity, \xe2\x80\x9cget off that bed this minute and listen\r\nto what I have to say to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne squirmed off the bed and sat rigidly on a chair beside it, her face\r\nswollen and tear-stained and her eyes fixed stubbornly on the floor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is a nice way for you to behave. Anne! Aren\xe2\x80\x99t you ashamed of\r\nyourself?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe hadn\xe2\x80\x99t any right to call me ugly and redheaded,\xe2\x80\x9d retorted Anne,\r\nevasive and defiant.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou hadn\xe2\x80\x99t any right to fly into such a fury and talk the way you did\r\nto her, Anne. I was ashamed of you--thoroughly ashamed of you. I\r\nwanted you to behave nicely to Mrs. Lynde, and instead of that you have\r\ndisgraced me. I\xe2\x80\x99m sure I don\xe2\x80\x99t know why you should lose your temper like\r\nthat just because Mrs. Lynde said you were red-haired and homely. You\r\nsay it yourself often enough.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, but there\xe2\x80\x99s such a difference between saying a thing yourself and\r\nhearing other people say it,\xe2\x80\x9d wailed Anne. \xe2\x80\x9cYou may know a thing is\r\nso, but you can\xe2\x80\x99t help hoping other people don\xe2\x80\x99t quite think it is. I\r\nsuppose you think I have an awful temper, but I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t help it. When\r\nshe said those things something just rose right up in me and choked me.\r\nI _had_ to fly out at her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you made a fine exhibition of yourself I must say. Mrs. Lynde\r\nwill have a nice story to tell about you everywhere--and she\xe2\x80\x99ll tell\r\nit, too. It was a dreadful thing for you to lose your temper like that,\r\nAnne.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust imagine how you would feel if somebody told you to your face that\r\nyou were skinny and ugly,\xe2\x80\x9d pleaded Anne tearfully.\r\n\r\nAn old remembrance suddenly rose up before Marilla. She had been a very\r\nsmall child when she had heard one aunt say of her to another, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat a\r\npity she is such a dark, homely little thing.\xe2\x80\x9d Marilla was every day of\r\nfifty before the sting had gone out of that memory.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t say that I think Mrs. Lynde was exactly right in saying what\r\nshe did to you, Anne,\xe2\x80\x9d she admitted in a softer tone. \xe2\x80\x9cRachel is too\r\noutspoken. But that is no excuse for such behavior on your part. She\r\nwas a stranger and an elderly person and my visitor--all three very good\r\nreasons why you should have been respectful to her. You were rude and\r\nsaucy and\xe2\x80\x9d--Marilla had a saving inspiration of punishment--\xe2\x80\x9cyou must go\r\nto her and tell her you are very sorry for your bad temper and ask her\r\nto forgive you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can never do that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne determinedly and darkly. \xe2\x80\x9cYou can\r\npunish me in any way you like, Marilla. You can shut me up in a dark,\r\ndamp dungeon inhabited by snakes and toads and feed me only on bread and\r\nwater and I shall not complain. But I cannot ask Mrs. Lynde to forgive\r\nme.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re not in the habit of shutting people up in dark damp dungeons,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said Marilla drily, \xe2\x80\x9cespecially as they\xe2\x80\x99re rather scarce in Avonlea. But\r\napologize to Mrs. Lynde you must and shall and you\xe2\x80\x99ll stay here in your\r\nroom until you can tell me you\xe2\x80\x99re willing to do it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall have to stay here forever then,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne mournfully, \xe2\x80\x9cbecause\r\nI can\xe2\x80\x99t tell Mrs. Lynde I\xe2\x80\x99m sorry I said those things to her. How can\r\nI? I\xe2\x80\x99m _not_ sorry. I\xe2\x80\x99m sorry I\xe2\x80\x99ve vexed you; but I\xe2\x80\x99m _glad_ I told her just\r\nwhat I did. It was a great satisfaction. I can\xe2\x80\x99t say I\xe2\x80\x99m sorry when I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\nnot, can I? I can\xe2\x80\x99t even _imagine_ I\xe2\x80\x99m sorry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps your imagination will be in better working order by the\r\nmorning,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla, rising to depart. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll have the night to\r\nthink over your conduct in and come to a better frame of mind. You said\r\nyou would try to be a very good girl if we kept you at Green Gables, but\r\nI must say it hasn\xe2\x80\x99t seemed very much like it this evening.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLeaving this Parthian shaft to rankle in Anne\xe2\x80\x99s stormy bosom, Marilla\r\ndescended to the kitchen, grievously troubled in mind and vexed in\r\nsoul. She was as angry with herself as with Anne, because, whenever she\r\nrecalled Mrs. Rachel\xe2\x80\x99s dumbfounded countenance her lips twitched with\r\namusement and she felt a most reprehensible desire to laugh.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER X. Anne\xe2\x80\x99s Apology\r\n\r\n\r\n|MARILLA said nothing to Matthew about the affair that evening; but when\r\nAnne proved still refractory the next morning an explanation had to be\r\nmade to account for her absence from the breakfast table. Marilla told\r\nMatthew the whole story, taking pains to impress him with a due sense of\r\nthe enormity of Anne\xe2\x80\x99s behavior.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a good thing Rachel Lynde got a calling down; she\xe2\x80\x99s a meddlesome\r\nold gossip,\xe2\x80\x9d was Matthew\xe2\x80\x99s consolatory rejoinder.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMatthew Cuthbert, I\xe2\x80\x99m astonished at you. You know that Anne\xe2\x80\x99s behavior\r\nwas dreadful, and yet you take her part! I suppose you\xe2\x80\x99ll be saying next\r\nthing that she oughtn\xe2\x80\x99t to be punished at all!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell now--no--not exactly,\xe2\x80\x9d said Matthew uneasily. \xe2\x80\x9cI reckon she\r\nought to be punished a little. But don\xe2\x80\x99t be too hard on her, Marilla.\r\nRecollect she hasn\xe2\x80\x99t ever had anyone to teach her right. You\xe2\x80\x99re--you\xe2\x80\x99re\r\ngoing to give her something to eat, aren\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen did you ever hear of me starving people into good behavior?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n demanded Marilla indignantly. \xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99ll have her meals regular, and\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll carry them up to her myself. But she\xe2\x80\x99ll stay up there until she\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwilling to apologize to Mrs. Lynde, and that\xe2\x80\x99s final, Matthew.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBreakfast, dinner, and supper were very silent meals--for Anne still\r\nremained obdurate. After each meal Marilla carried a well-filled tray\r\nto the east gable and brought it down later on not noticeably depleted.\r\nMatthew eyed its last descent with a troubled eye. Had Anne eaten\r\nanything at all?\r\n\r\nWhen Marilla went out that evening to bring the cows from the back\r\npasture, Matthew, who had been hanging about the barns and watching,\r\nslipped into the house with the air of a burglar and crept upstairs. As\r\na general thing Matthew gravitated between the kitchen and the little\r\nbedroom off the hall where he slept; once in a while he ventured\r\nuncomfortably into the parlor or sitting room when the minister came to\r\ntea. But he had never been upstairs in his own house since the spring he\r\nhelped Marilla paper the spare bedroom, and that was four years ago.\r\n\r\nHe tiptoed along the hall and stood for several minutes outside the\r\ndoor of the east gable before he summoned courage to tap on it with his\r\nfingers and then open the door to peep in.\r\n\r\nAnne was sitting on the yellow chair by the window gazing mournfully out\r\ninto the garden. Very small and unhappy she looked, and Matthew\xe2\x80\x99s heart\r\nsmote him. He softly closed the door and tiptoed over to her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnne,\xe2\x80\x9d he whispered, as if afraid of being overheard, \xe2\x80\x9chow are you\r\nmaking it, Anne?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne smiled wanly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPretty well. I imagine a good deal, and that helps to pass the time. Of\r\ncourse, it\xe2\x80\x99s rather lonesome. But then, I may as well get used to that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne smiled again, bravely facing the long years of solitary\r\nimprisonment before her.\r\n\r\nMatthew recollected that he must say what he had come to say without\r\nloss of time, lest Marilla return prematurely. \xe2\x80\x9cWell now, Anne, don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nyou think you\xe2\x80\x99d better do it and have it over with?\xe2\x80\x9d he whispered.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99ll have to be done sooner or later, you know, for Marilla\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\ndreadful deter-mined woman--dreadful determined, Anne. Do it right off,\r\nI say, and have it over.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean apologize to Mrs. Lynde?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes--apologize--that\xe2\x80\x99s the very word,\xe2\x80\x9d said Matthew eagerly. \xe2\x80\x9cJust\r\nsmooth it over so to speak. That\xe2\x80\x99s what I was trying to get at.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose I could do it to oblige you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne thoughtfully. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\r\nwould be true enough to say I am sorry, because I _am_ sorry now. I wasn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\na bit sorry last night. I was mad clear through, and I stayed mad all\r\nnight. I know I did because I woke up three times and I was just\r\nfurious every time. But this morning it was over. I wasn\xe2\x80\x99t in a temper\r\nanymore--and it left a dreadful sort of goneness, too. I felt so ashamed\r\nof myself. But I just couldn\xe2\x80\x99t think of going and telling Mrs. Lynde\r\nso. It would be so humiliating. I made up my mind I\xe2\x80\x99d stay shut up here\r\nforever rather than do that. But still--I\xe2\x80\x99d do anything for you--if you\r\nreally want me to--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell now, of course I do. It\xe2\x80\x99s terrible lonesome downstairs without\r\nyou. Just go and smooth things over--that\xe2\x80\x99s a good girl.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne resignedly. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll tell Marilla as soon as she\r\ncomes in I\xe2\x80\x99ve repented.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s right--that\xe2\x80\x99s right, Anne. But don\xe2\x80\x99t tell Marilla I said\r\nanything about it. She might think I was putting my oar in and I\r\npromised not to do that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWild horses won\xe2\x80\x99t drag the secret from me,\xe2\x80\x9d promised Anne solemnly.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow would wild horses drag a secret from a person anyhow?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Matthew was gone, scared at his own success. He fled hastily to the\r\nremotest corner of the horse pasture lest Marilla should suspect what\r\nhe had been up to. Marilla herself, upon her return to the house, was\r\nagreeably surprised to hear a plaintive voice calling, \xe2\x80\x9cMarilla\xe2\x80\x9d over\r\nthe banisters.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d she said, going into the hall.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sorry I lost my temper and said rude things, and I\xe2\x80\x99m willing to go\r\nand tell Mrs. Lynde so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well.\xe2\x80\x9d Marilla\xe2\x80\x99s crispness gave no sign of her relief. She had\r\nbeen wondering what under the canopy she should do if Anne did not give\r\nin. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll take you down after milking.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAccordingly, after milking, behold Marilla and Anne walking down the\r\nlane, the former erect and triumphant, the latter drooping and dejected.\r\nBut halfway down Anne\xe2\x80\x99s dejection vanished as if by enchantment. She\r\nlifted her head and stepped lightly along, her eyes fixed on the sunset\r\nsky and an air of subdued exhilaration about her. Marilla beheld the\r\nchange disapprovingly. This was no meek penitent such as it behooved her\r\nto take into the presence of the offended Mrs. Lynde.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you thinking of, Anne?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked sharply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m imagining out what I must say to Mrs. Lynde,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Anne\r\ndreamily.\r\n\r\nThis was satisfactory--or should have been so. But Marilla could not\r\nrid herself of the notion that something in her scheme of punishment was\r\ngoing askew. Anne had no business to look so rapt and radiant.\r\n\r\nRapt and radiant Anne continued until they were in the very presence\r\nof Mrs. Lynde, who was sitting knitting by her kitchen window. Then the\r\nradiance vanished. Mournful penitence appeared on every feature. Before\r\na word was spoken Anne suddenly went down on her knees before the\r\nastonished Mrs. Rachel and held out her hands beseechingly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Mrs. Lynde, I am so extremely sorry,\xe2\x80\x9d she said with a quiver in\r\nher voice. \xe2\x80\x9cI could never express all my sorrow, no, not if I used up\r\na whole dictionary. You must just imagine it. I behaved terribly to\r\nyou--and I\xe2\x80\x99ve disgraced the dear friends, Matthew and Marilla, who have\r\nlet me stay at Green Gables although I\xe2\x80\x99m not a boy. I\xe2\x80\x99m a dreadfully\r\nwicked and ungrateful girl, and I deserve to be punished and cast out\r\nby respectable people forever. It was very wicked of me to fly into a\r\ntemper because you told me the truth. It _was_ the truth; every word you\r\nsaid was true. My hair is red and I\xe2\x80\x99m freckled and skinny and ugly.\r\nWhat I said to you was true, too, but I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have said it. Oh, Mrs.\r\nLynde, please, please, forgive me. If you refuse it will be a lifelong\r\nsorrow on a poor little orphan girl, would you, even if she had a\r\ndreadful temper? Oh, I am sure you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t. Please say you forgive me,\r\nMrs. Lynde.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne clasped her hands together, bowed her head, and waited for the word\r\nof judgment.\r\n\r\nThere was no mistaking her sincerity--it breathed in every tone of her\r\nvoice. Both Marilla and Mrs. Lynde recognized its unmistakable ring.\r\nBut the former under-stood in dismay that Anne was actually enjoying\r\nher valley of humiliation--was reveling in the thoroughness of her\r\nabasement. Where was the wholesome punishment upon which she, Marilla,\r\nhad plumed herself? Anne had turned it into a species of positive\r\npleasure.\r\n\r\nGood Mrs. Lynde, not being overburdened with perception, did not see\r\nthis. She only perceived that Anne had made a very thorough apology and\r\nall resentment vanished from her kindly, if somewhat officious, heart.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere, there, get up, child,\xe2\x80\x9d she said heartily. \xe2\x80\x9cOf course I forgive\r\nyou. I guess I was a little too hard on you, anyway. But I\xe2\x80\x99m such an\r\noutspoken person. You just mustn\xe2\x80\x99t mind me, that\xe2\x80\x99s what. It can\xe2\x80\x99t be\r\ndenied your hair is terrible red; but I knew a girl once--went to school\r\nwith her, in fact--whose hair was every mite as red as yours when she\r\nwas young, but when she grew up it darkened to a real handsome auburn. I\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be a mite surprised if yours did, too--not a mite.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Mrs. Lynde!\xe2\x80\x9d Anne drew a long breath as she rose to her feet. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\nhave given me a hope. I shall always feel that you are a benefactor. Oh,\r\nI could endure anything if I only thought my hair would be a handsome\r\nauburn when I grew up. It would be so much easier to be good if one\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhair was a handsome auburn, don\xe2\x80\x99t you think? And now may I go out into\r\nyour garden and sit on that bench under the apple-trees while you and\r\nMarilla are talking? There is so much more scope for imagination out\r\nthere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLaws, yes, run along, child. And you can pick a bouquet of them white\r\nJune lilies over in the corner if you like.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs the door closed behind Anne Mrs. Lynde got briskly up to light a\r\nlamp.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s a real odd little thing. Take this chair, Marilla; it\xe2\x80\x99s easier\r\nthan the one you\xe2\x80\x99ve got; I just keep that for the hired boy to sit\r\non. Yes, she certainly is an odd child, but there is something kind of\r\ntaking about her after all. I don\xe2\x80\x99t feel so surprised at you and Matthew\r\nkeeping her as I did--nor so sorry for you, either. She may turn out all\r\nright. Of course, she has a queer way of expressing herself--a little\r\ntoo--well, too kind of forcible, you know; but she\xe2\x80\x99ll likely get over\r\nthat now that she\xe2\x80\x99s come to live among civilized folks. And then, her\r\ntemper\xe2\x80\x99s pretty quick, I guess; but there\xe2\x80\x99s one comfort, a child that\r\nhas a quick temper, just blaze up and cool down, ain\xe2\x80\x99t never likely to\r\nbe sly or deceitful. On the\r\nwhole, Marilla, I kind of like her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen Marilla went home Anne came out of the fragrant twilight of the\r\norchard with a sheaf of white narcissi in her hands.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI apologized pretty well, didn\xe2\x80\x99t I?\xe2\x80\x9d she said proudly as they went\r\ndown the lane. \xe2\x80\x9cI thought since I had to do it I might as well do it\r\nthoroughly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou did it thoroughly, all right enough,\xe2\x80\x9d was Marilla\xe2\x80\x99s comment.\r\nMarilla was dismayed at finding herself inclined to laugh over the\r\nrecollection. She had also an uneasy feeling that she ought to scold\r\nAnne for apologizing so well; but then, that was ridiculous! She\r\ncompromised with her conscience by saying severely:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope you won\xe2\x80\x99t have occasion to make many more such apologies. I hope\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ll try to control your temper now, Anne.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be so hard if people wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t twit me about my looks,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said Anne with a sigh. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t get cross about other things; but I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\n_so_ tired of being twitted about my hair and it just makes me boil right\r\nover. I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid you are\r\na very vain little girl.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow can I be vain when I know I\xe2\x80\x99m homely?\xe2\x80\x9d protested Anne. \xe2\x80\x9cI love\r\npretty things; and I hate to look in the glass and see something that\r\nisn\xe2\x80\x99t pretty. I pity it because it isn\xe2\x80\x99t beautiful.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHandsome is as handsome does,\xe2\x80\x9d quoted Marilla. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve had that said\r\nto me before, but I have my doubts about it,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked skeptical Anne,\r\nsniffing at her narcissi. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, aren\xe2\x80\x99t these flowers sweet!
