It gives you a lovely, comfortable feeling to apologize and\r\nbe forgiven, doesn\xe2\x80\x99t it? Aren\xe2\x80\x99t the stars bright tonight? If you could\r\nlive in a star, which one would you pick? I\xe2\x80\x99d like that lovely clear big\r\none away over there above that dark hill.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnne, do hold your tongue,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla, thoroughly worn out trying to\r\nfollow the gyrations of Anne\xe2\x80\x99s thoughts.\r\n\r\nAnne said no more until they turned into their own lane. A little gypsy\r\nwind came down it to meet them, laden with the spicy perfume of young\r\ndew-wet ferns. Far up in the shadows a cheerful light gleamed out\r\nthrough the trees from the kitchen at Green Gables. Anne suddenly came\r\nclose to Marilla and slipped her hand into the older woman\xe2\x80\x99s hard palm.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s lovely to be going home and know it\xe2\x80\x99s home,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cI love\r\nGreen Gables already, and I never loved any place before. No place ever\r\nseemed like home. Oh, Marilla, I\xe2\x80\x99m so happy. I could pray right now and\r\nnot find it a bit hard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSomething warm and pleasant welled up in Marilla\xe2\x80\x99s heart at touch of\r\nthat thin little hand in her own--a throb of the maternity she had\r\nmissed, perhaps. Its very unaccustomedness and sweetness disturbed\r\nher. She hastened to restore her sensations to their normal calm by\r\ninculcating a moral.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you\xe2\x80\x99ll be a good girl you\xe2\x80\x99ll always be happy, Anne. And you should\r\nnever find it hard to say your prayers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSaying one\xe2\x80\x99s prayers isn\xe2\x80\x99t exactly the same thing as praying,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nAnne meditatively. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I\xe2\x80\x99m going to imagine that I\xe2\x80\x99m the wind that is\r\nblowing up there in those tree tops. When I get tired of the trees I\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nimagine I\xe2\x80\x99m gently waving down here in the ferns--and then I\xe2\x80\x99ll fly over\r\nto Mrs. Lynde\xe2\x80\x99s garden and set the flowers dancing--and then I\xe2\x80\x99ll go\r\nwith one great swoop over the clover field--and then I\xe2\x80\x99ll blow over the\r\nLake of Shining Waters and ripple it all up into little sparkling waves.\r\nOh, there\xe2\x80\x99s so much scope for imagination in a wind! So I\xe2\x80\x99ll not talk\r\nany more just now, Marilla.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThanks be to goodness for that,\xe2\x80\x9d breathed Marilla in devout relief.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XI. Anne\xe2\x80\x99s Impressions of Sunday-School\r\n\r\n\r\n|WELL, how do you like them?\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla.\r\n\r\nAnne was standing in the gable room, looking solemnly at three new\r\ndresses spread out on the bed. One was of snuffy colored gingham which\r\nMarilla had been tempted to buy from a peddler the preceding summer\r\nbecause it looked so serviceable; one was of black-and-white checkered\r\nsateen which she had picked up at a bargain counter in the winter; and\r\none was a stiff print of an ugly blue shade which she had purchased that\r\nweek at a Carmody store.\r\n\r\nShe had made them up herself, and they were all made alike--plain skirts\r\nfulled tightly to plain waists, with sleeves as plain as waist and skirt\r\nand tight as sleeves could be.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll imagine that I like them,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne soberly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t want you to imagine it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla, offended. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, I can see\r\nyou don\xe2\x80\x99t like the dresses! What is the matter with them? Aren\xe2\x80\x99t they\r\nneat and clean and new?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen why don\xe2\x80\x99t you like them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99re--they\xe2\x80\x99re not--pretty,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne reluctantly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPretty!\xe2\x80\x9d Marilla sniffed. \xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t trouble my head about getting\r\npretty dresses for you. I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe in pampering vanity, Anne, I\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\ntell you that right off. Those dresses are good, sensible, serviceable\r\ndresses, without any frills or furbelows about them, and they\xe2\x80\x99re all\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ll get this summer. The brown gingham and the blue print will do\r\nyou for school when you begin to go. The sateen is for church and Sunday\r\nschool. I\xe2\x80\x99ll expect you to keep them neat and clean and not to tear\r\nthem. I should think you\xe2\x80\x99d be grateful to get most anything after those\r\nskimpy wincey things you\xe2\x80\x99ve been wearing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I _am_ grateful,\xe2\x80\x9d protested Anne. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I\xe2\x80\x99d be ever so much\r\ngratefuller if--if you\xe2\x80\x99d made just one of them with puffed sleeves.\r\nPuffed sleeves are so fashionable now. It would give me such a thrill,\r\nMarilla, just to wear a dress with puffed sleeves.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you\xe2\x80\x99ll have to do without your thrill. I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t any material\r\nto waste on puffed sleeves. I think they are ridiculous-looking things\r\nanyhow. I prefer the plain, sensible ones.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I\xe2\x80\x99d rather look ridiculous when everybody else does than plain and\r\nsensible all by myself,\xe2\x80\x9d persisted Anne mournfully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrust you for that! Well, hang those dresses carefully up in your\r\ncloset, and then sit down and learn the Sunday school lesson. I got\r\na quarterly from Mr. Bell for you and you\xe2\x80\x99ll go to Sunday school\r\ntomorrow,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla, disappearing downstairs in high dudgeon.\r\n\r\nAnne clasped her hands and looked at the dresses.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did hope there would be a white one with puffed sleeves,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nwhispered disconsolately. \xe2\x80\x9cI prayed for one, but I didn\xe2\x80\x99t much expect it\r\non that account. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t suppose God would have time to bother about\r\na little orphan girl\xe2\x80\x99s dress. I knew I\xe2\x80\x99d just have to depend on\r\nMarilla for it. Well, fortunately I can imagine that one of them is of\r\nsnow-white muslin with lovely lace frills and three-puffed sleeves.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe next morning warnings of a sick headache prevented Marilla from\r\ngoing to Sunday-school with Anne.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll have to go down and call for Mrs. Lynde, Anne,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99ll see that you get into the right class. Now, mind you behave\r\nyourself properly. Stay to preaching afterwards and ask Mrs. Lynde to\r\nshow you our pew. Here\xe2\x80\x99s a cent for collection. Don\xe2\x80\x99t stare at people\r\nand don\xe2\x80\x99t fidget. I shall expect you to tell me the text when you come\r\nhome.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne started off irreproachable, arrayed in the stiff black-and-white\r\nsateen, which, while decent as regards length and certainly not open to\r\nthe charge of skimpiness, contrived to emphasize every corner and angle\r\nof her thin figure. Her hat was a little, flat, glossy, new sailor, the\r\nextreme plainness of which had likewise much disappointed Anne, who\r\nhad permitted herself secret visions of ribbon and flowers. The latter,\r\nhowever, were supplied before Anne reached the main road, for being\r\nconfronted halfway down the lane with a golden frenzy of wind-stirred\r\nbuttercups and a glory of wild roses, Anne promptly and liberally\r\ngarlanded her hat with a heavy wreath of them. Whatever other people\r\nmight have thought of the result it satisfied Anne, and she tripped\r\ngaily down the road, holding her ruddy head with its decoration of pink\r\nand yellow very proudly.\r\n\r\nWhen she had reached Mrs. Lynde\xe2\x80\x99s house she found that lady gone.\r\nNothing daunted, Anne proceeded onward to the church alone. In the porch\r\nshe found a crowd of little girls, all more or less gaily attired in\r\nwhites and blues and pinks, and all staring with curious eyes at this\r\nstranger in their midst, with her extraordinary head adornment. Avonlea\r\nlittle girls had already heard queer stories about Anne. Mrs. Lynde said\r\nshe had an awful temper; Jerry Buote, the hired boy at Green Gables,\r\nsaid she talked all the time to herself or to the trees and flowers\r\nlike a crazy girl. They looked at her and whispered to each other behind\r\ntheir quarterlies. Nobody made any friendly advances, then or later\r\non when the opening exercises were over and Anne found herself in Miss\r\nRogerson\xe2\x80\x99s class.\r\n\r\nMiss Rogerson was a middle-aged lady who had taught a Sunday-school\r\nclass for twenty years. Her method of teaching was to ask the printed\r\nquestions from the quarterly and look sternly over its edge at the\r\nparticular little girl she thought ought to answer the question. She\r\nlooked very often at Anne, and Anne, thanks to Marilla\xe2\x80\x99s drilling,\r\nanswered promptly; but it may be questioned if she understood very much\r\nabout either question or answer.\r\n\r\nShe did not think she liked Miss Rogerson, and she felt very miserable;\r\nevery other little girl in the class had puffed sleeves. Anne felt that\r\nlife was really not worth living without puffed sleeves.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, how did you like Sunday school?\xe2\x80\x9d Marilla wanted to know when Anne\r\ncame home. Her wreath having faded, Anne had discarded it in the lane,\r\nso Marilla was spared the knowledge of that for a time.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t like it a bit. It was horrid.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnne Shirley!\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla rebukingly.\r\n\r\nAnne sat down on the rocker with a long sigh, kissed one of Bonny\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nleaves, and waved her hand to a blossoming fuchsia.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey might have been lonesome while I was away,\xe2\x80\x9d she explained. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd\r\nnow about the Sunday school. I behaved well, just as you told me. Mrs.\r\nLynde was gone, but I went right on myself. I went into the church, with\r\na lot of other little girls, and I sat in the corner of a pew by the\r\nwindow while the opening exercises went on. Mr. Bell made an awfully\r\nlong prayer. I would have been dreadfully tired before he got through\r\nif I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t been sitting by that window. But it looked right out on the\r\nLake of Shining Waters, so I just gazed at that and imagined all sorts\r\nof splendid things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have done anything of the sort. You should have listened\r\nto Mr. Bell.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut he wasn\xe2\x80\x99t talking to me,\xe2\x80\x9d protested Anne. \xe2\x80\x9cHe was talking to God\r\nand he didn\xe2\x80\x99t seem to be very much inter-ested in it, either. I think\r\nhe thought God was too far off though. There was a long row of white\r\nbirches hanging over the lake and the sunshine fell down through\r\nthem, \xe2\x80\x98way, \xe2\x80\x98way down, deep into the water. Oh, Marilla, it was like a\r\nbeautiful dream! It gave me a thrill and I just said, \xe2\x80\x98Thank you for it,\r\nGod,\xe2\x80\x99 two or three times.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot out loud, I hope,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla anxiously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, no, just under my breath. Well, Mr. Bell did get through at last\r\nand they told me to go into the classroom with Miss Rogerson\xe2\x80\x99s class.\r\nThere were nine other girls in it. They all had puffed sleeves. I tried\r\nto imagine mine were puffed, too, but I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t. Why couldn\xe2\x80\x99t I? It was\r\nas easy as could be to imagine they were puffed when I was alone in\r\nthe east gable, but it was awfully hard there among the others who had\r\nreally truly puffs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have been thinking about your sleeves in Sunday school.\r\nYou should have been attending to the lesson. I hope you knew it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes; and I answered a lot of questions. Miss Rogerson asked ever so\r\nmany. I don\xe2\x80\x99t think it was fair for her to do all the asking. There were\r\nlots I wanted to ask her, but I didn\xe2\x80\x99t like to because I didn\xe2\x80\x99t think\r\nshe was a kindred spirit. Then all the other little girls recited a\r\nparaphrase. She asked me if I knew any. I told her I didn\xe2\x80\x99t, but I could\r\nrecite, \xe2\x80\x98The Dog at His Master\xe2\x80\x99s Grave\xe2\x80\x99 if she liked. That\xe2\x80\x99s in the\r\nThird Royal Reader. It isn\xe2\x80\x99t a really truly religious piece of poetry,\r\nbut it\xe2\x80\x99s so sad and melancholy that it might as well be. She said it\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t do and she told me to learn the nineteenth paraphrase for next\r\nSunday. I read it over in church afterwards and it\xe2\x80\x99s splendid. There are\r\ntwo lines in particular that just thrill me.\r\n\r\n   \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Quick as the slaughtered squadrons fell\r\n   In Midian\xe2\x80\x99s evil day.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what \xe2\x80\x98squadrons\xe2\x80\x99 means nor \xe2\x80\x98Midian,\xe2\x80\x99 either, but it sounds\r\n_so_ tragical. I can hardly wait until next Sunday to recite it.\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll practice it all the week. After Sunday school I asked Miss\r\nRogerson--because Mrs. Lynde was too far away--to show me your pew.\r\nI sat just as still as I could and the text was Revelations, third\r\nchapter, second and third verses. It was a very long text. If I was a\r\nminister I\xe2\x80\x99d pick the short, snappy ones. The sermon was awfully long,\r\ntoo. I suppose the minister had to match it to the text. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t think\r\nhe was a bit interesting. The trouble with him seems to be that he\r\nhasn\xe2\x80\x99t enough imagination. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t listen to him very much. I just let\r\nmy thoughts run and I thought of the most surprising things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarilla felt helplessly that all this should be sternly reproved, but\r\nshe was hampered by the undeniable fact that some of the things Anne had\r\nsaid, especially about the minister\xe2\x80\x99s sermons and Mr. Bell\xe2\x80\x99s prayers,\r\nwere what she herself had really thought deep down in her heart for\r\nyears, but had never given expression to. It almost seemed to her that\r\nthose secret, unuttered, critical thoughts had suddenly taken visible\r\nand accusing shape and form in the person of this outspoken morsel of\r\nneglected humanity.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XII. A Solemn Vow and Promise\r\n\r\n\r\n|IT was not until the next Friday that Marilla heard the story of the\r\nflower-wreathed hat. She came home from Mrs. Lynde\xe2\x80\x99s and called Anne to\r\naccount.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnne, Mrs. Rachel says you went to church last Sunday with your hat\r\nrigged out ridiculous with roses and buttercups. What on earth put you\r\nup to such a caper? A pretty-looking object you must have been!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh. I know pink and yellow aren\xe2\x80\x99t becoming to me,\xe2\x80\x9d began Anne.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecoming fiddlesticks! It was putting flowers on your hat at all,\r\nno matter what color they were, that was ridiculous. You are the most\r\naggravating child!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t see why it\xe2\x80\x99s any more ridiculous to wear flowers on your hat\r\nthan on your dress,\xe2\x80\x9d protested Anne. \xe2\x80\x9cLots of little girls there had\r\nbouquets pinned on their dresses. What\xe2\x80\x99s the difference?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarilla was not to be drawn from the safe concrete into dubious paths of\r\nthe abstract.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t answer me back like that, Anne. It was very silly of you to do\r\nsuch a thing. Never let me catch you at such a trick again. Mrs. Rachel\r\nsays she thought she would sink through the floor when she saw you come\r\nin all rigged out like that. She couldn\xe2\x80\x99t get near enough to tell you\r\nto take them off till it was too late. She says people talked about it\r\nsomething dreadful. Of course they would think I had no better sense\r\nthan to let you go decked out like that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I\xe2\x80\x99m so sorry,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne, tears welling into her eyes. \xe2\x80\x9cI never\r\nthought you\xe2\x80\x99d mind. The roses and buttercups were so sweet and pretty\r\nI thought they\xe2\x80\x99d look lovely on my hat. Lots of the little girls had\r\nartificial flowers on their hats. I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid I\xe2\x80\x99m going to be a dreadful\r\ntrial to you. Maybe you\xe2\x80\x99d better send me back to the asylum. That would\r\nbe terrible; I don\xe2\x80\x99t think I could endure it; most likely I would go\r\ninto consumption; I\xe2\x80\x99m so thin as it is, you see. But that would be\r\nbetter than being a trial to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNonsense,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla, vexed at herself for having made the child\r\ncry. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t want to send you back to the asylum, I\xe2\x80\x99m sure. And I do wish you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t use such long\r\nwords. It sounds so funny in a little girl. I guess Diana \xe2\x80\x98ll like you\r\nwell enough. It\xe2\x80\x99s her mother you\xe2\x80\x99ve got to reckon with. If she doesn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nlike you it won\xe2\x80\x99t matter how much Diana does. If she has heard about\r\nyour outburst to Mrs. Lynde and going to church with buttercups round\r\nyour hat I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what she\xe2\x80\x99ll think of you. You must be polite and\r\nwell behaved, and don\xe2\x80\x99t make any of your startling speeches. For pity\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsake, if the child isn\xe2\x80\x99t actually trembling!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne _was_ trembling. Her face was pale and tense.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Marilla, you\xe2\x80\x99d be excited, too, if you were going to meet a little\r\ngirl you hoped to be your bosom friend and whose mother mightn\xe2\x80\x99t like\r\nyou,\xe2\x80\x9d she said as she hastened to get her hat.\r\n\r\nThey went over to Orchard Slope by the short cut across the brook and up\r\nthe firry hill grove. Mrs. Barry came to the kitchen door in answer to\r\nMarilla\xe2\x80\x99s knock. She was a tall black-eyed, black-haired woman, with a\r\nvery resolute mouth. She had the reputation of being very strict with\r\nher children.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do you do, Marilla?\xe2\x80\x9d she said cordially. \xe2\x80\x9cCome in. And this is the\r\nlittle girl you have adopted, I suppose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, this is Anne Shirley,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSpelled with an E,\xe2\x80\x9d gasped Anne, who, tremulous and excited as she was,\r\nwas determined there should be no misunderstanding on that important\r\npoint.\r\n\r\nMrs. Barry, not hearing or not comprehending, merely shook hands and\r\nsaid kindly:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow are you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am well in body although considerable rumpled up in spirit, thank you\r\nma\xe2\x80\x99am,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne gravely. Then aside to Marilla in an audible whisper,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere wasn\xe2\x80\x99t anything startling in that, was there, Marilla?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDiana was sitting on the sofa, reading a book which she dropped when the\r\ncallers entered. She was a very pretty little girl, with her mother\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nblack eyes and hair, and rosy cheeks, and the merry expression which was\r\nher inheritance from her father.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is my little girl Diana,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Barry. \xe2\x80\x9cDiana, you might take\r\nAnne out into the garden and show her your flowers. She reads entirely\r\ntoo much--\xe2\x80\x9d this to Marilla as the little girls went out--\xe2\x80\x9cand I can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nprevent her, for her father aids and abets her. She\xe2\x80\x99s always poring over\r\na book. I\xe2\x80\x99m glad she has the prospect of a playmate--perhaps it will\r\ntake her more out-of-doors.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOutside in the garden, which was full of mellow sunset light streaming\r\nthrough the dark old firs to the west of it, stood Anne and Diana,\r\ngazing bashfully at each other over a clump of gorgeous tiger lilies.\r\n\r\nThe Barry garden was a bowery wilderness of flowers which would have\r\ndelighted Anne\xe2\x80\x99s heart at any time less fraught with destiny. It was\r\nencircled by huge old willows and tall firs, beneath which flourished\r\nflowers that loved the shade. Prim, right-angled paths neatly bordered\r\nwith clamshells, intersected it like moist red ribbons and in the beds\r\nbetween old-fashioned flowers ran riot. There were rosy bleeding-hearts\r\nand great splendid crimson peonies; white, fragrant narcissi and thorny,\r\nsweet Scotch roses; pink and blue and white columbines and lilac-tinted\r\nBouncing Bets; clumps of southernwood and ribbon grass and mint; purple\r\nAdam-and-Eve, daffodils, and masses of sweet clover white with its\r\ndelicate, fragrant, feathery sprays; scarlet lightning that shot\r\nits fiery lances over prim white musk-flowers; a garden it was where\r\nsunshine lingered and bees hummed, and winds, beguiled into loitering,\r\npurred and rustled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Diana,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne at last, clasping her hands and speaking almost\r\nin a whisper, \xe2\x80\x9coh, do you think you can like me a little--enough to be\r\nmy bosom friend?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDiana laughed. Diana always laughed before she spoke.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, I guess so,\xe2\x80\x9d she said frankly. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m awfully glad you\xe2\x80\x99ve come to\r\nlive at Green Gables. The Delights of Anticipation\r\n\r\n\r\n|IT\xe2\x80\x99S time Anne was in to do her sewing,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla, glancing at the\r\nclock and then out into the yellow August afternoon where everything\r\ndrowsed in the heat. \xe2\x80\x9cShe stayed playing with Diana more than half an\r\nhour more \xe2\x80\x98n I gave her leave to; and now she\xe2\x80\x99s perched out there on\r\nthe woodpile talking to Matthew, nineteen to the dozen, when she knows\r\nperfectly well she ought to be at her work. Marilla\r\nalways wore her amethyst brooch to church. She would have thought it\r\nrather sacrilegious to leave it off--as bad as forgetting her Bible or\r\nher collection dime. That amethyst brooch was Marilla\xe2\x80\x99s most treasured\r\npossession. A seafaring uncle had given it to her mother who in turn\r\nhad bequeathed it to Marilla. I\r\nthought they would be lovely glimmering purple stones. When I saw a\r\nreal diamond in a lady\xe2\x80\x99s ring one day I was so disappointed I cried. Anne\xe2\x80\x99s Confession\r\n\r\n\r\n|ON the Monday evening before the picnic Marilla came down from her room\r\nwith a troubled face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnne,\xe2\x80\x9d she said to that small personage, who was shelling peas by the\r\nspotless table and singing, \xe2\x80\x9cNelly of the Hazel Dell\xe2\x80\x9d with a vigor and\r\nexpression that did credit to Diana\xe2\x80\x99s teaching, \xe2\x80\x9cdid you see anything\r\nof my amethyst brooch? Where did you put it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I put it back on the bureau. I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t it on a minute. Truly, I\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t mean to meddle, Marilla. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t think about its being wrong to\r\ngo in and try on the brooch; but I see now that it was and I\xe2\x80\x99ll never\r\ndo it again. That\xe2\x80\x99s one good thing about me. I never do the same naughty\r\nthing twice.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou didn\xe2\x80\x99t put it back,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla. \xe2\x80\x9cThat brooch isn\xe2\x80\x99t anywhere on\r\nthe bureau. You\xe2\x80\x99ve taken it out or something, Anne.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did put it back,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne quickly--pertly, Marilla thought. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t just remember whether I stuck it on the pincushion or laid it in\r\nthe china tray. But I\xe2\x80\x99m perfectly certain I put it back.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll go and have another look,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla, determining to be just.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you put that brooch back it\xe2\x80\x99s there still. If it isn\xe2\x80\x99t I\xe2\x80\x99ll know you\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t, that\xe2\x80\x99s all!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarilla went to her room and made a thorough search, not only over the\r\nbureau but in every other place she thought the brooch might possibly\r\nbe. It was not to be found and she returned to the kitchen.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnne, the brooch is gone. By your own admission you were the last\r\nperson to handle it. Now, what have you done with it? Tell me the truth\r\nat once. Did you take it out and lose it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I didn\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne solemnly, meeting Marilla\xe2\x80\x99s angry gaze\r\nsquarely. \xe2\x80\x9cI never took the brooch out of your room and that is the\r\ntruth, if I was to be led to the block for it--although I\xe2\x80\x99m not very\r\ncertain what a block is. So there, Marilla.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cso there\xe2\x80\x9d was only intended to emphasize her assertion, but\r\nMarilla took it as a display of defiance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI believe you are telling me a falsehood, Anne,\xe2\x80\x9d she said sharply. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nknow you are. There now, don\xe2\x80\x99t say anything more unless you are prepared\r\nto tell the whole truth. Go to your room and stay there until you are\r\nready to confess.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill I take the peas with me?\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne meekly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I\xe2\x80\x99ll finish shelling them myself. Do as I bid you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen Anne had gone Marilla went about her evening tasks in a very\r\ndisturbed state of mind. She was worried about her valuable brooch. What\r\nif Anne had lost it? And how wicked of the child to deny having taken\r\nit, when anybody could see she must have! With such an innocent face,\r\ntoo!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t sooner have had happen,\xe2\x80\x9d thought Marilla,\r\nas she nervously shelled the peas. \xe2\x80\x9cOf course, I don\xe2\x80\x99t suppose she meant\r\nto steal it or anything like that. She\xe2\x80\x99s just taken it to play with\r\nor help along that imagination of hers. She must have taken it, that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nclear, for there hasn\xe2\x80\x99t been a soul in that room since she was in it, by\r\nher own story, until I went up tonight. And the brooch is gone, there\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nnothing surer. I suppose she has lost it and is afraid to own up for\r\nfear she\xe2\x80\x99ll be punished. It\xe2\x80\x99s a dreadful thing to think she tells\r\nfalsehoods. It\xe2\x80\x99s a far worse thing than her fit of temper. It\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\nfearful responsibility to have a child in your house you can\xe2\x80\x99t trust.\r\nSlyness and untruthfulness--that\xe2\x80\x99s what she has displayed. I declare I\r\nfeel worse about that than about the brooch. If she\xe2\x80\x99d only have told the\r\ntruth about it I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t mind so much.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarilla went to her room at intervals all through the evening and\r\nsearched for the brooch, without finding it. A bedtime visit to the\r\neast gable produced no result. Anne persisted in denying that she knew\r\nanything about the brooch but Marilla was only the more firmly convinced\r\nthat she did.\r\n\r\nShe told Matthew the story the next morning. Matthew was confounded and\r\npuzzled; he could not so quickly lose faith in Anne but he had to admit\r\nthat circumstances were against her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re sure it hasn\xe2\x80\x99t fell down behind the bureau?\xe2\x80\x9d was the only\r\nsuggestion he could offer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve moved the bureau and I\xe2\x80\x99ve taken out the drawers and I\xe2\x80\x99ve looked\r\nin every crack and cranny\xe2\x80\x9d was Marilla\xe2\x80\x99s positive answer. \xe2\x80\x9cThe brooch\r\nis gone and that child has taken it and lied about it. That\xe2\x80\x99s the plain,\r\nugly truth, Matthew Cuthbert, and we might as well look it in the face.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell now, what are you going to do about it?\xe2\x80\x9d Matthew asked forlornly,\r\nfeeling secretly thankful that Marilla and not he had to deal with the\r\nsituation. He felt no desire to put his oar in this time.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99ll stay in her room until she confesses,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla grimly,\r\nremembering the success of this method in the former case. \xe2\x80\x9cThen we\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nsee. Perhaps we\xe2\x80\x99ll be able to find the brooch if she\xe2\x80\x99ll only tell\r\nwhere she took it; but in any case she\xe2\x80\x99ll have to be severely punished,\r\nMatthew.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell now, you\xe2\x80\x99ll have to punish her,\xe2\x80\x9d said Matthew, reaching for his\r\nhat. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve nothing to do with it, remember. You warned me off yourself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarilla felt deserted by everyone. She could not even go to Mrs. Lynde\r\nfor advice. She went up to the east gable with a very serious face and\r\nleft it with a face more serious still. Anne steadfastly refused to\r\nconfess. She persisted in asserting that she had not taken the brooch.\r\nThe child had evidently been crying and Marilla felt a pang of pity\r\nwhich she sternly repressed. By night she was, as she expressed it,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cbeat out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll stay in this room until you confess, Anne. You can make up your\r\nmind to that,\xe2\x80\x9d she said firmly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut the picnic is tomorrow, Marilla,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Anne. \xe2\x80\x9cYou won\xe2\x80\x99t keep me\r\nfrom going to that, will you? You\xe2\x80\x99ll just let me out for the afternoon,\r\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t you? Then I\xe2\x80\x99ll stay here as long as you like _afterwards_\r\ncheerfully. But I _must_ go to the picnic.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll not go to picnics nor anywhere else until you\xe2\x80\x99ve confessed,\r\nAnne.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Marilla,\xe2\x80\x9d gasped Anne.\r\n\r\nBut Marilla had gone out and shut the door.\r\n\r\nWednesday morning dawned as bright and fair as if expressly made to\r\norder for the picnic. Birds sang around Green Gables; the Madonna lilies\r\nin the garden sent out whiffs of perfume that entered in on viewless\r\nwinds at every door and window, and wandered through halls and rooms\r\nlike spirits of benediction. The birches in the hollow waved joyful\r\nhands as if watching for Anne\xe2\x80\x99s usual morning greeting from the east\r\ngable. But Anne was not at her window. When Marilla took her breakfast\r\nup to her she found the child sitting primly on her bed, pale and\r\nresolute, with tight-shut lips and gleaming eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMarilla, I\xe2\x80\x99m ready to confess.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d Marilla laid down her tray. Once again her method had succeeded;\r\nbut her success was very bitter to her. \xe2\x80\x9cLet me hear what you have to\r\nsay then, Anne.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI took the amethyst brooch,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne, as if repeating a lesson she\r\nhad learned. \xe2\x80\x9cI took it just as you said. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t mean to take it when\r\nI went in. But it did look so beautiful, Marilla, when I pinned it on my\r\nbreast that I was overcome by an irresistible temptation. I imagined how\r\nperfectly thrilling it would be to take it to Idlewild and play I was\r\nthe Lady Cordelia Fitzgerald. It would be so much easier to imagine I\r\nwas the Lady Cordelia if I had a real amethyst brooch on. Diana and\r\nI make necklaces of roseberries but what are roseberries compared to\r\namethysts? So I took the brooch. I thought I could put it back before\r\nyou came home. I went all the way around by the road to lengthen out the\r\ntime. When I was going over the bridge across the Lake of Shining Waters\r\nI took the brooch off to have another look at it. Oh, how it did shine\r\nin the sunlight! And then, when I was leaning over the bridge, it\r\njust slipped through my fingers--so--and went down--down--down, all\r\npurply-sparkling, and sank forevermore beneath the Lake of Shining\r\nWaters. And that\xe2\x80\x99s the best I can do at confessing, Marilla.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarilla felt hot anger surge up into her heart again. This child had\r\ntaken and lost her treasured amethyst brooch and now sat there calmly\r\nreciting the details thereof without the least apparent compunction or\r\nrepentance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnne, this is terrible,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, trying to speak calmly. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are the\r\nvery wickedest girl I ever heard of.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I suppose I am,\xe2\x80\x9d agreed Anne tranquilly. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I know I\xe2\x80\x99ll have to\r\nbe punished. It\xe2\x80\x99ll be your duty to punish me, Marilla. Won\xe2\x80\x99t you please\r\nget it over right off because I\xe2\x80\x99d like to go to the picnic with nothing\r\non my mind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPicnic, indeed! You\xe2\x80\x99ll go to no picnic today, Anne Shirley. That shall\r\nbe your punishment. And it isn\xe2\x80\x99t half severe enough either for what\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ve done!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot go to the picnic!\xe2\x80\x9d Anne sprang to her feet and clutched Marilla\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhand. \xe2\x80\x9cBut you _promised_ me I might! Oh, Marilla, I must go to the\r\npicnic. That was why I confessed. Punish me any way you like but that.\r\nOh, Marilla, please, please, let me go to the picnic. Think of the ice\r\ncream! For anything you know I may never have a chance to taste ice\r\ncream again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarilla disengaged Anne\xe2\x80\x99s clinging hands stonily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou needn\xe2\x80\x99t plead, Anne. You are not going to the picnic and that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nfinal. No, not a word.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne realized that Marilla was not to be moved. She clasped her hands\r\ntogether, gave a piercing shriek, and then flung herself face\r\ndownward on the bed, crying and writhing in an utter abandonment of\r\ndisappointment and despair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor the land\xe2\x80\x99s sake!\xe2\x80\x9d gasped Marilla, hastening from the room. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nbelieve the child is crazy. No child in her senses would behave as she\r\ndoes. If she isn\xe2\x80\x99t she\xe2\x80\x99s utterly bad. Oh dear, I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid Rachel was\r\nright from the first. But I\xe2\x80\x99ve put my hand to the plow and I won\xe2\x80\x99t look\r\nback.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat was a dismal morning. Marilla worked fiercely and scrubbed the\r\nporch floor and the dairy shelves when she could find nothing else to\r\ndo. Neither the shelves nor the porch needed it--but Marilla did. Then\r\nshe went out and raked the yard.\r\n\r\nWhen dinner was ready she went to the stairs and called Anne. A\r\ntear-stained face appeared, looking tragically over the banisters.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome down to your dinner, Anne.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t want any dinner, Marilla,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne, sobbingly. \xe2\x80\x9cI couldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\neat anything. My heart is broken. You\xe2\x80\x99ll feel remorse of conscience\r\nsomeday, I expect, for breaking it, Marilla, but I forgive you. Remember\r\nwhen the time comes that I forgive you. But please don\xe2\x80\x99t ask me to eat\r\nanything, especially boiled pork and greens. Boiled pork and greens are\r\nso unromantic when one is in affliction.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nExasperated, Marilla returned to the kitchen and poured out her tale\r\nof woe to Matthew, who, between his sense of justice and his unlawful\r\nsympathy with Anne, was a miserable man.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell now, she shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have taken the brooch, Marilla, or told stories\r\nabout it,\xe2\x80\x9d he admitted, mournfully surveying his plateful of unromantic\r\npork and greens as if he, like Anne, thought it a food unsuited to\r\ncrises of feeling, \xe2\x80\x9cbut she\xe2\x80\x99s such a little thing--such an interesting\r\nlittle thing. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you think it\xe2\x80\x99s pretty rough not to let her go to the\r\npicnic when she\xe2\x80\x99s so set on it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMatthew Cuthbert, I\xe2\x80\x99m amazed at you. I think I\xe2\x80\x99ve let her off entirely\r\ntoo easy. And she doesn\xe2\x80\x99t appear to realize how wicked she\xe2\x80\x99s been at\r\nall--that\xe2\x80\x99s what worries me most. If she\xe2\x80\x99d really felt sorry it wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nbe so bad. And you don\xe2\x80\x99t seem to realize it, neither; you\xe2\x80\x99re making\r\nexcuses for her all the time to yourself--I can see that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell now, she\xe2\x80\x99s such a little thing,\xe2\x80\x9d feebly reiterated Matthew. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd\r\nthere should be allowances made, Marilla. You know she\xe2\x80\x99s never had any\r\nbringing up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, she\xe2\x80\x99s having it now\xe2\x80\x9d retorted Marilla.\r\n\r\nThe retort silenced Matthew if it did not convince him. That dinner was\r\na very dismal meal. The only cheerful thing about it was Jerry Buote,\r\nthe hired boy, and Marilla resented his cheerfulness as a personal\r\ninsult.\r\n\r\nWhen her dishes were washed and her bread sponge set and her hens fed\r\nMarilla remembered that she had noticed a small rent in her best black\r\nlace shawl when she had taken it off on Monday afternoon on returning\r\nfrom the Ladies\xe2\x80\x99 Aid.\r\n\r\nShe would go and mend it. The shawl was in a box in her trunk. As\r\nMarilla lifted it out, the sunlight, falling through the vines that\r\nclustered thickly about the window, struck upon something caught in the\r\nshawl--something that glittered and sparkled in facets of violet light.\r\nMarilla snatched at it with a gasp. It was the amethyst brooch, hanging\r\nto a thread of the lace by its catch!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear life and heart,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla blankly, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat does this mean?\r\nHere\xe2\x80\x99s my brooch safe and sound that I thought was at the bottom of\r\nBarry\xe2\x80\x99s pond. Whatever did that girl mean by saying she took it and lost\r\nit? I declare I believe Green Gables is bewitched. I remember now that\r\nwhen I took off my shawl Monday afternoon I laid it on the bureau for a\r\nminute. I suppose the brooch got caught in it somehow. Well!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarilla betook herself to the east gable, brooch in hand. Anne had cried\r\nherself out and was sitting dejectedly by the window.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnne Shirley,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla solemnly, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve just found my brooch\r\nhanging to my black lace shawl. Now I want to know what that rigmarole\r\nyou told me this morning meant.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, you said you\xe2\x80\x99d keep me here until I confessed,\xe2\x80\x9d returned Anne\r\nwearily, \xe2\x80\x9cand so I decided to confess because I was bound to get to the\r\npicnic. I thought out a confession last night after I went to bed and\r\nmade it as interesting as I could. And I said it over and over so that I\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t forget it. But you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t let me go to the picnic after all,\r\nso all my trouble was wasted.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarilla had to laugh in spite of herself. But her conscience pricked\r\nher.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnne, you do beat all! But I was wrong--I see that now. I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nhave doubted your word when I\xe2\x80\x99d never known you to tell a story.\r\nOf course, it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t right for you to confess to a thing you hadn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ndone--it was very wrong to do so. But I drove you to it. So if you\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nforgive me, Anne, I\xe2\x80\x99ll forgive you and we\xe2\x80\x99ll start square again. And now\r\nget yourself ready for the picnic.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne flew up like a rocket.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Marilla, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it too late?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, it\xe2\x80\x99s only two o\xe2\x80\x99clock. They won\xe2\x80\x99t be more than well gathered yet\r\nand it\xe2\x80\x99ll be an hour before they have tea. Wash your face and comb your\r\nhair and put on your gingham. I\xe2\x80\x99ll fill a basket for you. There\xe2\x80\x99s plenty\r\nof stuff baked in the house. And I\xe2\x80\x99ll get Jerry to hitch up the sorrel\r\nand drive you down to the picnic ground.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Marilla,\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Anne, flying to the washstand. \xe2\x80\x9cFive minutes\r\nago I was so miserable I was wishing I\xe2\x80\x99d never been born and now I\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t change places with an angel!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat night a thoroughly happy, completely tired-out Anne returned to\r\nGreen Gables in a state of beatification impossible to describe.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Marilla, I\xe2\x80\x99ve had a perfectly scrumptious time. Scrumptious is a\r\nnew word I learned today. I heard Mary Alice Bell use it. Isn\xe2\x80\x99t it very\r\nexpressive? Everything was lovely. We had a splendid tea and then Mr.\r\nHarmon Andrews took us all for a row on the Lake of Shining Waters--six\r\nof us at a time. And Jane Andrews nearly fell overboard. She was leaning\r\nout to pick water lilies and if Mr. Andrews hadn\xe2\x80\x99t caught her by her\r\nsash just in the nick of time she\xe2\x80\x99d fallen in and prob\xe2\x80\x99ly been drowned.\r\nI wish it had been me. It would have been such a romantic experience to\r\nhave been nearly drowned. It would be such a thrilling tale to tell. And\r\nwe had the ice cream. Words fail me to describe that ice cream. Marilla,\r\nI assure you it was sublime.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat evening Marilla told the whole story to Matthew over her stocking\r\nbasket.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m willing to own up that I made a mistake,\xe2\x80\x9d she concluded candidly,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cbut I\xe2\x80\x99ve learned a lesson. I have to laugh when I think of Anne\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n\xe2\x80\x98confession,\xe2\x80\x99 although I suppose I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t for it really was a\r\nfalsehood. But it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t seem as bad as the other would have been,\r\nsomehow, and anyhow I\xe2\x80\x99m responsible for it. That child is hard to\r\nunderstand in some respects. But I believe she\xe2\x80\x99ll turn out all right\r\nyet. And there\xe2\x80\x99s one thing certain, no house will ever be dull that\r\nshe\xe2\x80\x99s in.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XV. A Tempest in the School Teapot\r\n\r\n\r\n|WHAT a splendid day!\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne, drawing a long breath. \xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t it good\r\njust to be alive on a day like this? I pity the people who aren\xe2\x80\x99t born\r\nyet for missing it. They may have good days, of course, but they can\r\nnever have this one. And it\xe2\x80\x99s splendider still to have such a lovely way\r\nto go to school by, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a lot nicer than going round by the road; that is so dusty\r\nand hot,\xe2\x80\x9d said Diana practically, peeping into her dinner basket and\r\nmentally calculating if the three juicy, toothsome, raspberry tarts\r\nreposing there were divided among ten girls how many bites each girl\r\nwould have.\r\n\r\nThe little girls of Avonlea school always pooled their lunches, and\r\nto eat three raspberry tarts all alone or even to share them only with\r\none\xe2\x80\x99s best chum would have forever and ever branded as \xe2\x80\x9cawful mean\xe2\x80\x9d the\r\ngirl who did it. And yet, when the tarts were divided among ten girls\r\nyou just got enough to tantalize you.\r\n\r\nThe way Anne and Diana went to school _was_ a pretty one. Anne thought\r\nthose walks to and from school with Diana couldn\xe2\x80\x99t be improved upon\r\neven by imagination. Going around by the main road would have been so\r\nunromantic; but to go by Lover\xe2\x80\x99s Lane and Willowmere and Violet Vale and\r\nthe Birch Path was romantic, if ever anything was.\r\n\r\nLover\xe2\x80\x99s Lane opened out below the orchard at Green Gables and stretched\r\nfar up into the woods to the end of the Cuthbert farm. It was the way by\r\nwhich the cows were taken to the back pasture and the wood hauled home\r\nin winter. Anne had named it Lover\xe2\x80\x99s Lane before she had been a month at\r\nGreen Gables.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot that lovers ever really walk there,\xe2\x80\x9d she explained to Marilla,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cbut Diana and I are reading a perfectly magnificent book and there\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\nLover\xe2\x80\x99s Lane in it. So we want to have one, too. And it\xe2\x80\x99s a very pretty\r\nname, don\xe2\x80\x99t you think? So romantic! We can\xe2\x80\x99t imagine the lovers into it,\r\nyou know. I like that lane because you can think out loud there without\r\npeople calling you crazy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne, starting out alone in the morning, went down Lover\xe2\x80\x99s Lane as far\r\nas the brook. Here Diana met her, and the two little girls went on\r\nup the lane under the leafy arch of maples--\xe2\x80\x9cmaples are such sociable\r\ntrees,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne; \xe2\x80\x9cthey\xe2\x80\x99re always rustling and whispering to\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x9d--until they came to a rustic bridge. Then they left the lane\r\nand walked through Mr. Barry\xe2\x80\x99s back field and past Willowmere. Beyond\r\nWillowmere came Violet Vale--a little green dimple in the shadow of Mr.\r\nAndrew Bell\xe2\x80\x99s big woods. \xe2\x80\x9cOf course there are no violets there now,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n Anne told Marilla, \xe2\x80\x9cbut Diana says there are millions of them in spring.\r\nOh, Marilla, can\xe2\x80\x99t you just imagine you see them? It actually takes away\r\nmy breath. I named it Violet Vale. Diana says she never saw the beat\r\nof me for hitting on fancy names for places. It\xe2\x80\x99s nice to be clever at\r\nsomething, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it? But Diana named the Birch Path. She wanted to, so\r\nI let her; but I\xe2\x80\x99m sure I could have found something more poetical than\r\nplain Birch Path. Anybody can think of a name like that. But the Birch\r\nPath is one of the prettiest places in the world, Marilla.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was. Other people besides Anne thought so when they stumbled on it.\r\nIt was a little narrow, twisting path, winding down over a long hill\r\nstraight through Mr. Bell\xe2\x80\x99s woods, where the light came down sifted\r\nthrough so many emerald screens that it was as flawless as the heart\r\nof a diamond. It was fringed in all its length with slim young birches,\r\nwhite stemmed and lissom boughed; ferns and starflowers and wild\r\nlilies-of-the-valley and scarlet tufts of pigeonberries grew thickly\r\nalong it; and always there was a delightful spiciness in the air and\r\nmusic of bird calls and the murmur and laugh of wood winds in the trees\r\noverhead. Now and then you might see a rabbit skipping across the road\r\nif you were quiet--which, with Anne and Diana, happened about once in\r\na blue moon. Down in the valley the path came out to the main road and\r\nthen it was just up the spruce hill to the school.\r\n\r\nThe Avonlea school was a whitewashed building, low in the eaves and\r\nwide in the windows, furnished inside with comfortable substantial\r\nold-fashioned desks that opened and shut, and were carved all over their\r\nlids with the initials and hieroglyphics of three generations of school\r\nchildren. The schoolhouse was set back from the road and behind it was\r\na dusky fir wood and a brook where all the children put their bottles of\r\nmilk in the morning to keep cool and sweet until dinner hour.\r\n\r\nMarilla had seen Anne start off to school on the first day of September\r\nwith many secret misgivings. Anne was such an odd girl. How would she\r\nget on with the other children? And how on earth would she ever manage\r\nto hold her tongue during school hours?\r\n\r\nThings went better than Marilla feared, however. Anne came home that\r\nevening in high spirits.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think I\xe2\x80\x99m going to like school here,\xe2\x80\x9d she announced. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think\r\nmuch of the master, through. He\xe2\x80\x99s all the time curling his mustache\r\nand making eyes at Prissy Andrews. Prissy is grown up, you know. She\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsixteen and she\xe2\x80\x99s studying for the entrance examination into Queen\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nAcademy at Charlottetown next year. Tillie Boulter says the master is\r\n_dead gone_ on her. She\xe2\x80\x99s got a beautiful complexion and curly brown hair\r\nand she does it up so elegantly. She sits in the long seat at the back\r\nand he sits there, too, most of the time--to explain her lessons, he\r\nsays. But Ruby Gillis says she saw him writing something on her slate\r\nand when Prissy read it she blushed as red as a beet and giggled; and\r\nRuby Gillis says she doesn\xe2\x80\x99t believe it had anything to do with the\r\nlesson.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnne Shirley, don\xe2\x80\x99t let me hear you talking about your teacher in that\r\nway again,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla sharply. \xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t go to school to criticize\r\nthe master. I guess he can teach _you_ something, and it\xe2\x80\x99s your business\r\nto learn. And I want you to understand right off that you are not to\r\ncome home telling tales about him. That is something I won\xe2\x80\x99t encourage.\r\nI hope you were a good girl.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed I was,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne comfortably. \xe2\x80\x9cIt wasn\xe2\x80\x99t so hard as you might\r\nimagine, either. I sit with Diana. Our seat is right by the window and\r\nwe can look down to the Lake of Shining Waters. There are a lot of nice\r\ngirls in school and we had scrumptious fun playing at dinnertime. It\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nso nice to have a lot of little girls to play with. But of course I like\r\nDiana best and always will. I _adore_ Diana. I\xe2\x80\x99m dreadfully far behind the\r\nothers. They\xe2\x80\x99re all in the fifth book and I\xe2\x80\x99m only in the fourth. I feel\r\nthat it\xe2\x80\x99s kind of a disgrace. But there\xe2\x80\x99s not one of them has such an\r\nimagination as I have and I soon found that out. We had reading and\r\ngeography and Canadian history and dictation today. And oh,\r\nMarilla, Jane Andrews told me that Minnie MacPherson told her that she\r\nheard Prissy Andrews tell Sara Gillis that I had a very pretty nose.\r\nMarilla, that is the first compliment I have ever had in my life and you\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t imagine what a strange feeling it gave me. Secretly she thought\r\nAnne\xe2\x80\x99s nose was a remarkable pretty one; but she had no intention of\r\ntelling her so.\r\n\r\nThat was three weeks ago and all had gone smoothly so far. And now, this\r\ncrisp September morning, Anne and Diana were tripping blithely down the\r\nBirch Path, two of the happiest little girls in Avonlea.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI guess Gilbert Blythe will be in school today,\xe2\x80\x9d said Diana. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s been\r\nvisiting his cousins over in New Brunswick all summer and he only came\r\nhome Saturday night. He just torments our lives out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDiana\xe2\x80\x99s voice indicated that she rather liked having her life tormented\r\nout than not.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGilbert Blythe?\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne. \xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t his name that\xe2\x80\x99s written up on the\r\nporch wall with Julia Bell\xe2\x80\x99s and a big \xe2\x80\x98Take Notice\xe2\x80\x99 over them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Diana, tossing her head, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I\xe2\x80\x99m sure he doesn\xe2\x80\x99t like Julia\r\nBell so very much. Not, of\r\ncourse,\xe2\x80\x9d she hastened to add, \xe2\x80\x9cthat anybody would.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne sighed. She didn\xe2\x80\x99t want her name written up. But it was a little\r\nhumiliating to know that there was no danger of it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNonsense,\xe2\x80\x9d said Diana, whose black eyes and glossy tresses had played\r\nsuch havoc with the hearts of Avonlea schoolboys that her name figured\r\non the porch walls in half a dozen take-notices. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s only meant as\r\na joke. And don\xe2\x80\x99t you be too sure your name won\xe2\x80\x99t ever be written up.\r\nCharlie Sloane is _dead gone_ on you. He told his mother--his _mother_,\r\nmind you--that you were the smartest girl in school. That\xe2\x80\x99s better than\r\nbeing good looking.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, it isn\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne, feminine to the core. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d rather be pretty\r\nthan clever. And I hate Charlie Sloane, I can\xe2\x80\x99t bear a boy with goggle\r\neyes. If anyone wrote my name up with his I\xe2\x80\x99d never _get_ over it, Diana\r\nBarry. But it _is_ nice to keep head of your class.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll have Gilbert in your class after this,\xe2\x80\x9d said Diana, \xe2\x80\x9cand he\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nused to being head of his class, I can tell you. He\xe2\x80\x99s only in the fourth\r\nbook although he\xe2\x80\x99s nearly fourteen. Four years ago his father was sick\r\nand had to go out to Alberta for his health and Gilbert went with him.\r\nThey were there three years and Gil didn\xe2\x80\x99t go to school hardly any\r\nuntil they came back. You won\xe2\x80\x99t find it so easy to keep head after this,\r\nAnne.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m glad,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne quickly. \xe2\x80\x9cI couldn\xe2\x80\x99t really feel proud of keeping\r\nhead of little boys and girls of just nine or ten. I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t speak to her now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen Mr. Phillips was in the back of the room hearing Prissy Andrews\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nLatin, Diana whispered to Anne, \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s Gilbert Blythe sitting right\r\nacross the aisle from you, Anne. He\r\nwas a tall boy, with curly brown hair, roguish hazel eyes, and a mouth\r\ntwisted into a teasing smile. Gilbert\r\nhad whisked the pin out of sight and was studying his history with the\r\nsoberest face in the world; but when the commotion subsided he looked at\r\nAnne and winked with inexpressible drollery.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think your Gilbert Blythe _is_ handsome,\xe2\x80\x9d confided Anne to Diana,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cbut I think he\xe2\x80\x99s very bold. It isn\xe2\x80\x99t good manners to wink at a strange\r\ngirl.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut it was not until the afternoon that things really began to happen.\r\n\r\nMr. Phillips was back in the corner explaining a problem in algebra to\r\nPrissy Andrews and the rest of the scholars were doing pretty much as\r\nthey pleased eating green apples, whispering, drawing pictures on their\r\nslates, and driving crickets harnessed to strings, up and down aisle.\r\nGilbert Blythe was trying to make Anne Shirley look at him and failing\r\nutterly, because Anne was at that moment totally oblivious not only\r\nto the very existence of Gilbert Blythe, but of every other scholar in\r\nAvonlea school itself. With her chin propped on her hands and her eyes\r\nfixed on the blue glimpse of the Lake of Shining Waters that the west\r\nwindow afforded, she was far away in a gorgeous dreamland hearing and\r\nseeing nothing save her own wonderful visions.\r\n\r\nGilbert Blythe wasn\xe2\x80\x99t used to putting himself out to make a girl look\r\nat him and meeting with failure. Carrots!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen Anne looked at him with a vengeance!\r\n\r\nShe did more than look. She sprang to her feet, her bright fancies\r\nfallen into cureless ruin. Everybody said \xe2\x80\x9cOh\xe2\x80\x9d in horrified delight. Diana gasped. Ruby\r\nGillis, who was inclined to be hysterical, began to cry. Tommy\r\nSloane let his team of crickets escape him altogether while he stared\r\nopen-mouthed at the tableau.\r\n\r\nMr. Phillips stalked down the aisle and laid his hand heavily on Anne\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nshoulder.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnne Shirley, what does this mean?\xe2\x80\x9d he said angrily. It was asking too much of flesh and blood to expect her to tell\r\nbefore the whole school that she had been called \xe2\x80\x9ccarrots.\xe2\x80\x9d Gilbert it\r\nwas who spoke up stoutly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was my fault Mr. Phillips. With a white,\r\nset face she obeyed. Mr. Phillips took a chalk crayon and wrote on the\r\nblackboard above her head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnn Shirley has a very bad temper. Ann Shirley must learn to control\r\nher temper,\xe2\x80\x9d and then read it out loud so that even the primer class,\r\nwho couldn\xe2\x80\x99t read writing, should understand it.\r\n\r\nAnne stood there the rest of the afternoon with that legend above her.\r\nShe did not cry or hang her head. With\r\nresentful eyes and passion-red cheeks she confronted alike Diana\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsympathetic gaze and Charlie Sloane\xe2\x80\x99s indignant nods and Josie Pye\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmalicious smiles. She would never speak to him!!\r\n\r\nWhen school was dismissed Anne marched out with her red head held high.\r\nGilbert Blythe tried to intercept her at the porch door.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m awfully sorry I made fun of your hair, Anne,\xe2\x80\x9d he whispered\r\ncontritely. \xe2\x80\x9cHonest I am. The iron has entered into my\r\nsoul, Diana.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDiana hadn\xe2\x80\x99t the least idea what Anne meant but she understood it was\r\nsomething terrible.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mustn\xe2\x80\x99t mind Gilbert making fun of your hair,\xe2\x80\x9d she said soothingly.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, he makes fun of all the girls. He laughs at mine because it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nso black. He\xe2\x80\x99s called me a crow a dozen times; and I never heard him\r\napologize for anything before, either.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s a great deal of difference between being called a crow and\r\nbeing called carrots,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne with dignity. \xe2\x80\x9cGilbert Blythe has hurt\r\nmy feelings _excruciatingly_, Diana.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt is possible the matter might have blown over without more\r\nexcruciation if nothing else had happened. But when things begin to\r\nhappen they are apt to keep on.\r\n\r\nAvonlea scholars often spent noon hour picking gum in Mr. Bell\xe2\x80\x99s spruce\r\ngrove over the hill and across his big pasture field. From there they\r\ncould keep an eye on Eben Wright\xe2\x80\x99s house, where the master boarded. When\r\nthey saw Mr. Phillips emerging therefrom they ran for the schoolhouse;\r\nbut the distance being about three times longer than Mr. Wright\xe2\x80\x99s lane\r\nthey were very apt to arrive there, breathless and gasping, some three\r\nminutes too late.\r\n\r\nOn the following day Mr. Phillips was seized with one of his spasmodic\r\nfits of reform and announced before going home to dinner, that he should\r\nexpect to find all the scholars in their seats when he returned. Anyone\r\nwho came in late would be punished.\r\n\r\nAll the boys and some of the girls went to Mr. Bell\xe2\x80\x99s spruce grove as\r\nusual, fully intending to stay only long enough to \xe2\x80\x9cpick a chew.\xe2\x80\x9d But\r\nspruce groves are seductive and yellow nuts of gum beguiling; they\r\npicked and loitered and strayed; and as usual the first thing that\r\nrecalled them to a sense of the flight of time was Jimmy Glover shouting\r\nfrom the top of a patriarchal old spruce \xe2\x80\x9cMaster\xe2\x80\x99s coming.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe girls who were on the ground, started first and managed to reach the\r\nschoolhouse in time but without a second to spare. The boys, who had to\r\nwriggle hastily down from the trees, were later; and Anne, who had not\r\nbeen picking gum at all but was wandering happily in the far end of the\r\ngrove, waist deep among the bracken, singing softly to herself, with a\r\nwreath of rice lilies on her hair as if she were some wild divinity\r\nof the shadowy places, was latest of all. Anne could run like a deer,\r\nhowever; run she did with the impish result that she overtook the boys\r\nat the door and was swept into the schoolhouse among them just as Mr.\r\nPhillips was in the act of hanging up his hat.\r\n\r\nMr. Phillips\xe2\x80\x99s brief reforming energy was over; he didn\xe2\x80\x99t want the\r\nbother of punishing a dozen pupils; but it was necessary to do something\r\nto save his word, so he looked about for a scapegoat and found it\r\nin Anne, who had dropped into her seat, gasping for breath, with a\r\nforgotten lily wreath hanging askew over one ear and giving her a\r\nparticularly rakish and disheveled appearance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnne Shirley, since you seem to be so fond of the boys\xe2\x80\x99 company we\r\nshall indulge your taste for it this afternoon,\xe2\x80\x9d he said sarcastically.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake those flowers out of your hair and sit with Gilbert Blythe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe other boys snickered. Diana, turning pale with pity, plucked the\r\nwreath from Anne\xe2\x80\x99s hair and squeezed her hand. Anne stared at the master\r\nas if turned to stone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you hear what I said, Anne?\xe2\x80\x9d queried Mr. Phillips sternly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne slowly \xe2\x80\x9cbut I didn\xe2\x80\x99t suppose you really meant it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI assure you I did\xe2\x80\x9d--still with the sarcastic inflection which all the\r\nchildren, and Anne especially, hated. It flicked on the raw. \xe2\x80\x9cObey me at\r\nonce.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor a moment Anne looked as if she meant to disobey. Then, realizing\r\nthat there was no help for it, she rose haughtily, stepped across the\r\naisle, sat down beside Gilbert Blythe, and buried her face in her arms\r\non the desk. Ruby Gillis, who got a glimpse of it as it went down,\r\ntold the others going home from school that she\xe2\x80\x99d \xe2\x80\x9cacksually never seen\r\nanything like it--it was so white, with awful little red spots in it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTo Anne, this was as the end of all things. It was bad enough to be\r\nsingled out for punishment from among a dozen equally guilty ones; it\r\nwas worse still to be sent to sit with a boy, but that that boy should\r\nbe Gilbert Blythe was heaping insult on injury to a degree utterly\r\nunbearable. Anne felt that she could not bear it and it would be of\r\nno use to try. Her whole being seethed with shame and anger and\r\nhumiliation.\r\n\r\nAt first the other scholars looked and whispered and giggled and nudged.\r\nBut as Anne never lifted her head and as Gilbert worked fractions as if\r\nhis whole soul was absorbed in them and them only, they soon returned\r\nto their own tasks and Anne was forgotten. When Mr. Phillips called the\r\nhistory class out Anne should have gone, but Anne did not move, and\r\nMr. Phillips, who had been writing some verses \xe2\x80\x9cTo Priscilla\xe2\x80\x9d before he\r\ncalled the class, was thinking about an obstinate rhyme still and never\r\nmissed her. Once, when nobody was looking, Gilbert took from his desk\r\na little pink candy heart with a gold motto on it, \xe2\x80\x9cYou are sweet,\xe2\x80\x9d and\r\nslipped it under the curve of Anne\xe2\x80\x99s arm. Whereupon Anne arose, took the\r\npink heart gingerly between the tips of her fingers, dropped it on the\r\nfloor, ground it to powder beneath her heel, and resumed her position\r\nwithout deigning to bestow a glance on Gilbert.\r\n\r\nWhen school went out Anne marched to her desk, ostentatiously took out\r\neverything therein, books and writing tablet, pen and ink, testament and\r\narithmetic, and piled them neatly on her cracked slate.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you taking all those things home for, Anne?\xe2\x80\x9d Diana wanted to\r\nknow, as soon as they were out on the road. She had not dared to ask the\r\nquestion before.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not coming back to school any more,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne. Diana gasped and\r\nstared at Anne to see if she meant it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill Marilla let you stay home?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99ll have to,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll _never_ go to school to that man\r\nagain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Anne!\xe2\x80\x9d Diana looked as if she were ready to cry. \xe2\x80\x9cI do think you\xe2\x80\x99re\r\nmean. What shall I do? Mr. Phillips will make me sit with that horrid\r\nGertie Pye--I know he will because she is sitting alone. Do come back,\r\nAnne.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d do almost anything in the world for you, Diana,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne sadly.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d let myself be torn limb from limb if it would do you any good. But\r\nI can\xe2\x80\x99t do this, so please don\xe2\x80\x99t ask it. You harrow up my very soul.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust think of all the fun you will miss,\xe2\x80\x9d mourned Diana. \xe2\x80\x9cWe are going\r\nto build the loveliest new house down by the brook; and we\xe2\x80\x99ll be playing\r\nball next week and you\xe2\x80\x99ve never played ball, Anne. It\xe2\x80\x99s tremendously\r\nexciting. And we\xe2\x80\x99re going to learn a new song--Jane Andrews is\r\npracticing it up now; and Alice Andrews is going to bring a new Pansy\r\nbook next week and we\xe2\x80\x99re all going to read it out loud, chapter about,\r\ndown by the brook. And you know you are so fond of reading out loud,\r\nAnne.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNothing moved Anne in the least. Her mind was made up. She would not go\r\nto school to Mr. Phillips again; she told Marilla so when she got home.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNonsense,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt isn\xe2\x80\x99t nonsense at all,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne, gazing at Marilla with solemn,\r\nreproachful eyes. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you understand, Marilla? I\xe2\x80\x99ve been insulted.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cInsulted fiddlesticks! You\xe2\x80\x99ll go to school tomorrow as usual.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, no.\xe2\x80\x9d Anne shook her head gently. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m not going back, Marilla. I\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nlearn my lessons at home and I\xe2\x80\x99ll be as good as I can be and hold my\r\ntongue all the time if it\xe2\x80\x99s possible at all. But I will not go back to\r\nschool, I assure you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarilla saw something remarkably like unyielding stubbornness looking\r\nout of Anne\xe2\x80\x99s small face. She understood that she would have trouble in\r\novercoming it; but she re-solved wisely to say nothing more just then.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll run down and see Rachel about it this evening,\xe2\x80\x9d she thought.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s no use reasoning with Anne now. She\xe2\x80\x99s too worked up and I\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nan idea she can be awful stubborn if she takes the notion. Far as I can\r\nmake out from her story, Mr. Phillips has been carrying matters with a\r\nrather high hand. But it would never do to say so to her. I\xe2\x80\x99ll just talk\r\nit over with Rachel. She\xe2\x80\x99s sent ten children to school and she ought to\r\nknow something about it. She\xe2\x80\x99ll have heard the whole story, too, by this\r\ntime.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarilla found Mrs. Lynde knitting quilts as industriously and cheerfully\r\nas usual.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose you know what I\xe2\x80\x99ve come about,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, a little\r\nshamefacedly.\r\n\r\nMrs. Rachel nodded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbout Anne\xe2\x80\x99s fuss in school, I reckon,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cTillie Boulter was\r\nin on her way home from school and told me about it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what to do with her,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla. \xe2\x80\x9cShe declares she\r\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t go back to school. I never saw a child so worked up. I\xe2\x80\x99ve been\r\nexpecting trouble ever since she started to school. I knew things were\r\ngoing too smooth to last. She\xe2\x80\x99s so high strung. What would you advise,\r\nRachel?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, since you\xe2\x80\x99ve asked my advice, Marilla,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Lynde\r\namiably--Mrs. Lynde dearly loved to be asked for advice--\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d just\r\nhumor her a little at first, that\xe2\x80\x99s what I\xe2\x80\x99d do. It\xe2\x80\x99s my belief that\r\nMr. Phillips was in the wrong. Of course, it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t do to say so to the\r\nchildren, you know. And of course he did right to punish her yesterday\r\nfor giving way to temper. But today it was different. The others who\r\nwere late should have been punished as well as Anne, that\xe2\x80\x99s what. And I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t believe in making the girls sit with the boys for punishment. It\r\nisn\xe2\x80\x99t modest. Tillie Boulter was real indignant. She took Anne\xe2\x80\x99s part\r\nright through and said all the scholars did too. Anne seems real popular\r\namong them, somehow. I never thought she\xe2\x80\x99d take with them so well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you really think I\xe2\x80\x99d better let her stay home,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla in\r\namazement.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. That is I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t say school to her again until she said it\r\nherself. Depend upon it, Marilla, she\xe2\x80\x99ll cool off in a week or so and\r\nbe ready enough to go back of her own accord, that\xe2\x80\x99s what, while, if\r\nyou were to make her go back right off, dear knows what freak or tantrum\r\nshe\xe2\x80\x99d take next and make more trouble than ever. The less fuss made the\r\nbetter, in my opinion. She won\xe2\x80\x99t miss much by not going to school, as\r\nfar as _that_ goes. Mr. Phillips isn\xe2\x80\x99t any good at all as a teacher. The\r\norder he keeps is scandalous, that\xe2\x80\x99s what, and he neglects the young\r\nfry and puts all his time on those big scholars he\xe2\x80\x99s getting ready for\r\nQueen\xe2\x80\x99s. He\xe2\x80\x99d never have got the school for another year if his uncle\r\nhadn\xe2\x80\x99t been a trustee--_the_ trustee, for he just leads the other two\r\naround by the nose, that\xe2\x80\x99s what. I declare, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what education\r\nin this Island is coming to.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Rachel shook her head, as much as to say if she were only at the\r\nhead of the educational system of the Province things would be much\r\nbetter managed.\r\n\r\nMarilla took Mrs. Rachel\xe2\x80\x99s advice and not another word was said to Anne\r\nabout going back to school. She learned her lessons at home, did her\r\nchores, and played with Diana in the chilly purple autumn twilights;\r\nbut when she met Gilbert Blythe on the road or encountered him in Sunday\r\nschool she passed him by with an icy contempt that was no whit thawed by\r\nhis evident desire to appease her. Even Diana\xe2\x80\x99s efforts as a peacemaker\r\nwere of no avail. Anne had evidently made up her mind to hate Gilbert\r\nBlythe to the end of life.\r\n\r\nAs much as she hated Gilbert, however, did she love Diana, with all the\r\nlove of her passionate little heart, equally intense in its likes and\r\ndislikes. One evening Marilla, coming in from the orchard with a basket\r\nof apples, found Anne sitting along by the east window in the twilight,\r\ncrying bitterly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhatever\xe2\x80\x99s the matter now, Anne?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s about Diana,\xe2\x80\x9d sobbed Anne luxuriously. \xe2\x80\x9cI love Diana so, Marilla.\r\nI cannot ever live without her. But I know very well when we grow up\r\nthat Diana will get married and go away and leave me. And oh, what shall\r\nI do? I hate her husband--I just hate him furiously. I\xe2\x80\x99ve been imagining\r\nit all out--the wedding and everything--Diana dressed in snowy garments,\r\nwith a veil, and looking as beautiful and regal as a queen; and me the\r\nbridesmaid, with a lovely dress too, and puffed sleeves, but with a\r\nbreaking heart hid beneath my smiling face. And then bidding Diana\r\ngoodbye-e-e--\xe2\x80\x9d Here Anne broke down entirely and wept with increasing\r\nbitterness.\r\n\r\nMarilla turned quickly away to hide her twitching face; but it was no\r\nuse; she collapsed on the nearest chair and burst into such a hearty and\r\nunusual peal of laughter that Matthew, crossing the yard outside, halted\r\nin amazement. When had he heard Marilla laugh like that before?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, Anne Shirley,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla as soon as she could speak, \xe2\x80\x9cif you\r\nmust borrow trouble, for pity\xe2\x80\x99s sake borrow it handier home. I should\r\nthink you had an imagination, sure enough.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XVI. Diana Is Invited to Tea with Tragic Results\r\n\r\n\r\n|OCTOBER was a beautiful month at Green Gables, when the birches in the\r\nhollow turned as golden as sunshine and the maples behind the orchard\r\nwere royal crimson and the wild cherry trees along the lane put on the\r\nloveliest shades of dark red and bronzy green, while the fields sunned\r\nthemselves in aftermaths.\r\n\r\nAnne reveled in the world of color about her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Marilla,\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed one Saturday morning, coming dancing in\r\nwith her arms full of gorgeous boughs, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m so glad I live in a world\r\nwhere there are Octobers. It would be terrible if we just skipped from\r\nSeptember to November, wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t it? Look at these maple branches. Don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nthey give you a thrill--several thrills? I\xe2\x80\x99m going to decorate my room\r\nwith them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMessy things,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla, whose aesthetic sense was not noticeably\r\ndeveloped. \xe2\x80\x9cYou clutter up your room entirely too much with out-of-doors\r\nstuff, Anne. Bedrooms were made to sleep in.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, and dream in too, Marilla. And you know one can dream so much\r\nbetter in a room where there are pretty things. I\xe2\x80\x99m going to put these\r\nboughs in the old blue jug and set them on my table.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMind you don\xe2\x80\x99t drop leaves all over the stairs then. I\xe2\x80\x99m going on a\r\nmeeting of the Aid Society at Carmody this afternoon, Anne, and I won\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nlikely be home before dark. You\xe2\x80\x99ll have to get Matthew and Jerry their\r\nsupper, so mind you don\xe2\x80\x99t forget to put the tea to draw until you sit\r\ndown at the table as you did last time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was dreadful of me to forget,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne apologetically, \xe2\x80\x9cbut that\r\nwas the afternoon I was trying to think of a name for Violet Vale and it\r\ncrowded other things out. Matthew was so good. He never scolded a bit.\r\nHe put the tea down himself and said we could wait awhile as well as\r\nnot. And I told him a lovely fairy story while we were waiting, so\r\nhe didn\xe2\x80\x99t find the time long at all. It was a beautiful fairy story,\r\nMarilla. I forgot the end of it, so I made up an end for it myself and\r\nMatthew said he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t tell where the join came in.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMatthew would think it all right, Anne, if you took a notion to get up\r\nand have dinner in the middle of the night. But you keep your wits about\r\nyou this time. And--I don\xe2\x80\x99t really know if I\xe2\x80\x99m doing right--it may make\r\nyou more addlepated than ever--but you can ask Diana to come over and\r\nspend the afternoon with you and have tea here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Marilla!\xe2\x80\x9d Anne clasped her hands. \xe2\x80\x9cHow perfectly lovely! You _are_\r\nable to imagine things after all or else you\xe2\x80\x99d never have understood how\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ve longed for that very thing. It will seem so nice and grown-uppish.\r\nNo fear of my forgetting to put the tea to draw when I have company. Oh,\r\nMarilla, can I use the rosebud spray tea set?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, indeed! The rosebud tea set! Well, what next? You know I never use\r\nthat except for the minister or the Aids. You\xe2\x80\x99ll put down the old brown\r\ntea set. But you can open the little yellow crock of cherry preserves.\r\nIt\xe2\x80\x99s time it was being used anyhow--I believe it\xe2\x80\x99s beginning to work.\r\nAnd you can cut some fruit cake and have some of the cookies and snaps.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can just imagine myself sitting down at the head of the table and\r\npouring out the tea,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne, shutting her eyes ecstatically. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd\r\nasking Diana if she takes sugar! I know she doesn\xe2\x80\x99t but of course I\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nask her just as if I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know. And then pressing her to take another\r\npiece of fruit cake and another helping of preserves. Oh, Marilla, it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\na wonderful sensation just to think of it. Can I take her into the spare\r\nroom to lay off her hat when she comes? And then into the parlor to\r\nsit?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. The sitting room will do for you and your company. But there\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\nbottle half full of raspberry cordial that was left over from the church\r\nsocial the other night. It\xe2\x80\x99s on the second shelf of the sitting-room\r\ncloset and you and Diana can have it if you like, and a cooky to eat\r\nwith it along in the afternoon, for I daresay Matthew \xe2\x80\x98ll be late coming\r\nin to tea since he\xe2\x80\x99s hauling potatoes to the vessel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne flew down to the hollow, past the Dryad\xe2\x80\x99s Bubble and up the spruce\r\npath to Orchard Slope, to ask Diana to tea. As a result just after\r\nMarilla had driven off to Carmody, Diana came over, dressed in _her_\r\nsecond-best dress and looking exactly as it is proper to look when asked\r\nout to tea. At other times she was wont to run into the kitchen without\r\nknocking; but now she knocked primly at the front door. And when Anne,\r\ndressed in her second best, as primly opened it, both little girls\r\nshook hands as gravely as if they had never met before. This unnatural\r\nsolemnity lasted until after Diana had been taken to the east gable to\r\nlay off her hat and then had sat for ten minutes in the sitting room,\r\ntoes in position.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow is your mother?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired Anne politely, just as if she had not\r\nseen Mrs. Barry picking apples that morning in excellent health and\r\nspirits.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is very well, thank you. I suppose Mr. Cuthbert is hauling potatoes\r\nto the _lily sands_ this afternoon, is he?\xe2\x80\x9d said Diana, who had ridden\r\ndown to Mr. Harmon Andrews\xe2\x80\x99s that morning in Matthew\xe2\x80\x99s cart.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. Our potato crop is very good this year. I hope your father\xe2\x80\x99s crop\r\nis good too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is fairly good, thank you. Have you picked many of your apples yet?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, ever so many,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne forgetting to be dignified and jumping up\r\nquickly. \xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s go out to the orchard and get some of the Red Sweetings,\r\nDiana. Marilla says we can have all that are left on the tree. Marilla\r\nis a very generous woman. She said we could have fruit cake and cherry\r\npreserves for tea. But it isn\xe2\x80\x99t good manners to tell your company what\r\nyou are going to give them to eat, so I won\xe2\x80\x99t tell you what she said we\r\ncould have to drink. Only it begins with an R and a C and it\xe2\x80\x99s bright\r\nred color. I love bright red drinks, don\xe2\x80\x99t you?
