They taste twice as good\r\nas any other color.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe orchard, with its great sweeping boughs that bent to the ground\r\nwith fruit, proved so delightful that the little girls spent most of the\r\nafternoon in it, sitting in a grassy corner where the frost had spared\r\nthe green and the mellow autumn sunshine lingered warmly, eating apples\r\nand talking as hard as they could. Diana had much to tell Anne of what\r\nwent on in school. She had to sit with Gertie Pye and she hated\r\nit; Gertie squeaked her pencil all the time and it just made\r\nher--Diana\xe2\x80\x99s--blood run cold; Ruby Gillis had charmed all her warts\r\naway, true\xe2\x80\x99s you live, with a magic pebble that old Mary Joe from the\r\nCreek gave her. You had to rub the warts with the pebble and then throw\r\nit away over your left shoulder at the time of the new moon and the\r\nwarts would all go. Charlie Sloane\xe2\x80\x99s name was written up with Em White\xe2\x80\x99s\r\non the porch wall and Em White was _awful mad_ about it; Sam Boulter had\r\n\xe2\x80\x9csassed\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Phillips in class and Mr. Phillips whipped him and Sam\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nfather came down to the school and dared Mr. Phillips to lay a hand on\r\none of his children again; and Mattie Andrews had a new red hood and a\r\nblue crossover with tassels on it and the airs she put on about it were\r\nperfectly sickening; and Lizzie Wright didn\xe2\x80\x99t speak to Mamie Wilson\r\nbecause Mamie Wilson\xe2\x80\x99s grown-up sister had cut out Lizzie Wright\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ngrown-up sister with her beau; and everybody missed Anne so and wished\r\nshe\xe2\x80\x99s come to school again; and Gilbert Blythe--\r\n\r\nBut Anne didn\xe2\x80\x99t want to hear about Gilbert Blythe. She jumped up\r\nhurriedly and said suppose they go in and have some raspberry cordial.\r\n\r\nAnne looked on the second shelf of the room pantry but there was no\r\nbottle of raspberry cordial there. Search revealed it away back on the\r\ntop shelf. Anne put it on a tray and set it on the table with a tumbler.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, please help yourself, Diana,\xe2\x80\x9d she said politely. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t believe\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll have any just now. I don\xe2\x80\x99t feel as if I wanted any after all those\r\napples.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDiana poured herself out a tumblerful, looked at its bright-red hue\r\nadmiringly, and then sipped it daintily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s awfully nice raspberry cordial, Anne,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t know\r\nraspberry cordial was so nice.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m real glad you like it. Take as much as you want. I\xe2\x80\x99m going to\r\nrun out and stir the fire up. There are so many responsibilities on a\r\nperson\xe2\x80\x99s mind when they\xe2\x80\x99re keeping house, isn\xe2\x80\x99t there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen Anne came back from the kitchen Diana was drinking her second\r\nglassful of cordial; and, being entreated thereto by Anne, she offered\r\nno particular objection to the drinking of a third. The tumblerfuls were\r\ngenerous ones and the raspberry cordial was certainly very nice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe nicest I ever drank,\xe2\x80\x9d said Diana. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s ever so much nicer than\r\nMrs. Lynde\xe2\x80\x99s, although she brags of hers so much. It doesn\xe2\x80\x99t taste a bit\r\nlike hers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should think Marilla\xe2\x80\x99s raspberry cordial would prob\xe2\x80\x99ly be much nicer\r\nthan Mrs. Lynde\xe2\x80\x99s,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne loyally. \xe2\x80\x9cMarilla is a famous cook. She is\r\ntrying to teach me to cook but I assure you, Diana, it is uphill work.\r\nThere\xe2\x80\x99s so little scope for imagination in cookery. You just have to go\r\nby rules. The last time I made a cake I forgot to put the flour in. I\r\nwas thinking the loveliest story about you and me, Diana. I thought you\r\nwere desperately ill with smallpox and everybody deserted you, but I\r\nwent boldly to your bedside and nursed you back to life; and then I took\r\nthe smallpox and died and I was buried under those poplar trees in the\r\ngraveyard and you planted a rosebush by my grave and watered it with\r\nyour tears; and you never, never forgot the friend of your youth who\r\nsacrificed her life for you. Oh, it was such a pathetic tale, Diana.\r\nThe tears just rained down over my cheeks while I mixed the cake. But\r\nI forgot the flour and the cake was a dismal failure. Flour is so\r\nessential to cakes, you know. Marilla was very cross and I don\xe2\x80\x99t wonder.\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99m a great trial to her. She was terribly mortified about the pudding\r\nsauce last week. We had a plum pudding for dinner on Tuesday and there\r\nwas half the pudding and a pitcherful of sauce left over. Marilla said\r\nthere was enough for another dinner and told me to set it on the pantry\r\nshelf and cover it. I meant to cover it just as much as could be, Diana,\r\nbut when I carried it in I was imagining I was a nun--of course I\xe2\x80\x99m a\r\nProtestant but I imagined I was a Catholic--taking the veil to bury a\r\nbroken heart in cloistered seclusion; and I forgot all about covering\r\nthe pudding sauce. I thought of it next morning and ran to the pantry.\r\nDiana, fancy if you can my extreme horror at finding a mouse drowned in\r\nthat pudding sauce! I lifted the mouse out with a spoon and threw it out\r\nin the yard and then I washed the spoon in three waters. Marilla was out\r\nmilking and I fully intended to ask her when she came in if I\xe2\x80\x99d give the\r\nsauce to the pigs; but when she did come in I was imagining that I was\r\na frost fairy going through the woods turning the trees red and yellow,\r\nwhichever they wanted to be, so I never thought about the pudding sauce\r\nagain and Marilla sent me out to pick apples. Well, Mr. and Mrs. Chester\r\nRoss from Spencervale came here that morning. You know they are very\r\nstylish people, especially Mrs. Chester Ross. When Marilla called me in\r\ndinner was all ready and everybody was at the table. I tried to be as\r\npolite and dignified as I could be, for I wanted Mrs. Chester Ross to\r\nthink I was a ladylike little girl even if I wasn\xe2\x80\x99t pretty. Everything\r\nwent right until I saw Marilla coming with the plum pudding in one hand\r\nand the pitcher of pudding sauce _warmed up_, in the other. Diana, that\r\nwas a terrible moment. I remembered everything and I just stood up in\r\nmy place and shrieked out \xe2\x80\x98Marilla, you mustn\xe2\x80\x99t use that pudding sauce.\r\nThere was a mouse drowned in it. I forgot to tell you before.\xe2\x80\x99 Oh,\r\nDiana, I shall never forget that awful moment if I live to be a hundred.\r\nMrs. Chester Ross just _looked_ at me and I thought I would sink through\r\nthe floor with mortification. She is such a perfect housekeeper and\r\nfancy what she must have thought of us. Marilla turned red as fire but\r\nshe never said a word--then. She just carried that sauce and pudding out\r\nand brought in some strawberry preserves. She even offered me some, but\r\nI couldn\xe2\x80\x99t swallow a mouthful. It was like heaping coals of fire on\r\nmy head. After Mrs. Chester Ross went away, Marilla gave me a dreadful\r\nscolding. Why, Diana, what is the matter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDiana had stood up very unsteadily; then she sat down again, putting her\r\nhands to her head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m--I\xe2\x80\x99m awful sick,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, a little thickly. \xe2\x80\x9cI--I--must go right\r\nhome.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, you mustn\xe2\x80\x99t dream of going home without your tea,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Anne in\r\ndistress. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll get it right off--I\xe2\x80\x99ll go and put the tea down this very\r\nminute.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must go home,\xe2\x80\x9d repeated Diana, stupidly but determinedly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet me get you a lunch anyhow,\xe2\x80\x9d implored Anne. \xe2\x80\x9cLet me give you a bit\r\nof fruit cake and some of the cherry preserves. Lie down on the sofa for\r\na little while and you\xe2\x80\x99ll be better. Where do you feel bad?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must go home,\xe2\x80\x9d said Diana, and that was all she would say. In vain\r\nAnne pleaded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never heard of company going home without tea,\xe2\x80\x9d she mourned. \xe2\x80\x9cOh,\r\nDiana, do you suppose that it\xe2\x80\x99s possible you\xe2\x80\x99re really taking the\r\nsmallpox? If you are I\xe2\x80\x99ll go and nurse you, you can depend on that. I\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nnever forsake you. But I do wish you\xe2\x80\x99d stay till after tea. Where do you\r\nfeel bad?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m awful dizzy,\xe2\x80\x9d said Diana.\r\n\r\nAnd indeed, she walked very dizzily. Anne, with tears of disappointment\r\nin her eyes, got Diana\xe2\x80\x99s hat and went with her as far as the Barry\r\nyard fence. Then she wept all the way back to Green Gables, where she\r\nsorrowfully put the remainder of the raspberry cordial back into the\r\npantry and got tea ready for Matthew and Jerry, with all the zest gone\r\nout of the performance.\r\n\r\nThe next day was Sunday and as the rain poured down in torrents from\r\ndawn till dusk Anne did not stir abroad from Green Gables. Monday\r\nafternoon Marilla sent her down to Mrs. Lynde\xe2\x80\x99s on an errand. In a very\r\nshort space of time Anne came flying back up the lane with tears rolling\r\ndown her cheeks. Into the kitchen she dashed and flung herself face\r\ndownward on the sofa in an agony.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhatever has gone wrong now, Anne?\xe2\x80\x9d queried Marilla in doubt and\r\ndismay. \xe2\x80\x9cI do hope you haven\xe2\x80\x99t gone and been saucy to Mrs. Lynde again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNo answer from Anne save more tears and stormier sobs!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnne Shirley, when I ask you a question I want to be answered. Sit\r\nright up this very minute and tell me what you are crying about.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne sat up, tragedy personified.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Lynde was up to see Mrs. Barry today and Mrs. Barry was in an\r\nawful state,\xe2\x80\x9d she wailed. \xe2\x80\x9cShe says that I set Diana _drunk_ Saturday\r\nand sent her home in a disgraceful condition. And she says I must be a\r\nthoroughly bad, wicked little girl and she\xe2\x80\x99s never, never going to let\r\nDiana play with me again. Oh, Marilla, I\xe2\x80\x99m just overcome with woe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarilla stared in blank amazement.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSet Diana drunk!\xe2\x80\x9d she said when she found her voice. \xe2\x80\x9cAnne are you or\r\nMrs. Barry crazy? What on earth did you give her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot a thing but raspberry cordial,\xe2\x80\x9d sobbed Anne. \xe2\x80\x9cI never thought\r\nraspberry cordial would set people drunk, Marilla--not even if they\r\ndrank three big tumblerfuls as Diana did. Oh, it sounds so--so--like\r\nMrs. Thomas\xe2\x80\x99s husband! But I didn\xe2\x80\x99t mean to set her drunk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDrunk fiddlesticks!\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla, marching to the sitting room pantry.\r\nThere on the shelf was a bottle which she at once recognized as one\r\ncontaining some of her three-year-old homemade currant wine for which\r\nshe was celebrated in Avonlea, although certain of the stricter sort,\r\nMrs. Barry among them, disapproved strongly of it. And at the same time\r\nMarilla recollected that she had put the bottle of raspberry cordial\r\ndown in the cellar instead of in the pantry as she had told Anne.\r\n\r\nShe went back to the kitchen with the wine bottle in her hand. Her face\r\nwas twitching in spite of herself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnne, you certainly have a genius for getting into trouble. You went\r\nand gave Diana currant wine instead of raspberry cordial. Didn\xe2\x80\x99t you\r\nknow the difference yourself?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never tasted it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne. \xe2\x80\x9cI thought it was the cordial. I meant\r\nto be so--so--hospitable. Diana got awfully sick and had to go home.\r\nMrs. Barry told Mrs. Lynde she was simply dead drunk. She just laughed\r\nsilly-like when her mother asked her what was the matter and went to\r\nsleep and slept for hours. Her mother smelled her breath and knew she\r\nwas drunk. She had a fearful headache all day yesterday. Mrs. Barry is\r\nso indignant. She will never believe but what I did it on purpose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should think she would better punish Diana for being so greedy as to\r\ndrink three glassfuls of anything,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla shortly. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, three\r\nof those big glasses would have made her sick even if it had only been\r\ncordial. Well, this story will be a nice handle for those folks who are\r\nso down on me for making currant wine, although I haven\xe2\x80\x99t made any for\r\nthree years ever since I found out that the minister didn\xe2\x80\x99t approve. I\r\njust kept that bottle for sickness. There, there, child, don\xe2\x80\x99t cry. I\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t see as you were to blame although I\xe2\x80\x99m sorry it happened so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must cry,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne. \xe2\x80\x9cMy heart is broken. The stars in their courses\r\nfight against me, Marilla. Diana and I are parted forever. Oh, Marilla,\r\nI little dreamed of this when first we swore our vows of friendship.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t be foolish, Anne. Mrs. Barry will think better of it when she\r\nfinds you\xe2\x80\x99re not to blame. I suppose she thinks you\xe2\x80\x99ve done it for a\r\nsilly joke or something of that sort. You\xe2\x80\x99d best go up this evening and\r\ntell her how it was.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy courage fails me at the thought of facing Diana\xe2\x80\x99s injured mother,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n sighed Anne. \xe2\x80\x9cI wish you\xe2\x80\x99d go, Marilla. You\xe2\x80\x99re so much more dignified\r\nthan I am. Likely she\xe2\x80\x99d listen to you quicker than to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I will,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla, reflecting that it would probably be the\r\nwiser course. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t cry any more, Anne. It will be all right.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarilla had changed her mind about it being all right by the time she\r\ngot back from Orchard Slope. Anne was watching for her coming and flew\r\nto the porch door to meet her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Marilla, I know by your face that it\xe2\x80\x99s been no use,\xe2\x80\x9d she said\r\nsorrowfully. \xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Barry won\xe2\x80\x99t forgive me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Barry indeed!\xe2\x80\x9d snapped Marilla. \xe2\x80\x9cOf all the unreasonable women\r\nI ever saw she\xe2\x80\x99s the worst. I told her it was all a mistake and you\r\nweren\xe2\x80\x99t to blame, but she just simply didn\xe2\x80\x99t believe me. And she rubbed\r\nit well in about my currant wine and how I\xe2\x80\x99d always said it couldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nhave the least effect on anybody. I just told her plainly that currant\r\nwine wasn\xe2\x80\x99t meant to be drunk three tumblerfuls at a time and that if a\r\nchild I had to do with was so greedy I\xe2\x80\x99d sober her up with a right good\r\nspanking.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarilla whisked into the kitchen, grievously disturbed, leaving a very\r\nmuch distracted little soul in the porch behind her. Presently Anne\r\nstepped out bareheaded into the chill autumn dusk; very determinedly and\r\nsteadily she took her way down through the sere clover field over the\r\nlog bridge and up through the spruce grove, lighted by a pale little\r\nmoon hanging low over the western woods. Mrs. Barry, coming to the door\r\nin answer to a timid knock, found a white-lipped eager-eyed suppliant on\r\nthe doorstep.\r\n\r\nHer face hardened. Mrs. Barry was a woman of strong prejudices and\r\ndislikes, and her anger was of the cold, sullen sort which is always\r\nhardest to overcome. To do her justice, she really believed Anne had\r\nmade Diana drunk out of sheer malice prepense, and she was honestly\r\nanxious to preserve her little daughter from the contamination of\r\nfurther intimacy with such a child.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you want?\xe2\x80\x9d she said stiffly.\r\n\r\nAnne clasped her hands.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Mrs. Barry, please forgive me. I did not mean to--to--intoxicate\r\nDiana. How could I? Just imagine if you were a poor little orphan girl\r\nthat kind people had adopted and you had just one bosom friend in all\r\nthe world. Do you think you would intoxicate her on purpose? I thought\r\nit was only raspberry cordial. I was firmly convinced it was raspberry\r\ncordial. Oh, please don\xe2\x80\x99t say that you won\xe2\x80\x99t let Diana play with me any\r\nmore. If you do you will cover my life with a dark cloud of woe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis speech which would have softened good Mrs. Lynde\xe2\x80\x99s heart in a\r\ntwinkling, had no effect on Mrs. Barry except to irritate her still\r\nmore. She was suspicious of Anne\xe2\x80\x99s big words and dramatic gestures and\r\nimagined that the child was making fun of her. So she said, coldly and\r\ncruelly:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think you are a fit little girl for Diana to associate with.\r\nYou\xe2\x80\x99d better go home and behave yourself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne\xe2\x80\x99s lips quivered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWon\xe2\x80\x99t you let me see Diana just once to say farewell?\xe2\x80\x9d she implored.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDiana has gone over to Carmody with her father,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Barry, going\r\nin and shutting the door.\r\n\r\nAnne went back to Green Gables calm with despair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy last hope is gone,\xe2\x80\x9d she told Marilla. \xe2\x80\x9cI went up and saw Mrs. Barry\r\nmyself and she treated me very insultingly. Marilla, I do _not_ think she\r\nis a well-bred woman. There is nothing more to do except to pray and I\r\nhaven\xe2\x80\x99t much hope that that\xe2\x80\x99ll do much good because, Marilla, I do not\r\nbelieve that God Himself can do very much with such an obstinate person\r\nas Mrs. Barry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnne, you shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t say such things\xe2\x80\x9d rebuked Marilla, striving to\r\novercome that unholy tendency to laughter which she was dismayed to find\r\ngrowing upon her. And indeed, when she told the whole story to Matthew\r\nthat night, she did laugh heartily over Anne\xe2\x80\x99s tribulations.\r\n\r\nBut when she slipped into the east gable before going to bed and found\r\nthat Anne had cried herself to sleep an unaccustomed softness crept into\r\nher face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoor little soul,\xe2\x80\x9d she murmured, lifting a loose curl of hair from the\r\nchild\xe2\x80\x99s tear-stained face. Then she bent down and kissed the flushed\r\ncheek on the pillow.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XVII. A New Interest in Life\r\n\r\n\r\n|THE next afternoon Anne, bending over her patchwork at the kitchen\r\nwindow, happened to glance out and beheld Diana down by the Dryad\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nBubble beckoning mysteriously. In a trice Anne was out of the house\r\nand flying down to the hollow, astonishment and hope struggling in\r\nher expressive eyes. But the hope faded when she saw Diana\xe2\x80\x99s dejected\r\ncountenance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour mother hasn\xe2\x80\x99t relented?\xe2\x80\x9d she gasped.\r\n\r\nDiana shook her head mournfully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; and oh, Anne, she says I\xe2\x80\x99m never to play with you again. I\xe2\x80\x99ve cried\r\nand cried and I told her it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t your fault, but it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t any use. I\r\nhad ever such a time coaxing her to let me come down and say good-bye to\r\nyou. She said I was only to stay ten minutes and she\xe2\x80\x99s timing me by the\r\nclock.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTen minutes isn\xe2\x80\x99t very long to say an eternal farewell in,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne\r\ntearfully. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, Diana, will you promise faithfully never to forget\r\nme, the friend of your youth, no matter what dearer friends may caress\r\nthee?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed I will,\xe2\x80\x9d sobbed Diana, \xe2\x80\x9cand I\xe2\x80\x99ll never have another bosom\r\nfriend--I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to have. I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t love anybody as I love you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Diana,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Anne, clasping her hands, \xe2\x80\x9cdo you _love_ me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, of course I do. Didn\xe2\x80\x99t you know that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d Anne drew a long breath. \xe2\x80\x9cI thought you _liked_ me of course but I\r\nnever hoped you _loved_ me. Why, Diana, I didn\xe2\x80\x99t think anybody could\r\nlove me. Nobody ever has loved me since I can remember. Oh, this is\r\nwonderful! It\xe2\x80\x99s a ray of light which will forever shine on the darkness\r\nof a path severed from thee, Diana. Oh, just say it once again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI love you devotedly, Anne,\xe2\x80\x9d said Diana stanchly, \xe2\x80\x9cand I always will,\r\nyou may be sure of that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I will always love thee, Diana,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne, solemnly extending her\r\nhand. \xe2\x80\x9cIn the years to come thy memory will shine like a star over my\r\nlonely life, as that last story we read together says. Diana, wilt\r\nthou give me a lock of thy jet-black tresses in parting to treasure\r\nforevermore?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you got anything to cut it with?\xe2\x80\x9d queried Diana, wiping away the\r\ntears which Anne\xe2\x80\x99s affecting accents had caused to flow afresh, and\r\nreturning to practicalities.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. I\xe2\x80\x99ve got my patchwork scissors in my apron pocket fortunately,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said Anne. She solemnly clipped one of Diana\xe2\x80\x99s curls. \xe2\x80\x9cFare thee well,\r\nmy beloved friend. Henceforth we must be as strangers though living side\r\nby side. But my heart will ever be faithful to thee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne stood and watched Diana out of sight, mournfully waving her hand\r\nto the latter whenever she turned to look back. Then she returned to\r\nthe house, not a little consoled for the time being by this romantic\r\nparting.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is all over,\xe2\x80\x9d she informed Marilla. \xe2\x80\x9cI shall never have another\r\nfriend. I\xe2\x80\x99m really worse off than ever before, for I haven\xe2\x80\x99t Katie\r\nMaurice and Violetta now. And even if I had it wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be the same.\r\nSomehow, little dream girls are not satisfying after a real friend.\r\nDiana and I had such an affecting farewell down by the spring. It will\r\nbe sacred in my memory forever. I used the most pathetic language I\r\ncould think of and said \xe2\x80\x98thou\xe2\x80\x99 and \xe2\x80\x98thee.\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98Thou\xe2\x80\x99 and \xe2\x80\x98thee\xe2\x80\x99 seem so\r\nmuch more romantic than \xe2\x80\x98you.\xe2\x80\x99 Diana gave me a lock of her hair and I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\ngoing to sew it up in a little bag and wear it around my neck all my\r\nlife. Please see that it is buried with me, for I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe I\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nlive very long. Perhaps when she sees me lying cold and dead before her\r\nMrs. Barry may feel remorse for what she has done and will let Diana\r\ncome to my funeral.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think there is much fear of your dying of grief as long as you\r\ncan talk, Anne,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla unsympathetically.\r\n\r\nThe following Monday Anne surprised Marilla by coming down from her room\r\nwith her basket of books on her arm and hip and her lips primmed up into\r\na line of determination.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m going back to school,\xe2\x80\x9d she announced. \xe2\x80\x9cThat is all there is left\r\nin life for me, now that my friend has been ruthlessly torn from me. In\r\nschool I can look at her and muse over days departed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99d better muse over your lessons and sums,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla, concealing\r\nher delight at this development of the situation. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you\xe2\x80\x99re going back\r\nto school I hope we\xe2\x80\x99ll hear no more of breaking slates over people\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nheads and such carryings on. Behave yourself and do just what your\r\nteacher tells you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll try to be a model pupil,\xe2\x80\x9d agreed Anne dolefully. \xe2\x80\x9cThere won\xe2\x80\x99t be\r\nmuch fun in it, I expect. Mr. Phillips said Minnie Andrews was a model\r\npupil and there isn\xe2\x80\x99t a spark of imagination or life in her. She is\r\njust dull and poky and never seems to have a good time. But I feel so\r\ndepressed that perhaps it will come easy to me now. I\xe2\x80\x99m going round by\r\nthe road. I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t bear to go by the Birch Path all alone. I should\r\nweep bitter tears if I did.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne was welcomed back to school with open arms. Her imagination had\r\nbeen sorely missed in games, her voice in the singing and her dramatic\r\nability in the perusal aloud of books at dinner hour. Ruby Gillis\r\nsmuggled three blue plums over to her during testament reading; Ella May\r\nMacPherson gave her an enormous yellow pansy cut from the covers of a\r\nfloral catalogue--a species of desk decoration much prized in Avonlea\r\nschool. Sophia Sloane offered to teach her a perfectly elegant new\r\npattern of knit lace, so nice for trimming aprons. Katie Boulter gave\r\nher a perfume bottle to keep slate water in, and Julia Bell copied\r\ncarefully on a piece of pale pink paper scalloped on the edges the\r\nfollowing effusion:\r\n\r\n\r\n  When twilight drops her curtain down\r\n  And pins it with a star\r\n  Remember that you have a friend\r\n  Though she may wander far.\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s so nice to be appreciated,\xe2\x80\x9d sighed Anne rapturously to Marilla\r\nthat night.\r\n\r\nThe girls were not the only scholars who \xe2\x80\x9cappreciated\xe2\x80\x9d her. When Anne\r\nwent to her seat after dinner hour--she had been told by Mr. Phillips to\r\nsit with the model Minnie Andrews--she found on her desk a big luscious\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cstrawberry apple.\xe2\x80\x9d Anne caught it up all ready to take a bite when she\r\nremembered that the only place in Avonlea where strawberry apples grew\r\nwas in the old Blythe orchard on the other side of the Lake of Shining\r\nWaters. Anne dropped the apple as if it were a red-hot coal and\r\nostentatiously wiped her fingers on her handkerchief. The apple lay\r\nuntouched on her desk until the next morning, when little Timothy\r\nAndrews, who swept the school and kindled the fire, annexed it as one\r\nof his perquisites. Charlie Sloane\xe2\x80\x99s slate pencil, gorgeously bedizened\r\nwith striped red and yellow paper, costing two cents where ordinary\r\npencils cost only one, which he sent up to her after dinner hour, met\r\nwith a more favorable reception. Anne was graciously pleased to accept\r\nit and rewarded the donor with a smile which exalted that infatuated\r\nyouth straightway into the seventh heaven of delight and caused him to\r\nmake such fearful errors in his dictation that Mr. Phillips kept him in\r\nafter school to rewrite it.\r\n\r\nBut as,\r\n\r\n  The Caesar\xe2\x80\x99s pageant shorn of Brutus\xe2\x80\x99 bust\r\n  Did but of Rome\xe2\x80\x99s best son remind her more,\r\n\r\nso the marked absence of any tribute or recognition from Diana Barry who\r\nwas sitting with Gertie Pye embittered Anne\xe2\x80\x99s little triumph.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDiana might just have smiled at me once, I think,\xe2\x80\x9d she mourned to\r\nMarilla that night. But the next morning a note most fearfully and\r\nwonderfully twisted and folded, and a small parcel were passed across to\r\nAnne.\r\n\r\nDear Anne (ran the former)\r\n\r\n\r\nMother says I\xe2\x80\x99m not to play with you or talk to you even in school. It\r\nisn\xe2\x80\x99t my fault and don\xe2\x80\x99t be cross at me, because I love you as much\r\nas ever. I miss you awfully to tell all my secrets to and I don\xe2\x80\x99t like\r\nGertie Pye one bit. I made you one of the new bookmarkers out of red\r\ntissue paper. They are awfully fashionable now and only three girls in\r\nschool know how to make them. When you look at it remember\r\n\r\nYour true friend\r\n\r\nDiana Barry.\r\n\r\n\r\nAnne read the note, kissed the bookmark, and dispatched a prompt reply\r\nback to the other side of the school.\r\n\r\n\r\nMy own darling Diana:--\r\n\r\nOf course I am not cross at you because you have to obey your mother.\r\nOur spirits can commune. I shall keep your lovely present forever.\r\nMinnie Andrews is a very nice little girl--although she has no\r\nimagination--but after having been Diana\xe2\x80\x99s busum friend I cannot be\r\nMinnie\xe2\x80\x99s. Please excuse mistakes because my spelling isn\xe2\x80\x99t very good\r\nyet, although much improoved.\r\n\r\nYours until death us do part\r\n\r\nAnne or Cordelia Shirley.\r\n\r\n\r\nP.S. I shall sleep with your letter under my pillow tonight. A. _or_ C.S.\r\n\r\n\r\nMarilla pessimistically expected more trouble since Anne had again begun\r\nto go to school. But none developed. Perhaps Anne caught something of\r\nthe \xe2\x80\x9cmodel\xe2\x80\x9d spirit from Minnie Andrews; at least she got on very well\r\nwith Mr. Phillips thenceforth. She flung herself into her studies heart\r\nand soul, determined not to be outdone in any class by Gilbert Blythe.\r\nThe rivalry between them was soon apparent; it was entirely good natured\r\non Gilbert\xe2\x80\x99s side; but it is much to be feared that the same thing\r\ncannot be said of Anne, who had certainly an unpraiseworthy tenacity for\r\nholding grudges. She was as intense in her hatreds as in her loves. She\r\nwould not stoop to admit that she meant to rival Gilbert in schoolwork,\r\nbecause that would have been to acknowledge his existence which Anne\r\npersistently ignored; but the rivalry was there and honors fluctuated\r\nbetween them. Now Gilbert was head of the spelling class; now Anne, with\r\na toss of her long red braids, spelled him down. One morning Gilbert had\r\nall his sums done correctly and had his name written on the blackboard\r\non the roll of honor; the next morning Anne, having wrestled wildly with\r\ndecimals the entire evening before, would be first. One awful day they\r\nwere ties and their names were written up together. It was almost as bad\r\nas a take-notice and Anne\xe2\x80\x99s mortification was as evident as Gilbert\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsatisfaction. When the written examinations at the end of each month\r\nwere held the suspense was terrible. The first month Gilbert came out\r\nthree marks ahead. The second Anne beat him by five. But her triumph was\r\nmarred by the fact that Gilbert congratulated her heartily before the\r\nwhole school. It would have been ever so much sweeter to her if he had\r\nfelt the sting of his defeat.\r\n\r\nMr. Phillips might not be a very good teacher; but a pupil so inflexibly\r\ndetermined on learning as Anne was could hardly escape making progress\r\nunder any kind of teacher. By the end of the term Anne and Gilbert were\r\nboth promoted into the fifth class and allowed to begin studying the\r\nelements of \xe2\x80\x9cthe branches\xe2\x80\x9d--by which Latin, geometry, French, and\r\nalgebra were meant. In geometry Anne met her Waterloo.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s perfectly awful stuff, Marilla,\xe2\x80\x9d she groaned. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sure I\xe2\x80\x99ll never\r\nbe able to make head or tail of it. There is no scope for imagination in\r\nit at all. Mr. Phillips says I\xe2\x80\x99m the worst dunce he ever saw at it.\r\nAnd Gil--I mean some of the others are so smart at it. It is extremely\r\nmortifying, Marilla.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEven Diana gets along better than I do. But I don\xe2\x80\x99t mind being beaten\r\nby Diana. Even although we meet as strangers now I still love her with\r\nan _inextinguishable_ love. It makes me very sad at times to think about\r\nher. But really, Marilla, one can\xe2\x80\x99t stay sad very long in such an\r\ninteresting world, can one?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XVIII. Anne to the Rescue\r\n\r\n\r\n|ALL things great are wound up with all things little. At first glance\r\nit might not seem that the decision of a certain Canadian Premier to\r\ninclude Prince Edward Island in a political tour could have much or\r\nanything to do with the fortunes of little Anne Shirley at Green Gables.\r\nBut it had.\r\n\r\nIt was a January the Premier came, to address his loyal supporters and\r\nsuch of his nonsupporters as chose to be present at the monster mass\r\nmeeting held in Charlottetown. Most of the Avonlea people were on\r\nPremier\xe2\x80\x99s side of politics; hence on the night of the meeting nearly\r\nall the men and a goodly proportion of the women had gone to town thirty\r\nmiles away. Mrs. Rachel Lynde had gone too. Mrs. Rachel Lynde was a\r\nred-hot politician and couldn\xe2\x80\x99t have believed that the political rally\r\ncould be carried through without her, although she was on the opposite\r\nside of politics. So she went to town and took her husband--Thomas would\r\nbe useful in looking after the horse--and Marilla Cuthbert with her.\r\nMarilla had a sneaking interest in politics herself, and as she thought\r\nit might be her only chance to see a real live Premier, she promptly\r\ntook it, leaving Anne and Matthew to keep house until her return the\r\nfollowing day.\r\n\r\nHence, while Marilla and Mrs. Rachel were enjoying themselves hugely\r\nat the mass meeting, Anne and Matthew had the cheerful kitchen at Green\r\nGables all to themselves. A bright fire was glowing in the old-fashioned\r\nWaterloo stove and blue-white frost crystals were shining on the\r\nwindowpanes. Matthew nodded over a _Farmers\xe2\x80\x99 Advocate_ on the sofa and\r\nAnne at the table studied her lessons with grim determination, despite\r\nsundry wistful glances at the clock shelf, where lay a new book that\r\nJane Andrews had lent her that day. Jane had assured her that it was\r\nwarranted to produce any number of thrills, or words to that effect, and\r\nAnne\xe2\x80\x99s fingers tingled to reach out for it. But that would mean Gilbert\r\nBlythe\xe2\x80\x99s triumph on the morrow. Anne turned her back on the clock shelf\r\nand tried to imagine it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t there.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMatthew, did you ever study geometry when you went to school?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell now, no, I didn\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d said Matthew, coming out of his doze with a\r\nstart.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish you had,\xe2\x80\x9d sighed Anne, \xe2\x80\x9cbecause then you\xe2\x80\x99d be able to sympathize\r\nwith me. You can\xe2\x80\x99t sympathize properly if you\xe2\x80\x99ve never studied it. It is\r\ncasting a cloud over my whole life. I\xe2\x80\x99m such a dunce at it, Matthew.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell now, I dunno,\xe2\x80\x9d said Matthew soothingly. \xe2\x80\x9cI guess you\xe2\x80\x99re all right\r\nat anything. Mr. Phillips told me last week in Blair\xe2\x80\x99s store at Carmody\r\nthat you was the smartest scholar in school and was making rapid\r\nprogress. \xe2\x80\x98Rapid progress\xe2\x80\x99 was his very words. There\xe2\x80\x99s them as runs down\r\nTeddy Phillips and says he ain\xe2\x80\x99t much of a teacher, but I guess he\xe2\x80\x99s all\r\nright.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMatthew would have thought anyone who praised Anne was \xe2\x80\x9call right.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sure I\xe2\x80\x99d get on better with geometry if only he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t change\r\nthe letters,\xe2\x80\x9d complained Anne. \xe2\x80\x9cI learn the proposition off by heart and\r\nthen he draws it on the blackboard and puts different letters from what\r\nare in the book and I get all mixed up. I don\xe2\x80\x99t think a teacher should\r\ntake such a mean advantage, do you? We\xe2\x80\x99re studying agriculture now and\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ve found out at last what makes the roads red. It\xe2\x80\x99s a great comfort.\r\nI wonder how Marilla and Mrs. Lynde are enjoying themselves. Mrs. Lynde\r\nsays Canada is going to the dogs the way things are being run at Ottawa\r\nand that it\xe2\x80\x99s an awful warning to the electors. She says if women were\r\nallowed to vote we would soon see a blessed change. What way do you\r\nvote, Matthew?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cConservative,\xe2\x80\x9d said Matthew promptly. To vote Conservative was part of\r\nMatthew\xe2\x80\x99s religion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I\xe2\x80\x99m Conservative too,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne decidedly. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m glad because\r\nGil--because some of the boys in school are Grits. I guess Mr. Phillips\r\nis a Grit too because Prissy Andrews\xe2\x80\x99s father is one, and Ruby Gillis\r\nsays that when a man is courting he always has to agree with the girl\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmother in religion and her father in politics. Is that true, Matthew?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell now, I dunno,\xe2\x80\x9d said Matthew.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you ever go courting, Matthew?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell now, no, I dunno\xe2\x80\x99s I ever did,\xe2\x80\x9d said Matthew, who had certainly\r\nnever thought of such a thing in his whole existence.\r\n\r\nAnne reflected with her chin in her hands.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt must be rather interesting, don\xe2\x80\x99t you think, Matthew? Ruby Gillis\r\nsays when she grows up she\xe2\x80\x99s going to have ever so many beaus on the\r\nstring and have them all crazy about her; but I think that would be too\r\nexciting. I\xe2\x80\x99d rather have just one in his right mind. But Ruby Gillis\r\nknows a great deal about such matters because she has so many big\r\nsisters, and Mrs. Lynde says the Gillis girls have gone off like hot\r\ncakes. Mr. Phillips goes up to see Prissy Andrews nearly every evening.\r\nHe says it is to help her with her lessons but Miranda Sloane is\r\nstudying for Queen\xe2\x80\x99s too, and I should think she needed help a lot more\r\nthan Prissy because she\xe2\x80\x99s ever so much stupider, but he never goes to\r\nhelp her in the evenings at all. There are a great many things in this\r\nworld that I can\xe2\x80\x99t understand very well, Matthew.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell now, I dunno as I comprehend them all myself,\xe2\x80\x9d acknowledged\r\nMatthew.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I suppose I must finish up my lessons. I won\xe2\x80\x99t allow myself to\r\nopen that new book Jane lent me until I\xe2\x80\x99m through. But it\xe2\x80\x99s a terrible\r\ntemptation, Matthew. Even when I turn my back on it I can see it there\r\njust as plain. Jane said she cried herself sick over it. I love a book\r\nthat makes me cry. But I think I\xe2\x80\x99ll carry that book into the sitting\r\nroom and lock it in the jam closet and give you the key. And you must\r\n_not_ give it to me, Matthew, until my lessons are done, not even if\r\nI implore you on my bended knees. It\xe2\x80\x99s all very well to say resist\r\ntemptation, but it\xe2\x80\x99s ever so much easier to resist it if you can\xe2\x80\x99t get\r\nthe key. And then shall I run down the cellar and get some russets,\r\nMatthew? Wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t you like some russets?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell now, I dunno but what I would,\xe2\x80\x9d said Matthew, who never ate\r\nrussets but knew Anne\xe2\x80\x99s weakness for them.\r\n\r\nJust as Anne emerged triumphantly from the cellar with her plateful of\r\nrussets came the sound of flying footsteps on the icy board walk outside\r\nand the next moment the kitchen door was flung open and in rushed Diana\r\nBarry, white faced and breathless, with a shawl wrapped hastily around\r\nher head. Anne promptly let go of her candle and plate in her surprise,\r\nand plate, candle, and apples crashed together down the cellar ladder\r\nand were found at the bottom embedded in melted grease, the next day,\r\nby Marilla, who gathered them up and thanked mercy the house hadn\xe2\x80\x99t been\r\nset on fire.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhatever is the matter, Diana?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Anne. \xe2\x80\x9cHas your mother relented\r\nat last?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Anne, do come quick,\xe2\x80\x9d implored Diana nervously. \xe2\x80\x9cMinnie May is\r\nawful sick--she\xe2\x80\x99s got croup. Young Mary Joe says--and Father and Mother\r\nare away to town and there\xe2\x80\x99s nobody to go for the doctor. Minnie May is\r\nawful bad and Young Mary Joe doesn\xe2\x80\x99t know what to do--and oh, Anne, I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\nso scared!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMatthew, without a word, reached out for cap and coat, slipped past\r\nDiana and away into the darkness of the yard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s gone to harness the sorrel mare to go to Carmody for the doctor,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said Anne, who was hurrying on hood and jacket. \xe2\x80\x9cI know it as well as\r\nif he\xe2\x80\x99d said so. Matthew and I are such kindred spirits I can read his\r\nthoughts without words at all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t believe he\xe2\x80\x99ll find the doctor at Carmody,\xe2\x80\x9d sobbed Diana. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nknow that Dr. Blair went to town and I guess Dr. Spencer would go too.\r\nYoung Mary Joe never saw anybody with croup and Mrs. Lynde is away. Oh,\r\nAnne!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t cry, Di,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne cheerily. \xe2\x80\x9cI know exactly what to do for\r\ncroup. You forget that Mrs. Hammond had twins three times. When you look\r\nafter three pairs of twins you naturally get a lot of experience. They\r\nall had croup regularly. Just wait till I get the ipecac bottle--you\r\nmayn\xe2\x80\x99t have any at your house. Come on now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe two little girls hastened out hand in hand and hurried through\r\nLover\xe2\x80\x99s Lane and across the crusted field beyond, for the snow was too\r\ndeep to go by the shorter wood way. Anne, although sincerely sorry\r\nfor Minnie May, was far from being insensible to the romance of the\r\nsituation and to the sweetness of once more sharing that romance with a\r\nkindred spirit.\r\n\r\nThe night was clear and frosty, all ebony of shadow and silver of snowy\r\nslope; big stars were shining over the silent fields; here and there the\r\ndark pointed firs stood up with snow powdering their branches and the\r\nwind whistling through them. Anne thought it was truly delightful to go\r\nskimming through all this mystery and loveliness with your bosom friend\r\nwho had been so long estranged.\r\n\r\nMinnie May, aged three, was really very sick. She lay on the kitchen\r\nsofa feverish and restless, while her hoarse breathing could be heard\r\nall over the house. Young Mary Joe, a buxom, broad-faced French girl\r\nfrom the creek, whom Mrs. Barry had engaged to stay with the children\r\nduring her absence, was helpless and bewildered, quite incapable of\r\nthinking what to do, or doing it if she thought of it.\r\n\r\nAnne went to work with skill and promptness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMinnie May has croup all right; she\xe2\x80\x99s pretty bad, but I\xe2\x80\x99ve seen them\r\nworse. First we must have lots of hot water. I declare, Diana, there\r\nisn\xe2\x80\x99t more than a cupful in the kettle! There, I\xe2\x80\x99ve filled it up, and,\r\nMary Joe, you may put some wood in the stove. I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to hurt your\r\nfeelings but it seems to me you might have thought of this before if\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99d any imagination. Now, I\xe2\x80\x99ll undress Minnie May and put her to bed\r\nand you try to find some soft flannel cloths, Diana. I\xe2\x80\x99m going to give\r\nher a dose of ipecac first of all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMinnie May did not take kindly to the ipecac but Anne had not brought up\r\nthree pairs of twins for nothing. Down that ipecac went, not only once,\r\nbut many times during the long, anxious night when the two little girls\r\nworked patiently over the suffering Minnie May, and Young Mary Joe,\r\nhonestly anxious to do all she could, kept up a roaring fire and heated\r\nmore water than would have been needed for a hospital of croupy babies.\r\n\r\nIt was three o\xe2\x80\x99clock when Matthew came with a doctor, for he had been\r\nobliged to go all the way to Spencervale for one. But the pressing need\r\nfor assistance was past. Minnie May was much better and was sleeping\r\nsoundly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was awfully near giving up in despair,\xe2\x80\x9d explained Anne. \xe2\x80\x9cShe got\r\nworse and worse until she was sicker than ever the Hammond twins were,\r\neven the last pair. I actually thought she was going to choke to death.\r\nI gave her every drop of ipecac in that bottle and when the last dose\r\nwent down I said to myself--not to Diana or Young Mary Joe, because I\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t want to worry them any more than they were worried, but I had\r\nto say it to myself just to relieve my feelings--\xe2\x80\x98This is the last\r\nlingering hope and I fear, tis a vain one.\xe2\x80\x99 But in about three minutes\r\nshe coughed up the phlegm and began to get better right away. You must\r\njust imagine my relief, doctor, because I can\xe2\x80\x99t express it in words. You\r\nknow there are some things that cannot be expressed in words.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I know,\xe2\x80\x9d nodded the doctor. He looked at Anne as if he were\r\nthinking some things about her that couldn\xe2\x80\x99t be expressed in words.\r\nLater on, however, he expressed them to Mr. and Mrs. Barry.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat little redheaded girl they have over at Cuthbert\xe2\x80\x99s is as smart as\r\nthey make \xe2\x80\x98em. I tell you she saved that baby\xe2\x80\x99s life, for it would have\r\nbeen too late by the time I got there. She seems to have a skill and\r\npresence of mind perfectly wonderful in a child of her age. I never saw\r\nanything like the eyes of her when she was explaining the case to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne had gone home in the wonderful, white-frosted winter morning, heavy\r\neyed from loss of sleep, but still talking unweariedly to Matthew as\r\nthey crossed the long white field and walked under the glittering fairy\r\narch of the Lover\xe2\x80\x99s Lane maples.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Matthew, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it a wonderful morning? The world looks like\r\nsomething God had just imagined for His own pleasure, doesn\xe2\x80\x99t it? Those\r\ntrees look as if I could blow them away with a breath--pouf! I\xe2\x80\x99m so glad\r\nI live in a world where there are white frosts, aren\xe2\x80\x99t you? And I\xe2\x80\x99m so\r\nglad Mrs. Hammond had three pairs of twins after all. If she hadn\xe2\x80\x99t I\r\nmightn\xe2\x80\x99t have known what to do for Minnie May. I\xe2\x80\x99m real sorry I was\r\never cross with Mrs. Hammond for having twins. But, oh, Matthew, I\xe2\x80\x99m so\r\nsleepy. I can\xe2\x80\x99t go to school. I just know I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t keep my eyes open\r\nand I\xe2\x80\x99d be so stupid. But I hate to stay home, for Gil--some of\r\nthe others will get head of the class, and it\xe2\x80\x99s so hard to get up\r\nagain--although of course the harder it is the more satisfaction you\r\nhave when you do get up, haven\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell now, I guess you\xe2\x80\x99ll manage all right,\xe2\x80\x9d said Matthew, looking at\r\nAnne\xe2\x80\x99s white little face and the dark shadows under her eyes. \xe2\x80\x9cYou just\r\ngo right to bed and have a good sleep. I\xe2\x80\x99ll do all the chores.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne accordingly went to bed and slept so long and soundly that it\r\nwas well on in the white and rosy winter afternoon when she awoke and\r\ndescended to the kitchen where Marilla, who had arrived home in the\r\nmeantime, was sitting knitting.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, did you see the Premier?\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Anne at once. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat did he look\r\nlike Marilla?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, he never got to be Premier on account of his looks,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMarilla. \xe2\x80\x9cSuch a nose as that man had! But he can speak. I was proud of\r\nbeing a Conservative. Rachel Lynde, of course, being a Liberal, had no\r\nuse for him. Your dinner is in the oven, Anne, and you can get yourself\r\nsome blue plum preserve out of the pantry. I guess you\xe2\x80\x99re hungry.\r\nMatthew has been telling me about last night. I must say it was\r\nfortunate you knew what to do. I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have had any idea myself, for\r\nI never saw a case of croup. There now, never mind talking till you\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nhad your dinner. I can tell by the look of you that you\xe2\x80\x99re just full up\r\nwith speeches, but they\xe2\x80\x99ll keep.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarilla had something to tell Anne, but she did not tell it just then\r\nfor she knew if she did Anne\xe2\x80\x99s consequent excitement would lift her\r\nclear out of the region of such material matters as appetite or dinner.\r\nNot until Anne had finished her saucer of blue plums did Marilla say:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Barry was here this afternoon, Anne. She wanted to see you, but I\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t wake you up. She says you saved Minnie May\xe2\x80\x99s life, and she is\r\nvery sorry she acted as she did in that affair of the currant wine. She\r\nsays she knows now you didn\xe2\x80\x99t mean to set Diana drunk, and she hopes\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ll forgive her and be good friends with Diana again. You\xe2\x80\x99re to go\r\nover this evening if you like for Diana can\xe2\x80\x99t stir outside the door\r\non account of a bad cold she caught last night. Now, Anne Shirley, for\r\npity\xe2\x80\x99s sake don\xe2\x80\x99t fly up into the air.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe warning seemed not unnecessary, so uplifted and aerial was Anne\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nexpression and attitude as she sprang to her feet, her face irradiated\r\nwith the flame of her spirit.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Marilla, can I go right now--without washing my dishes? I\xe2\x80\x99ll wash\r\nthem when I come back, but I cannot tie myself down to anything so\r\nunromantic as dishwashing at this thrilling moment.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, yes, run along,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla indulgently. \xe2\x80\x9cAnne Shirley--are you\r\ncrazy? Come back this instant and put something on you. I might as well\r\ncall to the wind. She\xe2\x80\x99s gone without a cap or wrap. Look at her tearing\r\nthrough the orchard with her hair streaming. It\xe2\x80\x99ll be a mercy if she\r\ndoesn\xe2\x80\x99t catch her death of cold.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne came dancing home in the purple winter twilight across the snowy\r\nplaces. Afar in the southwest was the great shimmering, pearl-like\r\nsparkle of an evening star in a sky that was pale golden and ethereal\r\nrose over gleaming white spaces and dark glens of spruce. The tinkles\r\nof sleigh bells among the snowy hills came like elfin chimes through\r\nthe frosty air, but their music was not sweeter than the song in Anne\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nheart and on her lips.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou see before you a perfectly happy person, Marilla,\xe2\x80\x9d she announced.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m perfectly happy--yes, in spite of my red hair. Just at present I\r\nhave a soul above red hair. Mrs. Barry kissed me and cried and said she\r\nwas so sorry and she could never repay me. I felt fearfully embarrassed,\r\nMarilla, but I just said as politely as I could, \xe2\x80\x98I have no hard\r\nfeelings for you, Mrs. Barry. I assure you once for all that I did not\r\nmean to intoxicate Diana and henceforth I shall cover the past with the\r\nmantle of oblivion.\xe2\x80\x99 That was a pretty dignified way of speaking wasn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nit, Marilla?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI felt that I was heaping coals of fire on Mrs. Barry\xe2\x80\x99s head. And Diana\r\nand I had a lovely afternoon. Diana showed me a new fancy crochet stitch\r\nher aunt over at Carmody taught her. Not a soul in Avonlea knows it but\r\nus, and we pledged a solemn vow never to reveal it to anyone else. Diana\r\ngave me a beautiful card with a wreath of roses on it and a verse of\r\npoetry:\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cIf you love me as I love you\r\n      Nothing but death can part us two.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd that is true, Marilla. We\xe2\x80\x99re going to ask Mr. Phillips to let us\r\nsit together in school again, and Gertie Pye can go with Minnie Andrews.\r\nWe had an elegant tea. Mrs. Barry had the very best china set out,\r\nMarilla, just as if I was real company. I can\xe2\x80\x99t tell you what a thrill\r\nit gave me. Nobody ever used their very best china on my account before.\r\nAnd we had fruit cake and pound cake and doughnuts and two kinds of\r\npreserves, Marilla. And Mrs. Barry asked me if I took tea and said \xe2\x80\x98Pa,\r\nwhy don\xe2\x80\x99t you pass the biscuits to Anne?\xe2\x80\x99 It must be lovely to be grown\r\nup, Marilla, when just being treated as if you were is so nice.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know about that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla, with a brief sigh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, anyway, when I am grown up,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne decidedly, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m always\r\ngoing to talk to little girls as if they were too, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll never laugh\r\nwhen they use big words. I know from sorrowful experience how that hurts\r\none\xe2\x80\x99s feelings. After tea Diana and I made taffy. The taffy wasn\xe2\x80\x99t very\r\ngood, I suppose because neither Diana nor I had ever made any before.\r\nDiana left me to stir it while she buttered the plates and I forgot and\r\nlet it burn; and then when we set it out on the platform to cool the cat\r\nwalked over one plate and that had to be thrown away. But the making of\r\nit was splendid fun. Then when I came home Mrs. Barry asked me to come\r\nover as often as I could and Diana stood at the window and threw kisses\r\nto me all the way down to Lover\xe2\x80\x99s Lane. I assure you, Marilla, that I\r\nfeel like praying tonight and I\xe2\x80\x99m going to think out a special brand-new\r\nprayer in honor of the occasion.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XIX. A Concert a Catastrophe and a Confession\r\n\r\n\r\n|MARILLA, can I go over to see Diana just for a minute?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Anne,\r\nrunning breathlessly down from the east gable one February evening.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t see what you want to be traipsing about after dark for,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMarilla shortly. \xe2\x80\x9cYou and Diana walked home from school together and\r\nthen stood down there in the snow for half an hour more, your tongues\r\ngoing the whole blessed time, clickety-clack. So I don\xe2\x80\x99t think you\xe2\x80\x99re\r\nvery badly off to see her again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut she wants to see me,\xe2\x80\x9d pleaded Anne. \xe2\x80\x9cShe has something very\r\nimportant to tell me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do you know she has?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause she just signaled to me from her window. We have arranged a\r\nway to signal with our candles and cardboard. We set the candle on the\r\nwindow sill and make flashes by passing the cardboard back and forth. So\r\nmany flashes mean a certain thing. It was my idea, Marilla.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll warrant you it was,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla emphatically. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd the next\r\nthing you\xe2\x80\x99ll be setting fire to the curtains with your signaling\r\nnonsense.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, we\xe2\x80\x99re very careful, Marilla. And it\xe2\x80\x99s so interesting. Two flashes\r\nmean, \xe2\x80\x98Are you there?\xe2\x80\x99 Three mean \xe2\x80\x98yes\xe2\x80\x99 and four \xe2\x80\x98no.\xe2\x80\x99 Five mean, \xe2\x80\x98Come\r\nover as soon as possible, because I have something important to reveal.\xe2\x80\x99\r\nDiana has just signaled five flashes, and I\xe2\x80\x99m really suffering to know\r\nwhat it is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you needn\xe2\x80\x99t suffer any longer,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla sarcastically. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\ncan go, but you\xe2\x80\x99re to be back here in just ten minutes, remember that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne did remember it and was back in the stipulated time, although\r\nprobably no mortal will ever know just what it cost her to confine the\r\ndiscussion of Diana\xe2\x80\x99s important communication within the limits of ten\r\nminutes. But at least she had made good use of them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Marilla, what do you think? You know tomorrow is Diana\xe2\x80\x99s birthday.\r\nWell, her mother told her she could ask me to go home with her from\r\nschool and stay all night with her. And her cousins are coming over from\r\nNewbridge in a big pung sleigh to go to the Debating Club concert at\r\nthe hall tomorrow night. And they are going to take Diana and me to the\r\nconcert--if you\xe2\x80\x99ll let me go, that is. You will, won\xe2\x80\x99t you, Marilla? Oh,\r\nI feel so excited.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can calm down then, because you\xe2\x80\x99re not going. You\xe2\x80\x99re better at home\r\nin your own bed, and as for that club concert, it\xe2\x80\x99s all nonsense, and\r\nlittle girls should not be allowed to go out to such places at all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sure the Debating Club is a most respectable affair,\xe2\x80\x9d pleaded Anne.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m not saying it isn\xe2\x80\x99t. But you\xe2\x80\x99re not going to begin gadding about\r\nto concerts and staying out all hours of the night. Pretty doings for\r\nchildren. I\xe2\x80\x99m surprised at Mrs. Barry\xe2\x80\x99s letting Diana go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut it\xe2\x80\x99s such a very special occasion,\xe2\x80\x9d mourned Anne, on the verge of\r\ntears. \xe2\x80\x9cDiana has only one birthday in a year. It isn\xe2\x80\x99t as if birthdays\r\nwere common things, Marilla. Prissy Andrews is going to recite \xe2\x80\x98Curfew\r\nMust Not Ring Tonight.\xe2\x80\x99 That is such a good moral piece, Marilla, I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\nsure it would do me lots of good to hear it. And the choir are going to\r\nsing four lovely pathetic songs that are pretty near as good as hymns.\r\nAnd oh, Marilla, the minister is going to take part; yes, indeed, he is;\r\nhe\xe2\x80\x99s going to give an address. That will be just about the same thing as\r\na sermon. Please, mayn\xe2\x80\x99t I go, Marilla?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou heard what I said, Anne, didn\xe2\x80\x99t you? Take off your boots now and go\r\nto bed. It\xe2\x80\x99s past eight.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s just one more thing, Marilla,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne, with the air of\r\nproducing the last shot in her locker. \xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Barry told Diana that we\r\nmight sleep in the spare-room bed. Think of the honor of your little\r\nAnne being put in the spare-room bed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s an honor you\xe2\x80\x99ll have to get along without. Go to bed, Anne, and\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t let me hear another word out of you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen Anne, with tears rolling over her cheeks, had gone sorrowfully\r\nupstairs, Matthew, who had been apparently sound asleep on the lounge\r\nduring the whole dialogue, opened his eyes and said decidedly:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell now, Marilla, I think you ought to let Anne go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t then,\xe2\x80\x9d retorted Marilla. \xe2\x80\x9cWho\xe2\x80\x99s bringing this child up,\r\nMatthew, you or me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell now, you,\xe2\x80\x9d admitted Matthew.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t interfere then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell now, I ain\xe2\x80\x99t interfering. It ain\xe2\x80\x99t interfering to have your own\r\nopinion. And my opinion is that you ought to let Anne go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99d think I ought to let Anne go to the moon if she took the notion,\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ve no doubt\xe2\x80\x9d was Marilla\xe2\x80\x99s amiable rejoinder. \xe2\x80\x9cI might have let her\r\nspend the night with Diana, if that was all. But I don\xe2\x80\x99t approve of this\r\nconcert plan. She\xe2\x80\x99d go there and catch cold like as not, and have her\r\nhead filled up with nonsense and excitement. It would unsettle her for\r\na week. I understand that child\xe2\x80\x99s disposition and what\xe2\x80\x99s good for it\r\nbetter than you, Matthew.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think you ought to let Anne go,\xe2\x80\x9d repeated Matthew firmly. Argument\r\nwas not his strong point, but holding fast to his opinion certainly was.\r\nMarilla gave a gasp of helplessness and took refuge in silence. The\r\nnext morning, when Anne was washing the breakfast dishes in the pantry,\r\nMatthew paused on his way out to the barn to say to Marilla again:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think you ought to let Anne go, Marilla.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor a moment Marilla looked things not lawful to be uttered. Then she\r\nyielded to the inevitable and said tartly:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well, she can go, since nothing else \xe2\x80\x98ll please you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne flew out of the pantry, dripping dishcloth in hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Marilla, Marilla, say those blessed words again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI guess once is enough to say them. This is Matthew\xe2\x80\x99s doings and I\r\nwash my hands of it. If you catch pneumonia sleeping in a strange bed or\r\ncoming out of that hot hall in the middle of the night, don\xe2\x80\x99t blame me,\r\nblame Matthew. Anne Shirley, you\xe2\x80\x99re dripping greasy water all over the\r\nfloor. I never saw such a careless child.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I know I\xe2\x80\x99m a great trial to you, Marilla,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne repentantly.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI make so many mistakes. But then just think of all the mistakes I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t make, although I might. I\xe2\x80\x99ll get some sand and scrub up the spots\r\nbefore I go to school. Oh, Marilla, my heart was just set on going to\r\nthat concert. I never was to a concert in my life, and when the other\r\ngirls talk about them in school I feel so out of it. You didn\xe2\x80\x99t know\r\njust how I felt about it, but you see Matthew did. Matthew understands\r\nme, and it\xe2\x80\x99s so nice to be understood, Marilla.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne was too excited to do herself justice as to lessons that morning in\r\nschool. Gilbert Blythe spelled her down in class and left her clear out\r\nof sight in mental arithmetic. Anne\xe2\x80\x99s consequent humiliation was\r\nless than it might have been, however, in view of the concert and the\r\nspare-room bed. She and Diana talked so constantly about it all day that\r\nwith a stricter teacher than Mr. Phillips dire disgrace must inevitably\r\nhave been their portion.\r\n\r\nAnne felt that she could not have borne it if she had not been going\r\nto the concert, for nothing else was discussed that day in school. The\r\nAvonlea Debating Club, which met fortnightly all winter, had had several\r\nsmaller free entertainments; but this was to be a big affair, admission\r\nten cents, in aid of the library. The Avonlea young people had been\r\npracticing for weeks, and all the scholars were especially interested in\r\nit by reason of older brothers and sisters who were going to take part.\r\nEverybody in school over nine years of age expected to go, except Carrie\r\nSloane, whose father shared Marilla\xe2\x80\x99s opinions about small girls going\r\nout to night concerts. Carrie Sloane cried into her grammar all the\r\nafternoon and felt that life was not worth living.\r\n\r\nFor Anne the real excitement began with the dismissal of school and\r\nincreased therefrom in crescendo until it reached to a crash of positive\r\necstasy in the concert itself. They had a \xe2\x80\x9cperfectly elegant tea;\xe2\x80\x9d and\r\nthen came the delicious occupation of dressing in Diana\xe2\x80\x99s little room\r\nupstairs. Diana did Anne\xe2\x80\x99s front hair in the new pompadour style and\r\nAnne tied Diana\xe2\x80\x99s bows with the especial knack she possessed; and they\r\nexperimented with at least half a dozen different ways of arranging\r\ntheir back hair. At last they were ready, cheeks scarlet and eyes\r\nglowing with excitement.\r\n\r\nTrue, Anne could not help a little pang when she contrasted her plain\r\nblack tam and shapeless, tight-sleeved, homemade gray-cloth coat with\r\nDiana\xe2\x80\x99s jaunty fur cap and smart little jacket. But she remembered in\r\ntime that she had an imagination and could use it.\r\n\r\nThen Diana\xe2\x80\x99s cousins, the Murrays from Newbridge, came; they all crowded\r\ninto the big pung sleigh, among straw and furry robes. Anne reveled in\r\nthe drive to the hall, slipping along over the satin-smooth roads with\r\nthe snow crisping under the runners. There was a magnificent sunset, and\r\nthe snowy hills and deep-blue water of the St. Lawrence Gulf seemed to\r\nrim in the splendor like a huge bowl of pearl and sapphire brimmed with\r\nwine and fire. Tinkles of sleigh bells and distant laughter, that seemed\r\nlike the mirth of wood elves, came from every quarter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Diana,\xe2\x80\x9d breathed Anne, squeezing Diana\xe2\x80\x99s mittened hand under the\r\nfur robe, \xe2\x80\x9cisn\xe2\x80\x99t it all like a beautiful dream? Do I really look the\r\nsame as usual? I feel so different that it seems to me it must show in\r\nmy looks.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou look awfully nice,\xe2\x80\x9d said Diana, who having just received a\r\ncompliment from one of her cousins, felt that she ought to pass it on.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve got the loveliest color.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe program that night was a series of \xe2\x80\x9cthrills\xe2\x80\x9d for at least one\r\nlistener in the audience, and, as Anne assured Diana, every succeeding\r\nthrill was thrillier than the last. When Prissy Andrews, attired in\r\na new pink-silk waist with a string of pearls about her smooth white\r\nthroat and real carnations in her hair--rumor whispered that the master\r\nhad sent all the way to town for them for her--\xe2\x80\x9cclimbed the slimy\r\nladder, dark without one ray of light,\xe2\x80\x9d Anne shivered in luxurious\r\nsympathy; when the choir sang \xe2\x80\x9cFar Above the Gentle Daisies\xe2\x80\x9d Anne gazed\r\nat the ceiling as if it were frescoed with angels; when Sam Sloane\r\nproceeded to explain and illustrate \xe2\x80\x9cHow Sockery Set a Hen\xe2\x80\x9d Anne laughed\r\nuntil people sitting near her laughed too, more out of sympathy with her\r\nthan with amusement at a selection that was rather threadbare even in\r\nAvonlea; and when Mr. Phillips gave Mark Antony\xe2\x80\x99s oration over the\r\ndead body of Caesar in the most heart-stirring tones--looking at Prissy\r\nAndrews at the end of every sentence--Anne felt that she could rise and\r\nmutiny on the spot if but one Roman citizen led the way.\r\n\r\nOnly one number on the program failed to interest her. When Gilbert\r\nBlythe recited \xe2\x80\x9cBingen on the Rhine\xe2\x80\x9d Anne picked up Rhoda Murray\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nlibrary book and read it until he had finished, when she sat rigidly\r\nstiff and motionless while Diana clapped her hands until they tingled.\r\n\r\nIt was eleven when they got home, sated with dissipation, but with the\r\nexceeding sweet pleasure of talking it all over still to come. Everybody\r\nseemed asleep and the house was dark and silent. Anne and Diana tiptoed\r\ninto the parlor, a long narrow room out of which the spare room opened.\r\nIt was pleasantly warm and dimly lighted by the embers of a fire in the\r\ngrate.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s undress here,\xe2\x80\x9d said Diana. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s so nice and warm.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHasn\xe2\x80\x99t it been a delightful time?\xe2\x80\x9d sighed Anne rapturously. \xe2\x80\x9cIt must\r\nbe splendid to get up and recite there. Do you suppose we will ever be\r\nasked to do it, Diana?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, of course, someday. They\xe2\x80\x99re always wanting the big scholars to\r\nrecite. Gilbert Blythe does often and he\xe2\x80\x99s only two years older than us.\r\nOh, Anne, how could you pretend not to listen to him? When he came to\r\nthe line,\r\n\r\n           \xe2\x80\x98_There\xe2\x80\x99s Another_, not _a sister_,\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nhe looked right down at you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDiana,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne with dignity, \xe2\x80\x9cyou are my bosom friend, but I cannot\r\nallow even you to speak to me of that person. Are you ready for bed?\r\nLet\xe2\x80\x99s run a race and see who\xe2\x80\x99ll get to the bed first.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe suggestion appealed to Diana. The two little white-clad figures flew\r\ndown the long room, through the spare-room door, and bounded on the bed\r\nat the same moment. And then--something--moved beneath them, there was a\r\ngasp and a cry--and somebody said in muffled accents:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMerciful goodness!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne and Diana were never able to tell just how they got off that bed\r\nand out of the room. They only knew that after one frantic rush they\r\nfound themselves tiptoeing shiveringly upstairs.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, who was it--_what_ was it?\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Anne, her teeth chattering with\r\ncold and fright.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was Aunt Josephine,\xe2\x80\x9d said Diana, gasping with laughter. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, Anne,\r\nit was Aunt Josephine, however she came to be there. Oh, and I know she\r\nwill be furious. It\xe2\x80\x99s dreadful--it\xe2\x80\x99s really dreadful--but did you ever\r\nknow anything so funny, Anne?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho is your Aunt Josephine?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s father\xe2\x80\x99s aunt and she lives in Charlottetown. She\xe2\x80\x99s awfully\r\nold--seventy anyhow--and I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe she was _ever_ a little girl. We\r\nwere expecting her out for a visit, but not so soon. She\xe2\x80\x99s awfully prim\r\nand proper and she\xe2\x80\x99ll scold dreadfully about this, I know. Well, we\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nhave to sleep with Minnie May--and you can\xe2\x80\x99t think how she kicks.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Josephine Barry did not appear at the early breakfast the next\r\nmorning. Mrs. Barry smiled kindly at the two little girls.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you have a good time last night? I tried to stay awake until you\r\ncame home, for I wanted to tell you Aunt Josephine had come and that you\r\nwould have to go upstairs after all, but I was so tired I fell asleep. I\r\nhope you didn\xe2\x80\x99t disturb your aunt, Diana.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDiana preserved a discreet silence, but she and Anne exchanged furtive\r\nsmiles of guilty amusement across the table. Anne hurried home after\r\nbreakfast and so remained in blissful ignorance of the disturbance which\r\npresently resulted in the Barry household until the late afternoon, when\r\nshe went down to Mrs. Lynde\xe2\x80\x99s on an errand for Marilla.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo you and Diana nearly frightened poor old Miss Barry to death last\r\nnight?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Lynde severely, but with a twinkle in her eye. \xe2\x80\x9cMrs.\r\nBarry was here a few minutes ago on her way to Carmody. She\xe2\x80\x99s feeling\r\nreal worried over it. Old Miss Barry was in a terrible temper when she\r\ngot up this morning--and Josephine Barry\xe2\x80\x99s temper is no joke, I can tell\r\nyou that. She wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t speak to Diana at all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt wasn\xe2\x80\x99t Diana\xe2\x80\x99s fault,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne contritely. \xe2\x80\x9cIt was mine. I\r\nsuggested racing to see who would get into bed first.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI knew it!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Lynde, with the exultation of a correct guesser.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI knew that idea came out of your head. Well, it\xe2\x80\x99s made a nice lot of\r\ntrouble, that\xe2\x80\x99s what. Old Miss Barry came out to stay for a month, but\r\nshe declares she won\xe2\x80\x99t stay another day and is going right back to town\r\ntomorrow, Sunday and all as it is. She\xe2\x80\x99d have gone today if they could\r\nhave taken her. She had promised to pay for a quarter\xe2\x80\x99s music lessons\r\nfor Diana, but now she is determined to do nothing at all for such a\r\ntomboy. Oh, I guess they had a lively time of it there this morning. The\r\nBarrys must feel cut up. Old Miss Barry is rich and they\xe2\x80\x99d like to keep\r\non the good side of her. Of course, Mrs. Barry didn\xe2\x80\x99t say just that to\r\nme, but I\xe2\x80\x99m a pretty good judge of human nature, that\xe2\x80\x99s what.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m such an unlucky girl,\xe2\x80\x9d mourned Anne. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m always getting into\r\nscrapes myself and getting my best friends--people I\xe2\x80\x99d shed my heart\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nblood for--into them too. Can you tell me why it is so, Mrs. Lynde?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s because you\xe2\x80\x99re too heedless and impulsive, child, that\xe2\x80\x99s what. You\r\nnever stop to think--whatever comes into your head to say or do you say\r\nor do it without a moment\xe2\x80\x99s reflection.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, but that\xe2\x80\x99s the best of it,\xe2\x80\x9d protested Anne. \xe2\x80\x9cSomething just flashes\r\ninto your mind, so exciting, and you must out with it. If you stop to\r\nthink it over you spoil it all. Haven\xe2\x80\x99t you never felt that yourself,\r\nMrs. Lynde?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNo, Mrs. Lynde had not. She shook her head sagely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must learn to think a little, Anne, that\xe2\x80\x99s what. The proverb you\r\nneed to go by is \xe2\x80\x98Look before you leap\xe2\x80\x99--especially into spare-room\r\nbeds.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Lynde laughed comfortably over her mild joke, but Anne remained\r\npensive. She saw nothing to laugh at in the situation, which to her\r\neyes appeared very serious. When she left Mrs. Lynde\xe2\x80\x99s she took her way\r\nacross the crusted fields to Orchard Slope. Diana met her at the kitchen\r\ndoor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour Aunt Josephine was very cross about it, wasn\xe2\x80\x99t she?\xe2\x80\x9d whispered\r\nAnne.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Diana, stifling a giggle with an apprehensive glance\r\nover her shoulder at the closed sitting-room door. \xe2\x80\x9cShe was fairly\r\ndancing with rage, Anne. She says she won\xe2\x80\x99t stay and I\xe2\x80\x99m sure\r\nI don\xe2\x80\x99t care. But Father and Mother do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy didn\xe2\x80\x99t you tell them it was my fault?\xe2\x80\x9d demanded Anne.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s likely I\xe2\x80\x99d do such a thing, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d said Diana with just scorn.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m no telltale, Anne Shirley, and anyhow I was just as much to blame\r\nas you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I\xe2\x80\x99m going in to tell her myself,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne resolutely.\r\n\r\nDiana stared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnne Shirley, you\xe2\x80\x99d never! why--she\xe2\x80\x99ll eat you alive!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t frighten me any more than I am frightened,\xe2\x80\x9d implored Anne. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nrather walk up to a cannon\xe2\x80\x99s mouth.
