I\xe2\x80\x99ve had practice in confessing,\r\nfortunately.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, she\xe2\x80\x99s in the room,\xe2\x80\x9d said Diana. \xe2\x80\x9cYou can go in if you want to. I\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t dare. And I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe you\xe2\x80\x99ll do a bit of good.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWith this encouragement Anne bearded the lion in its den--that is to\r\nsay, walked resolutely up to the sitting-room door and knocked faintly.\r\nA sharp \xe2\x80\x9cCome in\xe2\x80\x9d followed.\r\n\r\nMiss Josephine Barry, thin, prim, and rigid, was knitting fiercely by\r\nthe fire, her wrath quite unappeased and her eyes snapping through her\r\ngold-rimmed glasses. She wheeled around in her chair, expecting to see\r\nDiana, and beheld a white-faced girl whose great eyes were brimmed up\r\nwith a mixture of desperate courage and shrinking terror.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho are you?\xe2\x80\x9d demanded Miss Josephine Barry, without ceremony.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m Anne of Green Gables,\xe2\x80\x9d said the small visitor tremulously, clasping\r\nher hands with her characteristic gesture, \xe2\x80\x9cand I\xe2\x80\x99ve come to confess, if\r\nyou please.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cConfess what?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat it was all my fault about jumping into bed on you last night. I\r\nsuggested it. Diana would never have thought of such a thing, I am sure.\r\nDiana is a very ladylike girl, Miss Barry. So you must see how unjust it\r\nis to blame her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I must, hey? I rather think Diana did her share of the jumping at\r\nleast. Such carryings on in a respectable house!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut we were only in fun,\xe2\x80\x9d persisted Anne. \xe2\x80\x9cI think you ought to forgive\r\nus, Miss Barry, now that we\xe2\x80\x99ve apologized. And anyhow, please forgive\r\nDiana and let her have her music lessons. Diana\xe2\x80\x99s heart is set on her\r\nmusic lessons, Miss Barry, and I know too well what it is to set your\r\nheart on a thing and not get it. If you must be cross with anyone, be\r\ncross with me. I\xe2\x80\x99ve been so used in my early days to having people cross\r\nat me that I can endure it much better than Diana can.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMuch of the snap had gone out of the old lady\xe2\x80\x99s eyes by this time\r\nand was replaced by a twinkle of amused interest. But she still said\r\nseverely:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think it is any excuse for you that you were only in fun.\r\nLittle girls never indulged in that kind of fun when I was young. You\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t know what it is to be awakened out of a sound sleep, after a long\r\nand arduous journey, by two great girls coming bounce down on you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t _know_, but I can _imagine_,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne eagerly. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sure it must\r\nhave been very disturbing. But then, there is our side of it too. Have\r\nyou any imagination, Miss Barry? If you have, just put yourself in\r\nour place. We didn\xe2\x80\x99t know there was anybody in that bed and you nearly\r\nscared us to death. It was simply awful the way we felt. And then we\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t sleep in the spare room after being promised. I suppose you are\r\nused to sleeping in spare rooms. But just imagine what you would feel\r\nlike if you were a little orphan girl who had never had such an honor.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAll the snap had gone by this time. Miss Barry actually laughed--a\r\nsound which caused Diana, waiting in speechless anxiety in the kitchen\r\noutside, to give a great gasp of relief.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m afraid my imagination is a little rusty--it\xe2\x80\x99s so long since I used\r\nit,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cI dare say your claim to sympathy is just as strong as\r\nmine. It all depends on the way we look at it. Sit down here and tell me\r\nabout yourself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am very sorry I can\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne firmly. \xe2\x80\x9cI would like to, because\r\nyou seem like an interesting lady, and you might even be a kindred\r\nspirit although you don\xe2\x80\x99t look very much like it. But it is my duty to\r\ngo home to Miss Marilla Cuthbert. Miss Marilla Cuthbert is a very kind\r\nlady who has taken me to bring up properly. She is doing her best, but\r\nit is very discouraging work. You must not blame her because I jumped on\r\nthe bed. But before I go I do wish you would tell me if you will forgive\r\nDiana and stay just as long as you meant to in Avonlea.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think perhaps I will if you will come over and talk to me\r\noccasionally,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Barry.\r\n\r\nThat evening Miss Barry gave Diana a silver bangle bracelet and told the\r\nsenior members of the household that she had unpacked her valise.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve made up my mind to stay simply for the sake of getting better\r\nacquainted with that Anne-girl,\xe2\x80\x9d she said frankly. \xe2\x80\x9cShe amuses me, and\r\nat my time of life an amusing person is a rarity.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarilla\xe2\x80\x99s only comment when she heard the story was, \xe2\x80\x9cI told you so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n This was for Matthew\xe2\x80\x99s benefit.\r\n\r\nMiss Barry stayed her month out and over. She was a more agreeable guest\r\nthan usual, for Anne kept her in good humor. They became firm friends.\r\n\r\nWhen Miss Barry went away she said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRemember, you Anne-girl, when you come to town you\xe2\x80\x99re to visit me and\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll put you in my very sparest spare-room bed to sleep.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMiss Barry was a kindred spirit, after all,\xe2\x80\x9d Anne confided to Marilla.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t think so to look at her, but she is. You don\xe2\x80\x99t find it\r\nright out at first, as in Matthew\xe2\x80\x99s case, but after a while you come\r\nto see it. Kindred spirits are not so scarce as I used to think. It\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsplendid to find out there are so many of them in the world.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XX. A Good Imagination Gone Wrong\r\n\r\n\r\n|SPRING had come once more to Green Gables--the beautiful capricious,\r\nreluctant Canadian spring, lingering along through April and May in a\r\nsuccession of sweet, fresh, chilly days, with pink sunsets and miracles\r\nof resurrection and growth. The maples in Lover\xe2\x80\x99s Lane were red budded\r\nand little curly ferns pushed up around the Dryad\xe2\x80\x99s Bubble. Away up in\r\nthe barrens, behind Mr. Silas Sloane\xe2\x80\x99s place, the Mayflowers blossomed\r\nout, pink and white stars of sweetness under their brown leaves. All the\r\nschool girls and boys had one golden afternoon gathering them, coming\r\nhome in the clear, echoing twilight with arms and baskets full of\r\nflowery spoil.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m so sorry for people who live in lands where there are no\r\nMayflowers,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne. \xe2\x80\x9cDiana says perhaps they have something better,\r\nbut there couldn\xe2\x80\x99t be anything better than Mayflowers, could there,\r\nMarilla? And Diana says if they don\xe2\x80\x99t know what they are like they don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nmiss them. But I think that is the saddest thing of all. I think it\r\nwould be _tragic_, Marilla, not to know what Mayflowers are like and _not_\r\nto miss them. Do you know what I think Mayflowers are, Marilla? I think\r\nthey must be the souls of the flowers that died last summer and this\r\nis their heaven. But we had a splendid time today, Marilla. We had our\r\nlunch down in a big mossy hollow by an old well--such a _romantic_ spot.\r\nCharlie Sloane dared Arty Gillis to jump over it, and Arty did because\r\nhe wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t take a dare. Nobody would in school. It is very _fashionable_\r\nto dare. Mr. Phillips gave all the Mayflowers he found to Prissy Andrews\r\nand I heard him to say \xe2\x80\x98sweets to the sweet.\xe2\x80\x99 He got that out of a\r\nbook, I know; but it shows he has some imagination. I was offered some\r\nMayflowers too, but I rejected them with scorn. I can\xe2\x80\x99t tell you the\r\nperson\xe2\x80\x99s name because I have vowed never to let it cross my lips. We\r\nmade wreaths of the Mayflowers and put them on our hats; and when the\r\ntime came to go home we marched in procession down the road, two by two,\r\nwith our bouquets and wreaths, singing \xe2\x80\x98My Home on the Hill.\xe2\x80\x99 Oh, it was\r\nso thrilling, Marilla. All Mr. Silas Sloane\xe2\x80\x99s folks rushed out to see us\r\nand everybody we met on the road stopped and stared after us. We made a\r\nreal sensation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot much wonder! Such silly doings!\xe2\x80\x9d was Marilla\xe2\x80\x99s response.\r\n\r\nAfter the Mayflowers came the violets, and Violet Vale was empurpled\r\nwith them. Anne walked through it on her way to school with reverent\r\nsteps and worshiping eyes, as if she trod on holy ground.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSomehow,\xe2\x80\x9d she told Diana, \xe2\x80\x9cwhen I\xe2\x80\x99m going through here I don\xe2\x80\x99t really\r\ncare whether Gil--whether anybody gets ahead of me in class or not. But\r\nwhen I\xe2\x80\x99m up in school it\xe2\x80\x99s all different and I care as much as ever.\r\nThere\xe2\x80\x99s such a lot of different Annes in me. I sometimes think that is\r\nwhy I\xe2\x80\x99m such a troublesome person. If I was just the one Anne it would\r\nbe ever so much more comfortable, but then it wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be half so\r\ninteresting.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOne June evening, when the orchards were pink blossomed again, when the\r\nfrogs were singing silverly sweet in the marshes about the head of the\r\nLake of Shining Waters, and the air was full of the savor of clover\r\nfields and balsamic fir woods, Anne was sitting by her gable window.\r\nShe had been studying her lessons, but it had grown too dark to see the\r\nbook, so she had fallen into wide-eyed reverie, looking out past the\r\nboughs of the Snow Queen, once more bestarred with its tufts of blossom.\r\n\r\nIn all essential respects the little gable chamber was unchanged. The\r\nwalls were as white, the pincushion as hard, the chairs as stiffly\r\nand yellowly upright as ever. Yet the whole character of the room was\r\naltered. It was full of a new vital, pulsing personality that seemed to\r\npervade it and to be quite independent of schoolgirl books and dresses\r\nand ribbons, and even of the cracked blue jug full of apple blossoms\r\non the table. It was as if all the dreams, sleeping and waking, of its\r\nvivid occupant had taken a visible although unmaterial form and had\r\ntapestried the bare room with splendid filmy tissues of rainbow and\r\nmoonshine. Presently Marilla came briskly in with some of Anne\xe2\x80\x99s freshly\r\nironed school aprons. She hung them over a chair and sat down with\r\na short sigh. She had had one of her headaches that afternoon, and\r\nalthough the pain had gone she felt weak and \xe2\x80\x9ctuckered out,\xe2\x80\x9d as she\r\nexpressed it. Anne looked at her with eyes limpid with sympathy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do truly wish I could have had the headache in your place, Marilla. I\r\nwould have endured it joyfully for your sake.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI guess you did your part in attending to the work and letting me\r\nrest,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla. \xe2\x80\x9cYou seem to have got on fairly well and made fewer\r\nmistakes than usual. Of course it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t exactly necessary to starch\r\nMatthew\xe2\x80\x99s handkerchiefs! And most people when they put a pie in the oven\r\nto warm up for dinner take it out and eat it when it gets hot instead of\r\nleaving it to be burned to a crisp. But that doesn\xe2\x80\x99t seem to be your way\r\nevidently.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHeadaches always left Marilla somewhat sarcastic.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I\xe2\x80\x99m so sorry,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne penitently. \xe2\x80\x9cI never thought about that\r\npie from the moment I put it in the oven till now, although I felt\r\n_instinctively_ that there was something missing on the dinner table. I\r\nwas firmly resolved, when you left me in charge this morning, not to\r\nimagine anything, but keep my thoughts on facts. I did pretty well until\r\nI put the pie in, and then an irresistible temptation came to me to\r\nimagine I was an enchanted princess shut up in a lonely tower with a\r\nhandsome knight riding to my rescue on a coal-black steed. So that\r\nis how I came to forget the pie. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know I starched the\r\nhandkerchiefs. All the time I was ironing I was trying to think of a\r\nname for a new island Diana and I have discovered up the brook. It\xe2\x80\x99s the\r\nmost ravishing spot, Marilla. There are two maple trees on it and the\r\nbrook flows right around it. At last it struck me that it would be\r\nsplendid to call it Victoria Island because we found it on the Queen\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nbirthday. Both Diana and I are very loyal. But I\xe2\x80\x99m sorry about that pie\r\nand the handkerchiefs. I wanted to be extra good today because it\xe2\x80\x99s an\r\nanniversary. Do you remember what happened this day last year, Marilla?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I can\xe2\x80\x99t think of anything special.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Marilla, it was the day I came to Green Gables. I shall never\r\nforget it. It was the turning point in my life. Of course it wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nseem so important to you. I\xe2\x80\x99ve been here for a year and I\xe2\x80\x99ve been so\r\nhappy. Of course, I\xe2\x80\x99ve had my troubles, but one can live down troubles.\r\nAre you sorry you kept me, Marilla?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I can\xe2\x80\x99t say I\xe2\x80\x99m sorry,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla, who sometimes wondered how\r\nshe could have lived before Anne came to Green Gables, \xe2\x80\x9cno, not exactly\r\nsorry. If you\xe2\x80\x99ve finished your lessons, Anne, I want you to run over and\r\nask Mrs. Barry if she\xe2\x80\x99ll lend me Diana\xe2\x80\x99s apron pattern.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh--it\xe2\x80\x99s--it\xe2\x80\x99s too dark,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Anne.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cToo dark? Why, it\xe2\x80\x99s only twilight. I\xe2\x80\x99d like to catch you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t go through the Haunted Wood, Marilla,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Anne desperately.\r\n\r\nMarilla stared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Haunted Wood! Are you crazy? What under the canopy is the Haunted\r\nWood?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe spruce wood over the brook,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne in a whisper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFiddlesticks! Who\r\nhas been telling you such stuff?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNobody,\xe2\x80\x9d confessed Anne. \xe2\x80\x9cDiana and I just imagined the wood was\r\nhaunted. All the places around here are so--so--_commonplace_. We just got\r\nthis up for our own amusement. We began it in April. A haunted wood is\r\nso very romantic, Marilla. We chose the spruce grove because it\xe2\x80\x99s so\r\ngloomy. Oh, we have imagined the most harrowing things. There\xe2\x80\x99s a white\r\nlady walks along the brook just about this time of the night and wrings\r\nher hands and utters wailing cries. She appears when there is to be a\r\ndeath in the family. And\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x99s a headless man stalks up and down the path and skeletons glower\r\nat you between the boughs. I\xe2\x80\x99d be sure that white things\r\nwould reach out from behind the trees and grab me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid ever anyone hear the like!\xe2\x80\x9d ejaculated Marilla, who had\r\nlistened in dumb amazement. \xe2\x80\x9cAnne Shirley, do you mean to tell me you\r\nbelieve all that wicked nonsense of your own imagination?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot believe _exactly_,\xe2\x80\x9d faltered Anne. \xe2\x80\x9cAt least, I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe it in\r\ndaylight. That is when ghosts\r\nwalk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere are no such things as ghosts, Anne.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, but there are, Marilla,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Anne eagerly. \xe2\x80\x9cI know people who\r\nhave seen them. And they are respectable people. Charlie Sloane says\r\nthat his grandmother saw his grandfather driving home the cows one night\r\nafter he\xe2\x80\x99d been buried for a year. You know Charlie Sloane\xe2\x80\x99s grandmother\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t tell a story for anything. She\xe2\x80\x99s a very religious woman. And\r\nMrs. Thomas\xe2\x80\x99s father was pursued home one night by a lamb of fire with\r\nits head cut off hanging by a strip of skin. I\xe2\x80\x99ve had my doubts about that imagination\r\nof yours right along, and if this is going to be the outcome of it, I\r\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t countenance any such doings. You\xe2\x80\x99ll go right over to Barry\xe2\x80\x99s, and\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ll go through that spruce grove, just for a lesson and a warning to\r\nyou. Her imagination had run away with her and she held the spruce\r\ngrove in mortal dread after nightfall. But Marilla was inexorable. She\r\nmarched the shrinking ghost-seer down to the spring and ordered her\r\nto proceed straightaway over the bridge and into the dusky retreats of\r\nwailing ladies and headless specters beyond.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Marilla, how can you be so cruel?\xe2\x80\x9d sobbed Anne. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat would you\r\nfeel like if a white thing did snatch me up and carry me off?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll risk it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla unfeelingly. \xe2\x80\x9cYou know I always mean what I\r\nsay. I\xe2\x80\x99ll cure you of imagining ghosts into places. March, now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne marched. That is, she stumbled over the bridge and went shuddering\r\nup the horrible dim path beyond. Anne never forgot that walk. Bitterly\r\ndid she repent the license she had given to her imagination. The goblins\r\nof her fancy lurked in every shadow about her, reaching out their cold,\r\nfleshless hands to grasp the terrified small girl who had called them\r\ninto being. A white strip of birch bark blowing up from the hollow over\r\nthe brown floor of the grove made her heart stand still. The long-drawn\r\nwail of two old boughs rubbing against each other brought out the\r\nperspiration in beads on her forehead. The swoop of bats in the darkness\r\nover her was as the wings of unearthly creatures. When she reached Mr.\r\nWilliam Bell\xe2\x80\x99s field she fled across it as if pursued by an army of\r\nwhite things, and arrived at the Barry kitchen door so out of breath\r\nthat she could hardly gasp out her request for the apron pattern.\r\nDiana was away so that she had no excuse to linger. The dreadful\r\nreturn journey had to be faced. Anne went back over it with shut eyes,\r\npreferring to take the risk of dashing her brains out among the boughs\r\nto that of seeing a white thing. When she finally stumbled over the log\r\nbridge she drew one long shivering breath of relief.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, so nothing caught you?\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla unsympathetically.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Mar--Marilla,\xe2\x80\x9d chattered Anne, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll b-b-be contt-tented with\r\nc-c-commonplace places after this.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXI. A New Departure in Flavorings\r\n\r\n\r\n|DEAR ME, there is nothing but meetings and partings in this world, as\r\nMrs. Lynde says,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked Anne plaintively, putting her slate and books\r\ndown on the kitchen table on the last day of June and wiping her red\r\neyes with a very damp handkerchief. \xe2\x80\x9cWasn\xe2\x80\x99t it fortunate, Marilla, that\r\nI took an extra handkerchief to school today? I had a presentiment that\r\nit would be needed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never thought you were so fond of Mr. Phillips that you\xe2\x80\x99d require two\r\nhandkerchiefs to dry your tears just because he was going away,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMarilla.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think I was crying because I was really so very fond of him,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n reflected Anne. \xe2\x80\x9cI just cried because all the others did. It was\r\nRuby Gillis started it. Ruby Gillis has always declared she hated Mr.\r\nPhillips, but just as soon as he got up to make his farewell speech she\r\nburst into tears. Then all the girls began to cry, one after the other.\r\nI tried to hold out, Marilla. I tried to remember the time Mr. Phillips\r\nmade me sit with Gil--with a boy; and the time he spelled my name\r\nwithout an \xe2\x80\x98e\xe2\x80\x99 on the blackboard; and how he said I was the worst dunce\r\nhe ever saw at geometry and laughed at my spelling; and all the times he\r\nhad been so horrid and sarcastic; but somehow I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t, Marilla, and I\r\njust had to cry too. Jane Andrews has been talking for a month about how\r\nglad she\xe2\x80\x99d be when Mr. Phillips went away and she declared she\xe2\x80\x99d never\r\nshed a tear. Well, she was worse than any of us and had to borrow a\r\nhandkerchief from her brother--of course the boys didn\xe2\x80\x99t cry--because\r\nshe hadn\xe2\x80\x99t brought one of her own, not expecting to need it. Oh,\r\nMarilla, it was heartrending. Mr. Phillips made such a beautiful\r\nfarewell speech beginning, \xe2\x80\x98The time has come for us to part.\xe2\x80\x99 It was\r\nvery affecting. And he had tears in his eyes too, Marilla. Oh, I felt\r\ndreadfully sorry and remorseful for all the times I\xe2\x80\x99d talked in school\r\nand drawn pictures of him on my slate and made fun of him and Prissy.\r\nI can tell you I wished I\xe2\x80\x99d been a model pupil like Minnie Andrews. She\r\nhadn\xe2\x80\x99t anything on her conscience. The girls cried all the way home from\r\nschool. Carrie Sloane kept saying every few minutes, \xe2\x80\x98The time has come\r\nfor us to part,\xe2\x80\x99 and that would start us off again whenever we were in\r\nany danger of cheering up. I do feel dreadfully sad, Marilla. But one\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t feel quite in the depths of despair with two months\xe2\x80\x99 vacation\r\nbefore them, can they, Marilla? And besides, we met the new minister and\r\nhis wife coming from the station. For all I was feeling so bad about Mr.\r\nPhillips going away I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t help taking a little interest in a new\r\nminister, could I? His wife is very pretty. Not exactly regally lovely,\r\nof course--it wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t do, I suppose, for a minister to have a regally\r\nlovely wife, because it might set a bad example. Mrs. Lynde says the\r\nminister\xe2\x80\x99s wife over at Newbridge sets a very bad example because she\r\ndresses so fashionably. Our new minister\xe2\x80\x99s wife was dressed in blue\r\nmuslin with lovely puffed sleeves and a hat trimmed with roses.\r\nJane Andrews said she thought puffed sleeves were too worldly for\r\na minister\xe2\x80\x99s wife, but I didn\xe2\x80\x99t make any such uncharitable remark,\r\nMarilla, because I know what it is to long for puffed sleeves. Besides,\r\nshe\xe2\x80\x99s only been a minister\xe2\x80\x99s wife for a little while, so one should\r\nmake allowances, shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t they? They are going to board with Mrs. Lynde\r\nuntil the manse is ready.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIf Marilla, in going down to Mrs. Lynde\xe2\x80\x99s that evening, was actuated by\r\nany motive save her avowed one of returning the quilting frames she had\r\nborrowed the preceding winter, it was an amiable weakness shared by most\r\nof the Avonlea people. Many a thing Mrs. Lynde had lent, sometimes\r\nnever expecting to see it again, came home that night in charge of the\r\nborrowers thereof. A new minister, and moreover a minister with a wife,\r\nwas a lawful object of curiosity in a quiet little country settlement\r\nwhere sensations were few and far between.\r\n\r\nOld Mr. Bentley, the minister whom Anne had found lacking in\r\nimagination, had been pastor of Avonlea for eighteen years. He was a\r\nwidower when he came, and a widower he remained, despite the fact that\r\ngossip regularly married him to this, that, or the other one, every year\r\nof his sojourn. In the preceding February he had resigned his charge and\r\ndeparted amid the regrets of his people, most of whom had the affection\r\nborn of long intercourse for their good old minister in spite of his\r\nshortcomings as an orator. Since then the Avonlea church had enjoyed a\r\nvariety of religious dissipation in listening to the many and various\r\ncandidates and \xe2\x80\x9csupplies\xe2\x80\x9d who came Sunday after Sunday to preach on\r\ntrial. These stood or fell by the judgment of the fathers and mothers\r\nin Israel; but a certain small, red-haired girl who sat meekly in the\r\ncorner of the old Cuthbert pew also had her opinions about them and\r\ndiscussed the same in full with Matthew, Marilla always declining from\r\nprinciple to criticize ministers in any shape or form.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think Mr. Smith would have done, Matthew\xe2\x80\x9d was Anne\xe2\x80\x99s final\r\nsumming up. \xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Lynde says his delivery was so poor, but I think his\r\nworst fault was just like Mr. Bentley\xe2\x80\x99s--he had no imagination. And Mr.\r\nTerry had too much; he let it run away with him just as I did mine in\r\nthe matter of the Haunted Wood. Besides, Mrs. Lynde says his theology\r\nwasn\xe2\x80\x99t sound. Mr. Gresham was a very good man and a very religious man,\r\nbut he told too many funny stories and made the people laugh in church;\r\nhe was undignified, and you must have some dignity about a minister,\r\nmustn\xe2\x80\x99t you, Matthew? I thought Mr. Marshall was decidedly attractive;\r\nbut Mrs. Lynde says he isn\xe2\x80\x99t married, or even engaged, because she made\r\nspecial inquiries about him, and she says it would never do to have\r\na young unmarried minister in Avonlea, because he might marry in the\r\ncongregation and that would make trouble. Mrs. Lynde is a very farseeing\r\nwoman, isn\xe2\x80\x99t she, Matthew? I\xe2\x80\x99m very glad they\xe2\x80\x99ve called Mr. Allan. I\r\nliked him because his sermon was interesting and he prayed as if he\r\nmeant it and not just as if he did it because he was in the habit of it.\r\nMrs. Lynde says he isn\xe2\x80\x99t perfect, but she says she supposes we couldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nexpect a perfect minister for seven hundred and fifty dollars a year,\r\nand anyhow his theology is sound because she questioned him thoroughly\r\non all the points of doctrine. And she knows his wife\xe2\x80\x99s people and they\r\nare most respectable and the women are all good housekeepers. Mrs. Lynde\r\nsays that sound doctrine in the man and good housekeeping in the woman\r\nmake an ideal combination for a minister\xe2\x80\x99s family.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe new minister and his wife were a young, pleasant-faced couple, still\r\non their honeymoon, and full of all good and beautiful enthusiasms for\r\ntheir chosen lifework. Avonlea opened its heart to them from the start.\r\nOld and young liked the frank, cheerful young man with his high ideals,\r\nand the bright, gentle little lady who assumed the mistress-ship of the\r\nmanse. With Mrs. Allan Anne fell promptly and wholeheartedly in love.\r\nShe had discovered another kindred spirit.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Allan is perfectly lovely,\xe2\x80\x9d she announced one Sunday afternoon.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s taken our class and she\xe2\x80\x99s a splendid teacher. She said right away\r\nshe didn\xe2\x80\x99t think it was fair for the teacher to ask all the questions,\r\nand you know, Marilla, that is exactly what I\xe2\x80\x99ve always thought. She\r\nsaid we could ask her any question we liked and I asked ever so many.\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99m good at asking questions, Marilla.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI believe you\xe2\x80\x9d was Marilla\xe2\x80\x99s emphatic comment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNobody else asked any except Ruby Gillis, and she asked if there was\r\nto be a Sunday-school picnic this summer. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t think that was a\r\nvery proper question to ask because it hadn\xe2\x80\x99t any connection with the\r\nlesson--the lesson was about Daniel in the lions\xe2\x80\x99 den--but Mrs. Allan\r\njust smiled and said she thought there would be. Mrs. Allan has a\r\nlovely smile; she has such _exquisite_ dimples in her cheeks. I wish I had\r\ndimples in my cheeks, Marilla. I\xe2\x80\x99m not half so skinny as I was when I\r\ncame here, but I have no dimples yet. If I had perhaps I could influence\r\npeople for good. Mrs. Allan said we ought always to try to influence\r\nother people for good. She talked so nice about everything. I never knew\r\nbefore that religion was such a cheerful thing. I always thought it\r\nwas kind of melancholy, but Mrs. Allan\xe2\x80\x99s isn\xe2\x80\x99t, and I\xe2\x80\x99d like to be a\r\nChristian if I could be one like her. I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t want to be one like Mr.\r\nSuperintendent Bell.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s very naughty of you to speak so about Mr. Bell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla\r\nseverely. \xe2\x80\x9cMr. Bell is a real good man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, of course he\xe2\x80\x99s good,\xe2\x80\x9d agreed Anne, \xe2\x80\x9cbut he doesn\xe2\x80\x99t seem to get any\r\ncomfort out of it. If I could be good I\xe2\x80\x99d dance and sing all day because\r\nI was glad of it. I suppose Mrs. Allan is too old to dance and sing and\r\nof course it wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be dignified in a minister\xe2\x80\x99s wife. But I can just\r\nfeel she\xe2\x80\x99s glad she\xe2\x80\x99s a Christian and that she\xe2\x80\x99d be one even if she\r\ncould get to heaven without it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose we must have Mr. and Mrs. Allan up to tea someday soon,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMarilla reflectively. \xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99ve been most everywhere but here. Let me\r\nsee. Next Wednesday would be a good time to have them. But don\xe2\x80\x99t say a\r\nword to Matthew about it, for if he knew they were coming he\xe2\x80\x99d find some\r\nexcuse to be away that day. He\xe2\x80\x99d got so used to Mr. Bentley he didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nmind him, but he\xe2\x80\x99s going to find it hard to get acquainted with a new\r\nminister, and a new minister\xe2\x80\x99s wife will frighten him to death.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll be as secret as the dead,\xe2\x80\x9d assured Anne. \xe2\x80\x9cBut oh, Marilla, will\r\nyou let me make a cake for the occasion? I\xe2\x80\x99d love to do something for\r\nMrs. Allan, and you know I can make a pretty good cake by this time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can make a layer cake,\xe2\x80\x9d promised Marilla.\r\n\r\nMonday and Tuesday great preparations went on at Green Gables.\r\nHaving the minister and his wife to tea was a serious and important\r\nundertaking, and Marilla was determined not to be eclipsed by any of\r\nthe Avonlea housekeepers. Anne was wild with excitement and delight. She\r\ntalked it all over with Diana Tuesday night in the twilight, as they\r\nsat on the big red stones by the Dryad\xe2\x80\x99s Bubble and made rainbows in the\r\nwater with little twigs dipped in fir balsam.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEverything is ready, Diana, except my cake which I\xe2\x80\x99m to make in the\r\nmorning, and the baking-powder biscuits which Marilla will make just\r\nbefore teatime. I assure you, Diana, that Marilla and I have had a busy\r\ntwo days of it. It\xe2\x80\x99s such a responsibility having a minister\xe2\x80\x99s family to\r\ntea. I never went through such an experience before. You should just see\r\nour pantry. It\xe2\x80\x99s a sight to behold. We\xe2\x80\x99re going to have jellied chicken\r\nand cold tongue. We\xe2\x80\x99re to have two kinds of jelly, red and yellow, and\r\nwhipped cream and lemon pie, and cherry pie, and three kinds of cookies,\r\nand fruit cake, and Marilla\xe2\x80\x99s famous yellow plum preserves that she\r\nkeeps especially for ministers, and pound cake and layer cake, and\r\nbiscuits as aforesaid; and new bread and old both, in case the minister\r\nis dyspeptic and can\xe2\x80\x99t eat new. Mrs. Lynde says ministers are dyspeptic,\r\nbut I don\xe2\x80\x99t think Mr. Allan has been a minister long enough for it to\r\nhave had a bad effect on him. I just grow cold when I think of my layer\r\ncake. Oh, Diana, what if it shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be good! I dreamed last night that\r\nI was chased all around by a fearful goblin with a big layer cake for a\r\nhead.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99ll be good, all right,\xe2\x80\x9d assured Diana, who was a very comfortable\r\nsort of friend. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sure that piece of the one you made that we had for\r\nlunch in Idlewild two weeks ago was perfectly elegant.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; but cakes have such a terrible habit of turning out bad just\r\nwhen you especially want them to be good,\xe2\x80\x9d sighed Anne, setting a\r\nparticularly well-balsamed twig afloat. \xe2\x80\x9cHowever, I suppose I shall\r\njust have to trust to Providence and be careful to put in the flour. Oh,\r\nlook, Diana, what a lovely rainbow! Do you suppose the dryad will come\r\nout after we go away and take it for a scarf?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou know there is no such thing as a dryad,\xe2\x80\x9d said Diana. Diana\xe2\x80\x99s mother\r\nhad found out about the Haunted Wood and had been decidedly angry over\r\nit. As a result Diana had abstained from any further imitative flights\r\nof imagination and did not think it prudent to cultivate a spirit of\r\nbelief even in harmless dryads.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut it\xe2\x80\x99s so easy to imagine there is,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne. \xe2\x80\x9cEvery night before\r\nI go to bed, I look out of my window and wonder if the dryad is really\r\nsitting here, combing her locks with the spring for a mirror. Sometimes\r\nI look for her footprints in the dew in the morning. Oh, Diana, don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ngive up your faith in the dryad!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWednesday morning came. Anne got up at sunrise because she was too\r\nexcited to sleep. She had caught a severe cold in the head by reason of\r\nher dabbling in the spring on the preceding evening; but nothing short\r\nof absolute pneumonia could have quenched her interest in culinary\r\nmatters that morning. After breakfast she proceeded to make her cake.\r\nWhen she finally shut the oven door upon it she drew a long breath.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sure I haven\xe2\x80\x99t forgotten anything this time, Marilla. But do you\r\nthink it will rise? Just suppose perhaps the baking powder isn\xe2\x80\x99t good? I\r\nused it out of the new can. And Mrs. Lynde says you can never be sure of\r\ngetting good baking powder nowadays when everything is so adulterated.\r\nMrs. Lynde says the Government ought to take the matter up, but she says\r\nwe\xe2\x80\x99ll never see the day when a Tory Government will do it. Marilla, what\r\nif that cake doesn\xe2\x80\x99t rise?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ll have plenty without it\xe2\x80\x9d was Marilla\xe2\x80\x99s unimpassioned way of\r\nlooking at the subject.\r\n\r\nThe cake did rise, however, and came out of the oven as light and\r\nfeathery as golden foam. Anne, flushed with delight, clapped it together\r\nwith layers of ruby jelly and, in imagination, saw Mrs. Allan eating it\r\nand possibly asking for another piece!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll be using the best tea set, of course, Marilla,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cCan I\r\nfix the table with ferns and wild roses?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think that\xe2\x80\x99s all nonsense,\xe2\x80\x9d sniffed Marilla. \xe2\x80\x9cIn my opinion it\xe2\x80\x99s the\r\neatables that matter and not flummery decorations.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Barry had _her_ table decorated,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne, who was not entirely\r\nguiltless of the wisdom of the serpent, \xe2\x80\x9cand the minister paid her an\r\nelegant compliment. He said it was a feast for the eye as well as the\r\npalate.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, do as you like,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla, who was quite determined not to\r\nbe surpassed by Mrs. Barry or anybody else. \xe2\x80\x9cOnly mind you leave enough\r\nroom for the dishes and the food.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne laid herself out to decorate in a manner and after a fashion that\r\nshould leave Mrs. Barry\xe2\x80\x99s nowhere. Having abundance of roses and ferns\r\nand a very artistic taste of her own, she made that tea table such a\r\nthing of beauty that when the minister and his wife sat down to it they\r\nexclaimed in chorus over it loveliness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s Anne\xe2\x80\x99s doings,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla, grimly just; and Anne felt that Mrs.\r\nAllan\xe2\x80\x99s approving smile was almost too much happiness for this world.\r\n\r\nMatthew was there, having been inveigled into the party only goodness\r\nand Anne knew how. He had been in such a state of shyness and\r\nnervousness that Marilla had given him up in despair, but Anne took him\r\nin hand so successfully that he now sat at the table in his best clothes\r\nand white collar and talked to the minister not uninterestingly.\r\nHe never said a word to Mrs. Allan, but that perhaps was not to be\r\nexpected.\r\n\r\nAll went merry as a marriage bell until Anne\xe2\x80\x99s layer cake was passed.\r\nMrs. Allan, having already been helped to a bewildering variety,\r\ndeclined it. But Marilla, seeing the disappointment on Anne\xe2\x80\x99s face, said\r\nsmilingly:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, you must take a piece of this, Mrs. Allan. Anne made it on purpose\r\nfor you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn that case I must sample it,\xe2\x80\x9d laughed Mrs. Allan, helping herself to\r\na plump triangle, as did also the minister and Marilla.\r\n\r\nMrs. Allan took a mouthful of hers and a most peculiar expression\r\ncrossed her face; not a word did she say, however, but steadily ate away\r\nat it. Marilla saw the expression and hastened to taste the cake.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnne Shirley!\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat on earth did you put into that\r\ncake?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing but what the recipe said, Marilla,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Anne with a look of\r\nanguish. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it all right?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right! It\xe2\x80\x99s simply horrible. Mr. Allan, don\xe2\x80\x99t try to eat it. Anne,\r\ntaste it yourself. What flavoring did you use?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVanilla,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne, her face scarlet with mortification after tasting\r\nthe cake. \xe2\x80\x9cOnly vanilla. Oh, Marilla, it must have been the baking\r\npowder. I had my suspicions of that bak--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBaking powder fiddlesticks! Go and bring me the bottle of vanilla you\r\nused.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne fled to the pantry and returned with a small bottle partially\r\nfilled with a brown liquid and labeled yellowly, \xe2\x80\x9cBest Vanilla.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarilla took it, uncorked it, smelled it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMercy on us, Anne, you\xe2\x80\x99ve flavored that cake with _Anodyne Liniment_. I\r\nbroke the liniment bottle last week and poured what was left into an\r\nold empty vanilla bottle. I suppose it\xe2\x80\x99s partly my fault--I should have\r\nwarned you--but for pity\xe2\x80\x99s sake why couldn\xe2\x80\x99t you have smelled it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne dissolved into tears under this double disgrace.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI couldn\xe2\x80\x99t--I had such a cold!\xe2\x80\x9d and with this she fairly fled to the\r\ngable chamber, where she cast herself on the bed and wept as one who\r\nrefuses to be comforted.\r\n\r\nPresently a light step sounded on the stairs and somebody entered the\r\nroom.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Marilla,\xe2\x80\x9d sobbed Anne, without looking up, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m disgraced forever.\r\nI shall never be able to live this down. It will get out--things always\r\ndo get out in Avonlea. Diana will ask me how my cake turned out and I\r\nshall have to tell her the truth. I shall always be pointed at as the\r\ngirl who flavored a cake with anodyne liniment. Gil--the boys in school\r\nwill never get over laughing at it. Oh, Marilla, if you have a spark\r\nof Christian pity don\xe2\x80\x99t tell me that I must go down and wash the dishes\r\nafter this. I\xe2\x80\x99ll wash them when the minister and his wife are gone, but\r\nI cannot ever look Mrs. Allan in the face again. Perhaps she\xe2\x80\x99ll think I\r\ntried to poison her. Mrs. Lynde says she knows an orphan girl who tried\r\nto poison her benefactor. But the liniment isn\xe2\x80\x99t poisonous. It\xe2\x80\x99s meant\r\nto be taken internally--although not in cakes. Won\xe2\x80\x99t you tell Mrs. Allan\r\nso, Marilla?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSuppose you jump up and tell her so yourself,\xe2\x80\x9d said a merry voice.\r\n\r\nAnne flew up, to find Mrs. Allan standing by her bed, surveying her with\r\nlaughing eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear little girl, you mustn\xe2\x80\x99t cry like this,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, genuinely\r\ndisturbed by Anne\xe2\x80\x99s tragic face. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, it\xe2\x80\x99s all just a funny mistake\r\nthat anybody might make.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, no, it takes me to make such a mistake,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne forlornly. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd\r\nI wanted to have that cake so nice for you, Mrs. Allan.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I know, dear. And I assure you I appreciate your kindness and\r\nthoughtfulness just as much as if it had turned out all right. Now,\r\nyou mustn\xe2\x80\x99t cry any more, but come down with me and show me your flower\r\ngarden. Miss Cuthbert tells me you have a little plot all your own. I\r\nwant to see it, for I\xe2\x80\x99m very much interested in flowers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne permitted herself to be led down and comforted, reflecting that it\r\nwas really providential that Mrs. Allan was a kindred spirit. Nothing\r\nmore was said about the liniment cake, and when the guests went away\r\nAnne found that she had enjoyed the evening more than could have been\r\nexpected, considering that terrible incident. Nevertheless, she sighed\r\ndeeply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMarilla, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it nice to think that tomorrow is a new day with no\r\nmistakes in it yet?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll warrant you\xe2\x80\x99ll make plenty in it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla. \xe2\x80\x9cI never saw your\r\nbeat for making mistakes, Anne.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, and well I know it,\xe2\x80\x9d admitted Anne mournfully. \xe2\x80\x9cBut have you ever\r\nnoticed one encouraging thing about me, Marilla? I never make the same\r\nmistake twice.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know as that\xe2\x80\x99s much benefit when you\xe2\x80\x99re always making new\r\nones.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, don\xe2\x80\x99t you see, Marilla? There must be a limit to the mistakes one\r\nperson can make, and when I get to the end of them, then I\xe2\x80\x99ll be through\r\nwith them. That\xe2\x80\x99s a very comforting thought.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you\xe2\x80\x99d better go and give that cake to the pigs,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt isn\xe2\x80\x99t fit for any human to eat, not even Jerry Boute.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXII. Anne is Invited Out to Tea\r\n\r\n\r\n|AND what are your eyes popping out of your head about. Now?\xe2\x80\x9d asked\r\nMarilla, when Anne had just come in from a run to the post office. \xe2\x80\x9cHave\r\nyou discovered another kindred spirit?\xe2\x80\x9d Excitement hung around Anne like\r\na garment, shone in her eyes, kindled in every feature. She had come\r\ndancing up the lane, like a wind-blown sprite, through the mellow\r\nsunshine and lazy shadows of the August evening.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, Marilla, but oh, what do you think? I am invited to tea at the\r\nmanse tomorrow afternoon! Mrs. Allan left the letter for me at the post\r\noffice. Just look at it, Marilla. \xe2\x80\x98Miss Anne Shirley, Green Gables.\xe2\x80\x99\r\nThat is the first time I was ever called \xe2\x80\x98Miss.\xe2\x80\x99 Such a thrill as it\r\ngave me! I shall cherish it forever among my choicest treasures.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Allan told me she meant to have all the members of her\r\nSunday-school class to tea in turn,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla, regarding the\r\nwonderful event very coolly. \xe2\x80\x9cYou needn\xe2\x80\x99t get in such a fever over it.\r\nDo learn to take things calmly, child.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor Anne to take things calmly would have been to change her nature. All\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cspirit and fire and dew,\xe2\x80\x9d as she was, the pleasures and pains of life\r\ncame to her with trebled intensity. Marilla felt this and was vaguely\r\ntroubled over it, realizing that the ups and downs of existence would\r\nprobably bear hardly on this impulsive soul and not sufficiently\r\nunderstanding that the equally great capacity for delight might more\r\nthan compensate. Therefore Marilla conceived it to be her duty to drill\r\nAnne into a tranquil uniformity of disposition as impossible and alien\r\nto her as to a dancing sunbeam in one of the brook shallows. She did not\r\nmake much headway, as she sorrowfully admitted to herself. The downfall\r\nof some dear hope or plan plunged Anne into \xe2\x80\x9cdeeps of affliction.\xe2\x80\x9d The\r\nfulfillment thereof exalted her to dizzy realms of delight. Marilla had\r\nalmost begun to despair of ever fashioning this waif of the world into\r\nher model little girl of demure manners and prim deportment. Neither\r\nwould she have believed that she really liked Anne much better as she\r\nwas.\r\n\r\nAnne went to bed that night speechless with misery because Matthew had\r\nsaid the wind was round northeast and he feared it would be a rainy day\r\ntomorrow. The rustle of the poplar leaves about the house worried her,\r\nit sounded so like pattering raindrops, and the full, faraway roar of\r\nthe gulf, to which she listened delightedly at other times, loving its\r\nstrange, sonorous, haunting rhythm, now seemed like a prophecy of storm\r\nand disaster to a small maiden who particularly wanted a fine day. Anne\r\nthought that the morning would never come.\r\n\r\nBut all things have an end, even nights before the day on which you are\r\ninvited to take tea at the manse. The morning, in spite of Matthew\xe2\x80\x99s\r\npredictions, was fine and Anne\xe2\x80\x99s spirits soared to their highest.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Marilla, there is something in me today that makes me just love\r\neverybody I see,\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed as she washed the breakfast dishes.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t know how good I feel! Wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t it be nice if it could last? I\r\nbelieve I could be a model child if I were just invited out to tea every\r\nday. But oh, Marilla, it\xe2\x80\x99s a solemn occasion too. I feel so anxious.\r\nWhat if I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t behave properly? You know I never had tea at a\r\nmanse before, and I\xe2\x80\x99m not sure that I know all the rules of etiquette,\r\nalthough I\xe2\x80\x99ve been studying the rules given in the Etiquette Department\r\nof the Family Herald ever since I came here. I\xe2\x80\x99m so afraid I\xe2\x80\x99ll do\r\nsomething silly or forget to do something I should do. Would it be\r\ngood manners to take a second helping of anything if you wanted to _very_\r\nmuch?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe trouble with you, Anne, is that you\xe2\x80\x99re thinking too much about\r\nyourself. You should just think of Mrs. Allan and what would be nicest\r\nand most agreeable to her,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla, hitting for once in her life\r\non a very sound and pithy piece of advice. Anne instantly realized this.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are right, Marilla. I\xe2\x80\x99ll try not to think about myself at all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne evidently got through her visit without any serious breach of\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cetiquette,\xe2\x80\x9d for she came home through the twilight, under a great,\r\nhigh-sprung sky gloried over with trails of saffron and rosy cloud, in\r\na beatified state of mind and told Marilla all about it happily, sitting\r\non the big red-sandstone slab at the kitchen door with her tired curly\r\nhead in Marilla\xe2\x80\x99s gingham lap.\r\n\r\nA cool wind was blowing down over the long harvest fields from the rims\r\nof firry western hills and whistling through the poplars. One clear star\r\nhung over the orchard and the fireflies were flitting over in Lover\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nLane, in and out among the ferns and rustling boughs. Anne watched them\r\nas she talked and somehow felt that wind and stars and fireflies were\r\nall tangled up together into something unutterably sweet and enchanting.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Marilla, I\xe2\x80\x99ve had a most _fascinating_ time. I feel that I have not\r\nlived in vain and I shall always feel like that even if I should never\r\nbe invited to tea at a manse again. When I got there Mrs. Allan met me\r\nat the door. She was dressed in the sweetest dress of pale-pink organdy,\r\nwith dozens of frills and elbow sleeves, and she looked just like a\r\nseraph. I really think I\xe2\x80\x99d like to be a minister\xe2\x80\x99s wife when I grow up,\r\nMarilla. A minister mightn\xe2\x80\x99t mind my red hair because he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be\r\nthinking of such worldly things. But then of course one would have to\r\nbe naturally good and I\xe2\x80\x99ll never be that, so I suppose there\xe2\x80\x99s no use in\r\nthinking about it. Some people are naturally good, you know, and others\r\nare not. I\xe2\x80\x99m one of the others. Mrs. Lynde says I\xe2\x80\x99m full of original\r\nsin. No matter how hard I try to be good I can never make such a success\r\nof it as those who are naturally good. It\xe2\x80\x99s a good deal like geometry,\r\nI expect. But don\xe2\x80\x99t you think the trying so hard ought to count for\r\nsomething? Mrs. Allan is one of the naturally good people. I love her\r\npassionately. You know there are some people, like Matthew and Mrs.\r\nAllan that you can love right off without any trouble. And there are\r\nothers, like Mrs. Lynde, that you have to try very hard to love. You\r\nknow you _ought_ to love them because they know so much and are such\r\nactive workers in the church, but you have to keep reminding yourself of\r\nit all the time or else you forget. There was another little girl at the\r\nmanse to tea, from the White Sands Sunday school. Her name was Laurette\r\nBradley, and she was a very nice little girl. Not exactly a kindred\r\nspirit, you know, but still very nice. We had an elegant tea, and I\r\nthink I kept all the rules of etiquette pretty well. After tea Mrs.\r\nAllan played and sang and she got Lauretta and me to sing too.\r\nMrs. Allan says I have a good voice and she says I must sing in the\r\nSunday-school choir after this. You can\xe2\x80\x99t think how I was thrilled at\r\nthe mere thought. I\xe2\x80\x99ve longed so to sing in the Sunday-school choir,\r\nas Diana does, but I feared it was an honor I could never aspire to.\r\nLauretta had to go home early because there is a big concert in the\r\nWhite Sands Hotel tonight and her sister is to recite at it. Lauretta\r\nsays that the Americans at the hotel give a concert every fortnight in\r\naid of the Charlottetown hospital, and they ask lots of the White\r\nSands people to recite. Lauretta said she expected to be asked herself\r\nsomeday. I just gazed at her in awe. After she had gone Mrs. Allan and I\r\nhad a heart-to-heart talk. I told her everything--about Mrs. Thomas and\r\nthe twins and Katie Maurice and Violetta and coming to Green Gables and\r\nmy troubles over geometry. And would you believe it, Marilla? Mrs.\r\nAllan told me she was a dunce at geometry too. You don\xe2\x80\x99t know how that\r\nencouraged me. Mrs. Lynde came to the manse just before I left, and what\r\ndo you think, Marilla? The trustees have hired a new teacher and it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\na lady. Her name is Miss Muriel Stacy. Isn\xe2\x80\x99t that a romantic name? Mrs.\r\nLynde says they\xe2\x80\x99ve never had a female teacher in Avonlea before and she\r\nthinks it is a dangerous innovation. But I think it will be splendid\r\nto have a lady teacher, and I really don\xe2\x80\x99t see how I\xe2\x80\x99m going to live\r\nthrough the two weeks before school begins. I\xe2\x80\x99m so impatient to see\r\nher.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXIII. Anne Comes to Grief in an Affair of Honor\r\n\r\n\r\n|ANNE had to live through more than two weeks, as it happened. Almost a\r\nmonth having elapsed since the liniment cake episode, it was high time\r\nfor her to get into fresh trouble of some sort, little mistakes, such as\r\nabsentmindedly emptying a pan of skim milk into a basket of yarn balls\r\nin the pantry instead of into the pigs\xe2\x80\x99 bucket, and walking clean over\r\nthe edge of the log bridge into the brook while wrapped in imaginative\r\nreverie, not really being worth counting.\r\n\r\nA week after the tea at the manse Diana Barry gave a party.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSmall and select,\xe2\x80\x9d Anne assured Marilla. \xe2\x80\x9cJust the girls in our class.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey had a very good time and nothing untoward happened until after tea,\r\nwhen they found themselves in the Barry garden, a little tired of all\r\ntheir games and ripe for any enticing form of mischief which might\r\npresent itself. This presently took the form of \xe2\x80\x9cdaring.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDaring was the fashionable amusement among the Avonlea small fry just\r\nthen. It had begun among the boys, but soon spread to the girls, and all\r\nthe silly things that were done in Avonlea that summer because the doers\r\nthereof were \xe2\x80\x9cdared\xe2\x80\x9d to do them would fill a book by themselves.\r\n\r\nFirst of all Carrie Sloane dared Ruby Gillis to climb to a certain point\r\nin the huge old willow tree before the front door; which Ruby Gillis,\r\nalbeit in mortal dread of the fat green caterpillars with which said\r\ntree was infested and with the fear of her mother before her eyes if she\r\nshould tear her new muslin dress, nimbly did, to the discomfiture of the\r\naforesaid Carrie Sloane. Then Josie Pye dared Jane Andrews to hop on her\r\nleft leg around the garden without stopping once or putting her right\r\nfoot to the ground; which Jane Andrews gamely tried to do, but gave out\r\nat the third corner and had to confess herself defeated.\r\n\r\nJosie\xe2\x80\x99s triumph being rather more pronounced than good taste permitted,\r\nAnne Shirley dared her to walk along the top of the board fence which\r\nbounded the garden to the east. Now, to \xe2\x80\x9cwalk\xe2\x80\x9d board fences requires\r\nmore skill and steadiness of head and heel than one might suppose who\r\nhas never tried it. But Josie Pye, if deficient in some qualities\r\nthat make for popularity, had at least a natural and inborn gift, duly\r\ncultivated, for walking board fences. Josie walked the Barry fence with\r\nan airy unconcern which seemed to imply that a little thing like that\r\nwasn\xe2\x80\x99t worth a \xe2\x80\x9cdare.\xe2\x80\x9d Reluctant admiration greeted her exploit, for\r\nmost of the other girls could appreciate it, having suffered many things\r\nthemselves in their efforts to walk fences. Josie descended from her\r\nperch, flushed with victory, and darted a defiant glance at Anne.\r\n\r\nAnne tossed her red braids.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think it\xe2\x80\x99s such a very wonderful thing to walk a little, low,\r\nboard fence,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cI knew a girl in Marysville who could walk the\r\nridgepole of a roof.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t believe it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Josie flatly. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t believe anybody could\r\nwalk a ridgepole. _You_ couldn\xe2\x80\x99t, anyhow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCouldn\xe2\x80\x99t I?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Anne rashly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I dare you to do it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Josie defiantly. \xe2\x80\x9cI dare you to climb\r\nup there and walk the ridgepole of Mr. Barry\xe2\x80\x99s kitchen roof.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne turned pale, but there was clearly only one thing to be done. She\r\nwalked toward the house, where a ladder was leaning against the kitchen\r\nroof. All the fifth-class girls said, \xe2\x80\x9cOh!\xe2\x80\x9d partly in excitement, partly\r\nin dismay.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you do it, Anne,\xe2\x80\x9d entreated Diana. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll fall off and be\r\nkilled. Never mind Josie Pye. It isn\xe2\x80\x99t fair to dare anybody to do\r\nanything so dangerous.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must do it. My honor is at stake,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne solemnly. \xe2\x80\x9cI shall walk\r\nthat ridgepole, Diana, or perish in the attempt. If I am killed you are\r\nto have my pearl bead ring.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne climbed the ladder amid breathless silence, gained the ridgepole,\r\nbalanced herself uprightly on that precarious footing, and started to\r\nwalk along it, dizzily conscious that she was uncomfortably high up\r\nin the world and that walking ridgepoles was not a thing in which your\r\nimagination helped you out much. Nevertheless, she managed to take\r\nseveral steps before the catastrophe came. Then she swayed, lost her\r\nbalance, stumbled, staggered, and fell, sliding down over the sun-baked\r\nroof and crashing off it through the tangle of Virginia creeper\r\nbeneath--all before the dismayed circle below could give a simultaneous,\r\nterrified shriek.\r\n\r\nIf Anne had tumbled off the roof on the side up which she had ascended\r\nDiana would probably have fallen heir to the pearl bead ring then and\r\nthere. Fortunately she fell on the other side, where the roof extended\r\ndown over the porch so nearly to the ground that a fall therefrom was\r\na much less serious thing. Nevertheless, when Diana and the other\r\ngirls had rushed frantically around the house--except Ruby Gillis, who\r\nremained as if rooted to the ground and went into hysterics--they found\r\nAnne lying all white and limp among the wreck and ruin of the Virginia\r\ncreeper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnne, are you killed?\xe2\x80\x9d shrieked Diana, throwing herself on her knees\r\nbeside her friend. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, Anne, dear Anne, speak just one word to me and\r\ntell me if you\xe2\x80\x99re killed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTo the immense relief of all the girls, and especially of Josie Pye,\r\nwho, in spite of lack of imagination, had been seized with horrible\r\nvisions of a future branded as the girl who was the cause of Anne\r\nShirley\xe2\x80\x99s early and tragic death, Anne sat dizzily up and answered\r\nuncertainly:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, Diana, I am not killed, but I think I am rendered unconscious.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere?\xe2\x80\x9d sobbed Carrie Sloane. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, where, Anne?\xe2\x80\x9d Before Anne could\r\nanswer Mrs. Barry appeared on the scene. At sight of her Anne tried to\r\nscramble to her feet, but sank back again with a sharp little cry of\r\npain.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the matter? Where have you hurt yourself?\xe2\x80\x9d demanded Mrs. Barry.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy ankle,\xe2\x80\x9d gasped Anne. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, Diana, please find your father and ask him\r\nto take me home. I know I can never walk there. And I\xe2\x80\x99m sure I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nhop so far on one foot when Jane couldn\xe2\x80\x99t even hop around the garden.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarilla was out in the orchard picking a panful of summer apples when\r\nshe saw Mr. Barry coming over the log bridge and up the slope, with Mrs.\r\nBarry beside him and a whole procession of little girls trailing after\r\nhim. In his arms he carried Anne, whose head lay limply against his\r\nshoulder.\r\n\r\nAt that moment Marilla had a revelation. In the sudden stab of fear that\r\npierced her very heart she realized what Anne had come to mean to her.\r\nShe would have admitted that she liked Anne--nay, that she was very fond\r\nof Anne. But now she knew as she hurried wildly down the slope that Anne\r\nwas dearer to her than anything else on earth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Barry, what has happened to her?\xe2\x80\x9d she gasped, more white and shaken\r\nthan the self-contained, sensible Marilla had been for many years.\r\n\r\nAnne herself answered, lifting her head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t be very frightened, Marilla. I was walking the ridgepole and I\r\nfell off. I expect I have sprained my ankle. But, Marilla, I might have\r\nbroken my neck. Let us look on the bright side of things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI might have known you\xe2\x80\x99d go and do something of the sort when I let you\r\ngo to that party,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla, sharp and shrewish in her very relief.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBring her in here, Mr. Barry, and lay her on the sofa. Mercy me, the\r\nchild has gone and fainted!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was quite true. Overcome by the pain of her injury, Anne had one more\r\nof her wishes granted to her. She had fainted dead away.\r\n\r\nMatthew, hastily summoned from the harvest field, was straightway\r\ndispatched for the doctor, who in due time came, to discover that the\r\ninjury was more serious than they had supposed. Anne\xe2\x80\x99s ankle was broken.\r\n\r\nThat night, when Marilla went up to the east gable, where a white-faced\r\ngirl was lying, a plaintive voice greeted her from the bed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAren\xe2\x80\x99t you very sorry for me, Marilla?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was your own fault,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla, twitching down the blind and\r\nlighting a lamp.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd that is just why you should be sorry for me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne, \xe2\x80\x9cbecause\r\nthe thought that it is all my own fault is what makes it so hard. If I\r\ncould blame it on anybody I would feel so much better. But what would\r\nyou have done, Marilla, if you had been dared to walk a ridgepole?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d have stayed on good firm ground and let them dare away. Such\r\nabsurdity!\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla.\r\n\r\nAnne sighed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you have such strength of mind, Marilla. I haven\xe2\x80\x99t. I just felt\r\nthat I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t bear Josie Pye\xe2\x80\x99s scorn. She would have crowed over me\r\nall my life. And I think I have been punished so much that you needn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nbe very cross with me, Marilla. It\xe2\x80\x99s not a bit nice to faint, after all.\r\nAnd the doctor hurt me dreadfully when he was setting my ankle. I won\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nbe able to go around for six or seven weeks and I\xe2\x80\x99ll miss the new lady\r\nteacher. She won\xe2\x80\x99t be new any more by the time I\xe2\x80\x99m able to go to school.\r\nAnd Gil--everybody will get ahead of me in class. Oh, I am an afflicted\r\nmortal. But I\xe2\x80\x99ll try to bear it all bravely if only you won\xe2\x80\x99t be cross\r\nwith me, Marilla.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere, there, I\xe2\x80\x99m not cross,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re an unlucky child,\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x99s no doubt about that; but as you say, you\xe2\x80\x99ll have the suffering\r\nof it. Here now, try and eat some supper.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t it fortunate I\xe2\x80\x99ve got such an imagination?\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne. \xe2\x80\x9cIt will\r\nhelp me through splendidly, I expect. What do people who haven\xe2\x80\x99t any\r\nimagination do when they break their bones, do you suppose, Marilla?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne had good reason to bless her imagination many a time and oft during\r\nthe tedious seven weeks that followed. But she was not solely dependent\r\non it. She had many visitors and not a day passed without one or more of\r\nthe schoolgirls dropping in to bring her flowers and books and tell her\r\nall the happenings in the juvenile world of Avonlea.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEverybody has been so good and kind, Marilla,\xe2\x80\x9d sighed Anne happily,\r\non the day when she could first limp across the floor. \xe2\x80\x9cIt isn\xe2\x80\x99t very\r\npleasant to be laid up; but there is a bright side to it, Marilla. You\r\nfind out how many friends you have. Why, even Superintendent Bell came\r\nto see me, and he\xe2\x80\x99s really a very fine man. Not a kindred spirit, of\r\ncourse; but still I like him and I\xe2\x80\x99m awfully sorry I ever criticized his\r\nprayers. I believe now he really does mean them, only he has got into\r\nthe habit of saying them as if he didn\xe2\x80\x99t. He could get over that if he\xe2\x80\x99d\r\ntake a little trouble. I gave him a good broad hint. I told him how hard\r\nI tried to make my own little private prayers interesting. He told me\r\nall about the time he broke his ankle when he was a boy. It does seem\r\nso strange to think of Superintendent Bell ever being a boy. Even my\r\nimagination has its limits, for I can\xe2\x80\x99t imagine _that_. When I try to\r\nimagine him as a boy I see him with gray whiskers and spectacles, just\r\nas he looks in Sunday school, only small. Now, it\xe2\x80\x99s so easy to imagine\r\nMrs. Allan as a little girl. Mrs. Allan has been to see me fourteen\r\ntimes. Isn\xe2\x80\x99t that something to be proud of, Marilla? When a minister\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwife has so many claims on her time! She is such a cheerful person to\r\nhave visit you, too. She never tells you it\xe2\x80\x99s your own fault and she\r\nhopes you\xe2\x80\x99ll be a better girl on account of it. Mrs. Lynde always told\r\nme that when she came to see me; and she said it in a kind of way that\r\nmade me feel she might hope I\xe2\x80\x99d be a better girl but didn\xe2\x80\x99t really\r\nbelieve I would. Even Josie Pye came to see me. I received her as\r\npolitely as I could, because I think she was sorry she dared me to walk\r\na ridgepole. If I had been killed she would had to carry a dark burden\r\nof remorse all her life. Diana has been a faithful friend. She\xe2\x80\x99s been\r\nover every day to cheer my lonely pillow. But oh, I shall be so glad\r\nwhen I can go to school for I\xe2\x80\x99ve heard such exciting things about the\r\nnew teacher. The girls all think she is perfectly sweet. Diana says she\r\nhas the loveliest fair curly hair and such fascinating eyes. She dresses\r\nbeautifully, and her sleeve puffs are bigger than anybody else\xe2\x80\x99s in\r\nAvonlea. Every other Friday afternoon she has recitations and everybody\r\nhas to say a piece or take part in a dialogue. Oh, it\xe2\x80\x99s just glorious to\r\nthink of it. Josie Pye says she hates it but that is just because Josie\r\nhas so little imagination. Diana and Ruby Gillis and Jane Andrews are\r\npreparing a dialogue, called \xe2\x80\x98A Morning Visit,\xe2\x80\x99 for next Friday. And the\r\nFriday afternoons they don\xe2\x80\x99t have recitations Miss Stacy takes them\r\nall to the woods for a \xe2\x80\x98field\xe2\x80\x99 day and they study ferns and flowers\r\nand birds. And they have physical culture exercises every morning and\r\nevening. Mrs. Lynde says she never heard of such goings on and it all\r\ncomes of having a lady teacher. But I think it must be splendid and I\r\nbelieve I shall find that Miss Stacy is a kindred spirit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s one thing plain to be seen, Anne,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla, \xe2\x80\x9cand that is\r\nthat your fall off the Barry roof hasn\xe2\x80\x99t injured your tongue at all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXIV. Miss Stacy and Her Pupils Get Up a Concert\r\n\r\n\r\n|IT was October again when Anne was ready to go back to school--a\r\nglorious October, all red and gold, with mellow mornings when the\r\nvalleys were filled with delicate mists as if the spirit of autumn had\r\npoured them in for the sun to drain--amethyst, pearl, silver, rose, and\r\nsmoke-blue. The dews were so heavy that the fields glistened like cloth\r\nof silver and there were such heaps of rustling leaves in the hollows of\r\nmany-stemmed woods to run crisply through. The Birch Path was a canopy\r\nof yellow and the ferns were sear and brown all along it. There was a\r\ntang in the very air that inspired the hearts of small maidens tripping,\r\nunlike snails, swiftly and willingly to school; and it _was_ jolly to\r\nbe back again at the little brown desk beside Diana, with Ruby Gillis\r\nnodding across the aisle and Carrie Sloane sending up notes and Julia\r\nBell passing a \xe2\x80\x9cchew\xe2\x80\x9d of gum down from the back seat. Anne drew a long\r\nbreath of happiness as she sharpened her pencil and arranged her picture\r\ncards in her desk. Life was certainly very interesting.\r\n\r\nIn the new teacher she found another true and helpful friend. Miss Stacy\r\nwas a bright, sympathetic young woman with the happy gift of winning and\r\nholding the affections of her pupils and bringing out the best that was\r\nin them mentally and morally. Anne expanded like a flower under this\r\nwholesome influence and carried home to the admiring Matthew and the\r\ncritical Marilla glowing accounts of schoolwork and aims.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI love Miss Stacy with my whole heart, Marilla. She is so ladylike\r\nand she has such a sweet voice. When she pronounces my name I feel\r\n_instinctively_ that she\xe2\x80\x99s spelling it with an E. We had recitations\r\nthis afternoon. I just wish you could have been there to hear me recite\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Mary, Queen of Scots.\xe2\x80\x99 I just put my whole soul into it. Ruby Gillis\r\ntold me coming home that the way I said the line, \xe2\x80\x98Now for my father\xe2\x80\x99s\r\narm,\xe2\x80\x99 she said, \xe2\x80\x98my woman\xe2\x80\x99s heart farewell,\xe2\x80\x99 just made her blood run\r\ncold.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell now, you might recite it for me some of these days, out in the\r\nbarn,\xe2\x80\x9d suggested Matthew.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course I will,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne meditatively, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I won\xe2\x80\x99t be able to do\r\nit so well, I know. It won\xe2\x80\x99t be so exciting as it is when you have a\r\nwhole schoolful before you hanging breathlessly on your words. I know I\r\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t be able to make your blood run cold.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Lynde says it made _her_ blood run cold to see the boys climbing to\r\nthe very tops of those big trees on Bell\xe2\x80\x99s hill after crows\xe2\x80\x99 nests last\r\nFriday,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla. \xe2\x80\x9cI wonder at Miss Stacy for encouraging it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut we wanted a crow\xe2\x80\x99s nest for nature study,\xe2\x80\x9d explained Anne. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\r\nwas on our field afternoon. Field afternoons are splendid, Marilla.\r\nAnd Miss Stacy explains everything so beautifully. We have to write\r\ncompositions on our field afternoons and I write the best ones.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s very vain of you to say so then. You\xe2\x80\x99d better let your teacher say\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut she _did_ say it, Marilla. And indeed I\xe2\x80\x99m not vain about it. How can\r\nI be, when I\xe2\x80\x99m such a dunce at geometry? Although I\xe2\x80\x99m really beginning\r\nto see through it a little, too. Miss Stacy makes it so clear. Still,\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll never be good at it and I assure you it is a humbling reflection.\r\nBut I love writing compositions. Mostly Miss Stacy lets us choose\r\nour own subjects; but next week we are to write a composition on some\r\nremarkable person. It\xe2\x80\x99s hard to choose among so many remarkable people\r\nwho have lived. Mustn\xe2\x80\x99t it be splendid to be remarkable and have\r\ncompositions written about you after you\xe2\x80\x99re dead? Oh, I would dearly\r\nlove to be remarkable. I think when I grow up I\xe2\x80\x99ll be a trained nurse\r\nand go with the Red Crosses to the field of battle as a messenger of\r\nmercy. That is, if I don\xe2\x80\x99t go out as a foreign missionary. That would\r\nbe very romantic, but one would have to be very good to be a missionary,\r\nand that would be a stumbling block. We have physical culture exercises\r\nevery day, too. They make you graceful and promote digestion.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPromote fiddlesticks!\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla, who honestly thought it was all\r\nnonsense.\r\n\r\nBut all the field afternoons and recitation Fridays and physical culture\r\ncontortions paled before a project which Miss Stacy brought forward in\r\nNovember. This was that the scholars of Avonlea school should get up\r\na concert and hold it in the hall on Christmas Night, for the laudable\r\npurpose of helping to pay for a schoolhouse flag. The pupils one and\r\nall taking graciously to this plan, the preparations for a program\r\nwere begun at once. And of all the excited performers-elect none was so\r\nexcited as Anne Shirley, who threw herself into the undertaking heart\r\nand soul, hampered as she was by Marilla\xe2\x80\x99s disapproval. Marilla thought\r\nit all rank foolishness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s just filling your heads up with nonsense and taking time that\r\nought to be put on your lessons,\xe2\x80\x9d she grumbled. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t approve of\r\nchildren\xe2\x80\x99s getting up concerts and racing about to practices. It makes\r\nthem vain and forward and fond of gadding.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut think of the worthy object,\xe2\x80\x9d pleaded Anne. \xe2\x80\x9cA flag will cultivate a\r\nspirit of patriotism, Marilla.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFudge! There\xe2\x80\x99s precious little patriotism in the thoughts of any of\r\nyou. All you want is a good time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, when you can combine patriotism and fun, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it all right? Of\r\ncourse it\xe2\x80\x99s real nice to be getting up a concert. We\xe2\x80\x99re going to have\r\nsix choruses and Diana is to sing a solo. I\xe2\x80\x99m in two dialogues--\xe2\x80\x98The\r\nSociety for the Suppression of Gossip\xe2\x80\x99 and \xe2\x80\x98The Fairy Queen.\xe2\x80\x99 The boys\r\nare going to have a dialogue too. And I\xe2\x80\x99m to have two recitations,\r\nMarilla. I just tremble when I think of it, but it\xe2\x80\x99s a nice thrilly kind\r\nof tremble. And we\xe2\x80\x99re to have a tableau at the last--\xe2\x80\x98Faith, Hope and\r\nCharity.\xe2\x80\x99 Diana and Ruby and I are to be in it, all draped in white with\r\nflowing hair. I\xe2\x80\x99m to be Hope, with my hands clasped--so--and my eyes\r\nuplifted. I\xe2\x80\x99m going to practice my recitations in the garret. Don\xe2\x80\x99t be\r\nalarmed if you hear me groaning. I have to groan heartrendingly in one\r\nof them, and it\xe2\x80\x99s really hard to get up a good artistic groan, Marilla.\r\nJosie Pye is sulky because she didn\xe2\x80\x99t get the part she wanted in\r\nthe dialogue. She wanted to be the fairy queen. That would have been\r\nridiculous, for who ever heard of a fairy queen as fat as Josie? Fairy\r\nqueens must be slender. Jane Andrews is to be the queen and I am to be\r\none of her maids of honor. Josie says she thinks a red-haired fairy is\r\njust as ridiculous as a fat one, but I do not let myself mind what Josie\r\nsays. I\xe2\x80\x99m to have a wreath of white roses on my hair and Ruby Gillis\r\nis going to lend me her slippers because I haven\xe2\x80\x99t any of my own. It\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nnecessary for fairies to have slippers, you know. You couldn\xe2\x80\x99t imagine\r\na fairy wearing boots, could you? Especially with copper toes? We are\r\ngoing to decorate the hall with creeping spruce and fir mottoes with\r\npink tissue-paper roses in them. And we are all to march in two by two\r\nafter the audience is seated, while Emma White plays a march on the\r\norgan. Oh, Marilla, I know you are not so enthusiastic about it as I am,\r\nbut don\xe2\x80\x99t you hope your little Anne will distinguish herself?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll I hope is that you\xe2\x80\x99ll behave yourself. I\xe2\x80\x99ll be heartily glad when\r\nall this fuss is over and you\xe2\x80\x99ll be able to settle down. You are simply\r\ngood for nothing just now with your head stuffed full of dialogues and\r\ngroans and tableaus. As for your tongue, it\xe2\x80\x99s a marvel it\xe2\x80\x99s not clean\r\nworn out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne sighed and betook herself to the back yard, over which a young new\r\nmoon was shining through the leafless poplar boughs from an apple-green\r\nwestern sky, and where Matthew was splitting wood. Anne perched herself\r\non a block and talked the concert over with him, sure of an appreciative\r\nand sympathetic listener in this instance at least.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell now, I reckon it\xe2\x80\x99s going to be a pretty good concert. And I\r\nexpect you\xe2\x80\x99ll do your part fine,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, smiling down into her eager,\r\nvivacious little face. Anne smiled back at him. Those two were the best\r\nof friends and Matthew thanked his stars many a time and oft that he had\r\nnothing to do with bringing her up. That was Marilla\xe2\x80\x99s exclusive duty;\r\nif it had been his he would have been worried over frequent conflicts\r\nbetween inclination and said duty. As it was, he was free to, \xe2\x80\x9cspoil\r\nAnne\xe2\x80\x9d--Marilla\xe2\x80\x99s phrasing--as much as he liked. But it was not such a\r\nbad arrangement after all; a little \xe2\x80\x9cappreciation\xe2\x80\x9d sometimes does quite\r\nas much good as all the conscientious \xe2\x80\x9cbringing up\xe2\x80\x9d in the world.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXV. Matthew Insists on Puffed Sleeves\r\n\r\n\r\n|MATTHEW was having a bad ten minutes of it. He had come into the\r\nkitchen, in the twilight of a cold, gray December evening, and had sat\r\ndown in the woodbox corner to take off his heavy boots, unconscious of\r\nthe fact that Anne and a bevy of her schoolmates were having a practice\r\nof \xe2\x80\x9cThe Fairy Queen\xe2\x80\x9d in the sitting room. Presently they came trooping\r\nthrough the hall and out into the kitchen, laughing and chattering\r\ngaily. They did not see Matthew, who shrank bashfully back into the\r\nshadows beyond the woodbox with a boot in one hand and a bootjack in the\r\nother, and he watched them shyly for the aforesaid ten minutes as they\r\nput on caps and jackets and talked about the dialogue and the concert.\r\nAnne stood among them, bright eyed and animated as they; but Matthew\r\nsuddenly became conscious that there was something about her different\r\nfrom her mates. And what worried Matthew was that the difference\r\nimpressed him as being something that should not exist. Anne had a\r\nbrighter face, and bigger, starrier eyes, and more delicate features\r\nthan the other; even shy, unobservant Matthew had learned to take note\r\nof these things; but the difference that disturbed him did not consist\r\nin any of these respects. Then in what did it consist?\r\n\r\nMatthew was haunted by this question long after the girls had gone, arm\r\nin arm, down the long, hard-frozen lane and Anne had betaken herself\r\nto her books. He could not refer it to Marilla, who, he felt, would be\r\nquite sure to sniff scornfully and remark that the only difference she\r\nsaw between Anne and the other girls was that they sometimes kept their\r\ntongues quiet while Anne never did. This, Matthew felt, would be no\r\ngreat help.\r\n\r\nHe had recourse to his pipe that evening to help him study it out, much\r\nto Marilla\xe2\x80\x99s disgust. After two hours of smoking and hard reflection\r\nMatthew arrived at a solution of his problem.
