Anne was not dressed like\r\nthe other girls!\r\n\r\nThe more Matthew thought about the matter the more he was convinced that\r\nAnne never had been dressed like the other girls--never since she had\r\ncome to Green Gables. Marilla kept her clothed in plain, dark dresses,\r\nall made after the same unvarying pattern. If Matthew knew there was\r\nsuch a thing as fashion in dress it was as much as he did; but he was\r\nquite sure that Anne\xe2\x80\x99s sleeves did not look at all like the sleeves the\r\nother girls wore. He recalled the cluster of little girls he had seen\r\naround her that evening--all gay in waists of red and blue and pink\r\nand white--and he wondered why Marilla always kept her so plainly and\r\nsoberly gowned.\r\n\r\nOf course, it must be all right. Marilla knew best and Marilla was\r\nbringing her up. Probably some wise, inscrutable motive was to be served\r\nthereby. But surely it would do no harm to let the child have one pretty\r\ndress--something like Diana Barry always wore. Matthew decided that\r\nhe would give her one; that surely could not be objected to as an\r\nunwarranted putting in of his oar. Christmas was only a fortnight off.\r\nA nice new dress would be the very thing for a present. Matthew, with a\r\nsigh of satisfaction, put away his pipe and went to bed, while Marilla\r\nopened all the doors and aired the house.\r\n\r\nThe very next evening Matthew betook himself to Carmody to buy the\r\ndress, determined to get the worst over and have done with it. It would\r\nbe, he felt assured, no trifling ordeal. There were some things Matthew\r\ncould buy and prove himself no mean bargainer; but he knew he would be\r\nat the mercy of shopkeepers when it came to buying a girl\xe2\x80\x99s dress.\r\n\r\nAfter much cogitation Matthew resolved to go to Samuel Lawson\xe2\x80\x99s store\r\ninstead of William Blair\xe2\x80\x99s. To be sure, the Cuthberts always had gone to\r\nWilliam Blair\xe2\x80\x99s; it was almost as much a matter of conscience with them\r\nas to attend the Presbyterian church and vote Conservative. But William\r\nBlair\xe2\x80\x99s two daughters frequently waited on customers there and Matthew\r\nheld them in absolute dread. He could contrive to deal with them when he\r\nknew exactly what he wanted and could point it out; but in such a matter\r\nas this, requiring explanation and consultation, Matthew felt that he\r\nmust be sure of a man behind the counter. So he would go to Lawson\xe2\x80\x99s,\r\nwhere Samuel or his son would wait on him.\r\n\r\nAlas! Matthew did not know that Samuel, in the recent expansion of his\r\nbusiness, had set up a lady clerk also; she was a niece of his wife\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nand a very dashing young person indeed, with a huge, drooping pompadour,\r\nbig, rolling brown eyes, and a most extensive and bewildering smile. She\r\nwas dressed with exceeding smartness and wore several bangle bracelets\r\nthat glittered and rattled and tinkled with every movement of her hands.\r\nMatthew was covered with confusion at finding her there at all; and\r\nthose bangles completely wrecked his wits at one fell swoop.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat can I do for you this evening, Mr. Cuthbert?\xe2\x80\x9d Miss Lucilla Harris\r\ninquired, briskly and ingratiatingly, tapping the counter with both\r\nhands.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you any--any--any--well now, say any garden rakes?\xe2\x80\x9d stammered\r\nMatthew.\r\n\r\nMiss Harris looked somewhat surprised, as well she might, to hear a man\r\ninquiring for garden rakes in the middle of December.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI believe we have one or two left over,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cbut they\xe2\x80\x99re\r\nupstairs in the lumber room. I\xe2\x80\x99ll go and see.\xe2\x80\x9d During her absence\r\nMatthew collected his scattered senses for another effort.\r\n\r\nWhen Miss Harris returned with the rake and cheerfully inquired:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnything else tonight, Mr. Cuthbert?\xe2\x80\x9d Matthew took his courage in\r\nboth hands and replied: \xe2\x80\x9cWell now, since you suggest it, I might as\r\nwell--take--that is--look at--buy some--some hayseed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Harris had heard Matthew Cuthbert called odd. She now concluded\r\nthat he was entirely crazy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe only keep hayseed in the spring,\xe2\x80\x9d she explained loftily. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ve none\r\non hand just now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, certainly--certainly--just as you say,\xe2\x80\x9d stammered unhappy\r\nMatthew, seizing the rake and making for the door. At the threshold he\r\nrecollected that he had not paid for it and he turned miserably back.\r\nWhile Miss Harris was counting out his change he rallied his powers for\r\na final desperate attempt.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell now--if it isn\xe2\x80\x99t too much trouble--I might as well--that is--I\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nlike to look at--at--some sugar.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhite or brown?\xe2\x80\x9d queried Miss Harris patiently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh--well now--brown,\xe2\x80\x9d said Matthew feebly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s a barrel of it over there,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Harris, shaking her\r\nbangles at it. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s the only kind we have.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll--I\xe2\x80\x99ll take twenty pounds of it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Matthew, with beads of\r\nperspiration standing on his forehead.\r\n\r\nMatthew had driven halfway home before he was his own man again. It had\r\nbeen a gruesome experience, but it served him right, he thought, for\r\ncommitting the heresy of going to a strange store. When he reached\r\nhome he hid the rake in the tool house, but the sugar he carried in to\r\nMarilla.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBrown sugar!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Marilla. \xe2\x80\x9cWhatever possessed you to get so\r\nmuch? You know I never use it except for the hired man\xe2\x80\x99s porridge or\r\nblack fruit cake. Jerry\xe2\x80\x99s gone and I\xe2\x80\x99ve made my cake long ago. It\xe2\x80\x99s not\r\ngood sugar, either--it\xe2\x80\x99s coarse and dark--William Blair doesn\xe2\x80\x99t usually\r\nkeep sugar like that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI--I thought it might come in handy sometime,\xe2\x80\x9d said Matthew, making\r\ngood his escape.\r\n\r\nWhen Matthew came to think the matter over he decided that a woman was\r\nrequired to cope with the situation. Marilla was out of the question.\r\nMatthew felt sure she would throw cold water on his project at once.\r\nRemained only Mrs. Lynde; for of no other woman in Avonlea would Matthew\r\nhave dared to ask advice. To Mrs. Lynde he went accordingly, and that\r\ngood lady promptly took the matter out of the harassed man\xe2\x80\x99s hands.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPick out a dress for you to give Anne? To be sure I will. I\xe2\x80\x99m going to\r\nCarmody tomorrow and I\xe2\x80\x99ll attend to it. Have you something particular in\r\nmind? No? Well, I\xe2\x80\x99ll just go by my own judgment then. I believe a nice\r\nrich brown would just suit Anne, and William Blair has some new gloria\r\nin that\xe2\x80\x99s real pretty. Perhaps you\xe2\x80\x99d like me to make it up for her, too,\r\nseeing that if Marilla was to make it Anne would probably get wind of it\r\nbefore the time and spoil the surprise? Well, I\xe2\x80\x99ll do it. No, it isn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\na mite of trouble. I like sewing. I\xe2\x80\x99ll make it to fit my niece, Jenny\r\nGillis, for she and Anne are as like as two peas as far as figure goes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell now, I\xe2\x80\x99m much obliged,\xe2\x80\x9d said Matthew, \xe2\x80\x9cand--and--I dunno--but I\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nlike--I think they make the sleeves different nowadays to what they used\r\nto be. If it wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be asking too much I--I\xe2\x80\x99d like them made in the\r\nnew way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPuffs? Of course. You needn\xe2\x80\x99t worry a speck more about it, Matthew.\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll make it up in the very latest fashion,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Lynde. To herself\r\nshe added when Matthew had gone:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99ll be a real satisfaction to see that poor child wearing something\r\ndecent for once. The way Marilla dresses her is positively ridiculous,\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x99s what, and I\xe2\x80\x99ve ached to tell her so plainly a dozen times. I\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nheld my tongue though, for I can see Marilla doesn\xe2\x80\x99t want advice and she\r\nthinks she knows more about bringing children up than I do for all\r\nshe\xe2\x80\x99s an old maid. But that\xe2\x80\x99s always the way. Folks that has brought up\r\nchildren know that there\xe2\x80\x99s no hard and fast method in the world that\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nsuit every child. But them as never have think it\xe2\x80\x99s all as plain and\r\neasy as Rule of Three--just set your three terms down so fashion, and\r\nthe sum \xe2\x80\x98ll work out correct. But flesh and blood don\xe2\x80\x99t come under the\r\nhead of arithmetic and that\xe2\x80\x99s where Marilla Cuthbert makes her mistake.\r\nI suppose she\xe2\x80\x99s trying to cultivate a spirit of humility in Anne by\r\ndressing her as she does; but it\xe2\x80\x99s more likely to cultivate envy and\r\ndiscontent. I\xe2\x80\x99m sure the child must feel the difference between her\r\nclothes and the other girls\xe2\x80\x99. But to think of Matthew taking notice of\r\nit! That man is waking up after being asleep for over sixty years.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarilla knew all the following fortnight that Matthew had something on\r\nhis mind, but what it was she could not guess, until Christmas Eve, when\r\nMrs. Lynde brought up the new dress. Marilla behaved pretty well on the\r\nwhole, although it is very likely she distrusted Mrs. Lynde\xe2\x80\x99s diplomatic\r\nexplanation that she had made the dress because Matthew was afraid Anne\r\nwould find out about it too soon if Marilla made it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo this is what Matthew has been looking so mysterious over and\r\ngrinning about to himself for two weeks, is it?\xe2\x80\x9d she said a little\r\nstiffly but tolerantly. \xe2\x80\x9cI knew he was up to some foolishness. Well, I\r\nmust say I don\xe2\x80\x99t think Anne needed any more dresses. I made her three\r\ngood, warm, serviceable ones this fall, and anything more is sheer\r\nextravagance. There\xe2\x80\x99s enough material in those sleeves alone to make a\r\nwaist, I declare there is. You\xe2\x80\x99ll just pamper Anne\xe2\x80\x99s vanity, Matthew,\r\nand she\xe2\x80\x99s as vain as a peacock now. Well, I hope she\xe2\x80\x99ll be satisfied\r\nat last, for I know she\xe2\x80\x99s been hankering after those silly sleeves ever\r\nsince they came in, although she never said a word after the first. The\r\npuffs have been getting bigger and more ridiculous right along; they\xe2\x80\x99re\r\nas big as balloons now. Next year anybody who wears them will have to go\r\nthrough a door sideways.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nChristmas morning broke on a beautiful white world. It had been a very\r\nmild December and people had looked forward to a green Christmas; but\r\njust enough snow fell softly in the night to transfigure Avonlea. Anne\r\npeeped out from her frosted gable window with delighted eyes. The firs\r\nin the Haunted Wood were all feathery and wonderful; the birches\r\nand wild cherry trees were outlined in pearl; the plowed fields were\r\nstretches of snowy dimples; and there was a crisp tang in the air that\r\nwas glorious. Anne ran downstairs singing until her voice reechoed\r\nthrough Green Gables.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMerry Christmas, Marilla! Merry Christmas, Matthew! Isn\xe2\x80\x99t it a lovely\r\nChristmas? I\xe2\x80\x99m so glad it\xe2\x80\x99s white. Any other kind of Christmas doesn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nseem real, does it? I don\xe2\x80\x99t like green Christmases. They\xe2\x80\x99re not\r\ngreen--they\xe2\x80\x99re just nasty faded browns and grays. What makes people call\r\nthem green? Why--why--Matthew, is that for me? Oh, Matthew!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMatthew had sheepishly unfolded the dress from its paper swathings and\r\nheld it out with a deprecatory glance at Marilla, who feigned to be\r\ncontemptuously filling the teapot, but nevertheless watched the scene\r\nout of the corner of her eye with a rather interested air.\r\n\r\nAnne took the dress and looked at it in reverent silence. Oh, how pretty\r\nit was--a lovely soft brown gloria with all the gloss of silk; a skirt\r\nwith dainty frills and shirrings; a waist elaborately pintucked in the\r\nmost fashionable way, with a little ruffle of filmy lace at the neck.\r\nBut the sleeves--they were the crowning glory! Long elbow cuffs, and\r\nabove them two beautiful puffs divided by rows of shirring and bows of\r\nbrown-silk ribbon.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a Christmas present for you, Anne,\xe2\x80\x9d said Matthew shyly.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy--why--Anne, don\xe2\x80\x99t you like it? Well now--well now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor Anne\xe2\x80\x99s eyes had suddenly filled with tears.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLike it! Oh, Matthew!\xe2\x80\x9d Anne laid the dress over a chair and clasped\r\nher hands. \xe2\x80\x9cMatthew, it\xe2\x80\x99s perfectly exquisite. Oh, I can never thank you\r\nenough. Look at those sleeves! Oh, it seems to me this must be a happy\r\ndream.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, well, let us have breakfast,\xe2\x80\x9d interrupted Marilla. \xe2\x80\x9cI must say,\r\nAnne, I don\xe2\x80\x99t think you needed the dress; but since Matthew has got it\r\nfor you, see that you take good care of it. There\xe2\x80\x99s a hair ribbon Mrs.\r\nLynde left for you. It\xe2\x80\x99s brown, to match the dress. Come now, sit in.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t see how I\xe2\x80\x99m going to eat breakfast,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne rapturously.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBreakfast seems so commonplace at such an exciting moment. I\xe2\x80\x99d rather\r\nfeast my eyes on that dress. I\xe2\x80\x99m so glad that puffed sleeves are still\r\nfashionable. It did seem to me that I\xe2\x80\x99d never get over it if they went\r\nout before I had a dress with them. I\xe2\x80\x99d never have felt quite satisfied,\r\nyou see. It was lovely of Mrs. Lynde to give me the ribbon too. I feel\r\nthat I ought to be a very good girl indeed. It\xe2\x80\x99s at times like this I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\nsorry I\xe2\x80\x99m not a model little girl; and I always resolve that I will\r\nbe in future. But somehow it\xe2\x80\x99s hard to carry out your resolutions when\r\nirresistible temptations come. Still, I really will make an extra effort\r\nafter this.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen the commonplace breakfast was over Diana appeared, crossing the\r\nwhite log bridge in the hollow, a gay little figure in her crimson\r\nulster. Anne flew down the slope to meet her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMerry Christmas, Diana! And oh, it\xe2\x80\x99s a wonderful Christmas. I\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nsomething splendid to show you. Matthew has given me the loveliest\r\ndress, with _such_ sleeves. I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t even imagine any nicer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve got something more for you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Diana breathlessly. \xe2\x80\x9cHere--this\r\nbox. Aunt Josephine sent us out a big box with ever so many things in\r\nit--and this is for you. I\xe2\x80\x99d have brought it over last night, but it\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t come until after dark, and I never feel very comfortable coming\r\nthrough the Haunted Wood in the dark now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne opened the box and peeped in. First a card with \xe2\x80\x9cFor the Anne-girl\r\nand Merry Christmas,\xe2\x80\x9d written on it; and then, a pair of the daintiest\r\nlittle kid slippers, with beaded toes and satin bows and glistening\r\nbuckles.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne, \xe2\x80\x9cDiana, this is too much. I must be dreaming.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI call it providential,\xe2\x80\x9d said Diana. \xe2\x80\x9cYou won\xe2\x80\x99t have to borrow Ruby\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nslippers now, and that\xe2\x80\x99s a blessing, for they\xe2\x80\x99re two sizes too big for\r\nyou, and it would be awful to hear a fairy shuffling. Josie Pye would\r\nbe delighted. Mind you, Rob Wright went home with Gertie Pye from the\r\npractice night before last. Did you ever hear anything equal to that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAll the Avonlea scholars were in a fever of excitement that day, for the\r\nhall had to be decorated and a last grand rehearsal held.\r\n\r\nThe concert came off in the evening and was a pronounced success. The\r\nlittle hall was crowded; all the performers did excellently well, but\r\nAnne was the bright particular star of the occasion, as even envy, in\r\nthe shape of Josie Pye, dared not deny.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, hasn\xe2\x80\x99t it been a brilliant evening?\xe2\x80\x9d sighed Anne, when it was all\r\nover and she and Diana were walking home together under a dark, starry\r\nsky.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEverything went off very well,\xe2\x80\x9d said Diana practically. \xe2\x80\x9cI guess we\r\nmust have made as much as ten dollars. Mind you, Mr. Allan is going to\r\nsend an account of it to the Charlottetown papers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Diana, will we really see our names in print? It makes me thrill to\r\nthink of it. Your solo was perfectly elegant, Diana. I felt prouder than\r\nyou did when it was encored. I just said to myself, \xe2\x80\x98It is my dear bosom\r\nfriend who is so honored.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, your recitations just brought down the house, Anne. That sad one\r\nwas simply splendid.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I was so nervous, Diana. When Mr. Allan called out my name I really\r\ncannot tell how I ever got up on that platform. I felt as if a million\r\neyes were looking at me and through me, and for one dreadful moment I\r\nwas sure I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t begin at all. Then I thought of my lovely puffed\r\nsleeves and took courage. I knew that I must live up to those sleeves,\r\nDiana. So I started in, and my voice seemed to be coming from ever so\r\nfar away. I just felt like a parrot. It\xe2\x80\x99s providential that I practiced\r\nthose recitations so often up in the garret, or I\xe2\x80\x99d never have been able\r\nto get through. Did I groan all right?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, indeed, you groaned lovely,\xe2\x80\x9d assured Diana.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI saw old Mrs. Sloane wiping away tears when I sat down. It was\r\nsplendid to think I had touched somebody\xe2\x80\x99s heart. It\xe2\x80\x99s so romantic\r\nto take part in a concert, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it? Oh, it\xe2\x80\x99s been a very memorable\r\noccasion indeed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWasn\xe2\x80\x99t the boys\xe2\x80\x99 dialogue fine?\xe2\x80\x9d said Diana. \xe2\x80\x9cGilbert Blythe was just\r\nsplendid. Anne, I do think it\xe2\x80\x99s awful mean the way you treat Gil. Wait\r\ntill I tell you. When you ran off the platform after the fairy dialogue\r\none of your roses fell out of your hair. I saw Gil pick it up and put\r\nit in his breast pocket. There now. You\xe2\x80\x99re so romantic that I\xe2\x80\x99m sure you\r\nought to be pleased at that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s nothing to me what that person does,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne loftily. \xe2\x80\x9cI simply\r\nnever waste a thought on him, Diana.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat night Marilla and Matthew, who had been out to a concert for the\r\nfirst time in twenty years, sat for a while by the kitchen fire after\r\nAnne had gone to bed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell now, I guess our Anne did as well as any of them,\xe2\x80\x9d said Matthew\r\nproudly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, she did,\xe2\x80\x9d admitted Marilla. \xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s a bright child, Matthew. And\r\nshe looked real nice too. I\xe2\x80\x99ve been kind of opposed to this concert\r\nscheme, but I suppose there\xe2\x80\x99s no real harm in it after all. Anyhow, I\r\nwas proud of Anne tonight, although I\xe2\x80\x99m not going to tell her so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell now, I was proud of her and I did tell her so \xe2\x80\x98fore she went\r\nupstairs,\xe2\x80\x9d said Matthew. \xe2\x80\x9cWe must see what we can do for her some of\r\nthese days, Marilla. I guess she\xe2\x80\x99ll need something more than Avonlea\r\nschool by and by.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s time enough to think of that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla. \xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s only\r\nthirteen in March. Though tonight it struck me she was growing quite a\r\nbig girl. Mrs. Lynde made that dress a mite too long, and it makes Anne\r\nlook so tall. She\xe2\x80\x99s quick to learn and I guess the best thing we can do\r\nfor her will be to send her to Queen\xe2\x80\x99s after a spell. But nothing need\r\nbe said about that for a year or two yet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell now, it\xe2\x80\x99ll do no harm to be thinking it over off and on,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMatthew. \xe2\x80\x9cThings like that are all the better for lots of thinking\r\nover.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXVI. The Story Club Is Formed\r\n\r\n\r\n|JUNIOR Avonlea found it hard to settle down to humdrum existence\r\nagain. To Anne in particular things seemed fearfully flat, stale, and\r\nunprofitable after the goblet of excitement she had been sipping for\r\nweeks. Could she go back to the former quiet pleasures of those faraway\r\ndays before the concert? At first, as she told Diana, she did not really\r\nthink she could.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m positively certain, Diana, that life can never be quite the\r\nsame again as it was in those olden days,\xe2\x80\x9d she said mournfully, as if\r\nreferring to a period of at least fifty years back. \xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps after a\r\nwhile I\xe2\x80\x99ll get used to it, but I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid concerts spoil people for\r\neveryday life. I suppose that is why Marilla disapproves of them.\r\nMarilla is such a sensible woman. It must be a great deal better to be\r\nsensible; but still, I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe I\xe2\x80\x99d really want to be a sensible\r\nperson, because they are so unromantic. Mrs. Lynde says there is no\r\ndanger of my ever being one, but you can never tell. I feel just now\r\nthat I may grow up to be sensible yet. But perhaps that is only because\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99m tired. I simply couldn\xe2\x80\x99t sleep last night for ever so long. I just\r\nlay awake and imagined the concert over and over again. That\xe2\x80\x99s one\r\nsplendid thing about such affairs--it\xe2\x80\x99s so lovely to look back to them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEventually, however, Avonlea school slipped back into its old groove\r\nand took up its old interests. To be sure, the concert left traces. Ruby\r\nGillis and Emma White, who had quarreled over a point of precedence in\r\ntheir platform seats, no longer sat at the same desk, and a promising\r\nfriendship of three years was broken up. Josie Pye and Julia Bell did\r\nnot \xe2\x80\x9cspeak\xe2\x80\x9d for three months, because Josie Pye had told Bessie Wright\r\nthat Julia Bell\xe2\x80\x99s bow when she got up to recite made her think of a\r\nchicken jerking its head, and Bessie told Julia. None of the Sloanes\r\nwould have any dealings with the Bells, because the Bells had declared\r\nthat the Sloanes had too much to do in the program, and the Sloanes had\r\nretorted that the Bells were not capable of doing the little they had to\r\ndo properly. Finally, Charlie Sloane fought Moody Spurgeon MacPherson,\r\nbecause Moody Spurgeon had said that Anne Shirley put on airs about\r\nher recitations, and Moody Spurgeon was \xe2\x80\x9clicked\xe2\x80\x9d; consequently Moody\r\nSpurgeon\xe2\x80\x99s sister, Ella May, would not \xe2\x80\x9cspeak\xe2\x80\x9d to Anne Shirley all the\r\nrest of the winter. With the exception of these trifling frictions, work\r\nin Miss Stacy\xe2\x80\x99s little kingdom went on with regularity and smoothness.\r\n\r\nThe winter weeks slipped by. It was an unusually mild winter, with so\r\nlittle snow that Anne and Diana could go to school nearly every day by\r\nway of the Birch Path. On Anne\xe2\x80\x99s birthday they were tripping lightly\r\ndown it, keeping eyes and ears alert amid all their chatter, for Miss\r\nStacy had told them that they must soon write a composition on \xe2\x80\x9cA\r\nWinter\xe2\x80\x99s Walk in the Woods,\xe2\x80\x9d and it behooved them to be observant.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust think, Diana, I\xe2\x80\x99m thirteen years old today,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked Anne in an\r\nawed voice. \xe2\x80\x9cI can scarcely realize that I\xe2\x80\x99m in my teens. When I woke\r\nthis morning it seemed to me that everything must be different. You\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nbeen thirteen for a month, so I suppose it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t seem such a novelty\r\nto you as it does to me. It makes life seem so much more interesting.\r\nIn two more years I\xe2\x80\x99ll be really grown up. It\xe2\x80\x99s a great comfort to think\r\nthat I\xe2\x80\x99ll be able to use big words then without being laughed at.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRuby Gillis says she means to have a beau as soon as she\xe2\x80\x99s fifteen,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said Diana.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRuby Gillis thinks of nothing but beaus,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne disdainfully.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s actually delighted when anyone writes her name up in a\r\ntake-notice for all she pretends to be so mad. But I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid that is an\r\nuncharitable speech. Mrs. Allan says we should never make uncharitable\r\nspeeches; but they do slip out so often before you think, don\xe2\x80\x99t they? I\r\nsimply can\xe2\x80\x99t talk about Josie Pye without making an uncharitable speech,\r\nso I never mention her at all. You may have noticed that. I\xe2\x80\x99m trying to\r\nbe as much like Mrs. Allan as I possibly can, for I think she\xe2\x80\x99s perfect.\r\nMr. Allan thinks so too. Mrs. Lynde says he just worships the ground she\r\ntreads on and she doesn\xe2\x80\x99t really think it right for a minister to\r\nset his affections so much on a mortal being. But then, Diana, even\r\nministers are human and have their besetting sins just like everybody\r\nelse. I had such an interesting talk with Mrs. Allan about besetting\r\nsins last Sunday afternoon. There are just a few things it\xe2\x80\x99s proper\r\nto talk about on Sundays and that is one of them. My besetting sin is\r\nimagining too much and forgetting my duties. I\xe2\x80\x99m striving very hard\r\nto overcome it and now that I\xe2\x80\x99m really thirteen perhaps I\xe2\x80\x99ll get on\r\nbetter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn four more years we\xe2\x80\x99ll be able to put our hair up,\xe2\x80\x9d said Diana.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlice Bell is only sixteen and she is wearing hers up, but I think\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x99s ridiculous. I shall wait until I\xe2\x80\x99m seventeen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I had Alice Bell\xe2\x80\x99s crooked nose,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne decidedly, \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t--but there! I won\xe2\x80\x99t say what I was going to because it was\r\nextremely uncharitable. Besides, I was comparing it with my own nose and\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x99s vanity. I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid I think too much about my nose ever since I\r\nheard that compliment about it long ago. It really is a great comfort to\r\nme. Oh, Diana, look, there\xe2\x80\x99s a rabbit. That\xe2\x80\x99s something to remember for\r\nour woods composition. I really think the woods are just as lovely in\r\nwinter as in summer. They\xe2\x80\x99re so white and still, as if they were asleep\r\nand dreaming pretty dreams.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t mind writing that composition when its time comes,\xe2\x80\x9d sighed\r\nDiana. \xe2\x80\x9cI can manage to write about the woods, but the one we\xe2\x80\x99re to\r\nhand in Monday is terrible. The idea of Miss Stacy telling us to write a\r\nstory out of our own heads!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, it\xe2\x80\x99s as easy as wink,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s easy for you because you have an imagination,\xe2\x80\x9d retorted Diana,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cbut what would you do if you had been born without one? I suppose you\r\nhave your composition all done?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne nodded, trying hard not to look virtuously complacent and failing\r\nmiserably.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wrote it last Monday evening. It\xe2\x80\x99s called \xe2\x80\x98The Jealous Rival; or In\r\nDeath Not Divided.\xe2\x80\x99 I read it to Marilla and she said it was stuff and\r\nnonsense. Then I read it to Matthew and he said it was fine. That is\r\nthe kind of critic I like. It\xe2\x80\x99s a sad, sweet story. I just cried like\r\na child while I was writing it. It\xe2\x80\x99s about two beautiful maidens called\r\nCordelia Montmorency and Geraldine Seymour who lived in the same village\r\nand were devotedly attached to each other. Cordelia was a regal brunette\r\nwith a coronet of midnight hair and duskly flashing eyes. Geraldine was\r\na queenly blonde with hair like spun gold and velvety purple eyes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never saw anybody with purple eyes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Diana dubiously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNeither did I. I just imagined them. I wanted something out of the\r\ncommon. Geraldine had an alabaster brow too. I\xe2\x80\x99ve found out what an\r\nalabaster brow is. That is one of the advantages of being thirteen. You\r\nknow so much more than you did when you were only twelve.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, what became of Cordelia and Geraldine?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Diana, who was\r\nbeginning to feel rather interested in their fate.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey grew in beauty side by side until they were sixteen. Then Bertram\r\nDeVere came to their native village and fell in love with the fair\r\nGeraldine. He saved her life when her horse ran away with her in a\r\ncarriage, and she fainted in his arms and he carried her home three\r\nmiles; because, you understand, the carriage was all smashed up. I found\r\nit rather hard to imagine the proposal because I had no experience to\r\ngo by. I asked Ruby Gillis if she knew anything about how men proposed\r\nbecause I thought she\xe2\x80\x99d likely be an authority on the subject, having so\r\nmany sisters married. Ruby told me she was hid in the hall pantry when\r\nMalcolm Andres proposed to her sister Susan. She said Malcolm told Susan\r\nthat his dad had given him the farm in his own name and then said, \xe2\x80\x98What\r\ndo you say, darling pet, if we get hitched this fall?\xe2\x80\x99 And Susan said,\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes--no--I don\xe2\x80\x99t know--let me see\xe2\x80\x99--and there they were, engaged as\r\nquick as that. But I didn\xe2\x80\x99t think that sort of a proposal was a very\r\nromantic one, so in the end I had to imagine it out as well as I could.\r\nI made it very flowery and poetical and Bertram went on his knees,\r\nalthough Ruby Gillis says it isn\xe2\x80\x99t done nowadays. Geraldine accepted\r\nhim in a speech a page long. I can tell you I took a lot of trouble\r\nwith that speech. I rewrote it five times and I look upon it as my\r\nmasterpiece. Bertram gave her a diamond ring and a ruby necklace\r\nand told her they would go to Europe for a wedding tour, for he was\r\nimmensely wealthy. But then, alas, shadows began to darken over their\r\npath. Cordelia was secretly in love with Bertram herself and when\r\nGeraldine told her about the engagement she was simply furious,\r\nespecially when she saw the necklace and the diamond ring. All her\r\naffection for Geraldine turned to bitter hate and she vowed that she\r\nshould never marry Bertram. But she pretended to be Geraldine\xe2\x80\x99s friend\r\nthe same as ever. One evening they were standing on the bridge over a\r\nrushing turbulent stream and Cordelia, thinking they were alone, pushed\r\nGeraldine over the brink with a wild, mocking, \xe2\x80\x98Ha, ha, ha.\xe2\x80\x99 But Bertram\r\nsaw it all and he at once plunged into the current, exclaiming, \xe2\x80\x98I\r\nwill save thee, my peerless Geraldine.\xe2\x80\x99 But alas, he had forgotten he\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t swim, and they were both drowned, clasped in each other\xe2\x80\x99s arms.\r\nTheir bodies were washed ashore soon afterwards. They were buried in the\r\none grave and their funeral was most imposing, Diana. It\xe2\x80\x99s so much\r\nmore romantic to end a story up with a funeral than a wedding. As for\r\nCordelia, she went insane with remorse and was shut up in a lunatic\r\nasylum. I thought that was a poetical retribution for her crime.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow perfectly lovely!\xe2\x80\x9d sighed Diana, who belonged to Matthew\xe2\x80\x99s school\r\nof critics. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t see how you can make up such thrilling things out\r\nof your own head, Anne. I wish my imagination was as good as yours.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt would be if you\xe2\x80\x99d only cultivate it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne cheeringly. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\njust thought of a plan, Diana. Let you and me have a story club all our\r\nown and write stories for practice. I\xe2\x80\x99ll help you along until you can\r\ndo them by yourself. You ought to cultivate your imagination, you know.\r\nMiss Stacy says so. Only we must take the right way. I told her about\r\nthe Haunted Wood, but she said we went the wrong way about it in that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was how the story club came into existence. It was limited to Diana\r\nand Anne at first, but soon it was extended to include Jane Andrews\r\nand Ruby Gillis and one or two others who felt that their imaginations\r\nneeded cultivating. No boys were allowed in it--although Ruby Gillis\r\nopined that their admission would make it more exciting--and each member\r\nhad to produce one story a week.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s extremely interesting,\xe2\x80\x9d Anne told Marilla. \xe2\x80\x9cEach girl has to read\r\nher story out loud and then we talk it over. We are going to keep them\r\nall sacredly and have them to read to our descendants. We each write\r\nunder a nom-de-plume. Mine is Rosamond Montmorency. All the girls\r\ndo pretty well. Ruby Gillis is rather sentimental. She puts too much\r\nlovemaking into her stories and you know too much is worse than too\r\nlittle. Jane never puts any because she says it makes her feel so silly\r\nwhen she had to read it out loud. Jane\xe2\x80\x99s stories are extremely sensible.\r\nThen Diana puts too many murders into hers. She says most of the time\r\nshe doesn\xe2\x80\x99t know what to do with the people so she kills them off to get\r\nrid of them. I mostly always have to tell them what to write about, but\r\nthat isn\xe2\x80\x99t hard for I\xe2\x80\x99ve millions of ideas.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think this story-writing business is the foolishest yet,\xe2\x80\x9d scoffed\r\nMarilla. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll get a pack of nonsense into your heads and waste time\r\nthat should be put on your lessons. Reading stories is bad enough but\r\nwriting them is worse.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut we\xe2\x80\x99re so careful to put a moral into them all, Marilla,\xe2\x80\x9d explained\r\nAnne. \xe2\x80\x9cI insist upon that. All the good people are rewarded and all\r\nthe bad ones are suitably punished. I\xe2\x80\x99m sure that must have a wholesome\r\neffect. The moral is the great thing. Mr. Allan says so. I read one of\r\nmy stories to him and Mrs. Allan and they both agreed that the moral was\r\nexcellent. Only they laughed in the wrong places. I like it better when\r\npeople cry. Jane and Ruby almost always cry when I come to the pathetic\r\nparts. Diana wrote her Aunt Josephine about our club and her Aunt\r\nJosephine wrote back that we were to send her some of our stories. So\r\nwe copied out four of our very best and sent them. Miss Josephine Barry\r\nwrote back that she had never read anything so amusing in her life. That\r\nkind of puzzled us because the stories were all very pathetic and almost\r\neverybody died. But I\xe2\x80\x99m glad Miss Barry liked them. It shows our club\r\nis doing some good in the world. Mrs. Allan says that ought to be our\r\nobject in everything. I do really try to make it my object but I forget\r\nso often when I\xe2\x80\x99m having fun. I hope I shall be a little like Mrs. Allan\r\nwhen I grow up. Do you think there is any prospect of it, Marilla?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t say there was a great deal\xe2\x80\x9d was Marilla\xe2\x80\x99s encouraging\r\nanswer. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sure Mrs. Allan was never such a silly, forgetful little\r\ngirl as you are.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; but she wasn\xe2\x80\x99t always so good as she is now either,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne\r\nseriously. \xe2\x80\x9cShe told me so herself--that is, she said she was a dreadful\r\nmischief when she was a girl and was always getting into scrapes. I felt\r\nso encouraged when I heard that. Is it very wicked of me, Marilla,\r\nto feel encouraged when I hear that other people have been bad and\r\nmischievous? Mrs. Lynde says it is. Mrs. Lynde says she always feels\r\nshocked when she hears of anyone ever having been naughty, no matter how\r\nsmall they were. Mrs. Lynde says she once heard a minister confess that\r\nwhen he was a boy he stole a strawberry tart out of his aunt\xe2\x80\x99s pantry\r\nand she never had any respect for that minister again. Now, I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nhave felt that way. I\xe2\x80\x99d have thought that it was real noble of him to\r\nconfess it, and I\xe2\x80\x99d have thought what an encouraging thing it would be\r\nfor small boys nowadays who do naughty things and are sorry for them\r\nto know that perhaps they may grow up to be ministers in spite of it.\r\nThat\xe2\x80\x99s how I\xe2\x80\x99d feel, Marilla.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe way I feel at present, Anne,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla, \xe2\x80\x9cis that it\xe2\x80\x99s high time\r\nyou had those dishes washed. You\xe2\x80\x99ve taken half an hour longer than\r\nyou should with all your chattering. Learn to work first and talk\r\nafterwards.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXVII. Vanity and Vexation of Spirit\r\n\r\n\r\nMarilla, walking home one late April evening from an Aid meeting,\r\nrealized that the winter was over and gone with the thrill of delight\r\nthat spring never fails to bring to the oldest and saddest as well as to\r\nthe youngest and merriest. Marilla was not given to subjective analysis\r\nof her thoughts and feelings. She probably imagined that she was\r\nthinking about the Aids and their missionary box and the new carpet\r\nfor the vestry room, but under these reflections was a harmonious\r\nconsciousness of red fields smoking into pale-purply mists in the\r\ndeclining sun, of long, sharp-pointed fir shadows falling over the\r\nmeadow beyond the brook, of still, crimson-budded maples around a\r\nmirrorlike wood pool, of a wakening in the world and a stir of hidden\r\npulses under the gray sod. The spring was abroad in the land and\r\nMarilla\xe2\x80\x99s sober, middle-aged step was lighter and swifter because of its\r\ndeep, primal gladness.\r\n\r\nHer eyes dwelt affectionately on Green Gables, peering through its\r\nnetwork of trees and reflecting the sunlight back from its windows in\r\nseveral little coruscations of glory. Marilla, as she picked her steps\r\nalong the damp lane, thought that it was really a satisfaction to know\r\nthat she was going home to a briskly snapping wood fire and a table\r\nnicely spread for tea, instead of to the cold comfort of old Aid meeting\r\nevenings before Anne had come to Green Gables.\r\n\r\nConsequently, when Marilla entered her kitchen and found the fire black\r\nout, with no sign of Anne anywhere, she felt justly disappointed and\r\nirritated. She had told Anne to be sure and have tea ready at five\r\no\xe2\x80\x99clock, but now she must hurry to take off her second-best dress and\r\nprepare the meal herself against Matthew\xe2\x80\x99s return from plowing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll settle Miss Anne when she comes home,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla grimly, as\r\nshe shaved up kindlings with a carving knife and with more vim than was\r\nstrictly necessary. Matthew had come in and was waiting patiently for\r\nhis tea in his corner. \xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s gadding off somewhere with Diana, writing\r\nstories or practicing dialogues or some such tomfoolery, and never\r\nthinking once about the time or her duties. She\xe2\x80\x99s just got to be pulled\r\nup short and sudden on this sort of thing. I don\xe2\x80\x99t care if Mrs. Allan\r\ndoes say she\xe2\x80\x99s the brightest and sweetest child she ever knew. She may\r\nbe bright and sweet enough, but her head is full of nonsense and there\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nnever any knowing what shape it\xe2\x80\x99ll break out in next. Just as soon as\r\nshe grows out of one freak she takes up with another. But there! Here I\r\nam saying the very thing I was so riled with Rachel Lynde for saying at\r\nthe Aid today. I was real glad when Mrs. Allan spoke up for Anne, for\r\nif she hadn\xe2\x80\x99t I know I\xe2\x80\x99d have said something too sharp to Rachel before\r\neverybody. Anne\xe2\x80\x99s got plenty of faults, goodness knows, and far be it\r\nfrom me to deny it. But I\xe2\x80\x99m bringing her up and not Rachel Lynde, who\xe2\x80\x99d\r\npick faults in the Angel Gabriel himself if he lived in Avonlea. Just\r\nthe same, Anne has no business to leave the house like this when I told\r\nher she was to stay home this afternoon and look after things. I must\r\nsay, with all her faults, I never found her disobedient or untrustworthy\r\nbefore and I\xe2\x80\x99m real sorry to find her so now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell now, I dunno,\xe2\x80\x9d said Matthew, who, being patient and wise and,\r\nabove all, hungry, had deemed it best to let Marilla talk her wrath\r\nout unhindered, having learned by experience that she got through\r\nwith whatever work was on hand much quicker if not delayed by untimely\r\nargument. \xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps you\xe2\x80\x99re judging her too hasty, Marilla. Don\xe2\x80\x99t call her\r\nuntrustworthy until you\xe2\x80\x99re sure she has disobeyed you. Mebbe it can all\r\nbe explained--Anne\xe2\x80\x99s a great hand at explaining.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s not here when I told her to stay,\xe2\x80\x9d retorted Marilla. \xe2\x80\x9cI reckon\r\nshe\xe2\x80\x99ll find it hard to explain _that_ to my satisfaction. Of course I knew\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99d take her part, Matthew. But I\xe2\x80\x99m bringing her up, not you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was dark when supper was ready, and still no sign of Anne, coming\r\nhurriedly over the log bridge or up Lover\xe2\x80\x99s Lane, breathless and\r\nrepentant with a sense of neglected duties. Marilla washed and put away\r\nthe dishes grimly. Then, wanting a candle to light her way down the\r\ncellar, she went up to the east gable for the one that generally stood\r\non Anne\xe2\x80\x99s table. Lighting it, she turned around to see Anne herself\r\nlying on the bed, face downward among the pillows.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMercy on us,\xe2\x80\x9d said astonished Marilla, \xe2\x80\x9chave you been asleep, Anne?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d was the muffled reply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you sick then?\xe2\x80\x9d demanded Marilla anxiously, going over to the bed.\r\n\r\nAnne cowered deeper into her pillows as if desirous of hiding herself\r\nforever from mortal eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. But please, Marilla, go away and don\xe2\x80\x99t look at me. I\xe2\x80\x99m in the\r\ndepths of despair and I don\xe2\x80\x99t care who gets head in class or writes the\r\nbest composition or sings in the Sunday-school choir any more. Little\r\nthings like that are of no importance now because I don\xe2\x80\x99t suppose I\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\never be able to go anywhere again. My career is closed. Please, Marilla,\r\ngo away and don\xe2\x80\x99t look at me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid anyone ever hear the like?\xe2\x80\x9d the mystified Marilla wanted to know.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnne Shirley, whatever is the matter with you? What have you done? Get\r\nright up this minute and tell me. This minute, I say. There now, what is\r\nit?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne had slid to the floor in despairing obedience.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook at my hair, Marilla,\xe2\x80\x9d she whispered.\r\n\r\nAccordingly, Marilla lifted her candle and looked scrutinizingly at\r\nAnne\xe2\x80\x99s hair, flowing in heavy masses down her back. It certainly had a\r\nvery strange appearance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnne Shirley, what have you done to your hair? Why, it\xe2\x80\x99s _green!_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGreen it might be called, if it were any earthly color--a queer,\r\ndull, bronzy green, with streaks here and there of the original red\r\nto heighten the ghastly effect. Never in all her life had Marilla seen\r\nanything so grotesque as Anne\xe2\x80\x99s hair at that moment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, it\xe2\x80\x99s green,\xe2\x80\x9d moaned Anne. \xe2\x80\x9cI thought nothing could be as bad as\r\nred hair. But now I know it\xe2\x80\x99s ten times worse to have green hair. Oh,\r\nMarilla, you little know how utterly wretched I am.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI little know how you got into this fix, but I mean to find out,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMarilla. \xe2\x80\x9cCome right down to the kitchen--it\xe2\x80\x99s too cold up here--and\r\ntell me just what you\xe2\x80\x99ve done. I\xe2\x80\x99ve been expecting something queer for\r\nsome time. You haven\xe2\x80\x99t got into any scrape for over two months, and I\r\nwas sure another one was due. Now, then, what did you do to your hair?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI dyed it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDyed it! Dyed your hair! Anne Shirley, didn\xe2\x80\x99t you know it was a wicked\r\nthing to do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I knew it was a little wicked,\xe2\x80\x9d admitted Anne. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I thought it\r\nwas worth while to be a little wicked to get rid of red hair. I counted\r\nthe cost, Marilla. Besides, I meant to be extra good in other ways to\r\nmake up for it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla sarcastically, \xe2\x80\x9cif I\xe2\x80\x99d decided it was worth while\r\nto dye my hair I\xe2\x80\x99d have dyed it a decent color at least. I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have\r\ndyed it green.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I didn\xe2\x80\x99t mean to dye it green, Marilla,\xe2\x80\x9d protested Anne dejectedly.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I was wicked I meant to be wicked to some purpose. He said it would\r\nturn my hair a beautiful raven black--he positively assured me that it\r\nwould. How could I doubt his word, Marilla? I know what it feels like\r\nto have your word doubted. And Mrs. Allan says we should never suspect\r\nanyone of not telling us the truth unless we have proof that they\xe2\x80\x99re\r\nnot. I have proof now--green hair is proof enough for anybody. But I\r\nhadn\xe2\x80\x99t then and I believed every word he said _implicitly_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho said? Who are you talking about?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe peddler that was here this afternoon. I bought the dye from him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnne Shirley, how often have I told you never to let one of those\r\nItalians in the house! I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe in encouraging them to come\r\naround at all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I didn\xe2\x80\x99t let him in the house. I remembered what you told me, and I\r\nwent out, carefully shut the door, and looked at his things on the step.\r\nBesides, he wasn\xe2\x80\x99t an Italian--he was a German Jew. He had a big box\r\nfull of very interesting things and he told me he was working hard to\r\nmake enough money to bring his wife and children out from Germany. He\r\nspoke so feelingly about them that it touched my heart. I wanted to buy\r\nsomething from him to help him in such a worthy object. Then all at once\r\nI saw the bottle of hair dye. The peddler said it was warranted to dye\r\nany hair a beautiful raven black and wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t wash off. In a trice I\r\nsaw myself with beautiful raven-black hair and the temptation was\r\nirresistible. But the price of the bottle was seventy-five cents and I\r\nhad only fifty cents left out of my chicken money. I think the peddler\r\nhad a very kind heart, for he said that, seeing it was me, he\xe2\x80\x99d sell it\r\nfor fifty cents and that was just giving it away. So I bought it, and as\r\nsoon as he had gone I came up here and applied it with an old hairbrush\r\nas the directions said. I used up the whole bottle, and oh, Marilla,\r\nwhen I saw the dreadful color it turned my hair I repented of being\r\nwicked, I can tell you. And I\xe2\x80\x99ve been repenting ever since.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I hope you\xe2\x80\x99ll repent to good purpose,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla severely,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cand that you\xe2\x80\x99ve got your eyes opened to where your vanity has led you,\r\nAnne. Goodness knows what\xe2\x80\x99s to be done. I suppose the first thing is to\r\ngive your hair a good washing and see if that will do any good.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAccordingly, Anne washed her hair, scrubbing it vigorously with soap and\r\nwater, but for all the difference it made she might as well have been\r\nscouring its original red. The peddler had certainly spoken the truth\r\nwhen he declared that the dye wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t wash off, however his veracity\r\nmight be impeached in other respects.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Marilla, what shall I do?\xe2\x80\x9d questioned Anne in tears. \xe2\x80\x9cI can never\r\nlive this down. People have pretty well forgotten my other mistakes--the\r\nliniment cake and setting Diana drunk and flying into a temper with\r\nMrs. Lynde. But they\xe2\x80\x99ll never forget this. They will think I am not\r\nrespectable. Oh, Marilla, \xe2\x80\x98what a tangled web we weave when first we\r\npractice to deceive.\xe2\x80\x99 That is poetry, but it is true. And oh, how Josie\r\nPye will laugh! Marilla, I _cannot_ face Josie Pye. I am the unhappiest\r\ngirl in Prince Edward Island.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne\xe2\x80\x99s unhappiness continued for a week. During that time she went\r\nnowhere and shampooed her hair every day. Diana alone of outsiders knew\r\nthe fatal secret, but she promised solemnly never to tell, and it may\r\nbe stated here and now that she kept her word. At the end of the week\r\nMarilla said decidedly:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s no use, Anne. That is fast dye if ever there was any. Your hair\r\nmust be cut off; there is no other way. You can\xe2\x80\x99t go out with it looking\r\nlike that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne\xe2\x80\x99s lips quivered, but she realized the bitter truth of Marilla\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nremarks. With a dismal sigh she went for the scissors.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease cut it off at once, Marilla, and have it over. Oh, I feel that\r\nmy heart is broken. This is such an unromantic affliction. The girls in\r\nbooks lose their hair in fevers or sell it to get money for some good\r\ndeed, and I\xe2\x80\x99m sure I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t mind losing my hair in some such fashion\r\nhalf so much. But there is nothing comforting in having your hair cut\r\noff because you\xe2\x80\x99ve dyed it a dreadful color, is there? I\xe2\x80\x99m going to weep\r\nall the time you\xe2\x80\x99re cutting it off, if it won\xe2\x80\x99t interfere. It seems such\r\na tragic thing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne wept then, but later on, when she went upstairs and looked in the\r\nglass, she was calm with despair. Marilla had done her work thoroughly\r\nand it had been necessary to shingle the hair as closely as possible.\r\nThe result was not becoming, to state the case as mildly as may be. Anne\r\npromptly turned her glass to the wall.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll never, never look at myself again until my hair grows,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nexclaimed passionately.\r\n\r\nThen she suddenly righted the glass.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I will, too. I\xe2\x80\x99d do penance for being wicked that way. I\xe2\x80\x99ll look\r\nat myself every time I come to my room and see how ugly I am. And I\r\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t try to imagine it away, either. I never thought I was vain about\r\nmy hair, of all things, but now I know I was, in spite of its being\r\nred, because it was so long and thick and curly. I expect something will\r\nhappen to my nose next.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne\xe2\x80\x99s clipped head made a sensation in school on the following Monday,\r\nbut to her relief nobody guessed the real reason for it, not even Josie\r\nPye, who, however, did not fail to inform Anne that she looked like a\r\nperfect scarecrow.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t say anything when Josie said that to me,\xe2\x80\x9d Anne confided\r\nthat evening to Marilla, who was lying on the sofa after one of her\r\nheadaches, \xe2\x80\x9cbecause I thought it was part of my punishment and I ought\r\nto bear it patiently. It\xe2\x80\x99s hard to be told you look like a scarecrow\r\nand I wanted to say something back. But I didn\xe2\x80\x99t. I just swept her one\r\nscornful look and then I forgave her. It makes you feel very virtuous\r\nwhen you forgive people, doesn\xe2\x80\x99t it? I mean to devote all my energies\r\nto being good after this and I shall never try to be beautiful again. Of\r\ncourse it\xe2\x80\x99s better to be good. I know it is, but it\xe2\x80\x99s sometimes so hard\r\nto believe a thing even when you know it. I do really want to be good,\r\nMarilla, like you and Mrs. Allan and Miss Stacy, and grow up to be a\r\ncredit to you. Diana says when my hair begins to grow to tie a black\r\nvelvet ribbon around my head with a bow at one side. She says she\r\nthinks it will be very becoming. I will call it a snood--that sounds so\r\nromantic. But am I talking too much, Marilla? Does it hurt your head?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy head is better now. It was terrible bad this afternoon, though.\r\nThese headaches of mine are getting worse and worse. I\xe2\x80\x99ll have to see\r\na doctor about them. As for your chatter, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know that I mind\r\nit--I\xe2\x80\x99ve got so used to it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhich was Marilla\xe2\x80\x99s way of saying that she liked to hear it.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXVIII. An Unfortunate Lily Maid\r\n\r\n\r\n|OF course you must be Elaine, Anne,\xe2\x80\x9d said Diana. \xe2\x80\x9cI could never have\r\nthe courage to float down there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNor I,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ruby Gillis, with a shiver. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t mind floating down\r\nwhen there\xe2\x80\x99s two or three of us in the flat and we can sit up. It\xe2\x80\x99s fun\r\nthen. But to lie down and pretend I was dead--I just couldn\xe2\x80\x99t. I\xe2\x80\x99d die\r\nreally of fright.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course it would be romantic,\xe2\x80\x9d conceded Jane Andrews, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I know I\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t keep still. I\xe2\x80\x99d be popping up every minute or so to see where I\r\nwas and if I wasn\xe2\x80\x99t drifting too far out. And you know, Anne, that would\r\nspoil the effect.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut it\xe2\x80\x99s so ridiculous to have a redheaded Elaine,\xe2\x80\x9d mourned Anne. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m\r\nnot afraid to float down and I\xe2\x80\x99d love to be Elaine. But it\xe2\x80\x99s ridiculous\r\njust the same. Ruby ought to be Elaine because she is so fair and has\r\nsuch lovely long golden hair--Elaine had \xe2\x80\x98all her bright hair streaming\r\ndown,\xe2\x80\x99 you know. And Elaine was the lily maid. Now, a red-haired person\r\ncannot be a lily maid.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour complexion is just as fair as Ruby\xe2\x80\x99s,\xe2\x80\x9d said Diana earnestly, \xe2\x80\x9cand\r\nyour hair is ever so much darker than it used to be before you cut it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, do you really think so?\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Anne, flushing sensitively with\r\ndelight. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve sometimes thought it was myself--but I never dared to ask\r\nanyone for fear she would tell me it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t. Do you think it could be\r\ncalled auburn now, Diana?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, and I think it is real pretty,\xe2\x80\x9d said Diana, looking admiringly at\r\nthe short, silky curls that clustered over Anne\xe2\x80\x99s head and were held in\r\nplace by a very jaunty black velvet ribbon and bow.\r\n\r\nThey were standing on the bank of the pond, below Orchard Slope, where\r\na little headland fringed with birches ran out from the bank; at its tip\r\nwas a small wooden platform built out into the water for the convenience\r\nof fishermen and duck hunters. Ruby and Jane were spending the midsummer\r\nafternoon with Diana, and Anne had come over to play with them.\r\n\r\nAnne and Diana had spent most of their playtime that summer on and about\r\nthe pond. Idlewild was a thing of the past, Mr. Bell having ruthlessly\r\ncut down the little circle of trees in his back pasture in the spring.\r\nAnne had sat among the stumps and wept, not without an eye to the\r\nromance of it; but she was speedily consoled, for, after all, as she and\r\nDiana said, big girls of thirteen, going on fourteen, were too old for\r\nsuch childish amusements as playhouses, and there were more fascinating\r\nsports to be found about the pond. It was splendid to fish for trout\r\nover the bridge and the two girls learned to row themselves about in the\r\nlittle flat-bottomed dory Mr. Barry kept for duck shooting.\r\n\r\nIt was Anne\xe2\x80\x99s idea that they dramatize Elaine. They had studied\r\nTennyson\xe2\x80\x99s poem in school the preceding winter, the Superintendent of\r\nEducation having prescribed it in the English course for the Prince\r\nEdward Island schools. They had analyzed and parsed it and torn it to\r\npieces in general until it was a wonder there was any meaning at all\r\nleft in it for them, but at least the fair lily maid and Lancelot and\r\nGuinevere and King Arthur had become very real people to them, and Anne\r\nwas devoured by secret regret that she had not been born in Camelot.\r\nThose days, she said, were so much more romantic than the present.\r\n\r\nAnne\xe2\x80\x99s plan was hailed with enthusiasm. The girls had discovered that if\r\nthe flat were pushed off from the landing place it would drift down\r\nwith the current under the bridge and finally strand itself on another\r\nheadland lower down which ran out at a curve in the pond. They had often\r\ngone down like this and nothing could be more convenient for playing\r\nElaine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I\xe2\x80\x99ll be Elaine,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne, yielding reluctantly, for, although\r\nshe would have been delighted to play the principal character, yet\r\nher artistic sense demanded fitness for it and this, she felt, her\r\nlimitations made impossible. \xe2\x80\x9cRuby, you must be King Arthur and Jane\r\nwill be Guinevere and Diana must be Lancelot. But first you must be the\r\nbrothers and the father. We can\xe2\x80\x99t have the old dumb servitor because\r\nthere isn\xe2\x80\x99t room for two in the flat when one is lying down. We must\r\npall the barge all its length in blackest samite. That old black shawl\r\nof your mother\xe2\x80\x99s will be just the thing, Diana.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe black shawl having been procured, Anne spread it over the flat and\r\nthen lay down on the bottom, with closed eyes and hands folded over her\r\nbreast.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, she does look really dead,\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Ruby Gillis nervously,\r\nwatching the still, white little face under the flickering shadows of\r\nthe birches. \xe2\x80\x9cIt makes me feel frightened, girls. Do you suppose it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nreally right to act like this? Mrs. Lynde says that all play-acting is\r\nabominably wicked.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRuby, you shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t talk about Mrs. Lynde,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne severely. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\r\nspoils the effect because this is hundreds of years before Mrs. Lynde\r\nwas born. Jane, you arrange this. It\xe2\x80\x99s silly for Elaine to be talking\r\nwhen she\xe2\x80\x99s dead.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJane rose to the occasion. Cloth of gold for coverlet there was none,\r\nbut an old piano scarf of yellow Japanese crepe was an excellent\r\nsubstitute. A white lily was not obtainable just then, but the effect of\r\na tall blue iris placed in one of Anne\xe2\x80\x99s folded hands was all that could\r\nbe desired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, she\xe2\x80\x99s all ready,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jane. \xe2\x80\x9cWe must kiss her quiet brows\r\nand, Diana, you say, \xe2\x80\x98Sister, farewell forever,\xe2\x80\x99 and Ruby, you say,\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Farewell, sweet sister,\xe2\x80\x99 both of you as sorrowfully as you possibly\r\ncan. Anne, for goodness sake smile a little. You know Elaine \xe2\x80\x98lay as\r\nthough she smiled.\xe2\x80\x99 That\xe2\x80\x99s better. Now push the flat off.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe flat was accordingly pushed off, scraping roughly over an old\r\nembedded stake in the process. Diana and Jane and Ruby only waited long\r\nenough to see it caught in the current and headed for the bridge before\r\nscampering up through the woods, across the road, and down to the lower\r\nheadland where, as Lancelot and Guinevere and the King, they were to be\r\nin readiness to receive the lily maid.\r\n\r\nFor a few minutes Anne, drifting slowly down, enjoyed the romance of her\r\nsituation to the full. Then something happened not at all romantic. The\r\nflat began to leak. In a very few moments it was necessary for Elaine\r\nto scramble to her feet, pick up her cloth of gold coverlet and pall\r\nof blackest samite and gaze blankly at a big crack in the bottom of her\r\nbarge through which the water was literally pouring. That sharp stake at\r\nthe landing had torn off the strip of batting nailed on the flat. Anne\r\ndid not know this, but it did not take her long to realize that she was\r\nin a dangerous plight. At this rate the flat would fill and sink long\r\nbefore it could drift to the lower headland. Where were the oars? Left\r\nbehind at the landing!\r\n\r\nAnne gave one gasping little scream which nobody ever heard; she was\r\nwhite to the lips, but she did not lose her self-possession. There was\r\none chance--just one.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was horribly frightened,\xe2\x80\x9d she told Mrs. Allan the next day, \xe2\x80\x9cand it\r\nseemed like years while the flat was drifting down to the bridge and the\r\nwater rising in it every moment. I prayed, Mrs. Allan, most earnestly,\r\nbut I didn\xe2\x80\x99t shut my eyes to pray, for I knew the only way God could\r\nsave me was to let the flat float close enough to one of the bridge\r\npiles for me to climb up on it. You know the piles are just old tree\r\ntrunks and there are lots of knots and old branch stubs on them. It was\r\nproper to pray, but I had to do my part by watching out and right well\r\nI knew it. I just said, \xe2\x80\x98Dear God, please take the flat close to a pile\r\nand I\xe2\x80\x99ll do the rest,\xe2\x80\x99 over and over again. Under such circumstances you\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t think much about making a flowery prayer. But mine was answered,\r\nfor the flat bumped right into a pile for a minute and I flung the scarf\r\nand the shawl over my shoulder and scrambled up on a big providential\r\nstub. And there I was, Mrs. Allan, clinging to that slippery old pile\r\nwith no way of getting up or down. It was a very unromantic position,\r\nbut I didn\xe2\x80\x99t think about that at the time. You don\xe2\x80\x99t think much about\r\nromance when you have just escaped from a watery grave. I said a\r\ngrateful prayer at once and then I gave all my attention to holding on\r\ntight, for I knew I should probably have to depend on human aid to get\r\nback to dry land.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe flat drifted under the bridge and then promptly sank in midstream.\r\nRuby, Jane, and Diana, already awaiting it on the lower headland, saw it\r\ndisappear before their very eyes and had not a doubt but that Anne\r\nhad gone down with it. For a moment they stood still, white as sheets,\r\nfrozen with horror at the tragedy; then, shrieking at the tops of\r\ntheir voices, they started on a frantic run up through the woods, never\r\npausing as they crossed the main road to glance the way of the bridge.\r\nAnne, clinging desperately to her precarious foothold, saw their flying\r\nforms and heard their shrieks. Help would soon come, but meanwhile her\r\nposition was a very uncomfortable one.\r\n\r\nThe minutes passed by, each seeming an hour to the unfortunate lily\r\nmaid. Why didn\xe2\x80\x99t somebody come? Where had the girls gone? Suppose they\r\nhad fainted, one and all! Suppose nobody ever came! Suppose she grew so\r\ntired and cramped that she could hold on no longer! Anne looked at the\r\nwicked green depths below her, wavering with long, oily shadows, and\r\nshivered. Her imagination began to suggest all manner of gruesome\r\npossibilities to her.\r\n\r\nThen, just as she thought she really could not endure the ache in her\r\narms and wrists another moment, Gilbert Blythe came rowing under the\r\nbridge in Harmon Andrews\xe2\x80\x99s dory!\r\n\r\nGilbert glanced up and, much to his amazement, beheld a little white\r\nscornful face looking down upon him with big, frightened but also\r\nscornful gray eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnne Shirley! How on earth did you get there?\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed.\r\n\r\nWithout waiting for an answer he pulled close to the pile and extended\r\nhis hand. There was no help for it; Anne, clinging to Gilbert Blythe\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhand, scrambled down into the dory, where she sat, drabbled and furious,\r\nin the stern with her arms full of dripping shawl and wet crepe. It was\r\ncertainly extremely difficult to be dignified under the circumstances!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat has happened, Anne?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Gilbert, taking up his oars. \xe2\x80\x9cWe were\r\nplaying Elaine\xe2\x80\x9d explained Anne frigidly, without even looking at her\r\nrescuer, \xe2\x80\x9cand I had to drift down to Camelot in the barge--I mean the\r\nflat. The flat began to leak and I climbed out on the pile. The girls\r\nwent for help. Will you be kind enough to row me to the landing?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGilbert obligingly rowed to the landing and Anne, disdaining assistance,\r\nsprang nimbly on shore.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m very much obliged to you,\xe2\x80\x9d she said haughtily as she turned away.\r\nBut Gilbert had also sprung from the boat and now laid a detaining hand\r\non her arm.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnne,\xe2\x80\x9d he said hurriedly, \xe2\x80\x9clook here. Can\xe2\x80\x99t we be good friends? I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\nawfully sorry I made fun of your hair that time. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t mean to vex\r\nyou and I only meant it for a joke. Besides, it\xe2\x80\x99s so long ago. I think\r\nyour hair is awfully pretty now--honest I do. Let\xe2\x80\x99s be friends.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor a moment Anne hesitated. She had an odd, newly awakened\r\nconsciousness under all her outraged dignity that the half-shy,\r\nhalf-eager expression in Gilbert\xe2\x80\x99s hazel eyes was something that was\r\nvery good to see. Her heart gave a quick, queer little beat. But the\r\nbitterness of her old grievance promptly stiffened up her wavering\r\ndetermination. That scene of two years before flashed back into her\r\nrecollection as vividly as if it had taken place yesterday. Gilbert had\r\ncalled her \xe2\x80\x9ccarrots\xe2\x80\x9d and had brought about her disgrace before the whole\r\nschool. Her resentment, which to other and older people might be as\r\nlaughable as its cause, was in no whit allayed and softened by time\r\nseemingly. She hated Gilbert Blythe! She would never forgive him!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d she said coldly, \xe2\x80\x9cI shall never be friends with you, Gilbert\r\nBlythe; and I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to be!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right!\xe2\x80\x9d Gilbert sprang into his skiff with an angry color in his\r\ncheeks. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll never ask you to be friends again, Anne Shirley. And I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t care either!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe pulled away with swift defiant strokes, and Anne went up the steep,\r\nferny little path under the maples. She held her head very high, but\r\nshe was conscious of an odd feeling of regret. She almost wished she had\r\nanswered Gilbert differently. Of course, he had insulted her terribly,\r\nbut still--! Altogether, Anne rather thought it would be a relief to\r\nsit down and have a good cry. She was really quite unstrung, for the\r\nreaction from her fright and cramped clinging was making itself felt.\r\n\r\nHalfway up the path she met Jane and Diana rushing back to the pond in\r\na state narrowly removed from positive frenzy. They had found nobody at\r\nOrchard Slope, both Mr. and Mrs. Barry being away. Here Ruby Gillis had\r\nsuccumbed to hysterics, and was left to recover from them as best she\r\nmight, while Jane and Diana flew through the Haunted Wood and across the\r\nbrook to Green Gables. There they had found nobody either, for Marilla\r\nhad gone to Carmody and Matthew was making hay in the back field.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Anne,\xe2\x80\x9d gasped Diana, fairly falling on the former\xe2\x80\x99s neck\r\nand weeping with relief and delight, \xe2\x80\x9coh, Anne--we thought--you\r\nwere--drowned--and we felt like murderers--because we had made--you\r\nbe--Elaine. And Ruby is in hysterics--oh, Anne, how did you escape?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI climbed up on one of the piles,\xe2\x80\x9d explained Anne wearily, \xe2\x80\x9cand Gilbert\r\nBlythe came along in Mr. Andrews\xe2\x80\x99s dory and brought me to land.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Anne, how splendid of him! Why, it\xe2\x80\x99s so romantic!\xe2\x80\x9d said Jane,\r\nfinding breath enough for utterance at last. \xe2\x80\x9cOf course you\xe2\x80\x99ll speak to\r\nhim after this.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course I won\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d flashed Anne, with a momentary return of her old\r\nspirit. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I don\xe2\x80\x99t want ever to hear the word \xe2\x80\x98romantic\xe2\x80\x99 again, Jane\r\nAndrews. I\xe2\x80\x99m awfully sorry you were so frightened, girls. It is all my\r\nfault. I feel sure I was born under an unlucky star. Everything I do\r\ngets me or my dearest friends into a scrape. We\xe2\x80\x99ve gone and lost your\r\nfather\xe2\x80\x99s flat, Diana, and I have a presentiment that we\xe2\x80\x99ll not be\r\nallowed to row on the pond any more.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne\xe2\x80\x99s presentiment proved more trustworthy than presentiments are apt\r\nto do. Great was the consternation in the Barry and Cuthbert households\r\nwhen the events of the afternoon became known.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you ever have any sense, Anne?\xe2\x80\x9d groaned Marilla.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes, I think I will, Marilla,\xe2\x80\x9d returned Anne optimistically. A good\r\ncry, indulged in the grateful solitude of the east gable, had soothed\r\nher nerves and restored her to her wonted cheerfulness. \xe2\x80\x9cI think my\r\nprospects of becoming sensible are brighter now than ever.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t see how,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d explained Anne, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve learned a new and valuable lesson today.\r\nEver since I came to Green Gables I\xe2\x80\x99ve been making mistakes, and each\r\nmistake has helped to cure me of some great shortcoming. The affair of\r\nthe amethyst brooch cured me of meddling with things that didn\xe2\x80\x99t belong\r\nto me. The Haunted Wood mistake cured me of letting my imagination run\r\naway with me. The liniment cake mistake cured me of carelessness in\r\ncooking. Dyeing my hair cured me of vanity. I never think about my hair\r\nand nose now--at least, very seldom. And today\xe2\x80\x99s mistake is going to\r\ncure me of being too romantic. I have come to the conclusion that it is\r\nno use trying to be romantic in Avonlea. It was probably easy enough in\r\ntowered Camelot hundreds of years ago, but romance is not appreciated\r\nnow. I feel quite sure that you will soon see a great improvement in me\r\nin this respect, Marilla.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sure I hope so,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla skeptically.\r\n\r\nBut Matthew, who had been sitting mutely in his corner, laid a hand on\r\nAnne\xe2\x80\x99s shoulder when Marilla had gone out.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t give up all your romance, Anne,\xe2\x80\x9d he whispered shyly, \xe2\x80\x9ca little\r\nof it is a good thing--not too much, of course--but keep a little of it,\r\nAnne, keep a little of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXIX. An Epoch in Anne\xe2\x80\x99s Life\r\n\r\n\r\n|ANNE was bringing the cows home from the back pasture by way of Lover\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nLane. It was a September evening and all the gaps and clearings in the\r\nwoods were brimmed up with ruby sunset light. Here and there the lane\r\nwas splashed with it, but for the most part it was already quite shadowy\r\nbeneath the maples, and the spaces under the firs were filled with a\r\nclear violet dusk like airy wine. The winds were out in their tops, and\r\nthere is no sweeter music on earth than that which the wind makes in the\r\nfir trees at evening.\r\n\r\nThe cows swung placidly down the lane, and Anne followed them dreamily,\r\nrepeating aloud the battle canto from _Marmion_--which had also been part\r\nof their English course the preceding winter and which Miss Stacy had\r\nmade them learn off by heart--and exulting in its rushing lines and the\r\nclash of spears in its imagery. When she came to the lines\r\n\r\n       The stubborn spearsmen still made good\r\n       Their dark impenetrable wood,\r\n\r\nshe stopped in ecstasy to shut her eyes that she might the better fancy\r\nherself one of that heroic ring. When she opened them again it was to\r\nbehold Diana coming through the gate that led into the Barry field and\r\nlooking so important that Anne instantly divined there was news to be\r\ntold. But betray too eager curiosity she would not.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t this evening just like a purple dream, Diana? It makes me so glad\r\nto be alive. In the mornings I always think the mornings are best; but\r\nwhen evening comes I think it\xe2\x80\x99s lovelier still.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a very fine evening,\xe2\x80\x9d said Diana, \xe2\x80\x9cbut oh, I have such news, Anne.\r\nGuess. You can have three guesses.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCharlotte Gillis is going to be married in the church after all and\r\nMrs. Allan wants us to decorate it,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Anne.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. Charlotte\xe2\x80\x99s beau won\xe2\x80\x99t agree to that, because nobody ever has been\r\nmarried in the church yet, and he thinks it would seem too much like a\r\nfuneral. It\xe2\x80\x99s too mean, because it would be such fun. Guess again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJane\xe2\x80\x99s mother is going to let her have a birthday party?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDiana shook her head, her black eyes dancing with merriment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t think what it can be,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne in despair, \xe2\x80\x9cunless it\xe2\x80\x99s that\r\nMoody Spurgeon MacPherson saw you home from prayer meeting last night.\r\nDid he?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should think not,\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Diana indignantly. \xe2\x80\x9cI wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be likely\r\nto boast of it if he did, the horrid creature! I knew you couldn\xe2\x80\x99t guess\r\nit. Mother had a letter from Aunt Josephine today, and Aunt Josephine\r\nwants you and me to go to town next Tuesday and stop with her for the\r\nExhibition. There!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Diana,\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Anne, finding it necessary to lean up against a\r\nmaple tree for support, \xe2\x80\x9cdo you really mean it? But I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid Marilla\r\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t let me go. She will say that she can\xe2\x80\x99t encourage gadding about.\r\nThat was what she said last week when Jane invited me to go with them\r\nin their double-seated buggy to the American concert at the White Sands\r\nHotel. I wanted to go, but Marilla said I\xe2\x80\x99d be better at home learning\r\nmy lessons and so would Jane. I was bitterly disappointed, Diana. I felt\r\nso heartbroken that I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t say my prayers when I went to bed. But I\r\nrepented of that and got up in the middle of the night and said them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Diana, \xe2\x80\x9cwe\xe2\x80\x99ll get Mother to ask Marilla. She\xe2\x80\x99ll be\r\nmore likely to let you go then; and if she does we\xe2\x80\x99ll have the time\r\nof our lives, Anne. I\xe2\x80\x99ve never been to an Exhibition, and it\xe2\x80\x99s so\r\naggravating to hear the other girls talking about their trips. Jane and\r\nRuby have been twice, and they\xe2\x80\x99re going this year again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m not going to think about it at all until I know whether I can go\r\nor not,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne resolutely. \xe2\x80\x9cIf I did and then was disappointed, it\r\nwould be more than I could bear. But in case I do go I\xe2\x80\x99m very glad my\r\nnew coat will be ready by that time. Marilla didn\xe2\x80\x99t think I needed a new\r\ncoat. She said my old one would do very well for another winter and\r\nthat I ought to be satisfied with having a new dress. The dress is very\r\npretty, Diana--navy blue and made so fashionably. Marilla always makes\r\nmy dresses fashionably now, because she says she doesn\xe2\x80\x99t intend to have\r\nMatthew going to Mrs. Lynde to make them. I\xe2\x80\x99m so glad. It is ever so\r\nmuch easier to be good if your clothes are fashionable. At least, it is\r\neasier for me. I suppose it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t make such a difference to naturally\r\ngood people. But Matthew said I must have a new coat, so Marilla\r\nbought a lovely piece of blue broadcloth, and it\xe2\x80\x99s being made by a real\r\ndressmaker over at Carmody. It\xe2\x80\x99s to be done Saturday night, and I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\ntrying not to imagine myself walking up the church aisle on Sunday in\r\nmy new suit and cap, because I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid it isn\xe2\x80\x99t right to imagine such\r\nthings. But it just slips into my mind in spite of me. My cap is so\r\npretty. Matthew bought it for me the day we were over at Carmody. It is\r\none of those little blue velvet ones that are all the rage, with gold\r\ncord and tassels. Your new hat is elegant, Diana, and so becoming. When\r\nI saw you come into church last Sunday my heart swelled with pride to\r\nthink you were my dearest friend. Do you suppose it\xe2\x80\x99s wrong for us to\r\nthink so much about our clothes? Marilla says it is very sinful. But it\r\nis such an interesting subject, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarilla agreed to let Anne go to town, and it was arranged that\r\nMr. Barry should take the girls in on the following Tuesday. As\r\nCharlottetown was thirty miles away and Mr. Barry wished to go and\r\nreturn the same day, it was necessary to make a very early start. But\r\nAnne counted it all joy, and was up before sunrise on Tuesday morning.\r\nA glance from her window assured her that the day would be fine, for\r\nthe eastern sky behind the firs of the Haunted Wood was all silvery\r\nand cloudless.
