I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe in girls going to college with the men\r\nand cramming their heads full of Latin and Greek and all that nonsense.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I\xe2\x80\x99m going to study Latin and Greek just the same, Mrs. Lynde,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nAnne laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m going to take my Arts course right here at Green\r\nGables, and study everything that I would at college.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Lynde lifted her hands in holy horror.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnne Shirley, you\xe2\x80\x99ll kill yourself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot a bit of it. I shall thrive on it. Oh, I\xe2\x80\x99m not going to overdo\r\nthings. As \xe2\x80\x98Josiah Allen\xe2\x80\x99s wife,\xe2\x80\x99 says, I shall be \xe2\x80\x98mejum\xe2\x80\x99. But I\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nhave lots of spare time in the long winter evenings, and I\xe2\x80\x99ve no\r\nvocation for fancy work. I\xe2\x80\x99m going to teach over at Carmody, you know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know it. I guess you\xe2\x80\x99re going to teach right here in Avonlea.\r\nThe trustees have decided to give you the school.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Lynde!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Anne, springing to her feet in her surprise. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, I\r\nthought they had promised it to Gilbert Blythe!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo they did. But as soon as Gilbert heard that you had applied for it\r\nhe went to them--they had a business meeting at the school last night,\r\nyou know--and told them that he withdrew his application, and suggested\r\nthat they accept yours. He said he was going to teach at White Sands. Of\r\ncourse he knew how much you wanted to stay with Marilla, and I must\r\nsay I think it was real kind and thoughtful in him, that\xe2\x80\x99s what. Real\r\nself-sacrificing, too, for he\xe2\x80\x99ll have his board to pay at White Sands,\r\nand everybody knows he\xe2\x80\x99s got to earn his own way through college. So the\r\ntrustees decided to take you. I was tickled to death when Thomas came\r\nhome and told me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t feel that I ought to take it,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Anne. \xe2\x80\x9cI mean--I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nthink I ought to let Gilbert make such a sacrifice for--for me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI guess you can\xe2\x80\x99t prevent him now. He\xe2\x80\x99s signed papers with the White\r\nSands trustees. So it wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t do him any good now if you were to\r\nrefuse. Of course you\xe2\x80\x99ll take the school. You\xe2\x80\x99ll get along all right,\r\nnow that there are no Pyes going. Josie was the last of them, and a\r\ngood thing she was, that\xe2\x80\x99s what. There\xe2\x80\x99s been some Pye or other going to\r\nAvonlea school for the last twenty years, and I guess their mission in\r\nlife was to keep school teachers reminded that earth isn\xe2\x80\x99t their home.\r\nBless my heart! What does all that winking and blinking at the Barry\r\ngable mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDiana is signaling for me to go over,\xe2\x80\x9d laughed Anne. \xe2\x80\x9cYou know we keep\r\nup the old custom. Excuse me while I run over and see what she wants.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne ran down the clover slope like a deer, and disappeared in the firry\r\nshadows of the Haunted Wood. Mrs. Lynde looked after her indulgently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s a good deal of the child about her yet in some ways.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s a good deal more of the woman about her in others,\xe2\x80\x9d retorted\r\nMarilla, with a momentary return of her old crispness.\r\n\r\nBut crispness was no longer Marilla\xe2\x80\x99s distinguishing characteristic. As\r\nMrs. Lynde told her Thomas that night.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMarilla Cuthbert has got _mellow_. That\xe2\x80\x99s what.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne went to the little Avonlea graveyard the next evening to put fresh\r\nflowers on Matthew\xe2\x80\x99s grave and water the Scotch rosebush. She lingered\r\nthere until dusk, liking the peace and calm of the little place,\r\nwith its poplars whose rustle was like low, friendly speech, and its\r\nwhispering grasses growing at will among the graves. When she finally\r\nleft it and walked down the long hill that sloped to the Lake of Shining\r\nWaters it was past sunset and all Avonlea lay before her in a dreamlike\r\nafterlight--\xe2\x80\x9ca haunt of ancient peace.\xe2\x80\x9d There was a freshness in the\r\nair as of a wind that had blown over honey-sweet fields of clover. Home\r\nlights twinkled out here and there among the homestead trees. Beyond lay\r\nthe sea, misty and purple, with its haunting, unceasing murmur. The west\r\nwas a glory of soft mingled hues, and the pond reflected them all in\r\nstill softer shadings. The beauty of it all thrilled Anne\xe2\x80\x99s heart, and\r\nshe gratefully opened the gates of her soul to it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear old world,\xe2\x80\x9d she murmured, \xe2\x80\x9cyou are very lovely, and I am glad to\r\nbe alive in you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHalfway down the hill a tall lad came whistling out of a gate before the\r\nBlythe homestead. It was Gilbert, and the whistle died on his lips as he\r\nrecognized Anne. He lifted his cap courteously, but he would have passed\r\non in silence, if Anne had not stopped and held out her hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGilbert,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, with scarlet cheeks, \xe2\x80\x9cI want to thank you for\r\ngiving up the school for me. It was very good of you--and I want you to\r\nknow that I appreciate it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGilbert took the offered hand eagerly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt wasn\xe2\x80\x99t particularly good of me at all, Anne. I was pleased to be\r\nable to do you some small service. Are we going to be friends after\r\nthis? Have you really forgiven me my old fault?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne laughed and tried unsuccessfully to withdraw her hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI forgave you that day by the pond landing, although I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know\r\nit. What a stubborn little goose I was. I\xe2\x80\x99ve been--I may as well make a\r\ncomplete confession--I\xe2\x80\x99ve been sorry ever since.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe are going to be the best of friends,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gilbert, jubilantly. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\r\nwere born to be good friends, Anne. You\xe2\x80\x99ve thwarted destiny enough. I\r\nknow we can help each other in many ways. You are going to keep up your\r\nstudies, aren\xe2\x80\x99t you? So am I. Come, I\xe2\x80\x99m going to walk home with you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarilla looked curiously at Anne when the latter entered the kitchen.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho was that came up the lane with you, Anne?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGilbert Blythe,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Anne, vexed to find herself blushing. \xe2\x80\x9cI met\r\nhim on Barry\xe2\x80\x99s hill.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t think you and Gilbert Blythe were such good friends that you\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nstand for half an hour at the gate talking to him,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marilla with a\r\ndry smile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe haven\xe2\x80\x99t been--we\xe2\x80\x99ve been good enemies. But we have decided that it\r\nwill be much more sensible to be good friends in the future. Were we\r\nreally there half an hour? It seemed just a few minutes. But, you see,\r\nwe have five years\xe2\x80\x99 lost conversations to catch up with, Marilla.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnne sat long at her window that night companioned by a glad content.\r\nThe wind purred softly in the cherry boughs, and the mint breaths came\r\nup to her. The stars twinkled over the pointed firs in the hollow and\r\nDiana\xe2\x80\x99s light gleamed through the old gap.\r\n\r\nAnne\xe2\x80\x99s horizons had closed in since the night she had sat there after\r\ncoming home from Queen\xe2\x80\x99s; but if the path set before her feet was to be\r\nnarrow she knew that flowers of quiet happiness would bloom along it.\r\nThe joy of sincere work and worthy aspiration and congenial friendship\r\nwere to be hers; nothing could rob her of her birthright of fancy or her\r\nideal world of dreams. And there was always the bend in the road!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98God\xe2\x80\x99s in his heaven, all\xe2\x80\x99s right with the world,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Anne\r\nsoftly.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nEnd of Project Gutenberg\xe2\x80\x99s Anne of Green Gables, by Lucy Maud Montgomery\r\n\r\n*** END OF THIS PROJECT GUTENBERG EBOOK ANNE OF GREEN GABLES ***\r\n\r\n***** This file should be named 45-0.txt or 45-0.zip *****\r\nThis and all associated files of various formats will be found in:\r\n        http://www.gutenberg.org/4/45/\r\n\r\nProduced by David Widger and Charles Keller\r\n\r\nUpdated editions will replace the previous one--the old editions will\r\nbe renamed.\r\n\r\nCreating the works from print editions not protected by U.S. copyright\r\nlaw means that no one owns a United States copyright in these works,\r\nso the Foundation (and you!) can copy and distribute it in the United\r\nStates without permission and without paying copyright\r\nroyalties.
